Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
Qur'anic Evidence Against Natural Properties
The author cites a Qur'anic verse against the doctrine of natural properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single Qur'anic verse refutes the doctrine that physical objects act by their own natures.

Core Idea
Adjacent lands producing different crops refute natural determinism.

The author now turns to scriptural evidence. After establishing rational arguments against natural properties, he cites a Qur'anic verse that shows how diverse outcomes arise from similar natural conditions. This verse points to a single wise Agent rather than inherent natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the English translation. Notice the brevity of the citation — the author assumes you know the full verse.
The full verse (13:4) mentions different gardens and crops.
2
Understand the context
Adjacent tracts of land share the same climate, soil, and water, yet produce different yields. This challenges the idea that nature alone determines outcomes.
3
Connect to the refutation
If natural properties were the sole cause, adjacent lands would yield identical results. The diversity shows an external will — that of Allah.
The author uses this as evidence that the doctrine of natural properties is invalid.
4
Reflect on the alternative
Ask yourself: Who or what causes the differences? The verse implies a Creator who chooses to create diversity.
This leads to affirming tawheed and divine causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودلالة بطلان القول بالطبائع من نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ قِطَعٌ مُتَجَاوِرَاتٌ ﴾ الآية.
And evidence for the invalidity of the doctrine of natural properties from His saying, exalted: 'And in the earth are adjacent tracts.'
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a specific Qur'anic evidence against the doctrine of natural properties (al-taba'i'). The author cites the verse from Surah al-Ra'd (13:4): 'And in the earth are adjacent tracts.' The full verse describes how diverse lands, despite being adjacent, produce different crops, indicating that nature does not act by itself but is controlled by a wise Creator. The 'adjacent tracts' show that the differences are not due to inherent natures but to the will of a single Maker.

Key Terms
الطبائع (natural properties/natures)
بطلان القول بالطبائع (invalidity of the doctrine of natures)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That natural properties are sufficient to explain all phenomena.
Correction: The verse shows that similar natural conditions produce different outcomes, proving that an external will (Allah) is the cause.

Checkpoint
Which verse does the author use to refute the doctrine of natural properties?
A) 'And in the earth are adjacent tracts'
B) 'Were they created from nothing?'
C) 'I do not love those that set'
D) 'And of His signs is that He created you from dust'

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verse about adjacent tracts demonstrates that differences in creation cannot be attributed to natural properties but to the will of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see people around you with similar backgrounds but vastly different outcomes in faith or worldly success, do you instinctively attribute it to their 'nature' or to Allah's will? What does this lesson demand you to change in that default assumption?
?
Think of an area in your life where you feel trapped by your 'natural abilities' (e.g., a character flaw, intelligence, or skill). How does the truth that even adjacent lands produce different crops by Allah's will free you to seek His intervention for transformation?
?
If you truly believed that everything you call 'nature' is under Allah's direct control, how would your daily prayers and trust in Him change starting today? What specific outcome would you plead with Allah to alter, just as He changes crops on identical land?

Proof That Accidents Cannot Persist by Themselves
Proof that accidents cannot persist, using succession of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof from the succession of opposites that accidents cannot persist by themselves, and the consequences of denying this.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot persist by themselves, otherwise they would never cease.

This passage presents a key argument in classical kalam: if accidents could persist by their own essence, they would never cease to exist. Yet we observe the succession of opposite accidents in the same locus, proving they do cease. The argument also refutes those who claim accidents only become latent and manifest, showing this leads to the eternity of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the logical structure and key terms like 'عرض' (accident) and 'بقاء' (persistence).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main premise and conclusion
What is the central claim being argued for? The passage aims to prove that accidents cannot persist by themselves. Find the premises that support this, especially the observation of opposing accidents succeeding each other.
The conclusion is explicitly stated near the end.
3
Analyze the argument's structure
Break down the argument into its steps: (1) If an accident persisted by itself, its non-existence would be inconceivable. (2) But we see opposites succeeding each other, so accidents do cease. (3) Those who deny cessation and claim latency join the Dahriyya. (4) This also prevents the annihilation of bodies, implying eternity of the world.
The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Consider the refutation of denial of cessation
The passage anticipates an objection: some might deny that accidents cease and instead claim they become latent. How does the author refute this? He says they would then join the materialists (Dahriyya) and that it is impossible to annihilate bodies, leading to the eternity of the world.
This shows the logical consequences of the opposing view.
5
Connect to the broader theological context
Understand why this argument matters: if accidents did not cease, the world would be eternal, contradicting the Islamic doctrine of creation. The argument defends the temporal nature of the world.
This links to the kalam cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما لم ينتقل إليه لما مر ، فينتقل إليه ثم يعدمه في الثاني فيؤدي إلى اجتماع الضدين في محل الضد الأول، ولا جائز أن يوجب عدمه في الثاني لأنا نقول: وجد في أي محل حتى يوجب عدم العرض الباقي عن المحل في الثاني، ويقسم الكلام ويبطل كل قسم على ما ذكرنا في الفصل المتقدم؟ ولأنه لو جاز وجودهما في زمان واحد جاز أيضا في الثاني والثالث إذ ليس تنافيهما في الثاني أولى من تنافيهما في الأول، ولأن عدم الباقي في الثاني لوجود ضده ليس بأولى من عدم ضده في الثاني لوجوده، والحالة الثانية لكل واحد منهما حالة البقاء، فثبت بما قررنا أن العرض لو كان باقيًا لذاته لما تصور عدمه، وحيث رأينا تعاقب الأعراض المتضادة على المحال شاهدة علمنا بطلان قولهم ضرورة إلا أن يجحدوا عدم الأعراض وادعوا فيها الظهور والكمون فيلتحقون حينئذ بإخوانهم الدهرية، وبطلان ذلك قد مر، مع أنهم لا يتجاسرون على إظهار ذلك خوفا من معرة السيف، وكذا على مذهبهم لا يمكن القول بانعدام الأجسام، إذ من مذهب من يناظرهم من الجبائية والبهشمية إذ انعدام الأجسام لا تكون إلا بحدوث الفناء وهو عرض حادث لا في محل مضاد للأجسام والجواهر، فيوجب عدمها في الثاني، وحدوث العرض لا في محل مستحيل، ولا طريق للإعدام عندهم إلا هذا فإذا تعذر القول بجواز انعدام الأجسام والجواهر والأعراض جميعًا، ولهذا أحال الجاحظ منهم عدم الأجسام، وأجمع العقلاء سوى الجاحظ أن كل موجود يمتنع عليه العدم فهو قديم، فصار قولهم مؤديا إلى القول بقدم العالم
What did not transfer to it due to what passed, so it transfers to it then annihilates it in the second, leading to the conjunction of two opposites in the locus of the first opposite. It is not permissible that it necessitates its non-existence in the second, for we say: it existed in any locus such that it necessitates the non-existence of the remaining accident from the locus in the second. And it divides speech and invalidates each division according to what we mentioned in the preceding chapter? And because if their coexistence in one time were permissible, then it would also be permissible in the second and third, since their mutual opposition in the second is not more likely than their mutual opposition in the first. And because the non-existence of the remaining in the second due to the existence of its opposite is not more likely than the non-existence of its opposite in the second due to its existence, and the second state for each of them is a state of persistence. Thus it is established by what we have determined that if the accident were persistent by itself, its non-existence would be inconceivable. And when we see the succession of opposing accidents upon the locus as evidence, we know the falsity of their statement necessarily, unless they deny the non-existence of accidents and claim for them manifestation and latency, in which case they then join their brothers the Dahriyya. And the falsity of that has already passed, especially since they do not dare to manifest that for fear of the disgrace of the sword. Likewise, according to their doctrine, it is impossible to speak of the annihilation of bodies, since according to the doctrine of those who debate them from the Jabba'iyya and Bahshamiyya, the annihilation of bodies only occurs through the occurrence of fana', which is a transient accident not in a locus, opposed to bodies and substances, and it necessitates their non-existence in the second. And the occurrence of an accident not in a locus is impossible. And there is no way for annihilation according to them except this. So when it is impossible to permit the annihilation of bodies, substances, and all accidents — and for this reason al-Jahiz among them denied the annihilation of bodies — and all rational people except al-Jahiz agree that every existent that is impossible for non-existence is eternal, so their statement leads to the statement that the world is eternal.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument) demonstrating that accidents cannot persist (baqa') by themselves. The argument proceeds as follows: (1) If an accident were persistent by itself, it could never cease to exist, because persistence implies continued existence. (2) But we observe the succession of opposite accidents on the same locus (e.g., black and white cannot coexist; when one appears, the other disappears). This succession proves that accidents do cease. (3) If the opponent denies that accidents cease and claims they only become latent (kumun) and manifest (zuhur), they align with the Dahriyya (materialists who deny accidents altogether). (4) Moreover, if accidents cannot cease, then bodies cannot cease either, because their annihilation requires a transient accident (fana') which is impossible as an accident not in a locus. (5) All rational people except al-Jahiz agree that anything that cannot cease is eternal, so the opponent's view implies the eternity of the world, which is false. Thus the original claim that accidents persist by themselves is refuted.

Key Terms
عرض
بقاء
ضد
عدم
فناء
جوهر
ظهور
كمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument assumes cessation without proof, or that accidents can persist and only become latent.
Correction: The argument is based on observed succession of opposites, which is empirical evidence. The claim of latency is refuted because it leads to absurd consequences.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the consequence if accidents persisted by themselves?
A) They would be eternal.
B) They would never cease.
C) They would become substances.
D) They would depend on God's will.

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot persist by themselves because we observe their cessation through the succession of opposites; denying this leads to the absurd consequence that the world is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every accident you experience—pleasure, pain, even your own thoughts—must eventually cease, how does that reshape your attachment to them? What would it mean to live as if this were true right now?
?
The proof shows that if anything could persist by itself, it would be eternal. How does this truth about accidents compel you to turn your heart only to the One who truly does persist without end?
?
The opponents' denial of the cessation of accidents leads them to the absurd conclusion that the world is eternal. Where in your own life have you acted as though some temporary creation—status, comfort, or opinion—would never pass away? What would it take to abandon that illusion today?

Proof that Ability is Concurrent with the Act
Al-Nasafi argues that power (qudra) cannot precede the act and must be simultaneous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ability (istita'a) must be an accident concurrent with the act, and how a preceding power leads to logical contradictions.

Core Idea
Power concurrent with the act, not before it — or absurdities follow.

Al-Nasafi presents a rigorous argument to settle the theological debate on whether ability precedes the act. Many Mu'tazila held that power is prior; al-Nasafi shows that this leads to impossible conclusions. In this lesson, you will follow his logical chain and see why ability is necessarily simultaneous with the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow come through before interpreting.
This is a dense logical proof; it may require re-reading.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that al-Nasafi begins by establishing that power (qudra) is an accident that does not endure. This is a crucial foundation.
Many Mu'tazila agreed on this point.
3
Consider the consequence if power precedes
If power preceded the act and did not endure to the second moment, it would be non-existent at the time of the act. This leads to a series of contradictions.
Pay attention to the words 'فيكون الفعل مستحيل الوجود من القادر واجب الوجود من العاجز'.
4
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
Al-Nasafi shows that if preceding power were possible, then power and incapacity would become indistinguishable, and the act would be impossible from the powerful and necessary from the incapable.
This is the core of the proof.
5
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends with a question: if the act can exist without power, what purpose does power serve? This emphasizes the need for simultaneous cause.
This question underscores the absurdity of the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أن الاستطاعة التي يتعلق بها وجود الفعل عرض وهو مستحيل البقاء فنقول: الاستطاعة عرض يكون مع الفعل. إن القدرة لما كانت غير قابلة للبقاء، وساعدنا عليه كثير من المعتزلة، وأثبتنا ذلك بالدليل على من أبي القول، ولو كانت هي متقدمة على الفعل ولا بقاء إلى الثاني من الأوقات كانت عدمًا وقت وجود الفعل، فيوجد الفعل ولا قدرة، وهي لا تنعدم عمن يجوز قيامها به إلا عما يضادها وهو العجز، ومن انعدمت قدرته يستحيل اتصافه بكونه قادرًا، ومن قام به العجز يستحيل أن لا يكون عاجزًا، فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الفعل مستحيل الوجود من القادر واجب الوجود من العاجز، ومن هذا قوله، فهو قد أصيب بعقله ولم يشعر. يحققه أن الفعل لو جاز وجوده مع انعدام القدرة بل وجب هي لا تنعدم إلا إلى عجز، واستحال وجوده مع وجودها لكانت القدرة هي العجز والعجز هو القدرة، فإنا لا نعرف العجز إلا بأن يستحيل معه الفعل والقدرة إلا بأن يجب بها الفعل ويثبت. ثم يقال لهم: لما كان الفعل متصور الوجود مع انعدام القدرة بل هو واجب الوجود في هذه الحالة، فأي فائدة لوجود القدرة؟ وأي حاجة إليها؟ وأي أثر؟
And when it is established that the ability (istitāʿa) by which the existence of the act is connected is an accident and it is impossible for it to endure, we say: The ability is an accident that is with the act. Indeed, when power (qudra) was not susceptible to endurance—and many of the Muʿtazila agreed with us on this, and we proved it by evidence against whoever refused the statement—and if it were prior to the act with no endurance to the second moment, it would be non-existence at the time of the act's existence. Then the act would occur with no power, and it (power) is not annihilated from one in whom it is allowed to subsist except by what opposes it, which is incapacity (ʿajz). And one whose power is annihilated, it is impossible for him to be described as powerful. And one in whom incapacity subsists, it is impossible that he should not be incapable. So according to this estimation, the act would be impossible of existence from the powerful and necessary of existence from the incapable. And from this is his statement; he has indeed been struck in his mind without feeling. It verifies that if the act were possible of existence with the annihilation of power—rather it would be necessary—and it (power) is only annihilated to incapacity, and its existence with its (power's) existence is impossible, then power would be incapacity and incapacity would be power. For we do not know incapacity except as that with which the act is impossible, and power except as that by which the act is necessitated and established. Then it is said to them: Since the act is conceivable of existence with the annihilation of power—rather it is necessary of existence in this state—what benefit is there in the existence of power? And what need for it? And what effect?
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlāl) that ability (istitāʿa) must be an accident concurrent with the act, not preceding it. The argument proceeds as follows: it has been established that power (qudra) is an accident that does not endure, a point agreed upon by many Muʿtazila. If power preceded the act and did not endure to the second moment, it would be non-existent at the time of the act. Consequently, the act would occur when there is no power. But power is only annihilated by its opposite, incapacity (ʿajz). If power is annihilated, the person becomes incapable, and a person with incapacity cannot be powerful. So the act would be impossible from a powerful agent and necessary from an incapable one—a contradiction. This leads to the conclusion that power and incapacity would become identical, because the act would be possible without power and impossible with it. The final rhetorical question emphasizes the uselessness of precursing power: if the act can exist without it, what purpose does power serve?

Key Terms
الاستطاعة
القدرة
العجز
العرض
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ability must precede the act to allow free choice.
Correction: Al-Nasafi proves that preceding power leads to logical absurdities, so ability must be simultaneous with the act.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's argument, what would happen if power (qudra) preceded the act and did not endure?
A) The act would occur while the agent is simultaneously powerful and incapable.
B) The act would be impossible because the agent would lack power.
C) The act would occur without power, and the agent would be incapable, making the act necessary from an incapable agent.
D) The agent would continue to have power without interruption.

Key Takeaway
Ability must be concurrent with the act; otherwise, power and incapacity become indistinguishable, leading to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you feel as if your own power was sufficient, or do you sense that God gave you the ability at that very moment? In light of this lesson's argument that ability is concurrent with the act, reflect on a specific action you did today. Did you take credit for the ability, or did you acknowledge God's immediate grant of it? Commit to saying 'bi qudratillah' (by God's power) before your next action.
?
If power cannot precede the act, then every moment you are acting, God is creating both the power and the act. Does this make you feel helpless or dependent? Examine your heart: Is there resentment in feeling that your actions are not truly yours? Or does it increase your reliance on God? Spend a minute in silent acknowledgment of your absolute need for His power at every instant.
?
The passage argues that if power preceded the act, the act would be impossible from the powerful and necessary from the incapable—a logical absurdity. Have you ever acted while feeling incapable, thinking you could manage on your strength alone? Think of a time you tried to worship while feeling weak. Did you still succeed? According to this lesson, your power was given at that very moment. How does that change your understanding of 'I couldn't do it'? What would it mean to pray with the certainty that God grants ability exactly when needed?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
Power Must Exist at the Time of the Act
Nasafi proves that created power must be present at the moment of action using analogy of tools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why created power must be present at the moment of action, based on the deficiency of created power and the absurd consequences of its absence.

Core Idea
Power must be simultaneous with the act; otherwise, absurdities follow.

Nasafi presents a tight logical proof that created power (qudra) must coincide with the act. He begins by noting the deficiency of created power and its need for tools, contrasting it with Allah's perfect power. Then he argues by analogy and deeper reasoning that if power were absent at the time of the act, it would lead to contradictions undermining the very inference of God's power from creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: created power is deficient
Read the first part where Nasafi states that created power requires tools to complete it, while Allah's power is complete and independent. This sets the foundation.
Note the contrast between created and divine power.
2
Follow the analogy argument
Nasafi argues that if an act could occur without power, then it could also occur without its completion means (hand, leg, knife), which is absurd. Trace this analogy carefully.
The analogy is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the deeper proof
Read the part where Nasafi shows that if power is absent, the act's existence would indicate lack of power in the agent, contradicting the inference of God's power from creation.
This connects to tawhid.
4
Consider the verification
Nasafi concludes that if power is not present at the time of the act, then power before and after are equally nonexistent, which is impossible. Understand this final step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: لما كانت القدرة المحدثة ناقصة ولم يكن بد من وجود الآلات والأسباب لتكمله هي ، إذ الآلة أعدت لتكميل القدرة الناقصة، ولهذا يستحيل أن يكون الله تعالى فاعلاً بالآلة؛ لأن قدرته كاملة غير ناقصة فكانت مستغنية عما يكمل به، ثم لو جاز وجود الفعل مع انعدام حقيقة القدرة جاز أيضا مع انعدام ما أعد تتميمًا لها فيقبض الإنسان حال عدم اليد، ويمشي حال عدم الرجل، ويقطع حال عدم السكين، وحيث استحال ذلك وإن كانت هذه الآلات موجودة قبل ذلك بلا فصل فكذا في حقيقة القدرة. وفساد هذا مما لا يخفى على من رزق أدنى لب، فكذا هذا. والذي يقرر ذلك : أن القدرة لو لم يكن بها فعل وهي موجودة ويكون بها وهي غير موجودة فيكون سبب وجود الفعل عدم القدرة لا وجودها، فيصير القول به قولا بوجود الفعل لعدم القدرة، فيكون الفعل دليلاً على أن ليس الفاعل بقادر ولا على أنه قادر ، وبالفعل استدلوا على أن الله تعالى قادر، فبطل موضع الاستدلال بالشاهد ووقع على العكس ، وكان ينبغي أن يستدل بوجود الفعل على أن الفاعل ليس بقادر ، وفيه بطلان القول بالتوحيد، ولأن القدرة إذا كانت لا تنفع وهي موجودة، فوجودها وقت الوجود وعدمها بمنزلة كانت أولى أن لا تنفع وهي معدومة، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالفعل من غير القدرة وإهدارها، وهذا محال. يحققه أنها إذا لم تكن موجودة وقت الفعل فلا فرق بين قدرة توجد قبل الفعل وبين قدرة توجد بعد الفعل لاستوائهما في العدم وقت الفعل، والقول بكونها بعد الفعل محال، فكذا هذا.
Then we say: Since created power is deficient and there is no escape from the existence of tools and causes to complete it—because the tool is prepared to complete deficient power—therefore it is impossible for Allah to act through a tool, because His power is complete, not deficient, and thus independent of what completes it. Then if the existence of an act were permissible with the non-existence of the reality of power, it would also be permissible with the non-existence of what was prepared to complete it: a person would grasp without a hand, walk without a leg, cut without a knife. Since that is impossible, even though these tools existed just before, so too for the reality of power. The corruption of this is not hidden from one who has been granted the slightest kernel of intellect, so likewise this. What establishes that is: if the act could occur without power while power exists, and could occur with power while power does not exist, then the cause of the act's existence would be the absence of power, not its presence. Thus this position becomes a statement that the act exists due to the absence of power, so the act would indicate that the agent is not powerful, not that he is powerful. Yet by the act they infer that Allah is powerful, so the place of inference from the witnessed is invalidated and turned to the opposite, and one ought to infer from the act's existence that the agent is not powerful. That implies the invalidation of the statement of tawhid. And because if power is of no benefit when it exists, then its existence at the time of existence and its non-existence are equal, and it is even more fitting that it be of no benefit when it is non-existent. That necessitates affirming the act without power and nullifying power, and this is impossible. He verifies it: if it is not present at the time of the act, then there is no difference between a power that exists before the act and a power that exists after the act, because both are nonexistent at the time of the act; and the statement that it is after the act is impossible, so likewise this.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a proof that power (qudra) must be present at the moment of action. Nasafi begins by noting that created power is deficient and requires tools and causes to complete it, whereas God's power is complete and independent of tools. He then argues by analogy: if an act could occur without power, then it could also occur without its completion means (like hand, leg, knife), which is absurd. He then deepens the argument: if power exists but act is without it, or power is absent at the time of act, then absence of power becomes the cause, and the act would indicate lack of power in the agent, contradicting the inference of God's power from creation. He further states that if power is useless when present, it is even more useless when absent, leading to the nullification of power. Finally, he verifies that if power is absent at the time of the act, power before and after are equally nonexistent at that time, which is absurd. Thus power must be present simultaneously with the act.

Key Terms
قدرة
آلة
فعل
اضطرار
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power only needs to exist before the act, not necessarily at the same time.
Correction: This passage demonstrates that if power is not simultaneous, the act would indicate the agent's lack of power, leading to absurdities that contradict tawhid.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi argue that if power is not present at the time of the act, it would lead to absurdity in determining the agent's ability?
A) It would make the act impossible to occur.
B) It would make the act indicate that the agent is not powerful.
C) It would require the act to be eternal.
D) It would negate the existence of the act entirely.

Key Takeaway
Created power must be present simultaneously with the act; otherwise, the act would falsely indicate the agent's impotence, contradicting the inference of God's power from creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own actions, do you truly believe that your ability to act comes solely from the power Allah has given you at that moment? When you sin, do you attribute it to a lack of divine power or to your own deficient power? How does this truth change how you approach your daily actions and dependence on Allah?
?
If power must be simultaneous with the act, then every moment you perform an action, you are directly dependent on Allah's creation of that power. Does that realization fill you with humility and gratitude? How will you remind yourself of this dependency tomorrow?
?
The passage warns that if power were absent during the act, the act would indicate the agent's impotence. How often do you act without acknowledging that your ability to do so is a gift from Allah at that very instant? What would it take for you to live with constant awareness of this simultaneous power?

The Impossibility of Action by a Non-Contemporaneous Power
The author states the objection and provides the hand analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that a power not contemporaneous with its act cannot produce it, regardless of whether the power is prior by one moment or many.

Core Idea
All non-contemporaneous powers are equally absent at the time of action.

This lesson presents a key objection against the Basrian Mu'tazilite view that a power can exist prior to its act. The argument uses the simple idea that if a power is not present when the act occurs, it cannot be the cause of that act. The author shows that this is true whether the power is absent for one time or many, because the absence is the same.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Basrian claim
The Basrians held that a power can precede its act in time. The objection here targets that view.
The author assumes the Basrian position for argument.
2
Read the first part of the passage
Read from 'قيل لهم' to 'فكذا هذا'. This is the initial objection and the hand analogy.
3
Identify the key premise
The author argues that a power not contemporaneous with the act is impossible. He then extends this to show that a power prior by one time is also impossible because both are non-existent at the time of the act.
4
Consider the hand analogy
Do you not see that striking with a hand that existed and then ceased is impossible even if the hand was present just before? The same applies to power.
The analogy drives home the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : إذا كانت هذه القدرة الثابتة لفعل آخر لا لهذا الفعل ويستحيل تعلق الفعل المقارن لها بها بل تعلقه بالقدرة الأولى وحصوله بها لا بهذه، وكان وجود هذه القدرة في حق الفعل وعدمها بمنزلة أن القدرة على فعل لا يوجب زوال العجز عن فعل آخر، ولزمكم جميع ما ألزمناكم، ولا تخلص لكم بوجه من الوجوه. والذي يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه أن الخصوم ساعدونا على استحالة وجود الفعل بقدرة متقدمة عليه بأوقات كثيرة، فكذا يستحيل وجوده بقدرة متقدمة عليه بوقت واحد لاستوائهما في العدم وقت الفعل، ألا ترى أن البطش بيد كانت ثم انعدمت محال وإن اتصل وجود الفعل بزمان وجود اليد إذا كانت منعدمة وقت الفعل. كما أنه محال في زمان وجد بعد زمان انعدام اليد بمدة مديدة لاستوائهما في عدم اليد زمان البطش فكذا هذا، ويخرج هذا الكلام على طريق السؤال من وجهين: أحدهما من حيث القدرة، وهو أن القدرة لما لم تكن إلا للفعل وقد تخلو عنه وقتا لم لا يجوز أن تخلو عنه أوقاتا؟ وهذا السؤال يرد أيضا على من يقول ببقاء القدرة من البصريين. والثاني: من حيث الفعل، وهو أنه لما جاز الفصل بالقدرة في الوقت الثاني وإن كانت القدرة فيه منعدمة لم لا يجوز الفعل بها في العاشر وإن كانت منعدمة وتقرير السؤال بالآلة كاليد والسكين وغيرهما، أنه لما استحال حصول الفعل بهما بعد العدم في الوقت العاشر من زمان العدم، فكذا استحال في الثاني منه لاستواء الوقتين في العدم.
It was said to them: If this power established for another act, not for this act, and it is impossible for the act simultaneous with it to be linked to it rather its linkage is to the first power and its occurrence by it not by this one, and the existence of this power with respect to the act and its non-existence are like the fact that power over an act does not necessitate removal of incapacity from another act, then all that we charged you with is binding upon you, and you have no escape in any way. And what indicates the correctness of what we hold is that the opponents agreed with us on the impossibility of the existence of an act by a power prior to it by many times, so likewise it is impossible for it to exist by a power prior to it by one time because they are equal in non-existence at the time of the act. Do you not see that striking with a hand that existed and then ceased is impossible even if the existence of the act is connected to the time of existence of the hand if the hand is non-existent at the time of the act. Just as it is impossible at a time that occurs after the time of the hand's non-existence by a long period because they are equal in the non-existence of the hand at the time of striking, so too this. And this statement issues as a question in two ways: one of them from the perspective of power, namely that since power is only for the act and it may be vacant of it for a time, why may it not be vacant for times? And this question also applies to those who hold the persistence of power from the Basrians. And the second: from the perspective of the act, namely that since separation by power in the second time is permissible even though power is non-existent in it, why may not the act be done by it in the tenth even though it is non-existent? And the formulation of the question by the instrument like the hand and knife etc., that since the occurrence of the act by them after non-existence in the tenth time of the time of non-existence is impossible, so too it is impossible in the second because the two times are equal in non-existence.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the Mu'tazilite view that a power can exist prior to its act. The author argues that if a power not contemporaneous with the act cannot produce it, then a power earlier by one time is also impossible because both are absent at the moment of the act. He then divides the argument into two questions: one concerning the power itself, and one concerning the act. The first question asks why power cannot be empty of its act for many times if it can be empty for one. The second asks why if an act can be at two times removed from its power, it cannot be at ten times removed, using the analogy of an instrument like a hand: if you cannot strike with a hand that existed but is now gone, it doesn't matter whether the hand was gone for one moment or many.

Key Terms
قدرة
فعل
بطش
يد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a power can act even if it is not present at the moment of action, as long as it existed earlier.
Correction: The argument shows that non-existence at the time of action prevents causation, regardless of how long the power has been absent.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why is an act impossible by a power that is prior by one time?
A) Because one time is too long.
B) Because the power is non-existent at the time of the act.
C) Because the Basrians deny it.
D) Because the hand analogy fails.

Key Takeaway
Non-existence of a power at the time of action makes it impossible for that power to produce the act, whether the interval is short or long.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that a non-contemporaneous power cannot produce an action. How does that reshape your understanding of relying on past deeds or past spiritual states? Are you trusting in a past 'power' that is no longer present with you today? What will you do to ensure your power to obey is present each moment?
?
Think of a sin you commit repeatedly despite having 'resolved' in the past. Are you relying on a past intention to produce the action of obedience now, while your will is absent at the moment? What must change so that your power to obey is present with you each moment?
?
If God's power alone is always contemporaneous with every moment, why do you still act as if your own capacities exist independent of Him? Reflect on a moment today when you attributed your action to your own 'ability' without recognizing His enabling. How will you consciously acknowledge His power in your next action?

Two Perspectives on the Power-Act Relationship
The author presents two questions and uses the instrument analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct questions arising from the objection: one focusing on the power's ability to be vacant from its act, and the other on the act's distance from its power.

Core Idea
If a power can be empty for one moment, why not for many? If an act can happen at a later time, why not much later?

After establishing the core objection, the author raises two specific questions that further challenge the Basrian position. The first question concerns the power itself: if a power can exist without its act for one moment, why can it not do so for many? The second concerns the act: if an act can be performed at a time removed from its power, why can it not be performed at a much later time? These questions are also supported by the instrument analogy.

Guided Path
1
Review the overall objection
Recall that the main argument showed the impossibility of an act by a non-contemporaneous power. Now the author explores two consequences.
2
Read the two questions
Read from 'ويخرج هذا الكلام' to 'استواء الوقتين في العدم'. Identify the two questions: one about power, one about act.
3
Understand the power question
If power is only for the act and can be empty for one time, why not for many? This applies even to the Basrian view of power's persistence.
4
Understand the act question
If separation by power in the second time is permissible, why not in the tenth? The instrument analogy shows that both are impossible because the instrument is absent.
The analogy of hand and knife is used.
5
Relate to the main argument
Both questions highlight that the Basrians cannot set an arbitrary limit on the temporal gap between power and act.

If God were to withdraw that power for even an instant, you could do nothing. Is there any area of your life where you have acted as if your own power is permanent? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of your complete dependence on God's ongoing creative act?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
The two meanings of qudra and the three states of man
Al-Nasafi refutes the intermediate state claim by distinguishing two meanings of qudra and three human states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of qudra (power) according to the opponent's own doctrine and the three states of man regarding any action, and how al-Nasafi uses this to refute the claim of an intermediate state between power and inability.

Core Idea
There are only three states for action: impossibility, possibility, and actuality—no in-between.

Al-Nasafi begins by diagnosing the opponent's error as stemming from ignorance of their own doctrine and of the nature of possibility. He then clarifies that 'power' (qudra) has two distinct meanings, and based on that, he outlines three exhaustive states for any human action. This passage is a key refutation of the claim that there can be an intermediate state between power and inability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of qudra
In the opponent's view, qudra refers to two things: the soundness of causes and instruments, and the real power (an unknown meaning that produces the act). Understand each definition and how they relate to the occurrence of an action.
The first meaning is often called 'power of causes' and the second is the 'real power'.
2
Consider the three states of man
Al-Nasafi describes three states: absence of both powers, presence of only the first power, and presence of both powers. For each state, note whether the act is impossible, possible, or existent.
The state where only causes are present makes the act possible, meaning it can become actual if the person intends it.
3
See how the refutation works
The opponent's claim of an intermediate state is refuted because these three states cover all possibilities. There is no fourth state between power and inability, as the opponent alleged.
Al-Nasafi's classification is exhaustive and leaves no room for the intermediate state.
4
Connect to the broader kalam debate
This classification is used to argue about the timing of power relative to the act. It sets the stage for later discussions on whether power exists before, during, or after the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأقول في الكشف عن هذا التمويه – وبالله التوفيق: إن هذا الكلام مع هذا التطويل والتهويل صدر عن الجهل بمذاهب الخصوم وجعل ما هو عند خصمه اسم لشيئين متغايرين اسمًا لشيء واحد، وعن الجهل بحقيقة الإمكان، وذلك لأن عند خصمه القدرة اسم لشيئين: أحدهما: سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات، وهي تسمى قدرة لحدوث القدرة فيها في المعتاد عند قصد الفصل وامتناع حدوثها عند فواتها. والثاني: المعنى المجهول للفعل وهو حقيقة القدرة، وهو الذي نتكلم فيه بعد وقبل. وللإنسان أحوال ثلاثة: حالة عدم الأسباب وفساد الآلات أو عدمها، وكان الفعل فيها ممتنعاً لا لفساد الآلات بل لانعدام القدرة، إلا أن وجود القدرة مع فسادها ممتنع. وحالة وجود سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات وعدم حقيقة القدرة وكان الفعل فيه ممكنا إذ لو قصد الفعل لحصلت له حقيقة القدرة وإن لم يقصد لم يحصل. وحالة وجود سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات ووجود القدرة التي اختلفنا في وجودها وقت الفعل وعدمها وكان الفعل فيها موجودًا فكانت حالة وجود الفعل حالة وجود القدرتين جميعًا، وحالة إمكانه حالة إحدى القدرتين وهي قدرة الأسباب والآلات لا غير، وحالة امتناعه حالة فوات القدرتين جميعًا.
I say in uncovering this obfuscation – and with God is success: This speech, with all its length and terror, issued from ignorance of the doctrines of the opponents and from making what in the opponent's view is a name for two distinct things a name for one thing, and from ignorance of the reality of possibility. That is because in the opponent's view 'power' (qudra) is a name for two things: one: the soundness of causes (salamat al-asbab) and the validity of instruments (sihhat al-alat), and it is called power because power occurs in it in the customary way when one intends the act and its occurrence is prevented when it is lost. The second: the unknown meaning (al-ma'na al-majhul) for the act, which is the reality of power (haqiqat al-qudra), and it is what we discuss after and before. Man has three states: a state of absence of causes and corruption of instruments or their absence, and the act therein is impossible (mumtani') not due to the corruption of instruments but due to the non-existence of power, except that the existence of power with its corruption is impossible. And a state of existence of soundness of causes and validity of instruments and absence of the reality of power, and the act therein is possible (mumkin), for if he intended the act, the reality of power would be acquired for him, and if he did not intend, it would not be acquired. And a state of existence of soundness of causes and validity of instruments and existence of the power about which we disagreed regarding its existence at the time of the act and its non-existence, and the act therein is existent. So the state of existence of the act is a state of existence of both powers together, and the state of its possibility is a state of one of the two powers, namely the power of causes and instruments only, and the state of its impossibility is a state of the loss of both powers together.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by al-Nasafi of a previous argument that claimed there is an intermediate state between power (qudra) and inability ('ajz). Al-Nasafi begins by stating that the lengthy and bombastic argument stems from two forms of ignorance: ignorance of the opponents' actual doctrines, and ignorance of the real nature of possibility (imkan). He then explains the error: the opponent treats 'power' as a single term, whereas in the opponent's own school it actually refers to two distinct things. The first meaning is the soundness of causes (al-asbab) and the validity of instruments (al-alat); this is called power because real power customarily occurs in it when one intends an act, and it is absent when these are lost. The second meaning is the real power (haqiqat al-qudra), which is an unknown meaning (ma'na majhul) that produces the act. Al-Nasafi notes that this real power is what he discusses both before and after the act. Based on these two meanings, al-Nasafi then describes the three possible states of a human being with respect to any action. First, the state where causes and instruments are absent or corrupted: the act is impossible, not because of the instruments' corruption but because power is absent; it is impossible for power to exist when instruments are corrupt. Second, the state where causes and instruments are present but real power is absent: the act is possible (mumkin), meaning that if the person intends it, real power will be acquired; if not, it will not be acquired. Third, the state where both causes/instruments and real power are present: the act actually occurs. Al-Nasafi then summarizes that the existence of the act corresponds to the existence of both powers, its possibility corresponds to the existence of only the first power (causes and instruments), and its impossibility corresponds to the loss of both powers. This classification definitively refutes the claim of an intermediate state between power and inability: the three states cover all possible cases.

Key Terms
الْقُدْرَة
الْإِمْكَان
الْإِمْتِنَاع
سَلَامَة الْأَسْبَاب
صِحَّة الْآلَات
حَقِيقَة الْقُدْرَة
الْمَعْنَى الْمَجْهُول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is an intermediate state between power and inability.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows there are only three exhaustive states: impossibility, possibility, and actuality, based on the two meanings of qudra.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, how many states of man regarding an action are there, and what defines the state where the act is possible?
A) Three states; the possible state is when both powers are present.
B) Two states; the possible state is when causes are sound but real power is absent.
C) Three states; the possible state is when causes are sound but real power is absent, so the act can be performed if intended.
D) Four states; the possible state is when the act actually occurs.

Key Takeaway
The human action is governed by three states based on the presence or absence of the two types of power, leaving no room for an intermediate state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intend to do an action, do you truly believe that the power to accomplish it comes from God alone, or do you secretly attribute it to your own abilities? This passage teaches that real power (haqiqat al-qudra) is a mysterious gift that appears only when you act. How can you train yourself to feel that dependence every time you move your hand or speak a word?
?
Consider the state of possibility: when all the causes are in place but you have not yet intended the act. In that moment, are you aware that the act is not yet within your power? How does this realization affect your reliance on God's timely granting of power? Commit to a specific dua you will make before any action to acknowledge that real power is from Him alone.
?
Al-Nasafi says the opponent's error came from ignorance of the reality of possibility. Have you ever fallen into a similar error by imagining that your future actions are already guaranteed by your current abilities? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that your power to act is renewed moment by moment by the Creator?

Definition of 'ajz and its two types
Al-Nasafi defines 'ajz as opposite of qudra and divides it into two types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's definition of 'ajz as the opposite of qudra and the distinction between two types of inability: one permanent and one temporary that can be overcome by intention.

Core Idea
Inability is not always permanent; some inability can be overcome by intending the act.

Al-Nasafi provides a nuanced definition of inability ('ajz). He explains that it is the opposite of power (qudra) and that rational people see no middle ground between them. This passage clarifies that inability arises from two distinct causes: loss of external means or loss of intrinsic power. It then divides inability into two types based on whether recovery of power is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage aloud and then read the English translation. Note the structure: definition, cause, example, classification.
The passage ends abruptly, but the main ideas are complete.
2
Identify the definition of 'ajz
Find where al-Nasafi defines 'ajz. He says it is the opposite of qudra, with no intermediate state.
This is a key foundational concept in kalam.
3
Understand the two ways inability is established
Inability arises either from the loss of sound causes and instruments, or from the loss of real power (haqiqat al-qudra). Notice the example of the man and the 100 ratls.
The example illustrates the second way.
4
Distinguish the two types of 'ajz
Al-Nasafi names two types: one where no power is hoped for (due to lost causes) and one where power is hoped for (due to lost real power). Understand the different outcomes for the 'impossible' object.
The first type is absolute; the second leads to mumkin.
5
Reflect on theological implications
Consider how this classification affects human responsibility and divine power. Why might al-Nasafi need to distinguish these types?
This connects to broader Maturidi thought on human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العجز يقابل القدرة، ولن يعرف العقلاء بينهما واسطة في حق من يصح اتصافه بها كالاجتماع والافتراق والحركة والسكون في حالة البقاء، ثم إذا فاتت سلامة الأسباب والآلات ثبت العجز، وإذا فاتت حقيقة القدرة ثبت العجز أيضا على ما بينا أن الإنسان بالآلة يحمل مائة رطل ثم قد لا يقدر إلا على حمل خمسين رطلاً مع بقاء سلامة الأسباب، فهو إذا عاجز وإن لم تفت القدرة الأولى، والفعل مع العجز غير متحقق، وجاء من هذا أن العجز على نوعين: عجز لا يرجى ثبوت القدرة عقيب قصد الفعل مقارنا للفعل في غالب العادة وهو العجز الثابت عند فوت سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات. وعجز يرجى ثبوت القدرة بل تحصل القدرة عقيب وجود القصد مقارناً للفعل كالموجود المتحقق لوجوده في غالب العادة عقيب قصد الفعل مقارنا للفعل بناء للأمر على المعتاد، فالمعجوز عنه لفوت هذه القدرة يسمى ممكنا، والمعجوز عنه لفوت القدرة الأولى التي لا يرجى ثبوت القدرة الحقيقة مع فوتها يسمى
Then inability ('ajz) corresponds to power, and the rational people do not know any intermediate between them regarding the one who can be described with them, like conjunction and separation and motion and rest in the state of remaining. Then if the soundness of causes and instruments is lost, inability is established; and if the reality of power is lost, inability is also established, as we explained that a man with an instrument carries one hundred ratls, then he may only be able to carry fifty ratls while the soundness of causes remains; so he is then incapable ('ajiz) even if the first power is not lost, and the act with inability is not realized. From this it follows that inability is of two types: an inability for which the establishment of power is not hoped for after intending the act, concurrent with the act in the usual custom, and it is the inability established when the soundness of causes and validity of instruments is lost. And an inability for which the establishment of power is hoped for, rather power occurs after the existence of intention concurrent with the act, like the existent that is realized for its existence in the usual custom after intending the act, concurrent with the act, building the matter on customary practice. So that which is impossible (al-ma'juz 'anhu) due to the loss of this power is called possible (mumkin), and that which is impossible due to the loss of the first power, for which the establishment of real power is not hoped for when it is lost, is called
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of inability ('ajz). Al-Nasafi states that 'ajz is the opposite of power (qudra), and rational people recognize no intermediate state between them for those who can be described by them – just as there is no intermediate between conjunction and separation, motion and rest, in terms of remaining in one state. He then explains that inability is established either when the soundness of causes and instruments is lost, or when the reality of power (haqiqat al-qudra) is lost. To illustrate the latter, he gives an example: a man who can carry one hundred ratls using an instrument may later be unable to carry even fifty ratls even though the instrument remains sound; that man is incapable because the real power is gone, even though the first power (the instrument) is still present. From this, al-Nasafi concludes that 'ajz has two types. The first type is an inability for which the acquisition of power is not hoped for after intending the act; it is the inability arising from the loss of sound causes and instruments. The second type is an inability for which the acquisition of power is hoped for; indeed, power occurs after intentional effort, as is customarily observed. Then al-Nasafi explains that the thing impossible due to the loss of the second type of power (where hope exists) is called 'possible' (mumkin) because it can be realized by intending. The thing impossible due to the loss of the first type of power (where hope does not exist) is left unnamed as the text ends abruptly, but it is presumably called 'impossible' (mumtani') in contrast. This classification refines the concept of inability: not all inability is irremediable; some is only temporary and can be overcome by intention.

Key Terms

الْقُدْرَة
الْمُعْجُوزُ عَنْهُ
الْمُمْكِن
سَلَامَة الْأَسْبَاب
صِحَّة الْآلَات
حَقِيقَة الْقُدْرَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All inability is permanent and irreversible; once incapable, one cannot become capable again.
Correction: Al-Nasafi identifies a type of inability where real power is lost but can be recovered by intending the act, making the object possible (mumkin) rather than absolutely impossible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what distinguishes the second type of 'ajz from the first?
A) It is caused by the loss of both causes and real power.
B) It occurs when the first power (causes) is lost, and no real power is hoped for.
C) It occurs when real power is lost but sound causes remain, and real power can be acquired by intending the act.
D) It is a permanent state with no possibility of action.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's classification of 'ajz into two types shows that not all inability is absolute; some can be overcome through intention, which has implications for human responsibility and the concept of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a time when you felt powerless to change a situation. Was that feeling rooted in a loss of external means or a loss of inner real power? How does this lesson's distinction between the two types of 'ajz call you to re-evaluate your own self-assessment? What will you do differently the next time you face such a feeling?
?
Al-Nasafi teaches that some inability can be overcome by sincere intention. Is there something you have been convinced is impossible for you that might actually be within reach if you truly intended it? What concrete step can you take today to test that possibility?
?
The passage says rational people see no intermediate between power and inability. Are you living in a gray zone of half-hearted effort, deceiving yourself that you are 'trying' when you have not fully engaged your real power? What is blocking you from committing fully to what you claim to want?

Abu Hashim's Concession and Its Refutation
Abu Hashim's concession and its refutation by intuition and causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Hashim's proposal of eternal inactivity is both intuitively absurd and logically inconsistent with the principle of causality.

Core Idea
Abu Hashim's eternal inactivity is rationally impossible and violates the cause-effect relationship.

This passage presents a crucial turning point in the debate about the capable agent. Abu Hashim attempts to resolve an objection by allowing the agent to remain inactive forever, but the author demonstrates that this concession is both intuitively absurd and logically inconsistent with the nature of capability and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's position
Notice that Abu Hashim permits the capable agent to be free of action and omission for all eternity, even with all means available.
3
Understand the intuitive refutation
The author argues that every rational person knows intuitively that a capable agent cannot remain inactive eternally. Reflect on why this seems self-evident.
4
Understand the causal refutation
The author introduces the cause-effect analogy: capability (istita'a) is like a cause that must produce its effect. Eternal inactivity would mean the cause exists without its effect, which is impossible.
5
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this argument applies to divine attributes: Allah's power must be exercised, otherwise it would be absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما رأى أبو هاشم توجه هذا الإلزام عليهم سوى بين الأمرين، وأجاز خلو القادر - مع توفر آلاته وانعدام الموانع أبد الدهر عن الفعل والترك لا يفعل فعلاً ولا يباشر مأمورًا ولا ضده الذي هو تركه ولا يرتكب منهيًا ولا ضده الذي هو تركه. وهذا مما يعرفه كل عاقل ببديهة عقله أن خلو القادر عن الحركة والسكون الاختياريين محال ممتنع مع هذا هو قول باطل هادم لقواعد مذهبه، وذلك لأن الاستطاعة تجري مع العلة من المعلول، وهو ابتداء دليل لنا في [؟] وخلو العلة عن المعلول ممتنع، وفيما قاله أبو هاشم خلوها عنه أبد الدهر، وفيما قاله غيره من أهل نحلته خلوها عنه زمانًا واحدًا، وكل ذلك باطل.
When Abu Hashim saw this objection directed against them, he equated the two matters and permitted the capable agent—with the availability of his instruments and the absence of obstacles for all eternity—to be free of act and omission, not doing any act, nor performing a commanded act nor its opposite which is its omission, nor refraining from a prohibited act nor its opposite which is its omission. And this is something that every rational person knows by the intuition of his intellect: that the freedom of the capable agent from voluntary motion and rest is impossible and absurd. Along with this, it is a false statement that destroys the foundations of his school. That is because capability (al-istita'a) runs with the cause (al-'illa) from the effect (al-ma'lul), and this is the beginning of a proof for us that the freedom of the cause from the effect is impossible. And as for what Abu Hashim said—its freedom from it for all eternity—and as for what others of his sect said—its freedom from it for one moment—all of that is false.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage recounts Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i's response to the earlier objection. To avoid the dilemma, he equates the two moments and allows the capable agent to remain completely inactive for all eternity, even with all tools available. The author then refutes this by appealing to common sense: every rational person knows that a capable agent cannot be devoid of voluntary motion forever. He then introduces the analogy with cause and effect: capability (istita'a) runs like a cause with its effect, so the cause cannot exist without the effect. Abu Hashim's eternal freedom implies the cause existing without effect, which is impossible. The same applies to those who allow it for one moment.

الاستطاعة
العلة

بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Abu Hashim's position is a viable compromise in the debate about divine attributes and human action.
Correction: The author shows that allowing eternal inactivity is absurd to common sense and violates the necessary connection between capability and action, making the position self-defeating.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's concession of eternal inactivity is rationally impossible and violates the necessary relationship between cause and effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have the ability to pray, give charity, and seek knowledge, yet you delay these acts. ?
The lesson teaches that capability necessitates action. In what area of your life does your capability (knowledge, resources, health) clearly not match your deeds? If your capability is a cause, what does its lack of effect reveal about the sincerity of your intention? Repent and act without delay.

The Impossibility of a Cause Without Its Effect
The author applies the cause-effect principle to refute the Mu'tazila, using motion as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili concept of specification of legal causes is rationally impossible because it implies a cause existing without its effect, leading to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
A cause must produce its effect; otherwise, absurdities follow.

In this passage, the author refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their allowance for a cause to exist without its effect leads to contradictions. Using the example of motion and a moving body, he argues that if motion could exist without making the body moving, then motion would be in a non-moving body, which is impossible. This establishes the necessary connection between cause and effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms: cause (العلة), effect (المعلول), motion (الحركة), body (الجسم), moving (المتحرك).
The Arabic includes parentheses; they are part of the original text.
2
Identify the example and its absurd consequence
The author uses the example of motion subsisting in a body as the cause of the body becoming moving. If the cause could exist without the effect, then motion could subsist in a body that is not moving. This is rationally absurd. Similarly for blackness and every attribute.
The absurdity is that an attribute would exist in something lacking that attribute.
3
Understand the broader implication for causality
The author uses this example to argue against the Mu'tazili position that power can be free of action. The principle that cause and effect are inseparably connected is upheld by Sunni scholars and is a matter of rational consensus.
This connects to larger debates about causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا أبى المحققون من أئمتنا في الفقه المتمسكون بمذهب السنة والجماعة القول بتخصيص العلل الشرعية ونسبوه إلى المعتزلة، لما فيه من القول بخلو العلة عن المعلول، وكذا قالوا باقتران المعلولات بعللها ولم يجوزوا تقدم العلل عليها. ثم ما يحقق استحالة القول بجواز خلو العلة عن المعلول أزمنة كثيرة أو زمانًا واحدًا أن قيام الحركة بالجسم علة الصيرورته متحركا (فكذا السواد مع الأسود وكذا كل صفة مع ما قامت به، وكان في القول بما يقوله المعتزلة جواز خلو قيام الحركة بالجسم عن صيرورته متحركا) فتكون الحركة قائمة بما ليس بمتحرك والسواد قائمًا بما ليس بأسود، وهذا بإجماع العقلاء باطل خارج عن المعقول، فكذا هذا.
Because of this, the verifiers among our imams in jurisprudence, who adhere to the school of the Sunnah and the community, rejected the specification of legal causes and attributed it to the Mu'tazila, because it involves the claim of the cause being free of the effect. And likewise they affirmed the conjunction of effects with their causes and did not permit the priority of causes over them. Then what confirms the impossibility of the claim permitting the cause to be free of the effect for many times or for one time is that the subsistence of motion in a body is the cause of its becoming moving (and likewise blackness with the black, and likewise every attribute with that in which it subsists). And it would follow from the statement of what the Mu'tazila say—permitting the subsistence of motion in a body without its becoming moving—that motion would subsist in what is not moving, and blackness would subsist in what is not black. And this, by consensus of rational people, is false and outside the rational. So likewise this [is false].
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy. The author first notes that the Sunni jurists reject the Mu'tazili concept of specification of legal causes because it implies a cause existing without its effect. Then he provides a concrete example: motion (qiyam al-haraka) in a body is the cause of its becoming moving. If it were possible for the cause to exist without the effect, then motion could subsist in a body without that body being moving, which is absurd. The same applies to blackness and every attribute. By consensus of rational people, this is impossible. Therefore, the Mu'tazili position that power can be free of action is equally impossible.

المعلول
الحركة
الجسم
المتحرك
السواد
الأسود
المحققون
العلل الشرعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe a cause can exist without its effect (e.g., power without action, specification of legal causes).
Correction: This passage shows that if a cause could exist without its effect, we would have absurdities like motion in a non-moving body, which is rationally impossible.

Checkpoint

B) Motion and a moving body
C) Life and knowledge
D) Power and will

Key Takeaway
The necessary connection between cause and effect is rationally evident; denying it leads to absurdity, as shown by the example of motion and a moving body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Power as Cause: Refuting the Bahshamiyya's Distinction
Author argues power is cause; Bahshamiyya object with sabab/'illa distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that power is a cause for action and the Bahshamiyya's objection that it is merely a reason.

Core Idea
Power is a cause, not just a reason, for voluntary acts.

This passage presents a key argument in the Maturidi theory of action: that power (qudra) functions as a cause ('illa) for the act, not merely as a reason (sabab). The author uses the contrast between involuntary and voluntary movement to prove this, and then introduces the Bahshamiyya's competing view. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Maturidi position on divine causality and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Note how the author constructs the proof by comparing two types of movement.
The passage ends abruptly; the Bahshamiyya's definition is cut off.
2
Understand the proof
Identify the key premise: there is no act without power. Then note the equality of involuntary and voluntary movements except for power's attachment. That attachment makes the act voluntary.
The author uses this to show power runs as a cause.
3
Contrast the Bahshamiyya view
The Bahshamiyya reject that power is a cause; they say it is a reason. Consider what difference this makes for attributing acts to agents.
Their distinction is not accepted by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل ما ادعينا من جريان القدرة مع الفعل مجرى العلل المعلولات أن لا مع فعل لمن لا قدرة له ولا ثبوت له إلا بذاتها، وما افترقت حركة المرتعش وحركة المختار في كون الثانية فعلاً لمن قام به دون الأولى إلا لثبوت الثانية بقدرته، وخروج الأولى عن قدرة من قامت هي به إذ فيما وراء تعلق القدرة بأحدهما دون الأخرى منهما مساواة من جمع الوجوه. والبهشمية يأبون القول بأن القدرة تجري مجرى العلل للأفعال، ويزعمون أنها تجري مجرى الأسباب للمسببات ويقولون في الفرق بين السبب والعلة: إن
And the proof of what we claim—that power runs with action in the manner of causes with effects—is that there is no act with one who has no power, and no establishment [of the act] except by its essence. And what distinguishes the movement of the tremoring person from the movement of the voluntary agent, in that the second is an act for the one in whom it subsists while the first is not, except by the establishment of the second by his power, and the first's being outside the power of the one in whom it subsists, since beyond the attachment of power to one of them without the other, there is equality in all respects. And the Bahshamiyya refuse the statement that power runs in the manner of causes for actions, and they claim that it runs in the manner of reasons (asbab) for effects (musabbabat), and they say concerning the difference between the reason (al-sabab) and the cause (al-'illa): that...
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage provides the proof that power is like a cause for action. The author points out that there is no action without power, and that the difference between involuntary movement (like that of a tremoring person) and voluntary movement is that the latter is established by power. Since both movements are similar in other respects, the distinguishing factor must be power, showing that power is the cause of the act. Then he mentions the Bahshamiyya (a Mu'tazili subsect) who reject this causal relationship; they say power is like a reason (sabab) rather than a cause ('illa). They define the difference between sabab and 'illa, but the text cuts off. The author clearly disagrees with their view.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Bahshamiyya's distinction between sabab and 'illa is a valid objection that weakens the causal link between power and action.
Correction: The author demonstrates that power must be a cause because it is the sole factor distinguishing voluntary from involuntary movement. Without causality, no act can be attributed to the agent.

?

?
When you sin, do you think of it as something that happened to you or as something you did? How would viewing your power as the cause of your actions change the way you seek forgiveness and resolve to change?

intermediate
6 min
The Impossibility of a Cause Being Devoid of Its Effect
Nasafi proves the impossibility of a cause existing without its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a cause cannot exist without its effect, as proven by Nasafi's reductio argument using the examples of motion and blackness.

Core Idea
A cause without its effect is as absurd as motion without a moving body.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi refutes the claim that a cause can be separated from its effect, even for a moment. He grounds his argument in the consensus of rational people and uses clear analogies to show the logical contradiction. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi first dismisses the opposing view as false before providing a proof.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Nasafi gives the example of motion: motion is the cause of a body becoming moving. If a cause could exist without its effect, motion could exist in a body without that body being moving. Identify why this is impossible.
The same applies to blackness and a black object.
3
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider why this argument leads the Hanafi scholars to reject the Mu'tazila view of 'particularization of legal causes'. How does this relate to the principle that effects must accompany their causes?
This lesson establishes a key causal principle in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدهر، وفيما قاله غيره من أهل نحلته خلوها عنه زمانا واحدًا، وكل ذلك باطل. ولهذا أبى المحققون من أئمتنا في الفقه المتمسكون بمذهب السنة والجماعة القول بتخصيص العلل الشرعية ونسبوه إلى المعتزلة، لما فيه من القول بخلو العلة عن المعلول، وكذا قالوا باقتران المعلولات بعللها ولم يجوزوا تقدم العلل عليها. ثم ما يحقق استحالة القول بجواز خلو العلة عن المعلول أزمنة كثيرة أو زمانًا واحدًا أن قيام الحركة بالجسم علة الصيرورته متحركا فكذا السواد مع الأسود وكذا كل صفة مع ما قامت به، وكان في القول بما يقوله المعتزلة جواز خلو قيام الحركة بالجسم عن صيرورته متحركا فتكون الحركة قائمة بما ليس بمتحرك والسواد قائمًا بما ليس بأسود، وهذا بإجماع العقلاء باطل خارج عن المعقول، فكذا هذا.
Eternity, and in what others of his sect said, [is] its being devoid of it for a single time, and all that is false. Because of this, the verifiers among our imams in fiqh, who adhere to the way of the Sunna and the community, refused the [view of] particularization of legal causes and attributed it to the Mu'tazila, because of what is in it of the statement that the cause is devoid of the effect. Likewise they affirmed the conjunction of effects with their causes and did not permit the precedence of causes over them. Then what verifies the impossibility of the statement permitting the cause to be devoid of the effect for many times or a single time is that the subsistence of motion in the body is the cause of its becoming moving, and similarly blackness with the black thing, and similarly every attribute with that which it subsists in. And in the statement of what the Mu'tazila say—permitting the subsistence of motion in the body to be devoid of its becoming moving—the motion would subsist in what is not moving, and blackness would subsist in what is not black. And this, by consensus of the rational people, is false and outside the rational. So likewise this.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the impossibility of a cause existing without its effect, using a reductio ad absurdum. The author first notes that some have claimed that a cause can be devoid of its effect for a time, but he declares this false. Then he states that the verifiers among the Hanafi imams refused the Mu'tazilī view of 'particularization of legal causes' because it implies that a cause can be without its effect. Instead, they affirmed the conjunction of effects with their causes and denied the precedence of causes over them. The proof: the subsistence of motion in a body is the cause of its becoming moving; if it were possible for the cause to exist without the effect, then motion could subsist in a body without that body being moving, which is absurd. The same applies to blackness and a black object. Since rational people agree that a motion cannot exist in a non-moving body, the same holds for all causes and effects. Thus the claim that a cause can be separate from its effect is impossible.

تخصيص العلل الشرعية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause can exist for a period of time without producing its effect (e.g., motion in a body without the body moving).
Correction: Nasafi proves this leads to logical absurdity, as motion cannot subsist in a non-moving body, by rational consensus.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Nasafi use to prove that a cause cannot be devoid of its effect?
A) Fire and burning
B) Movement and the moving body
C) Heat and cold
D) Writing and the writer

Key Takeaway
A cause cannot exist without its effect, as proven by the absurdity of motion without a moving body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Power as the Cause of Action
Nasafi proves that power stands to action as cause to effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi proves that power is the cause that distinguishes voluntary actions from involuntary movements.

Core Idea
Power is what makes an act your own.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a concise argument for the causal role of power in human action. By contrasting voluntary movement with involuntary trembling, he shows that the key difference lies in the presence of power. Read on to see the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the argument settle before analyzing.
2
Notice the contrast between two movements
Focus on the difference between حركة المرتعش (tremor) and حركة المختار (voluntary movement). What makes one an 'action' and the other not?
The key is that only the second subsists by the agent's power.
3
Understand the conclusion
Nasafi argues that apart from power, the two movements are equal in all respects. Thus power is the cause that makes an act attributable to the agent.
This establishes the parallel between power and cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل ما ادعينا من جريان القدرة مع الفعل مجرى العلل المعلولات أن لا مع فعل لمن لا قدرة له ولا ثبوت له إلا بذاتها، وما افترقت حركة المرتعش وحركة المختار في كون الثانية فعلاً لمن قام به دون الأولى إلا لثبوت الثانية بقدرته، وخروج الأولى عن قدرة من قامت هي به إذ فيما وراء تعلق القدرة بأحدهما دون الأخرى منهما مساواة من جمع الوجوه.
And the proof of what we claim—that power runs with action in the course of causes to effects—is that there is no action for one who has no power, and no subsistence for the action except by the essence of power. And the tremor movement and the voluntary movement differed only in that the second is an action for the one in whom it subsists unlike the first, due to the second's subsistence by his power, and the first's exclusion from the power of the one in whom it subsists, since beyond the attachment of power to one of them without the other, they are equal from every aspect.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the relationship between power and action is like that between cause and effect. Nasafi argues: (1) One who has no power has no action, and action subsists only by the essence of power. (2) The difference between involuntary trembling (حركة المرتعش) and voluntary movement (حركة المختار) is that only the latter is attributed as an 'action' to the agent because it subsists by his power. The tremor movement is not within the power of the one who moves, so it is not his action. Apart from the attachment of power to one and not the other, the two movements are equal in all other respects. Thus power is what makes an act an act, i.e., the cause of the action's being the agent's own doing.
