Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some deny that power is a cause of action, claiming that actions can occur without power.
Correction: Nasafi shows that without power, there is no action. The contrast between tremor and voluntary movement proves that power is what makes an act attributable to the agent.

Checkpoint

A) The agent's intention

C) The movement subsists by the agent's power
D) The movement is external

Key Takeaway
Power is the cause that makes an action attributable to the agent, distinguishing voluntary acts from involuntary movements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Your prayer—do you rush through it as if it's a tremor, or do you stand with presence as a voluntary act of worship? If power is what makes an action count, what does that demand from you the next time you stand before Allah?

Refuting the Bahshamiyya's Cause-Reason Distinction
Nasafi refutes the Bahshamiyya's distinction between cause and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Bahshamiyya's distinction between cause and reason fails to solve the problem of causal necessity in kalam.

Core Idea
Renaming cause as reason does not escape the necessity of the effect.

Nasafi now engages a specific Mu'tazili subgroup, the Bahshamiyya, who attempt to avoid saying that God's power is a 'cause' of actions. They propose a terminological distinction between cause and reason. In this passage, Nasafi dismantles their argument and shows that the same problem persists regardless of the term used.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the technical terms: سبب, علة, حال, ذات.
2
Identify the Bahshamiyya's definition
Find where they define cause (علة) as necessitating a state (حال) and reason (سبب) as necessitating an essence (ذات). Understand their example of motion and the state of being moving.
This distinction is meant to allow power to be a 'reason' rather than a 'cause'.
3
Follow Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi first dismisses the distinction as 'nonsense' (هذيان), referring to earlier refutations. Then he points out that even if power is a reason, it still necessitates the act by its essence, so separation is impossible.
The contradiction: they admit reason necessitates by essence, yet they want to avoid the implication of constant conjunction.
4
Connect to the broader issue
This argument is part of the larger debate about whether God's power is always connected to its effect. The Bahshamiyya attempt to avoid determinism, but Nasafi shows their move fails.
The refutation relies on the principle that anything that necessitates by essence cannot be without its effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبهشمية يأبون القول بأن القدرة تجري مجرى العلل للأفعال، ويزعمون أنها تجري مجرى الأسباب للمسببات ويقولون في الفرق بين السبب والعلة: إن العلة ما توجب حالاً، والسبب ما توجب ذاتا، وكذا سمعت بعضهم ويعنون بذلك أن الحركة علة لثبوت حال للجسم يفارق بها ذاتاً ليست بمتحركة، فكانت الحال مقتضاة للحركة، ومقتضية لكون الجسم متحركا على ما بينا هذا الهذيان في مسألة الصفات وكشفنا عن وجوه بطلانه. والقدرة توجب ذاتا وهو الفعل، والفعل في نفسه ليس بحال بل الحال من مقتضيات الفعل، وهذا كله مع بطلانه لا ينفعهم لأنهم أقروا أن السبب يوجب المسبب وإيجابه إياه باعتبار ذاته، فلا يجوز خلو عن ذلك الإيجاب، وفيه ما أوردنا على سلفه.
And the Bahshamiyya refuse the statement that power runs in the course of causes for actions, and they claim that it runs in the course of reasons for caused things. And they say concerning the difference between the reason and the cause: the cause is that which necessitates a state accidentally, and the reason is that which necessitates an essence. Thus I heard some of them, and they mean by that that motion is a cause for the subsistence of a state in the body by which it differs from an essence that is not moving, so the state is necessitated by motion and necessitates the body's being moving, as we have explained this nonsense in the issue of attributes and uncovered aspects of its falsehood. And power necessitates an essence, which is the act, and the act in itself is not a state, but rather the state is from the necessitants of the act. And all this, despite its falsehood, does not benefit them, because they have affirmed that the reason necessitates the caused thing, and its necessitation of it is by virtue of its essence, so it is not permissible to be devoid of that necessitation. And in it is what we have brought against his predecessors.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Bahshamiyya's attempt to avoid calling power a cause by distinguishing between 'cause' (علة) and 'reason' (سبب). They define cause as that which necessitates a state (like motion causing the state of being moving), and reason as that which necessitates an essence (like power necessitating the act as an essence). Nasafi dismisses this as nonsense, already refuted in the discussion of attributes. He points out that even if power is a 'reason,' they still admit that a reason necessitates its caused thing by its essence, so it cannot be devoid of that necessitation—thus the same problem remains: power must be inseparable from its effect (the act). This refutation shows that the distinction does not solve the issue of separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think renaming 'cause' to 'reason' avoids the problem of necessary connection between power and its effect.
Correction: Any factor that necessitates an effect by its essence cannot be separated from that effect, regardless of terminology.

Checkpoint

C) Cause is physical, reason is metaphysical

Key Takeaway
The Bahshamiyya's terminological shift from cause to reason does not eliminate the logical necessity between power and its effect.

Refuting the Mu'tazili Reading of 'Take with Strength'
Refuting Mu'tazili interpretation of the strength verse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi interpretation of the verse 'Take what We have given you with strength' refutes the Mu'tazili claim that ability precedes the act.

Core Idea
The verse proves ability must be simultaneous with the act, not before.

The Mu'tazila use this verse to argue that ability precedes the act. But as we will see, the verse actually supports the opposite: that strength must exist at the time of the action. This lesson unpacks the logical refutation and shows how the author reinterprets the verse to defend the Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili claim
The Mu'tazila argue that the word 'strength' in the verse refers to an ability that must exist before the act, to enable it. This leads to the doctrine of 'ability before the act'.
2
Read the author's response
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the author first interprets the Mu'tazila's proof verses as referring to a different kind of ability (safety of means), then presents the verse as his own proof.
3
Analyze the key argument
Focus on the sentence: 'for it is only taking with strength if the strength exists at the time of taking.' Think about what this implies about the temporal relationship.
4
Note the impossibility of survival
The author argues that if strength existed before, it would have to survive until the act, which is impossible for an accident. This is a crucial point.
This assumes the Ash'ari/Maturidi view that accidents do not endure.
5
Consider the alternative interpretation
Finally, note that some scholars interpret 'strength' as 'earnestness and perseverance', which completely removes any reference to ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونصرف العناية بعد هذا إلى دفع شبهات الخصوم وحل إشكالاتهم فنقول وبالله المعونة: ما تتعلق به المعتزلة من الآيات التي فيها إشارة إلى تقدم الاستطاعة، محمول على الاستطاعة الأولى التي هي سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات، وقوله تعالى: (خُذُوا مَا آتَيْنَاكُم بِقُوَّةٍ ) دليلنا ؛ لأنه إنما يصير أخذا بالقوة إذا كانت القوة وقت الأخذ موجودة، فأما إذا كانت هي معدومة وقت الأخذ فلا يكون أخذا بقوة كالأخذ باليد، ثم ليس من ضرورة الأخذ بالقوة تقدمها على الأخذ بل وجودها عند الأخذ، ومن ضرورة وجودها عند (٥) الأخذ أن لا تكون موجودة قبله (٦) لاستحالة بقائها. على (۷) أن أهل (۸) التأويل قالوا : ( خُذُواْ مَا ءَاتَيْنَكُم بِقُوَّةٍ ) أي: بجد ومواظبة.
We now turn our attention to refuting the doubts of the opponents and solving their problems. We say, and with Allah is assistance: What the Mu'tazila cling to from the verses that indicate the precedence of ability (istita'a) is to be interpreted as referring to the first ability, which is the safety of means and soundness of instruments. And the saying of the Exalted: 'Take what We have given you with strength' is our proof; for it is only taking with strength if the strength exists at the time of taking. If it is nonexistent at the time of taking, then it is not taking with strength, like taking with the hand. Further, it is not necessary for taking with strength that strength precedes the taking, but rather its existence at the time of taking. And it is necessary for its existence at the time of taking that it was not existing before it, because its survival (baqa') is impossible. Moreover, the people of interpretation (ahl al-ta'wil) said: 'Take what We have given you with strength' means with earnestness and perseverance.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili argument that the verse 'Take what We have given you with strength' proves that ability precedes the act. The author argues that the 'strength' in the verse refers to the initial ability (safety of means and soundness of instruments), not the ability for the act itself. Moreover, the very phrase 'taking with strength' implies that the strength must be present at the time of taking, not before. If strength were not present at the time, it would not be taking with strength. Additionally, since strength (as an accident) cannot survive, if it preceded the act, it would be nonexistent at the time of the act. The author also mentions that some interpreters explain 'strength' as earnestness and perseverance, which further removes the notion of a separate preceding ability.

Key Terms

القوة
الأخذ
السلامة
الآلات
التأويل
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Take with strength' proves that ability must exist before the act.
Correction: The verse actually shows that strength must be present at the time of the act, not before. It does not support the Mu'tazili doctrine of ability preceding the act.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Ability as Accident: Why It Cannot Precede the Act
Proving that ability cannot precede the act because it is an accident that cannot survive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi argument concludes that ability must be simultaneous with the act based on the nature of accidents and the impossibility of their survival.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot survive; thus ability must coincide with the act.

This passage presents a concise logical argument rooted in the nature of accidents. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, builds on the premise that originated ability is an accident—a property that does not persist over time. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the Maturidi position on the relationship between human ability and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then read the literal translation. Note the compact style and the logical flow.
Focus on the conditional reasoning: 'if...then' structures.
2
Identify the core premise
Identify the statement that 'the originated ability is an accident, and the accident's survival is impossible.' This is the foundational premise of the argument.
An 'accident' (ard) is a property that does not subsist by itself and, in Maturidi theology, cannot remain over two moments.
3
Trace the logical consequence
Follow the reasoning: if ability preceded the act, it would perish before the act occurs, making the act impossible without ability. Alternatively, if ability does not survive, and it precedes, then at the moment of the act ability is absent, making the act impossible or necessary—both absurd.
The argument reduces the idea of ability preceding the act to a contradiction.
4
Examine the objection and response
Consider the objection based on 'tajaddud al-mithl' (renewal of the like). Understand how the author distinguishes two cases: if the renewed ability is for the same act, then it is simultaneous (conceding the point); if it is for a different future act, then the current act lacks ability altogether.
The objection attempts to salvage the precedence of ability by positing a new ability at the time of the act, but the argument shows this fails.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Summarize the argument in your own words: ability cannot precede the act; it must exist at the same moment as the act.
This conclusion supports the Maturidi view that human ability is created by Allah concurrently with the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك أيضًا أن القدرة الحادثة عرض والعرض يستحيل بقاؤه فلو كانت سابقة على الفعل لانعدمت حال وجود الفعل فيستحيل الفعل بدون القدرة ، وإذا ثبت أن الاستطاعة ليست بباقية فلو تقدمت على الفعل لانعدمت وقت الفعل وصار حصول الفعل في حال وجود القدرة مستحيلا وفي انعدامها واجبًا وهذا محال فإن قيل : القدرة موجودة وقت الفعل على القول بتجدد المثل. قلنا: القدرة التي تحدث مقارنة لفعل إن كانت قدرة هذا الفعل المقترن ثبت المُدَّعَى وإن كانت قدرة فعل آخر تتعقبها كان كل فعل وجد وجد بلا قدرة. راجع: حاشية زين الحنفي على المسايرة ص ۱۱۷.
And among that also is that the originated ability (al-qudra al-haditha) is an accident (ard), and the accident's survival (baqa') is impossible. So if it preceded the act, it would become nonexistent at the moment of the act's existence, and then the act becomes impossible without ability. And if it is established that ability does not survive, then if it preceded the act, it would be nonexistent at the time of the act, and the occurrence of the act in the state of the existence of ability becomes impossible, and in its nonexistence it becomes obligatory, and this is absurd. If it is said: 'Ability exists at the time of the act according to the theory of the renewal of the like (tajaddud al-mithl),' we say: The ability that occurs concomitant with the act – if it is the ability of this concomitant act, then the claim is established; and if it is the ability of another act that follows it, then every act that occurs would occur without ability.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is an additional argument from the nature of accidents. It argues that ability (qudra) is an accident, and accidents cannot persist (survival is impossible). Therefore, if ability preceded the act, by the time the act occurs, the ability would have ceased to exist, making the act impossible. The author then addresses a potential objection: the theory of tajaddud al-mithl (renewal of the like), which suggests that a similar accident can be created anew at each moment. The response is that if a new ability arises at the moment of the act, then that new ability is the one causing the act, not the preceding one. Thus the preceding ability is irrelevant, and the argument stands.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
العرض (al-'ard)
البقاء (al-baqa)
تجدد المثل (tajaddud al-mithl)
عرض مستحيل البقاء (accident impossible of survival)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ability can persist as an accident across time, allowing it to precede the act.
Correction: Accidents cannot survive; therefore ability must be simultaneous with the act.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the argument from the accident?
A) Accidents are created.
B) Accidents cannot survive.
C) Ability is an accident.
D) The act is created.

Key Takeaway
Because accidents cannot survive, ability must exist simultaneously with the act it enables.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that your ability to perform any action is an accident created at the very moment of the act, and it cannot last. Have you ever taken credit for an achievement as if your ability was a permanent possession? What would change if you truly saw every ability as a fresh gift from Allah, vanishing the moment after the act?
?
The author argues that if ability preceded the act, the act would be impossible or necessary, both absurd. Consider a sin you committed while feeling ‘powerless’ to resist. According to this lesson, the ability to resist was only present at the moment of the act. Does this challenge your excuses? How can you prepare yourself now to ask Allah for ability at the critical moment?
?
If every act requires a newly created ability, then your entire life is a series of divinely given capacities that cannot be stored or saved. How does this affect your attitude towards tomorrow? Will you wake up assuming you have ability, or will you beg Allah to grant it when the time comes?

One Power Cannot Serve Two Opposites
Author argues one power cannot be for two opposites, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that a single power (qudra) cannot simultaneously enable two opposite actions.

Core Idea
A single power is limited to one opposite at a time.

This lesson examines a key argument in Islamic theology about the nature of power (qudra). The author demonstrates that if a single power could produce two opposite effects, contradictory states would coexist, which is impossible. The argument is supported by everyday examples that illustrate the limitation of capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses several 'ولأن' (and because) to structure the argument.
2
Follow the logical argument
The author presents a chain of reasoning. Start from the first claim about power types, then the disbeliever/believer example, then the impossibility of combining opposites simultaneously, then the non-endurance of power.
The core logical point is that two opposites cannot exist together at the moment power is present.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the examples of the chronically ill, the sick, the child, etc. How do they show that a single capacity cannot handle opposite actions like ascending and descending?
These examples are meant as direct empirical demonstrations, not just analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعصمة زيغ لم يطمئن القلب عند الوجود، فثبت أن قوة كل نوع غير قوة النوع الآخر، ولأن أحدًا لا يطلق القول بأن الكافر معصوم وأن المؤمن مخذول، ولو كان مع كل واحد منهما ما يصلح للأمرين جميعًا لم يكن أحدهما بالشهادة له بالخذلان والآخر بالشهادة له بالتوفيق والعصمة أولى من القلب أو يوصل كل واحد منهما بأنه موفق مخذول وذلك باطل، ولأن الجمع بين الضدين حال وجود القدرة غير ممكن، وهي لا تبقى ليفعل بها ضدًا في وقت والضد الآخر في وقت آخر، فلا يتصور أن تكون قدرة لهما بل كانت قدرة لما وقع بها وكذلك الزمن والعليل والطفل يقدرون على الزحف من الجبل مع العجز عن الارتقاء فيه وكذا العليل ينحدر من السلم على جهد واستمساك ولا يقدر على الارتقاء فيه وكذلك الصحيح ينزل البئر بالجبل ولا يصح منه الارتقاء عنه، وكذا الحي على تفريق أجزاء نفسه ولا يقدر على جمعها، ويقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه الانكسار من الزجاج وغيره من الأجسام، ولا يقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه الانحيار، وسوى هذا لهم حجج أخر أعرضنا عن ذكرها.
With infallibility [is] deviation; the heart is not at ease at the moment of existence. So it is established that the power of each type is other than the power of the other type. And because no one says that the disbeliever is infallible and that the believer is abandoned. And if there were with each of them what is suitable for both matters, then one of them would not be more deserving of being testified as abandoned and the other of being testified as successful and infallible than the heart, or each one of them would be both successful and abandoned, and that is false. And because the combination of two opposites at the time of the existence of power is not possible, and power does not remain so that one opposite can be done with it at one time and the other opposite at another time. So it is inconceivable that a power be for them both; rather, it is a power for what actually occurred through it. Similarly, the chronically ill, the sick, and the child are able to crawl down from a mountain while being unable to ascend it. Likewise, the sick descends the ladder with effort and holding on and cannot ascend it. Likewise, the healthy descends into the well with the rope [؟] and it is not sound for him to ascend from it. Likewise, the living is able to separate the parts of his own self and cannot bring them together. And he is able to cause the event that occurs after it—the breaking of glass and other bodies—and he is not able to cause the event that occurs after it—the confinement [؟]. And besides this, they have other proofs which we have refrained from mentioning.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that a single power (qudra) cannot be for two opposites (diddan). The author begins by stating that it is established that the power of one type is different from that of another type. He then argues by common sense: no one claims that a disbeliever is infallible and a believer is abandoned; if each had a power suitable for both states, each would have to be both successful and abandoned, which is false. The core argument: combining two opposites while power exists is impossible. Power is contemporaneous with the act, not enduring to allow one opposite at one time and another at another. Therefore, a power can only be for the act that actually occurs, not for both. The author provides everyday examples: a chronically ill person, a sick person, and a child can crawl down a mountain but cannot ascend; a sick person can descend a ladder with difficulty but cannot ascend; a healthy person can descend into a well with a rope but cannot ascend; a living being can separate parts of itself (e.g., moving limbs) but cannot reunite them; one can cause breakage but not the opposite (reassembly). These illustrate that a single capacity enables one type of action and not its opposite. The author concludes that there are further proofs not mentioned here.

Key Terms
قدرة
ضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The examples are merely analogical and not direct proof.
Correction: The author intends the examples as direct empirical demonstrations that one power cannot produce two opposite effects.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for denying that one power can be for two opposites?
A) Because power is temporary
B) Because the two opposites cannot coexist at the same moment
C) Because power is created by God
D) Because the examples demonstrate it indirectly

Key Takeaway
One power cannot produce two opposite actions simultaneously or sequentially.

If not, what does that tell you about your dependence on Allah’s tawfiq in every single action? Will you now begin each action by asking Him for the specific power to do good?
?
The lesson says power does not remain to be used for a different opposite later. Think of a habit you struggle with—a sin you repeat. Does this verse make you realize that each time you commit that sin, you are using a power that was never meant for obedience? How will that change the way you view your own capacity to change?

Nasafi's Refutation of Power Preceding the Act
Nasafi refutes objection using cause-effect simultaneity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the cause-effect relationship to argue that divine power and the act are simultaneous.

Core Idea
Power and act are simultaneous, like cause and effect.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a common objection against the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that God's power is connected to the act at the moment of its occurrence. The objectors claim that power must temporally precede the act because once the act exists, power is no longer needed. Nasafi responds by drawing an analogy with cause and effect, which exist together. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objector's claim: that the connection of power to existence is impossible because power is needed only before the act; once the act exists, power becomes superfluous.
The objection is a logical one: if power is simultaneous, why would it be needed? Nasafi's response addresses this.
2
Identify Nasafi's response and the central analogy
Nasafi states that power runs like causes (علل). He then gives the example of blackness and the subject being black: they exist together.
The cause-effect simultaneity is key to understanding the argument.
3
Understand why cause and effect must be simultaneous
The principle: 'it is impossible for the effect to exist with a nonexistent cause.' This means the effect depends on the cause at every moment of its existence.
4
Consider the other examples given
Nasafi briefly mentions knowledge and the known, movement, death, and life. All illustrate the same simultaneity.
These examples reinforce the universality of the principle beyond the blackness case.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
This argument defends the orthodox position that God's power is directly connected to the act at the time of its occurrence, preserving divine omnipotence without temporal gaps.
It also upholds the Maturidi view that power and act are simultaneous, not successive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم: إن تعلق القدرة بالوجود محال، لأن القدرة يحتاج إليها ليفعل فإذا وجد الفعل استغنى عنها. قلنا: قد مر أن القدرة تجري مجرى العلل والعلة مع المعلول يوجدان كقيام السواد واتصاف المحل بأنه أسود، ولا يقال إنه لما صار أسود استغنى عن قيام السواد به، وإنما يحتاج إلى قيام السواد به ليصير أسود، بل يقال: يحصلان جميعًا معا، لاستحالة حصول المعلول بعلة معدومة، وكذا العلم بالشيء مع صيرورته معلوماً، وكذا الحركة والموت والحياة.
Their saying: The connection of power to existence is impossible, because power is needed for the act to occur; when the act exists, it becomes independent of it. We say: It has been mentioned that power runs like causes, and the cause and the effect exist together, like the existence of blackness and the subject's being described as black. It is not said that when it became black it became independent of the blackness inhering in it; rather, it needs the blackness inhering in it to become black. But it is said: Both occur together, because it is impossible for the effect to exist with a nonexistent cause. Likewise, knowledge of a thing with its becoming known, and likewise movement, death, and life.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that power must precede the act; if the act exists, power is no longer needed, so power cannot coexist with the act. The response uses the analogy of a cause and its effect: they coexist temporally. For example, blackness and the subject being black: the blackness is the cause of the subject being black, and they occur simultaneously. It would be incorrect to say that once the subject is black, it no longer needs the blackness. Similarly, power and the act are simultaneous. This is because it is impossible for an effect to exist without its cause. Other examples include knowledge and the known, movement, death, and life.

Key Terms
تعلق القدرة
وجود
علة
معلول
سواد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power must temporally precede the act because the act becomes independent of power once it exists.
Correction: Nasafi argues that power and act are simultaneous, using the cause-effect relationship as a proof; the act does not become independent of power because power is the cause of the act, and cause and effect coexist.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the relation of power to the act compare to that of a cause and its effect?
A) Power precedes the act like a cause precedes its effect
B) Power and the act exist together like cause and effect
C) Power is unnecessary once the act exists
D) Power is like a condition that precedes the act

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument shows that power and act coexist, just as cause and effect must coexist, refuting the objection that power precedes the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you feel that your power to do it is present only at the moment of doing it, or do you feel it is something you possess beforehand? How does Nasafi's argument challenge your assumption about when your power is actually needed?
?
Think of a sin you struggle with. You may tell yourself: 'I have the power to stop, but I'll use it later.' Based on this lesson, where does that 'later power' actually exist? What does it mean that power and act must be simultaneous?
?
Nasafi uses the example of life and death to show simultaneity. Your life is the cause of your actions; without it, actions are impossible. If you knew that your life could end at any moment, how would that change the urgency of using your power to obey Allah right now, not later?

The Reply to the Objection: Power and Action Coexist
The author refutes the claim that power and action cannot coexist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the simultaneous existence of power and action using the analogy of cause and effect.

Core Idea
Power and action coexist just as cause and effect do.

The objection claims that power cannot coexist with action because power is only needed before action. The author responds by drawing on the principle of causality, showing that cause and effect occur together. This lesson unpacks that argument and its analogies.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim that power and action cannot be simultaneous because power is only needed to produce the action; once the action exists, power is no longer required.
The objection seems logical at first glance.
2
Identify the reply's core analogy
The author compares power to a cause and action to an effect, arguing that cause and effect exist together.
This is the key move in the argument.
3
Examine the blackness example
The blackness in a body (cause) and the attribute of being black (effect) occur simultaneously; one does not say the body no longer needs blackness after becoming black.
The example illustrates coexistence clearly.
4
Consider the additional analogies
Knowledge and the known, movement and the mover, death and the dead, life and the living – all these pairs coexist.
These reinforce the principle.
5
Reflect on the underlying principle
The principle is that an effect cannot arise from a non-existent cause, so cause and effect must coexist.
This is a fundamental causal axiom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم: إن تعلق القدرة بالوجود محال، لأن القدرة يحتاج إليها ليفعل فإذا وجد الفعل استغنى عنها. قلنا: قد مر أن القدرة تجري مجرى العلل والعلة مع المعلول يوجدان كقيام السواد واتصاف المحل بأنه أسود، ولا يقال إنه لما صار أسود استغنى عن قيام السواد به، وإنما يحتاج إلى قيام السواد به ليصير أسود، بل قيل : يحصلان جميعًا معا، لاستحالة حصول المعلول بعلة معدومة، وكذا العلم بالشيء مع صيرورته معلوماً، وكذا الحركة والموت والحياة.
Their statement: The attachment of power (qudra) to existence is impossible, because power is needed to act; so when the action exists, it becomes independent of it. We said: It has already passed that power runs the course of causes (ilal) and the cause with the effect occur together, like the existence of blackness (sawad) and the attribute (ittisaf) of the locus (mahall) as being black, and it is not said that when it became black it became independent of the existence of blackness in it, rather it needs the existence of blackness in it to become black. Rather it is said: they both occur together, due to the impossibility of the effect occurring with a non-existent cause. Similarly, knowledge of a thing with its becoming known, and likewise movement, death, and life.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the doctrine that power and its effect occur simultaneously. The objectors argue that power is needed to produce an action; once the action exists, power is no longer needed—so they cannot coexist. The reply draws an analogy with cause and effect: in the natural world, the cause and its effect exist together. For example, blackness in a body: the body is black by virtue of blackness inhering in it; one does not say that after the body becomes black it no longer requires the blackness. Rather, the blackness and the attribute of being black exist concurrently. The reason is that an effect cannot arise from a nonexistent cause. Other examples include knowledge and the known, movement and the mover, death and the dead, life and the living. In all these cases, the cause (power, blackness, etc.) and effect coexist.

Key Terms
تعلق القدرة
القدرة
العلة
المعلول
قيام السواد
اتصاف المحل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That cause must temporally precede effect
Correction: The author argues that cause and effect can exist simultaneously, as demonstrated by the blackness example and the principle that an effect cannot come from a non-existent cause.

Checkpoint

A) Effect can exist without cause.
B) Cause and effect can exist simultaneously.
C) Power must precede action.
D) Blackness causes the body to become dark.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by showing that cause and effect coexist, so power and action also coexist simultaneously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does this realization change how you view your own autonomy? Will you make a habit of acknowledging God's power in every moment of your actions starting today?
?

?

If all knowledge requires a simultaneous cause, how does that affect your gratitude for every bit of understanding you have? Can you commit to thanking Allah for every new insight as it happens?

Precedence of Divine Power and Simultaneity of Human Power
The author distinguishes between divine inventive power and human acquisitive power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power precedes its object while human power is simultaneous with it, and how this relates to the Maturidi doctrine of acquisition (kasb).

Core Idea
Divine power invents from nothing; human power acquires what is already created.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla clarifies a foundational Maturidi doctrine: the distinction between divine power of invention (ikhtira') and human power of acquisition (kasb). The author explains that because Allah creates ex nihilo, His power must precede the thing created. Human power, however, does not create but rather acquires, so it coexists with the action. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Maturidi position on free will and divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the technical terms: قدرة الاختراع and قدرة الكسب.
The passage uses a comparative structure: divine power first, then human power.
2
Identify the condition for divine power
The author states that divine power is attached to invention (ikhtira'), and for invention the power must affect both non-existence and existence. Ask yourself: why does this necessitate precedence?
3
Contrast with human power
Human power is not for invention but for acquisition (kasb). The condition is different: it does not need to precede, only to coexist with the already-created act. Find the sentence that states this.
4
Connect to the broader kalām issue
The passage ends by linking this distinction to the discussion of capacity (istita'a) and creation of actions (khalq al-af'al). How does the Maturidi view differ from the Mu'tazila according to the passage?
Test yourself with the checkpoint below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثير من أصحابنا يقولون بأن قدرة الباري جل جلاله متعلقة بالاختراع ومن شرط الاختراع أن تؤثر قدرة المخترع في العدم والوجود جميعًا حال حدوث المقدور، وذلك يوجب سبق القدرة للمقدور ليصبح تأثيرها في العدم، والقدرة المحدثة غير صالحة للاختراع بل هي صالحة لكون المخترع كسبًا للقادر فلم يكن من شرطها التقدم على المقدور، بل من شرطها وجود المخترع ليتعلق بها فيكون كسبًا له، وعن هذا المعنى قيل : إن مسألة الاستطاعة قريبة لمسألة خلق الأفعال، فإن عندهم لما كانت قدرة العبد للاختراع لابد من أن تكون سابقة، وعندنا لما لم تكن للاختراع لم تكن سابقة بل كانت مقارنة لما وجد بالاختراع، ولهذا المعنى جرى العرف من المتكلمين بإيراد هذه المسألة عقيب مسألة خلق أفعال العباد.
And many of our companions say that the power (qudra) of the Creator, may His majesty be exalted, is attached to invention (ikhtira'), and from the condition of invention is that the power of the inventor affects non-existence and existence both at the time of the occurrence of the originated (maqdur), and that necessitates the precedence of the power over the originated in order for its effect on non-existence to be valid. And the created power is not suitable for invention, rather it is suitable for the originated to be an acquisition (kasb) for the capable one, so it is not a condition for it to precede the originated; rather its condition is the existence of the originated for it to be attached, so that it becomes an acquisition for him. And due to this meaning, it is said that the issue of capacity (istita'a) is close to the issue of creation of actions (khalq al-af'al), for according to them, since the slave's power is for invention, it must be prior; and according to us, since it is not for invention, it is not prior but simultaneous with what came into existence by invention. And for this meaning, the customary practice of theologians has been to bring this issue after the issue of creation of servants' actions.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage explains the Maturidi doctrine on the relationship between power and its object. The author states that many of the Maturidi scholars hold that divine power is a power of invention (ikhtira'), meaning it creates something from nothing. For invention, the power must affect both non-existence and existence at the moment the thing is originated. This requires that the power precede its object in order to affect non-existence. In contrast, human power is not a power of invention; it is a power of acquisition (kasb). The role of human power is to acquire actions that are created by Allah. Therefore, human power does not need to precede its object; rather, it can be simultaneous with the object's existence, because it attaches to an already-created act. The passage then relates this to the famous kalām issue of capacity (istita'a) and the creation of actions. The Mu'tazila, who believe human power creates actions, must hold that power precedes the action. The Maturidis, who believe actions are created by Allah and humans acquire them, hold that power is simultaneous with the action. That is why, in standard kalām works, the discussion of capacity follows the discussion of the creation of actions.

Key Terms
قدرة الاختراع
قدرة الكسب
الاختراع
الكسب
الاستطاعة
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think human power can create like divine power, or that human power must precede the action to cause it.
Correction: Human power is acquisitive, not creative; it does not cause the action but acquires it, so it is simultaneous, not prior.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the condition for human power (qudra muhdatha) regarding the time of the action?
A) It must precede the action.
B) It must be simultaneous with the action.
C) It must follow the action.
D) It is unrelated to the action's time.

Key Takeaway
Divine power precedes its object because it creates from nothing; human power is simultaneous because it acquires what is already created by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you feel your power to commit it was there before the act, or do you sense it arising only in the moment? How does knowing that your power is simultaneous—not prior—affect your view of your own responsibility before Allah?
?
If divine power invents from nothing and your power only acquires what is already created, how does this change your understanding of tawakkul? Are you more reliant on Allah's invention or your own acquisition? What would it mean to truly trust in His inventive power for your next action?
?
The Mu'tazila believed human power is creative like Allah's, which leads to power preceding action. But the Maturidi view strips you of creative power and leaves you with acquisition. Does this make you feel less accountable or more? Can you accept that you are not the originator of your deeds, yet you will still be judged for them? How does that sit in your heart?

The Coexistence of Power and Action: Resolving the Asymmetry Objection
The author refutes objections about power attaching to existing things and cites philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the coexistence of divine power with action by addressing the objection that this would imply symmetrical attribution, and how he uses examples and the philosophical concept of 'precedence in rank' to establish causal asymmetry.

Core Idea
Cause and effect coexist but are related asymmetrically by rank, not time.

In this passage, the author continues his defense of the view that divine power attaches to actions at the moment they occur. He tackles a key objection: if power and action coexist, why attribute the action to power rather than power to the action? The answer lies in a careful analysis of causal relationships, supported by everyday examples and a nuanced understanding of precedence rooted in the philosophical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma presented
The author presents a dilemma to the opponent: does power attach to an existent that has been finished with (past) or not yet finished with (present/future)? Consider the implications of each option.
The dilemma forces the opponent to choose between two options, each with consequences.
2
Examine the examples of coexistence
The author lists several examples: blackness with black, movement with mover, death with dead, life with living, the stick's throwing, and sunrise with day. Notice that in each pair, cause and effect occur together; yet we intuitively understand the causal direction.
These examples illustrate that coexistence does not negate causality.
3
Grasp the concept of precedence in rank
The philosophers cited explain that cause precedes effect not in time but in rank. Understand what 'rank' means here: ontological dependency, not chronological sequence.
This key philosophical distinction resolves the symmetry objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : إن تعلق القدرة يستحيل بأي موجود ؟ بموجود وقع عنه الفراغ؟ أم بموجود لم يقع عنه الفراغ بعد؟ فإن قال بالأول، فهو مسلم غير أن الأعراض ليس لها حالة الفراغ إذ هي الحالة الثانية في حالة الوجود، وهي لا تبقى فتنعدم في تلك الحالة، وإن قال بالثاني فهو غير مسلّم أن القدرة لا تتعلق به ويوجدان معا على ما مر، وهذا هو الجواب عن قولهم : أن القدرة لو كانت توجد مع الفعل لم يكن إضافة الفعل إلى القدرة أولى من إضافة القدرة إلى الفعل فإن السواد باتصاف المحل بكونه أسود، وكذا الحركة مع المتحرك والموت مع الميت والحياة مع الحي العصا اتصافها وخروج من اليد مع الإلقاء، وطلوع الشمس مع وجود النهار كل ذلك يوجدان معاً ولا تخفى العلة والمعلول، ويعرف ببديهة العقل حصول أحدهما بالآخر، واستحالة إضافة العلة إلى الحكم، ولهذا قالت الفلاسفة: العلة متقدمة على المعلول لا تقدماً زمنيًا بل تقدمًا من حيث الرتبة، وحقيقة الكلام ما وجميع الصفات مع
Then we say to them: The attachment of power is impossible to any existing thing? To an existing thing that one has finished with? Or to an existing thing that one has not yet finished with? If they say the first, it is granted, but accidents have no state of finishing because they are in the second state of existence, and they do not subsist, so they become non-existent in that state. If they say the second, then it is not granted that power does not attach to it, and they occur together as previously. And this is the answer to their statement: If power existed with the action, then the attribution of the action to power would not be more appropriate than the attribution of power to the action. For blackness is the locus's being black, and similarly movement with the mover, death with the dead, life with the living, the stick's being thrown and its exit from the hand with the throwing, and the sun's rising with the existence of day; all of these occur together and the cause and effect are not hidden, and it is known by the intuition of reason that one obtains by the other, and the impossibility of attributing the cause to the judgment. For this, the philosophers said: The cause precedes the effect not in time but in rank. And the reality of the speech is what... and all the attributes with...
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation. The author presents a dilemma: is the power attached to an existent that has been finished with (i.e., a past action) or to an existent that has not yet been finished with (i.e., present or future)? If the former, it is granted that power cannot attach to a past action, but accidents do not have a state of finishing because they exist only momentarily and do not subsist; hence the objection fails. If the latter, the author asserts that the power and the action occur together, as established earlier. The author then addresses a further objection: if power coexists with action, then the action could just as well be attributed to power as power to action, implying no causal relationship. The reply uses examples: blackness and the attribute of black, movement and the mover, death and the dead, life and the living, the stick and its throwing, sunrise and daytime. In each pair, the cause and effect coexist and the relationship is understood by reason. The author then cites the philosophers' view that the cause precedes the effect not in time but in rank (bi-hasab al-rutba). The passage ends abruptly.

العلة والمعلول
بديهة العقل
تقدم الرتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That coexistence of cause and effect implies they are symmetrically linked, so either could be the cause of the other.
Correction: The examples and the philosophical concept of precedence in rank show that cause and effect coexist asymmetrically; the cause is ontologically prior even if temporally simultaneous.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers cited in this passage, in what sense does cause precede effect?
A) Temporally.
B) In rank (rutba).
C) In essence only.
D) Not at all.

Key Takeaway
Divine power and action coexist, but the cause-effect relationship is preserved through ontological precedence in rank, not temporal sequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you woke up this morning and saw the daylight, did you consider that the cause (sunrise) and effect (daylight) are simultaneous yet the effect is entirely dependent on the cause? How does this reshape your awareness that every action you perform is simultaneously caused by Allah's power, leaving no room for your own independence? Will you today catch yourself in a moment of action and whisper, 'This is only by His power coexisting with my action'?
?
Think of a recent moment where you felt proud of an achievement—perhaps a success at work or a good deed. Did you instinctively attribute it to your own power first? The philosophers' 'rank precedence' means that even though your power and the action occurred together, your power is ontologically secondary. How can you train your heart to habitually recognize that the cause (your power) is itself an effect of Allah's creation? What would it cost you to genuinely believe that?
?
The passage argues that actions (accidents) have no persistent state after they occur—they vanish instantly. Your last word, your last thought, your last glance—they are already gone. Does this fragility make you realize that your life is nothing but a sequence of non-persisting moments sustained by Allah? If you truly internalized that, would you still cling to your past mistakes as if they define you, or your past virtues as if they are your own?

Proof of Simultaneous Cause and Effect
The author proves that cause and effect must occur simultaneously, refuting an opponent's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how cause and effect can be simultaneous when they are mutually conditioning, and why this undermines the opponent's claim about power and action.

Core Idea
Mutual conditionality forces cause and effect to coexist.

In this passage, the author tackles a key objection to the simultaneity of cause and effect. The opponent argues that if action depends on power and power depends on action, they cannot coexist. The author demonstrates that this mutual dependence actually proves simultaneity, using the example of blackness and its attribution. By understanding this argument, you will see how causality in kalam is not always temporal priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice the author first states a general premise about cause and effect, then applies it to the specific case of power and action, and finally uses the example of blackness to illustrate mutual conditionality.
3
Analyse the mutual conditionality argument
Focus on the last part: if each is a condition for the other, then each would require the other to precede, leading to impossibility. Therefore, they must occur together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مر أن حصول العلة في هذه الفصول لو تقدمت لانعدمت وقت حصول المعلول، وحصوله بعلة معدومة غير متصور فلابد من حصولهما معاً ، فكذا فيما نحن فيه. وبهذا يبطل ما زعموا أن الفعل لو لم يوجد حتى تحصل القدرة ولن تحصل هي حتى يوجد الفعل لا يتصور وجودهما كما ضرب من المثال، فإن الاتصاف بالسواد مع وجود السواد يوجدان معا ولا يتصور وجود أحدهما بدون ذلك، وإنما ذلك فيما يتعلق كل واحد منهما بصاحبه تعلق الشرط بالمشروط، فيكون وجود هذا شرطًا لوجود ذلك ووجود ذلك شرطًا لوجود هذا، ووجود المشروط يكون بعد وجود الشرط فيصير شرط وجود كل واحد منهما تقدم صاحبه عليه وهذا محال. فأما فيما يوجدان معا فعليه أكثر الأمور.
It is known that the occurrence of the cause in these chapters, if it preceded, would cease at the time of the occurrence of the effect, and its occurrence by a non-existent cause is inconceivable, so they must occur together, and likewise in our subject. And by this is invalidated what they claimed: that if the action were not to exist until power exists, and power would not exist until the action exists, their existence is inconceivable, as they gave an example. For the attribution of blackness with the existence of blackness occurs together, and the existence of one of them without that is inconceivable. That is only the case where each one is related to its companion as condition to conditioned, so the existence of this is a condition for the existence of that, and the existence of that is a condition for the existence of this, and the existence of the conditioned would be after the existence of the condition, so the condition of the existence of each one would be the priority of its companion over it, and this is impossible. As for things that occur together, most matters are upon that.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing the simultaneity of cause and effect. The author argues that if the cause preceded the effect, the cause would cease to exist at the moment the effect occurs, making the effect's existence by a non-existent cause impossible. Thus cause and effect must be simultaneous. He applies this to the relationship between power (qudra) and action (fi'l) to refute the opponent's claim that if action does not exist until power is obtained, and power does not exist until action exists, the two cannot coexist. The author uses the example of blackness: the attribute of being black (ittiṣāf bi-sawād) and the existence of blackness (wujūd as-sawād) necessarily coexist; one cannot exist without the other. This is a case of mutual conditionality: each is a condition for the other, but if each were required to precede the other, an infinite regress results, which is impossible. Therefore the two must occur together. The author concludes that most matters requiring mutual condition are simultaneous.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
قدرة
فعل
شرط
مشروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cause must always temporally precede its effect.
Correction: When cause and effect are mutually conditioning, they must coexist; otherwise an infinite regress of prerequisites results.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to illustrate mutual simultaneity?
A) Heat and fire.
B) Blackness and the attribute of blackness.
C) Power and action in the seen world.
D) Condition and conditioned in temporal succession.

Key Takeaway
When two things are each a condition for the other, they must exist simultaneously; otherwise an infinite regress of prerequisites is inevitable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you think your actions create results, or do you see that the cause and effect are simultaneous—your decision and its outcome already together in God's decree? How would living with this awareness change your reliance on Him?
?
Think of a time you felt stuck, waiting for a condition to be met before you could act. Does this lesson show you that the condition may already be present with the action? What step can you take right now, trusting that God has already given you what you need?
?
If cause and effect are simultaneous, then your current state is directly caused by God's constant creation. Are you aware of His presence in this very moment? How can you turn your next thought or action into an acknowledgment that He is the cause sustaining you?

The Ash'ari View: Agreement in Essence, Difference in Terminology
Al-Nasafi compares Maturidi and Ash'ari views on human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi critique of the Ash'ari position on human actions, and why the author considers the difference merely terminological.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris and Maturidis essentially agree—only their terminology differs.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla addresses the relationship between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools on the issue of human actions. Al-Nasafi acknowledges the closeness of the two positions but criticizes the Ash'ari refusal to call the human act 'fi'l', pointing out that this contradicts their own usage and the language of the Quran and common speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's tone and the key terms.
Pay attention to the Quranic citation and the reference to Barghuth.
2
Identify the author's critique
The author points out a contradiction in the Ash'ari position. Find where he uses evidence from Quran, language, and al-Ash'ari's own books.
The contradiction is central to the argument.
3
Reflect on the concluding statement
The author concludes that the difference is only in expression, not in doctrinal reality. Consider why this matters for understanding the two schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب الأشاعرة قريب من الماتريدية. ثم الأشعرية إن وافقونا في حقيقة المذهب فقد زعمت أن ما هو مقدور العبد يسمى كسبًا ولا يسمى فعلاً له كما لا يسمى خلقًا، وهذا منه امتناع عن إطلاق ما أطلق الله بقوله: "وَافْعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ" وغير ذلك من الآيات، وأطلق جميع أهل اللسان قولهم: فعل فلان كذا، وفلان كريم الفعال، وأطلق هو وجميع المتكلمين، فإن الناس بأجمعهم يسمون هذا الباب باب خلق أفعال العباد كذا ذكر الأشعري في كتبه وكذا جميع أصحابه، والامتناع عن إطلاق ما أطلق هو بنفسه تناقض. تبع الأشعري في هذا الرأي أبا عيسى محمد بن عيسى الملقب ببرغوث وهو في الحقيقة اختلاف في العبارة دون حقيقة المذهب.
The madhhab of the Ash'aris is close to the Maturidiyya. Then the Ash'ariyya, if they agree with us in the reality of the madhhab, they claim that what is within the capacity of the servant is called kasb (acquisition) and is not called a fi'l (act) for him, nor is it called khalq (creation). This is a refusal to apply what Allah applied in His saying: "And do good" and other verses. And all people of language say: 'So-and-so did such-and-such' and 'so-and-so is generous of deed.' And he (al-Ash'ari) and all the mutakallimun apply [this term], for all people call this chapter 'the chapter of the creation of the acts of the servants.' Thus al-Ash'ari mentioned in his books, and likewise all his companions. And refusal to apply what he himself applied is a contradiction. Al-Ash'ari followed in this opinion Abu 'Isa Muhammad ibn 'Isa, nicknamed Barghuth, and it is in reality a difference in expression, not in the reality of the madhhab.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage addresses the relationship between Maturidi and Ash'ari views on human actions. The author begins by stating that the Ash'ari position is close to the Maturidi position in essence. Then he explains the difference: the Ash'aris call the human's role in an action 'kasb' (acquisition) and refuse to call it 'fi'l' (act) or 'khalq' (creation). The author criticizes this refusal because Allah Himself commanded 'do good' (if'alu al-khayr), using the term fi'l. Moreover, ordinary language attributes acts to people, saying 'so-and-so did such-and-such.' Even al-Ash'ari himself, in his books, titled his discussion 'the creation of the acts of the servants' (khalq af'al al-'ibad), thus using the term fi'l. The author points out that refusing to use a term that he himself used is a contradiction. Then he notes that al-Ash'ari followed Barghuth in this, but the difference is ultimately verbal, not substantial. The Maturidi view is that the action can be attributed to the human in one sense (kasb) and to God in another (khalq), but the Ash'ari insistence on a specific terminology does not change the underlying agreement that God creates all actions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Ash'ari rejection of calling human acts 'fi'l' is a fundamental theological difference from the Maturidis.
Correction: The author argues that it is merely a difference in terminology, not in the underlying doctrine (haqiqat al-madhhab).

Checkpoint

B) A difference in expression
C) The Maturidis deny human capacity
D) The Ash'aris believe humans create their own actions

Key Takeaway
The difference between Maturidis and Ash'aris on human actions is primarily in terminology, not in the underlying doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author accuses al-Ash'ari of contradicting himself in his use of 'fi'l'. Where in your own practice of faith do you use terms inconsistently? Are you willing to be corrected by clear evidence from revelation and common usage?
?
If the Ash'aris and Maturidis agree in reality, what does that say about the unity of Ahl al-Sunnah? How can you personally help bridge such terminological differences rather than magnify them?

Refuting Fatalism: The Threefold Proof Against the Jabriyya
The author refutes the Jabriyya by citing Qur'anic commands, reward and punishment, and rational impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Jabriyya by appealing to scripture, reason, and self-evident necessity.

Core Idea
God's commands and accountability prove that humans have real agency.

The Jabriyya claim that all actions are directly from God with no human role. This lesson examines the author's response: such a view contradicts clear Qur'anic verses, rational principles, and common sense. We will see why commanding, promising, and punishing only make sense if humans truly act.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once without analysis. Notice the author's threefold structure: scripture, intellect, and necessity.
The passage is incomplete but the main arguments are clear.
2
Identify the scriptural evidence
Find the Qur'anic verses cited that attribute actions to people, such as 'Do whatever you wish' and 'Reward for what they used to do.'
These verses imply human capacity to act.
3
Understand the rational argument
The author argues that commanding without ability is rationally impossible. Reflect on why a command requires the possibility of obedience.
This is the core logical refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان بطلان قول الجبرية. ومذهب الجبرية باطل بدلالة السمع والعقل والضرورة التي تصير دافع ذلك مكابرًا. فأما السمع فمن نحو قوله تعالى: "اعْمَلُوا مَا شِنْتُمْ وَافْعَلُوا الْخَيْرَ"، وفي الجزاء: "كَذَلِكَ يُرِيهِمُ اللَّهُ أَعْمَالَهُمْ حَسَرَاتٍ عَلَيْهِمْ" وقوله تعالى: "وَمَنْ يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ" وقوله: "جَزَاءً بِمَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ" وغير ذلك مما أثبت لهم أسماء العمال ولفعلهم اسم الفعل بالأمر والنهي والوعد والوعيد، وليس في الإضافة إلى الله تعالى نفي ذلك بل هي الله تعالى بأن خلقها على ما وأوجدها بعد أن لم تكن، وللخلق على ما كسبوها وفعلوها على أن الله تعالى أمر ونهى ومحال الأمر بما لا فعل للمأمور أو المنهى، وقال الله تعالى: "إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْمُرُ بِالْعَدْلِ وَالْإِحْسَانِ" ولو جاز الأمر بذلك بلا معنى الفعل في الحقيقة لجاز الأمر بشيء يكون لأمس أو للعام الأول أو بإنشاء الخلائق، وإن كان لا معنى لذلك في أمر الخلق، ثم في العقل قبيح أن يضاف إلى الله تعالى الطاعة والمعصية، وارتكاب
Demonstration of the invalidity of the statement of the Jabriyya. And the madhhab of the Jabriyya is invalid by evidence of transmitted text (sam'), intellect ('aql), and necessity (darūra) which makes the one who rejects it a stubborn disputant. As for transmitted text, from His saying: "Do whatever you wish and do good," and in retribution: "Thus Allah will show them their works as regrets upon them," and His saying: "Whoever does an atom's weight [of good]," and His saying: "Reward for what they used to do," and other [verses] that establish for them the names of doers and for their actions the name of action by command, prohibition, promise, and threat. And there is no negation of that in attributing [action] to Allah. Rather, it is Allah who created them as such and brought them into existence after they were not. And for the creation, by what they acquired and did, given that Allah commanded and prohibited. And it is impossible to command something where the commanded person has no action [to obey] or the one prohibited [cannot refrain]. And Allah said: "Indeed, Allah commands justice and excellence." And if it were permissible to command that without real meaning of action, then it would be permissible to command something that belongs to yesterday or last year or to create creatures, although that has no meaning in the matter of the creation. Then by reason, it is ugly (qabīḥ) to attribute obedience and sin to Allah, and perpetration...
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jabriyya, who deny any human agency and claim that all actions are directly from God without any role for the human. The author argues that such a view is invalid based on three sources: 1) Scripture (sam'): The Qur'an commands people to do good (if'alu al-khayr) and promises reward for their deeds, which implies they have the capacity to act. The verses on judgment day show actions being attributed to people. 2) Reason ('aql): It is logically impossible (muhal) for God to command a person to do something if that person has no actual ability to act. Commanding without the possibility of fulfillment is absurd. 3) Necessity (darūra): The self-evident common sense that people distinguish between actions they perform and those they suffer. The author also argues that attributing actions to God as Creator does not negate human acquisition; God creates the action and the human acquires it. He further argues that if commands could be given without real action, then one could command something impossible like yesterday's event. Finally, by reason, it is morally repugnant (qabīḥ) to attribute disobedience to God directly; rather, the human is the agent of sin. The passage is cut off but the main points are established.

Key Terms
جبرية
سمع
عقل
ضرورة
أمر
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates all actions, humans have no responsibility.
Correction: The author shows that commands and rewards logically require human agency; God creates the action, but humans acquire it and are accountable.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that commanding action without real human ability is impossible?
A) Because God never issues impossible commands
B) Because it would render reward and punishment meaningless
C) Because it would be like commanding something that cannot be done, which is rationally absurd
D) Because the Qur'an explicitly states humans have free will

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya are refuted by scripture, reason, and common sense: commands require ability, reward requires action, and attributing sin to God is repugnant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your actions are your own, or do you sometimes excuse your sins as 'from Allah'? The author says attributing sin to Allah is qabih. Have you ever shifted blame to Allah for your own choices? Will you repent and take responsibility starting today?
?
The passage says it is impossible to command someone with no ability. If you claim you have no power to obey Allah in certain areas, are you doubting the wisdom of Allah's commands? What is one area where you feel powerless, and how can this argument change your approach to that struggle?
?
You will be shown your deeds on the Day of Judgment. Do your current actions reflect that you expect reward or punishment? If you are living as if your actions don't matter, how will you change starting today?

The Impossibility of One Act Under Two Powers
Imam Nasafi refutes the claim of one act under two powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single act cannot be under the power of two agents, based on the rational consistency between the witnessed and unseen worlds.

Core Idea
One act under two powers is as impossible as combining black and white.

Imam Nasafi presents a powerful logical refutation of the claim that a single act could be within the power of two agents. He shows that such a claim violates the consistent rational principles that govern both the seen and unseen worlds. In this lesson, we will unpack his argument and see how it safeguards divine unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the opponents' claim followed by the author's refutation.
The argument relies on the analogy between witnessed and unseen worlds.
2
Understand the opponents' argument
Identify what the opponents are claiming: that one act can be under two powers in the unseen, using the witnessed world as a precedent.
3
Analyze the refutation by analogy
Imam Nasafi compares the opponents' claim to claiming that black and white, or motion and rest, can coexist in the unseen while being impossible in the witnessed. Ask yourself why this comparison is effective.
The analogy highlights the logical absurdity of the opponents' position.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Understand why the author concludes that this claim is a departure from reason, an invalidation of proofs, and a violation of intuition.
This conclusion reinforces the principle that possibility is consistent across all realms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: وكذا دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين في الشاهد، ولا يختلف الشاهد والغائب في الجائز والممتنع كما في الجمع بين السواد والبياض، فصار مدعي إمكان دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين في الغائب كونه ممتنعا في الشاهد كمدعي إمكان الجمع بين السواد والبياض والحركة والسكون في الغائب، مع كون ذلك مستحيلاً في الشاهد، وهذا خروج عن قضية المعقول، وإبطال الدلائل ونقض البداهة.
They said: And likewise, the entering of one object of power under the power of two agents in the witnessed (world), and the witnessed and the unseen do not differ in what is possible and what is impossible, just as in the combination of black and white. So the claimant of the possibility of one object of power under the power of two agents in the unseen, it being impossible in the witnessed, is like the claimant of the possibility of the combination between black and white and motion and rest in the unseen, while that is impossible in the witnessed. And this is a departure from the verdict of reason, an invalidation of proofs, and a violation of intuition.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that a single act can be within the power of two agents (e.g., God and human). The author reports the opponents' argument (beginning with 'They said') that in the witnessed world, one act cannot be under two powers, and the same must hold for the unseen world. Then the author turns this against them, arguing that the one who claims the possibility of one act under two powers in the unseen is like someone who claims that black and white, or motion and rest, can coexist in the unseen while they are impossible in the witnessed. Since such combinations are impossible in the witnessed and the laws of possibility are consistent across seen and unseen, the opponents' claim is logically absurd. The author concludes that this position is a departure from rational judgment, an invalidation of proofs, and a violation of intuitive knowledge (badāha).

Key Terms
مقدور واحد
قدرة قادرين
الشاهد والغائب
السواد والبياض
الحركة والسكون
قضية المعقول
البداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an act can be fully attributed to both God and a human agent simultaneously without logical conflict.
Correction: This passage shows that such a claim leads to the logical impossibility of combining opposites, therefore it is rationally untenable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of claiming that a single act can be under the power of two agents in the unseen?
A) It is like claiming black and white can coexist, which is impossible.
B) It is possible if both agents agree.
C) It leads to shirk.
D) It is a necessary truth.

Key Takeaway
The consistency of possibility between the witnessed and unseen worlds proves that a single act cannot be under the power of two agents, because that would be like combining two opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by telling yourself that 'God willed it and I did it,' without realizing that such thinking logically places you and God as co-agents of the same act? How does this lesson confront that evasion of responsibility?
?
When you pray for something and then take action, do you ever stop to ask whether your action is truly yours or God's? If one act cannot have two agents, what does that mean for your personal accountability in every choice you make today?
?
If you truly believe that God alone creates every action, including your own, does that fact terrify you or liberate you? And if it does neither, are you actually believing it, or just giving it lip service?
