The First Horn of the Dilemma: Servant Has No Power
Nasafi states the first branch of the dilemma and its jabr conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the servant any power to refrain from God's act leads to pure compulsion (jabr), which is rationally and theologically corrupt.

Core Idea
If the servant cannot refuse, the act is jabr—a view already refuted by necessity.

Imam Nasafi begins his refutation of shared agency by presenting a logical dilemma. The first possibility is that when God wills an act, the servant has no power to refrain from it. This leads directly to the doctrine of pure compulsion (jabr), which Nasafi dismisses as corrupt and already refuted. Let's examine this first branch and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its translation. Notice the logical structure: an if-then conditional leading to a conclusion.
2
Identify the key condition
Focus on the phrase 'if he does not have it [power to refrain]'. This triggers the definition of a compelled act.
Nasafi defines the compelled act as one where God creates and the servant cannot repel it.
3
Understand the examples
Consider the examples: colors, forms, pulse movements. These are natural phenomena beyond human control. Nasafi uses them to illustrate jabr.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Nasafi says this view is 'corrupt known by necessity and reason'. Think about why claiming all human acts are like involuntary movements is rationally unsound.
This echoes earlier arguments against the Jabriyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان هذا: أن الله تعالى إذا أراد إيجاد فعل العبد لا يخلو الأمر إما أن كان للعبد قدرة الامتناع أو لم يكن، فإن لم يكن فهو الاضطرار المحض والجبر الصريح إذ الفعل الضروري ما يتولى الله تعالى تخليقه وليس للعبد قدرة دفعه عن نفسه والامتناع عنه كالألوان والهيئات وحركات العروق النابضة وأشباه ذلك، والقول به فاسد يعرف فساده بالضرورة والعقل على ما مر في إبطال كلام الجبرية، وإن كان للعبد قدرة الامتناع فلا يحصل هذا الفعل، فلا يثبت ما هو مقدور الله تعالى إذا اختار العبد الامتناع عنه، فيؤدي إلى تعجيز الله جل الله عن ذلك وذلك باطل، وكذا لو أراد العبد تحصيله إن لم تكن لله تعالى قدرة الامتناع عنه فهو مضطر فيما يفعل؛ لأن فعل ما لا قدرة للفاعل على الامتناع عنه وتحصيل ضده فعل ضروري والقول به كفر، وإن كان لله تعالى قدرة الامتناع وكان العبد مضطرًا إذ ليس له قدرة استجلابه عند قصده إلى ذلك إذا امتنع الله تعالى، ولا قدرة دفعه إذا أوجده الله تعالى. وهذا هو حد الفعل الاضطراري، ولأن من أفعال العباد ما هو قبيح وسفه وإيجاد القبيح قبيح وإيجاد السفه سفه، وموجد ذلك سفيه لأن الإيجاد فوق المباشرة والاكتساب عندكم، ثم مباشرة القبيح قبيحة ومباشرة السفه سفه فالإيجاد أولى أن يكون كذلك، وكذا الموجد أولى أن يكون سفيها من المباشرة، وكذا يلزمون الظلم والجور على هذا الوجه. على أن ادعاء معنى وراء الوجود تصير به الذات فاعلاً ادعاء أمر غير معقول، فكان مدعي ذلك خارجًا عن قضية العقول. وهذه هي الشبه المعروفة لهم اقتصرنا عليه وأعرضنا عما سواها مخافة التطويل.
The explanation of this: that when Allah Almighty wills to bring into existence an act of the servant, the matter is not free from either the servant having the power to refrain or not. If he does not have it, it is pure compulsion and explicit predestination (jabr), for the necessary act is that which Allah Almighty undertakes to create and the servant has no power to repel it from himself or refrain from it, like colors, forms, pulse movements, and the like. Holding this opinion is corrupt, and its corruption is known by necessity and reason, as has preceded in the refutation of the Jabriyya. If the servant has the power to refrain, then this act does not occur, so what is the object of power of Allah Almighty is not established when the servant chooses to refrain from it, leading to incapacitating Allah — exalted is Allah above that — and that is false. Likewise, if the servant wants to acquire it, if Allah Almighty does not have the power to refrain from it, then the servant is compelled in what he does, because an act over which the agent has no power to refrain or to bring about its opposite is a necessary act, and holding this is disbelief. And if Allah Almighty has the power to refrain, then the servant is compelled, because he has no power to bring it about when he intends it if Allah refrains, nor power to repel it if Allah brings it into existence. This is the definition of the compelled act. And because some of the acts of servants are ugly (qabīḥ) and foolish (safah), and bringing the ugly into existence is ugly, and bringing foolishness into existence is foolish. The one who brings it into existence is foolish, because bringing into existence (ījād) is above direct action and acquisition in your view. Then direct action of the ugly is ugly, and direct action of foolishness is foolish, so bringing into existence is even more so. Likewise, the one who brings into existence is even more foolish than the one who directly acts. And likewise, they are led to (attributing) injustice and tyranny in this manner. Moreover, the claim of a meaning beyond existence by which the essence becomes an agent is a claim of something unintelligible, so the claimant of that is outside the verdict of intellects. And these are the well-known doubts of theirs; we have limited ourselves to these and turned away from others for fear of prolongation.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the doctrine of shared agency, containing several sub-arguments. First, the author presents a dilemma: if God wills to create an act of the servant, either the servant has the power (qudra) to refrain from that act or not. If the servant lacks that power, then the act is purely compelled (iḍṭirār) and jabr, which is false and already refuted. If the servant has the power to refrain, then when he refrains, God's intended act does not occur, which incapacitates God (taʿjīz) — an impossibility. In a parallel scenario, if the servant wants to do an act, either God has the power to prevent it or not. If God lacks that power, the servant is compelled in doing it (since God cannot prevent it), which is disbelief. If God has the power to prevent, then the servant is again compelled because he cannot bring it about if God withholds. So in all cases, the doctrine leads to either jabr or taʿjīz. Second, the author argues from the existence of evil acts (qabīḥ, safah) among human actions: if God creates these acts (since He is the 'mūjid' above direct action and acquisition), then God would be the author of evil and thus be foolish or unjust, which is impossible. Third, the author refutes the idea that there is a 'meaning beyond existence' that makes an essence an agent; this is unintelligible and outside rational discourse. The author concludes by stating that these are the well-known doubts of the opponents, and he has limited himself to refuting them.

Key Terms
اضطرار
جبر
فعل ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying human power altogether is a valid form of Islamic predestination.
Correction: Nasafi argues that such a view is rationally corrupt and leads to jabr, which has already been refuted. The servant must have some capacity to avoid being purely compelled.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the servant has no power to refrain from the act God wills?
A) The act is attributed to both.
B) The act is pure compulsion (jabr).
C) The act is evil.
D) God becomes incapacitated.

Key Takeaway
Denying the servant any power to refuse God's act reduces the action to jabr, a doctrine already deemed corrupt by necessity and reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever feel as though you had no choice—like your desires or circumstances forced you? How does this passage challenge that feeling? What would you say to Allah if you claimed you were compelled?
?
The passage says the view that the servant has no power is 'corrupt by necessity and reason'. Have you ever blamed your actions on your upbringing or environment as if you had no power to resist? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed you have the power to refrain from sin?
?
If you genuinely accept that you have the power to refrain from sin, what is one specific sin you will commit to refraining from starting today? How will you actively use that power when temptation arises?

The Second Horn and Its Parallel: Incapacity or Compulsion
Nasafi presents the second horn of the dilemma and its parallel, leading to taʿjīz or iḍṭirār.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if the servant has power to refrain, shared agency leads either to incapacitating God or to the servant being compelled, both impossible.

Core Idea
If the servant can refuse, God's will fails; if God can refuse, the servant is compelled.

Nasafi now explores the other horn of the dilemma: what if the servant does have the power to refrain from the act God wills? This leads to a cascade of problems. First, God's intended act may not occur, implying God's incapacity. Then a parallel scenario where the servant wants to act also ends in compulsion. This passage demonstrates that shared agency inevitably results in either taʿjīz (incapacitating God) or iḍṭirār (compulsion), both theologically unacceptable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice it has two parts: first, the case where servant chooses to refrain; second, the parallel where servant wants to act and God's power to prevent is considered.
2
Trace the first sub-argument
If the servant has power to refrain and exercises it, God's act does not occur. This would mean God is unable to achieve what He wills—taʿjīz. Why is this impossible?
Nasafi declares this false (bāṭil) immediately.
3
Analyze the parallel sub-argument
Now consider the servant wanting to do an act. If God cannot prevent it, the act is necessary for the servant (compulsion = disbelief). If God can prevent, the servant is again compelled because he depends on God's permission.
Thus in both possibilities, the servant ends up compelled.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Notice how Nasafi concludes by defining the compelled act: an act the agent cannot bring about or repel. This shows that shared agency always reduces to compulsion or taʿjīz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, in a moment of pride, felt that your obedience to Allah was purely your own achievement, forgetting that without His will you could not even raise a finger? What does this dilemma reveal about that feeling?
?
Think of a sin you committed. At the time, did you believe that you alone chose to do it, and that Allah had no hand in it? Or did you think Allah compelled you? How does this passage shatter both assumptions and leave you with only one option?
?
If you truly accept that nothing happens except by Allah's will and power, and that your own 'ability' is only an acquisition (kasb), then why do you still fear the future as if your plans depend on your own power? What would it mean to live each moment with that awareness?

Argument from Evil and Refutation of 'Meaning Beyond Existence'
Nasafi argues from evil and ridicules the notion of a 'meaning beyond existence'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses the existence of evil acts to argue that shared agency would make God the author of evil, and why the notion of a 'meaning beyond existence' is unintelligible.

Core Idea
If God creates all acts, He would be the author of evil—impossible; a 'meaning' beyond existence is irrational.

After the logical dilemma, Nasafi presents two additional arguments. First, he argues from the existence of evil and foolish human acts: if God brings these into existence (as shared agency claims), then God would be guilty of ugliness and folly. Second, he refutes the notion that there is a 'meaning beyond existence' that makes an essence an agent, calling it unintelligible. These arguments reinforce the rejection of shared agency by showing its theological and philosophical absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the shift from the dilemma to new arguments: first ethical, then metaphysical.
2
Understand the ethical argument
Nasafi points out that some human acts are ugly (qabīḥ) and foolish (safah). If God creates them via ījād, then God is the author of ugliness and folly—unacceptable.
He uses the opponents' own hierarchy: ījād is above direct action, so it is even worse.
3
Analyze the metaphysical critique
The phrase 'meaning beyond existence' refers to the Mu'tazilite claim that God's act of creation requires an additional attribute. Nasafi dismisses this as unintelligible and outside rational discourse.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi states that these are the well-known doubts of the opponents and he has refuted them. Consider how this closure ties the arguments together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان هذا: أن الله تعالى إذا أراد إيجاد فعل العبد لا يخلو الأمر إما أن كان للعبد قدرة الامتناع أو لم يكن، فإن لم يكن فهو الاضطرار المحض والجبر الصريح إذ الفعل الضروري ما يتولى الله تعالى تخليقه وليس للعبد قدرة دفعه عن نفسه والامتناع عنه كالألوان والهيئات وحركات العروق النابضة وأشباه ذلك، والقول به فاسد يعرف فساده بالضرورة والعقل على ما مر في إبطال كلام الجبرية، وإن كان للعبد قدرة الامتناع فلا يحصل هذا الفعل، فلا يثبت ما هو مقدور الله تعالى إذا اختار العبد الامتناع عنه، فيؤدي إلى تعجيز الله جل الله عن ذلك وذلك باطل، وكذا لو أراد العبد تحصيله إن لم تكن لله تعالى قدرة الامتناع عنه فهو مضطر فيما يفعل؛ لأن فعل ما لا قدرة للفاعل على الامتناع عنه وتحصيل ضده فعل ضروري والقول به كفر، وإن كان لله تعالى قدرة الامتناع وكان العبد مضطرًا إذ ليس له قدرة استجلابه عند قصده إلى ذلك إذا امتنع الله تعالى، ولا قدرة دفعه إذا أوجده الله تعالى. وهذا هو حد الفعل الاضطراري، ولأن من أفعال العباد ما هو قبيح وسفه وإيجاد القبيح قبيح وإيجاد السفه سفه، وموجد ذلك سفيه لأن الإيجاد فوق المباشرة والاكتساب عندكم، ثم مباشرة القبيح قبيحة ومباشرة السفه سفه فالإيجاد أولى أن يكون كذلك، وكذا الموجد أولى أن يكون سفيها من المباشرة، وكذا يلزمون الظلم والجور على هذا الوجه. على أن ادعاء معنى وراء الوجود تصير به الذات فاعلاً ادعاء أمر غير معقول، فكان مدعي ذلك خارجًا عن قضية العقول. وهذه هي الشبه المعروفة لهم اقتصرنا عليه وأعرضنا عما سواها مخافة التطويل.
The explanation of this: that when Allah Almighty wills to bring into existence an act of the servant, the matter is not free from either the servant having the power to refrain or not. If he does not have it, it is pure compulsion and explicit predestination (jabr), for the necessary act is that which Allah Almighty undertakes to create and the servant has no power to repel it from himself or refrain from it, like colors, forms, pulse movements, and the like. Holding this opinion is corrupt, and its corruption is known by necessity and reason, as has preceded in the refutation of the Jabriyya. If the servant has the power to refrain, then this act does not occur, so what is the object of power of Allah Almighty is not established when the servant chooses to refrain from it, leading to incapacitating Allah — exalted is Allah above that — and that is false. Likewise, if the servant wants to acquire it, if Allah Almighty does not have the power to refrain from it, then the servant is compelled in what he does, because an act over which the agent has no power to refrain or to bring about its opposite is a necessary act, and holding this is disbelief. And if Allah Almighty has the power to refrain, then the servant is compelled, because he has no power to bring it about when he intends it if Allah refrains, nor power to repel it if Allah brings it into existence. This is the definition of the compelled act. And because some of the acts of servants are ugly (qabīḥ) and foolish (safah), and bringing the ugly into existence is ugly, and bringing foolishness into existence is foolish. The one who brings it into existence is foolish, because bringing into existence (ījād) is above direct action and acquisition in your view. Then direct action of the ugly is ugly, and direct action of foolishness is foolish, so bringing into existence is even more so. Likewise, the one who brings into existence is even more foolish than the one who directly acts. And likewise, they are led to (attributing) injustice and tyranny in this manner. Moreover, the claim of a meaning beyond existence by which the essence becomes an agent is a claim of something unintelligible, so the claimant of that is outside the verdict of intellects. And these are the well-known doubts of theirs; we have limited ourselves to these and turned away from others for fear of prolongation.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the doctrine of shared agency, containing several sub-arguments. First, the author presents a dilemma: if God wills to create an act of the servant, either the servant has the power (qudra) to refrain from that act or not. If the servant lacks that power, then the act is purely compelled (iḍṭirār) and jabr, which is false and already refuted. If the servant has the power to refrain, then when he refrains, God's intended act does not occur, which incapacitates God (taʿjīz) — an impossibility. In a parallel scenario, if the servant wants to do an act, either God has the power to prevent it or not. If God lacks that power, the servant is compelled in doing it (since God cannot prevent it), which is disbelief. If God has the power to prevent, then the servant is again compelled because he cannot bring it about if God withholds. So in all cases, the doctrine leads to either jabr or taʿjīz. Second, the author argues from the existence of evil acts (qabīḥ, safah) among human actions: if God creates these acts (since He is the 'mūjid' above direct action and acquisition), then God would be the author of evil and thus be foolish or unjust, which is impossible. Third, the author refutes the idea that there is a 'meaning beyond existence' that makes an essence an agent; this is unintelligible and outside rational discourse. The author concludes by stating that these are the well-known doubts of the opponents, and he has limited himself to refuting them.

Key Terms
قبيح
سفه
إيجاد
مباشرة
اكتساب
معنى وراء الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since God is all-powerful, He can create evil acts without being blamed.
Correction: Nasafi argues that creating evil is itself evil, and God is beyond doing evil. Shared agency would force this conclusion, so it must be rejected.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's ethical argument, what would be the consequence if God creates acts that are ugly or foolish?
A) God's actions are beyond moral categories.
B) God would be the author of evil, which is impossible.
C) Only the direct action is blameworthy, not the creation.
D) The acts would not be truly ugly because God created them.

Key Takeaway
Shared agency implies God creates evil, which is impossible, and the notion of a 'meaning beyond existence' is irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you honestly feel that you are the one who chose it, or do you sometimes internally blame divine decree? How does Nasafi's argument from the ugliness of evil force you to accept full personal responsibility for your actions?
?
Are you ever tempted to use complex theological theories to avoid the simple truth that God is just and you are accountable? This lesson calls a 'meaning beyond existence' unintelligible—do you have any intellectual escape hatches that you need to abandon for the sake of clear faith?
?
If you truly believed that God creates every evil deed, would that make you less diligent in avoiding sin? Reflect on your own life: does the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb) help you take ownership of your choices, or does it sometimes feel like an excuse? What must change in your heart today?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Proof from 'Wa Allah Khalaqakum Wa Ma Ta'malun'
Al-Nasafi argues that the verse proves actions are created and refutes a grammatical objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' (37:96) serves as a proof that Allah creates human actions, and how al-Nasafi defends this interpretation using Arabic grammar.

Core Idea
The verse proves Allah creates your actions, not just objects.

This lesson examines one of the key Qur'anic proofs for the Māturīdī position on human actions. Al-Nasafi presents the verse 'Wa Allah khalaqakum wa ma ta'malun' and explains that grammatically, 'ma ta'malun' means 'your action', not 'the things you make'. He then refutes an objection based on other verses. Understanding his argument requires careful attention to Arabic grammar as used by the classical scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Try to grasp the overall flow: the proof, the objection, and the reply.
The passage uses technical grammar terms; don't worry if they are unfamiliar.
2
Identify the grammatical rule about 'ma' + verb
Focus on the claim that when 'ma' is directly attached to a verb (without a pronoun), the combination expresses the verbal noun (masdar). This is according to Sibawayh.
Al-Nasafi contrasts this with the case when a pronoun (ha') is added, which then expresses the object.
3
Understand the objection and al-Nasafi's answer
The opponents cite verses like 'Do you worship what you carve?' to argue that 'ma' refers to the carved object. Al-Nasafi responds by distinguishing the literal meaning from the implied pronoun, insisting on the default rule.
He also provides supporting evidence from another verse: 'A recompense for what they used to do'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على صحة ما ذهبنا إليه قوله تعالى: وَاللَّهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ (أي وعملكم) فإن كلمة ما إذا اتصلت بالفعل صارا عبارة عن المصدر تقول: أعجبني ما صنعت أي صنعك، والخصوم يعترضون على هذا ويقولون: المراد منه المعمول أي خلقكم ومعمولكم وهي الأصنام المعمولة وهي أجسام ولا خلاف في كونها مخلوقة نظيره قوله تعالى: أَتَعْبُدُونَ مَا تَنْحِتُونَ وهم ما كانوا يعبدون فعلهم الذي هو النحت بل كانوا يعبدون المنحوت، وكذا قوله: تَلْقَفُ مَا يَأْفِكُونَ. والجواب أن كلمة ما إذا اتصلت بالفعل كان مجموعها عبارة عن المصدر عند الإطلاق هذا هو مذهب سيبويه وإن خالفه الأخفش في ذلك حيث جوز سيبويه أن يقال أعجبني ما قمت أي قيامك، ولو كان ذا عبارة عن المفعول لما جاز ذلك إلا في الفعل المتعدي، والأخفش لا يجوزه إلا في المعتدي، غير أن العارفين بكلام العرب المتبحرين في علم النحو مالوا إلى تصحيح قول سيبوبه، وإذا ذكر العائد وهو الهاء فقيل: أعجبني ما صنعته حينئذ تكون عبارة عن المفعول، وفي كل موضع جعل عبارة عن المفعول كان بإضمار الهاء وهو عدول عن ظاهر الكلام بالدليل ولا دليل فمن ادعى قيام الدليل فليبرزوه، والدليل على أنه عند الإطلاق ينصرف إلى ما بينا قوله تعالى: جَزَاء بِمَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ أي بعملهم دون
And among what indicates the correctness of what we have held is His saying, the Exalted: 'And Allah created you and what you do' (i.e., and your action). For the word 'ma' when attached to a verb, the two together become an expression for the verbal noun (masdar). You say: 'I liked what you did' meaning your doing. And the opponents object to this and say: What is meant by it is the object acted upon, i.e., He created you and your wrought thing, and that is the idols which are wrought, and they are bodies, and there is no disagreement about them being created. Analogous to it is His saying, the Exalted: 'Do you worship what you carve?' And they used to worship not their own act which is carving, but rather they used to worship the carved thing. Likewise His saying: 'It swallows what they faked.' And the answer is that the word 'ma' when attached to a verb, their total becomes an expression for the verbal noun when absolute (unqualified). This is the school of Sibawayh, though al-Akhfash disagreed with him in that. For Sibawayh allowed that one may say 'I liked what you stood' meaning your standing; and if it were an expression for the object, that would not be permissible except with a transitive verb. And al-Akhfash does not allow it except with a transitive verb. However, those knowledgeable in the speech of the Arabs, the deeply versed in the science of grammar, inclined to validate the saying of Sibawayh. And when the referent (al-'ā'id) i.e., the ha' (suffix pronoun) is mentioned, and it is said: 'I liked what you made it' (a'jabanī mā ṣana'tahu), then it becomes an expression for the object. And in every instance where it is made an expression for the object, it is by implying the ha', and that is a departure from the apparent meaning (ẓāhir al-kalām) by evidence, and there is no evidence. So whoever claims the existence of evidence, let him produce it. And the evidence that when absolute (unqualified) it refers to what we have explained is His saying, the Exalted: 'A recompense for what they used to do' i.e., for their action, not ...
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the Māturīdī position that human actions are created by Allah, using the Qur'anic verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' (37:96). The author argues that the word 'ma' (what) when directly attached to a verb forms a construction that expresses the verbal noun (masdar), i.e., the action itself, not the object. Thus the verse means 'Allah created you and your action'. The opponents object by saying that 'ma' here refers to the object acted upon (the idols), pointing to other verses like 'Do you worship what you carve?' where worshipping is directed to the carved thing, not the act of carving. The author refutes this by explaining the grammatical rule: according to Sibawayh, when 'ma' is attached to a verb without a suffix pronoun (the ha'), it indicates the verbal noun. Only when the pronoun is added (e.g., 'ma ṣana'tahu') does it refer to the object. Since the verse lacks the pronoun, the default is the verbal noun. This is supported by another verse 'A recompense for what they used to do' (56:24), where 'ma' with a verb clearly means their action. The author stresses that the opponents' interpretation requires an implicit pronoun (ha') which is a departure from the apparent meaning without evidence. Thus the proper meaning is that Allah creates both human beings and their actions.

المصدر
المفعول
سيبويه
الأخفش
العائد
الهاء
ظاهر الكلام
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Wa Allah khalaqakum wa ma ta'malun' only refers to created objects (idols) and not to human actions.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows through grammatical analysis that 'ma ta'malun' without a pronoun means 'your action', based on the rule of Sibawayh that 'ma' + verb expresses the verbal noun. The object interpretation would require an implied pronoun, which is unsupported.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what does the construction 'ma' + verb (without a pronoun) typically express?
A) The object of the verb
B) The verbal noun (action)
C) The subject of the verb
D) The time of the action

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi uses Arabic grammatical analysis to show that the simple attachment of 'ma' to a verb indicates the action itself, making the verse a clear proof that Allah creates human actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the verse 'Allah created you and what you do,' do you truly believe that every action you have ever done—including your decisions and deeds—is created by Allah? How does this reshape your view of your own achievements and failures?
?
In your daily life, when you succeed or fail, do you attribute the action to yourself or to Allah? Reflect on a recent good deed: did you feel it was solely your effort, or did you acknowledge that Allah created that action in you? What would change if you consistently believed that He creates all your actions?
?
The opponents in this passage argued that humans create their own acts of worship (like carving idols) even if the object is created by Allah. Are there areas in your life where you subtly claim independent power to create your own deeds? How can you cultivate a deeper dependence on Allah for every action, from the smallest to the greatest?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Proof from Knowledge to the Impossibility of Human Creative Power
Proves that humans cannot create their own actions due to lack of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle that a perfect act requires a knowledgeable agent is used to prove that humans cannot create their own actions.

Core Idea
A perfect act proves a knowledgeable agent; humans lack that knowledge, so they cannot create.

This lesson examines a key argument from the Maturidi tradition against attributing creative power to humans. The author begins by noting a concession from Mu'tazili opponents that a well-crafted act requires a knowledgeable creator. He then applies this self-evident principle to show that since humans are ignorant of how their own actions come into existence, they cannot truly create them. The argument reinforces the Sunni position that all creation is from Allah alone.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conceded principle
The passage starts with a concession that a solid, perfect act requires an agent with knowledge. Recognize that even opponents agree on this principle.
2
See how the principle is self-evident
The author argues that this principle is rooted in innate reason; anyone seeing a perfect craft immediately knows the craftsman has knowledge.
This is a rational first principle, not derived from revelation.
3
Apply the principle to human actions
If humans had creative power, they would need knowledge of the act's details (how it emerges, its measures, states). But they are clearly ignorant.
4
Draw the conclusion
The lack of knowledge proves the lack of creative power. The author also notes that humans cannot even conceive of the act's creation, so it is beyond their power.
This completes the proof that human creative power is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مساعدة الخصوم فإنهم ساعدونا أن من شرط خروج الفعل محكمًا متقنا كون الفاعل عالما بذلك، ولذا كان الفعل المحكم المتقن دليل كون الفاعل عالمًا وإن لم يكن عند الكعبي دليلا على العلم لا في الشاهد ولا في الغائب، وعند الجبائي يكون دليلاً على العلم لا في الشاهد دون الغائب على ما سبق في مسألة الصفات وكذا شهادات المعارف، فإن كون الفعل المحكم المتقن مفتقرا إلى كون الفاعل له عالما به مما تمكّن في بداهة العقول وأوائل الفكر، حتى إن من رأى فعلاً محكمًا متقنا حكم بأول الفكرة وسابق البديهة بكون فاعله عالما حاذقا في الصناعة، ومن جوز حصول مثله ممن لا علم له به تسارع الناس إلى تسفيهه ونسبته إلى الغباوة والجهل، وإذا ثبتت هذه القاعدة فنقول : لو كان للعبد قدرة تخليق فعله لكان ينبغي أن يكون له العلم بكيفية خروجه من العدم إلى الوجود وبما يخرج عليه فعله من المقادير والأحوال والأوصاف، وحيث رأينا جهله بهذه المعاني وانعدام علمه بها دل أن لا قدرة له على فعله من حيث التخليق. ألا ترى أنه لو كان جاهلا بالفعل أصلا لكان خارجا عن قدرته أصلا، فإذا كان جاهلا بالتخليق كان ذلك خارجا عن قدرته، ولا شك في جهله بما بينا فإنه لاشك أنه لا يقدر في عقله كيفية خروج الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود، ولا يتصور ذلك أيضا في وهمه فما ينبغي أن يكون له عليه من حيث التخليق قدرة، وكذا لا يبلغ علمه مقدار فعله ويقصر عن الوقوف على القدر الذي يشغله من الهواء والمكان وعدد أجزاء المكان الذي قطعه بتحريك يده وبقطع كل جزء لا يتجزأ من
As for the concession of the opponents, they conceded to us that among the conditions for an act to emerge as solid and perfect is that the agent be knowledgeable of it. Therefore, the solid, perfect act is evidence that the agent is knowledgeable, even if according to al-Ka'bi it is not evidence for knowledge, neither in the witnessed nor in the unseen, and according to al-Jubba'i it is evidence for knowledge in the witnessed but not in the unseen, as previously in the issue of attributes and likewise the testimonies of the intellects. For the fact that the solid, perfect act requires that its agent be knowledgeable of it is firmly established in the innate reason and primary thoughts, such that whoever sees a solid, perfect act judges at first thought and prior intuition that its agent is knowledgeable and skilled in the craft. And whoever permits the occurrence of the like from one who has no knowledge of it, people hasten to declare him foolish and attribute him to stupidity and ignorance. And when this principle is established, we say: If the servant had the power to create his action, he would need to have knowledge of how it emerges from non-existence into existence, and of the measures, states, and attributes upon which his action emerges. And since we see his ignorance of these meanings and his lack of knowledge of them, it indicates that he has no power over his action in terms of creation. Do you not see that if he were ignorant of the action entirely, it would be entirely outside his power? So if he is ignorant of creation, then that is outside his power. And there is no doubt about his ignorance of what we have explained, for undoubtedly he does not conceive in his mind the manner of a thing's emergence from non-existence into existence, nor does he imagine it in his fantasy. So he should not have power over it in terms of creation. Likewise, his knowledge does not reach the extent of his action, and falls short of comprehending the amount that it occupies of air and space and the number of parts of the space that he traverses by moving his hand, and by cutting every indivisible part of
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that humans cannot have creative power over their actions. The author begins by citing a concession from opponents (the Mu'tazila) that a perfect act requires a knowledgeable agent. This is a rational principle agreed upon even by al-Ka'bi and al-Jubba'i, though they differed on its scope. The author emphasizes that this principle is self-evident: anyone seeing a well-crafted act immediately knows the agent has skill and knowledge. Once this principle is established, the argument proceeds: if a human had power to create his own action, he would need to know exactly how that action comes into existence, including its precise measurements and qualities. But humans are clearly ignorant of such details; they do not know how a thing transitions from non-existence to existence, nor can they conceive it. Therefore, they cannot have creative power over their actions. The author reinforces this by noting that total ignorance of an act implies total lack of power over it, and the same applies to ignorance of creation. He concludes that human knowledge is insufficient even to grasp the full extent of their own movements, such as the space and parts traversed. Hence, creative power is impossible for humans.

Key Terms
تخليق
قدرة
العلم
الفعل المحكم المتقن
بداهة العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle that a perfect act requires a knowledgeable agent only applies to complex human artifacts, not to ordinary human actions like moving a hand.
Correction: The author extends this principle to all actions, including simple movements. If even a simple movement requires knowledge of its details (which humans lack), then humans cannot have creative power over any action.

Checkpoint
On what self-evident principle does the author base his proof that humans cannot create their actions?
A) Every action requires a free will.
B) A solid and perfect act requires its agent to be knowledgeable.
C) Humans are ignorant of their own actions.
D) The Mu'tazila conceded the point.

Key Takeaway
Because humans are fundamentally ignorant of the creative process, they cannot have the power to create their own actions; all creative power belongs to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may feel that you are the one who chooses and performs your actions. But can you, even for a moment, explain how any single movement of your hand comes into existence from nothing? If you cannot, will you accept that you are not the creator of your own actions, and that every movement is given by Allah? How would this change your sense of ownership over your deeds?
?
When you sin, do you blame yourself? But if you cannot create your actions, is your sin truly your creation? This lesson strips you of the power to claim your good deeds as your own, but also of the burden of being the ultimate cause of your evil. Does that free you from guilt, or does it make you more accountable for what you acquire? Reflect: will you now turn to Allah in gratitude for every good action and in repentance for every evil, knowing that He alone creates?
?
Is there any part of your life where you pride yourself on your skills or achievements? This lesson tells you that even the simplest act—moving your hand—requires knowledge you do not possess. Does that humble you? Will you now see every success as a gift from the All-Knowing Creator and every failure as a reminder of your need for His power? What will change in your daily prayers and reliance on Him?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Ignorance of Details Negates Human Power Over Action
Al-Nasafi proves the servant's ignorance of action details negates his power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that human ignorance of the detailed components of an action negates human power over that action, and how this supports the Maturidi position on divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Ignorance of details means no power over action.

Al-Nasafi presents a detailed argument from ignorance: the servant does not know the countless parts of air, time, and moral value involved in even a simple action. Since power requires knowledge of what one is doing, the servant cannot be the true agent. This passage is a key Maturidi proof for attributing all actions to God while preserving human moral responsibility through descriptive categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the flow of the argument. Note the repeated emphasis on ignorance (جهل) and its connection to lack of power (قدرة).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three aspects of ignorance
The author lists three ways the servant is ignorant of his action's details: ignorance of spatial parts (air, place), ignorance of temporal parts (indivisible moments), and ignorance of moral quality (good/evil). Mark these in the text.
Each aspect leads to a separate conclusion about ignorance.
3
Follow the conclusion
After establishing ignorance from these three aspects, the author concludes that the servant has no power over the action. Instead, the action depends on another's power (God's). Notice the final distinction: the servant knows the action only by its descriptive categories (movement, obedience, etc.).
This distinction preserves human accountability.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this argument upholds divine omnipotence while allowing for human moral responsibility. The servant's knowledge of the moral category (obedience/disobedience) is sufficient for reward and punishment, but not for causal power.
This is a classic Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهواء تحصل في يده حركة وبمجموع تلك الحركات يحصل ما قصد إليه من الفعل فإذا لم يكن عالما بعدد أجزاء الهواء والمكان لم يكن عالما بعدد الحركات التي حصل فيها الفعل الذي قصد فلم يكن عالما بقدر الفعل وكذا يكون جاهلا بقدر الزمان الذي يشغله بفعله وفي كل جزء لا يتجزأ من الزمان يحصل فعل على حدة، فكان الجهل بأجزاء الزمان جهلا بعدد الافعال التي يحصل بمجموعها ما هو المقصود. وكذا يخرج عن حسن وقبح لا يبلغ علمه قدرهما بل يحصل اعتقاد الكافر والمبتدع على قبح لا علم لهما به البتة بل يظن كل واحد منهما أن اعتقاده في غاية الحسن ونهاية الصحة وهو موصوف بضد ذلك، ولما ثبت جهله بفعله من هذه الوجوه دل أن لا قدرة له عليه من هذه الوجوه، فكان من هذه الوجوه متعلقا بقدرة غيره وهو يعلم به من حيث إنه حركة أو سكونا طاعة أو معصية مأمور به أو منهي عنه، فكان له من هذه الوجوه عليه قدرة
Air: movement occurs in his hand, and by the totality of those movements what is intended of the action occurs. So if he is not knowledgeable of the number of parts of the air and the place, he is not knowledgeable of the number of movements in which the intended action occurred, so he is not knowledgeable of the measure of the action. Likewise, he is ignorant of the measure of time which he occupies with his action, and in every indivisible part of time a separate action occurs. So ignorance of the parts of time is ignorance of the number of actions by whose totality what is intended occurs. Likewise, [knowledge] excludes good and evil; his knowledge does not reach their measure. Rather, the belief of the disbeliever and the innovator occurs upon evil, they have no knowledge of it at all, but each one of them thinks that his belief is in utmost goodness and utmost health, while he is described by the opposite of that. And since his ignorance of his action is established from these aspects, it indicates that he has no power over it from these aspects. So from these aspects, it is dependent on the power of another, while he knows it insofar as it is movement or stillness, obedience or disobedience, commanded or prohibited. So from these aspects, that other has power over it.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the human servant does not have power (qudra) over his action because he is ignorant of its detailed components. The author argues as follows: The intended action occurs through a series of movements in the air. The servant does not know the number of parts of air and place, so he does not know the number of movements. Hence he does not know the measure (qadr) of the action. Likewise, he is ignorant of the time the action takes, and each indivisible part of time contains a separate action. Ignorance of these parts means ignorance of the total number of actions. Additionally, the servant's knowledge does not extend to the true good or evil of his action; for example, the disbeliever and innovator think their belief is good, but it is actually evil. Since ignorance is established from these aspects, it follows that the servant has no power over the action from these aspects. Instead, the action is dependent on another's power (i.e., God's). The servant only knows the action in terms of its descriptive categories (movement/stillness, obedience/disobedience, etc.). Thus, from these aspects, God has power over it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing the moral category of an action (obedience/disobedience) implies power over it.
Correction: The author distinguishes between descriptive knowledge (knowing the action as obedience or disobedience) and causal power over the action. Ignorance of detailed components negates power, but descriptive knowledge is sufficient for moral accountability.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the servant has no power over his action?
A) Because the servant does not know the number of parts of air and time.
B) Because the servant is always disobedient.
C) Because the action is too small.
D) Because the servant does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The servant's ignorance of the detailed components of his action proves that true agency belongs to God alone, while human responsibility is limited to knowing the moral category of the action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have performed countless actions today—how many of their spatial and temporal components did you know? Does this change your view of your own agency?
?
When you sin, you often think you know exactly what you are doing. But this lesson shows you are ignorant of the true moral quality. How does that affect your repentance?
?
If your power over even a simple movement is so limited, how should you approach your intention and dependence on God for every action?

The Opponents' Dilemma: Concede or Contradict
Al-Nasafi presents three conditional scenarios for opponents' responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three conditional scenarios Al-Nasafi presents to expose the contradictions in the opponents' stance regarding divine power and human action.

Core Idea
Opponents cannot consistently deny divine power without falling into contradiction.

Al-Nasafi systematically dismantles the opposition by examining the logical consequences of their claims. He presents three possible responses to his argument, showing that each leads either to submission to truth (invalidating their own doctrine) or to blatant contradiction with scripture and reason. This lesson walks through these scenarios, revealing the inescapable dilemma faced by those who deny that all actions depend on divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once to understand the flow of Al-Nasafi's argument. Notice the conditional structure: 'If they... then...'
This passage is a logical demonstration, not just narration.
2
Identify the three scenarios
Look for the three 'If' clauses: (1) if they concede, (2) if they claim knowledge, (3) if they acknowledge ignorance yet claim power. Mark each one and its consequence.
The third scenario is key: they admit ignorance but still claim power, which contradicts the Qur'an and reason.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Consider why the third scenario is a contradiction. Al-Nasafi says they have 'contradicted and opposed the Book of Allah by making knowledge thereof a condition for the affirmation of power.'
The Qur'anic condition: knowledge is required for power to be valid.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Understand that this dilemma forces opponents to either accept the truth or persist in stubbornness. This sets the stage for Al-Nasafi's next argument about the necessity of divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ساعدت الخصوم على هذا فقد انقادوا للحق وأبطلوا مذهبهم، وإن ادعوا ثبوت علم للعبد بهذه الوجوه كابروا وعاندوا وادعوا ما يعرفون كذبهم فيه بيقين، وإن سلموا فقد العلم وثبوت الجهل بهذه الوجوه ومع ذلك أثبتوا للعباد قدرة الفعل مع جهلهم بهذه الوجوه فقد ناقضوا وعاندوا كتاب الله تعالى في جعله العلم بذلك شرطا لثبوت القدرة وخرجوا أيضا عن شهادات المعارف، وإن زعموا أن الأفعال من هذه الوجوه ليست بداخله تحت قدرة العبد لانعدام شريطة القدرة وليست بداخله أيضا تحت قدرة الله ولا حاصلة بها، فقد جعلوا حصول الفعل من هذه الوجوه بذاته من غير حاجة إلى مع كونه حدثًا، ولو جاز ذا في الفعل لجاز في جميع المعالم، ولو جاز ذا في جميع العالم لبطلت الدلالة على ثبوت الصانع مع أن التكليف بهذا الفعل، والثواب والعقاب عليه لما جاز ولم يقبح مع أنه من هذه الوجوه غير داخل تحت قدرته لماذا لم يجز لو كان الفعل من هذه الوجوه داخلاً تحت قدرة الله تعالى؟ فأي فرق في حق العبد بين أن يكون الفعل من هذه الوجوه داخلاً تحت قدرة الله تعالى وبين أن لا يكون داخلاً تحت قدرة أحد إذا كان خارجا عن قدرته من هذه الوجوه؟ فإذا تقرر بما ذكرنا ما ادعينا من انعدام شريطة ثبوت قدرة التخليق للعبد والله الموفق.
If the opponents concede to this, then they have submitted to the truth and invalidated their doctrine. And if they claim the existence of knowledge for the servant concerning these aspects, they act in obstinacy and stubbornness, and claim what they know with certainty is false. And if they acknowledge knowledge and the establishment of ignorance in these aspects, yet with that they affirm for servants the power of action while they are ignorant of these aspects, then they have contradicted and opposed the Book of Allah by making knowledge thereof a condition for the affirmation of power, and they have also departed from the testimonies of the intellects. And if they claim that actions from these aspects are not within the servant's power due to the absence of the condition of power, and also are not within God's power nor produced by Him, then they have made the occurrence of the action from these aspects by itself without need for a cause despite its being originated. And if this were permissible for the action, it would be permissible for all [created] things. And if this were permissible for all the world, the proof for the existence of the Maker would be invalidated. Moreover, the obligation of this action and the reward and punishment upon it would not be permissible and would not be good, while it is from these aspects not within His power. Why would it not be permissible if the action from these aspects were within the power of Allah? What difference, with respect to the servant, between the action being from these aspects within the power of Allah and its not being within the power of anyone if it is outside his power from these aspects? So it has been established by what we mentioned what we claimed: the absence of the condition for the affirmation of the power of creation for the servant. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage discusses the consequences of the opponents' positions. The author presents three conditional scenarios. First, if opponents concede the argument, they have submitted to truth. Second, if they claim the servant has knowledge of these aspects, they are obstinate. Third, if they accept ignorance yet still claim power, they contradict the Qur'an and reason. Then the author addresses a fourth claim: that the action from these aspects is neither under the servant's power nor under God's power. This would imply the action exists by itself without a creator, which is absurd because it is originated (hadith). If this were allowed for actions, it would extend to all things, invalidating the proof for the existence of the Maker. Moreover, if the action is not under God's power, why would it be permissible for Him to command, reward, or punish? Thus the condition for the servant's power of creation (qudrat al-takhliq) is absent.

Key Terms
شرط
شريطة
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowledge is not a necessary condition for power; the action can occur without knowledge.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the Qur'an and intellect require knowledge as a condition for power; therefore, ignorance negates the possibility of effective power.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Nasafi say about opponents who claim the servant has knowledge of these aspects?
A) They have submitted to truth.
B) They are obstinate and lying.
C) They have contradicted the Qur'an.
D) They have proven their doctrine.

Key Takeaway
Opponents of divine power face a dilemma: concede to truth, claim false knowledge, or contradict scripture and reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a belief or habit even after clear proof from revelation and reason showed it was wrong? What would it cost you to submit fully to the truth right now?
?
Al-Nasafi argues that affirming power while ignorant is contradictory. In what areas of your life do you claim ability or control over matters you have not genuinely sought to understand? How can you humbly exchange that claim for seeking knowledge?
?
This passage forces a choice: either concede to truth or live in contradiction. Is there any area of your faith where you are tolerating contradiction instead of surrendering? What one step can you take today to align your conviction and action with what you know to be true?

The Absurdity of Denying Divine Power
Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that actions are neither under human nor divine power, showing its absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi refutes the claim that actions are neither under human nor divine power, showing it leads to the absurdity of a world without a Creator.

Core Idea
Denying both human and divine power for actions implies an uncaused world, negating the proof for God's existence.

Having shown the inconsistency of opponents who claim human power without knowledge, Al-Nasafi now addresses a more radical position: that actions are not under anyone's power—neither the servant's nor God's. This leads to a cascade of absurdities. If actions exist without a cause, then all things could do so, and the entire universe could be self-originated, destroying the proof for the Creator. Moreover, divine command and judgment become meaningless. This lesson unpacks this argument, culminating in the conclusion that the servant cannot possess the power of creation (takhliq).

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth scenario
Focus on the opponent's claim: actions are outside both the servant's power and God's power. Note the phrase 'not within the servant's power... also not within God's power.'
This is the most extreme position.
2
Trace the consequences
Al-Nasafi then deduces: if actions exist by themselves (without a cause), then all things could. That would invalidate the argument for the necessary existence of God. Follow this logical chain step by step.
The key point: a world without a creator is absurd.
3
Examine the theological problem
Consider why divine commands and recompense would be unjust if actions are not under God's power. The passage asks: 'What difference... between its being within God's power and not?'
This highlights the necessity of divine power for moral accountability.
4
Conclude with the condition of creation power
The final sentence asserts that the condition for the power of creation (takhliq) in the servant is absent. Understand how the whole argument leads to this conclusion.
The condition is knowledge, which the servant lacks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If the opponents concede to this, then they have submitted to the truth and invalidated their doctrine. And if they claim the existence of knowledge for the servant concerning these aspects, they act in obstinacy and stubbornness, and claim what they know with certainty is false. And if they acknowledge knowledge and the establishment of ignorance in these aspects, yet with that they affirm for servants the power of action while they are ignorant of these aspects, then they have contradicted and opposed the Book of Allah by making knowledge thereof a condition for the affirmation of power, and they have also departed from the testimonies of the intellects. And if this were permissible for the action, it would be permissible for all [created] things. ?

?

Causality and Created Things

Actions Occur Contrary to the Servant's Intention
Nasafi demonstrates that actions occur opposite to intent and proves divine origin through a trilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that actions often occur with qualities opposite to what the agent intended, and why this proves a divine originator.

Core Idea
Actions emerge with qualities the agent did not intend, pointing to a sole originator, God.

This passage addresses a common observation: a person intends a good action, but it turns out harmful or evil. Nasafi argues that this mismatch shows that the ultimate originator of the action's quality is not the human agent but God, using examples and a logical trilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first part of the passage where Nasafi sets up his argument with examples of walking and disbelief.
Focus on the contrast between intention and outcome.
2
Understand the examples
Consider the examples: a person walks intending no harm but suffers pain; a disbeliever intends his disbelief as good but it is evil. Notice that the outcome contradicts the intention.
3
Follow the trilemma
Nasafi presents three possibilities for the cause of the evil quality: from the agent, from the action itself, or from a free agent. Understand why the first two are impossible.
The first leads to doctrines of natures and stars, the second to self-subsistence of actions.
4
Conclude with divine origin
The only viable option is that a free agent (God) originates the action with its quality. This supports the Maturidi doctrine of kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويباشر ذلك بجوارحه يريد حصول ذلك كله على صفة الحسن دون القبيح ومعلوم أن حصول ذلك على ما يضاد الحسن وهو القبح لا على الحسن الذي قصده. وأما ما يعرف ذلك فيه بالحس فإن الإنسان يتأذى بالمشي الدائم ويتألم بالقيام الممتد اللازم، وكذا بكل فعل ثابر المرء على اكتسابه وواظب على ارتكابه، ولا شك أن الفعل لا يقصد إليه الفاعل ليتألم به ويتأذى، ومع هذا حصل مؤذيًا متعبًا فصح ما ادعينا من خروج الفعل على ضد ما يقصده العبد من الوصف ما لم يقصده، فإذا لم يوجد على ذلك دل أن له موجد خرج الموجود على ما أراده. يحقق هذا أن خروج الكفر وعبادة الصنم على ما يقصده الكافر من الحسن وصوابًا وطاعة الله تعالى يتقرب بها إليه يضاد الحكمة وينافيها، وخروجه على ما خرج عليه من القبح مما يقتضيه التدبير الصائب والحكمة البالغة. وجوده على هذا الوصف وإن لم يقصده الكافر إما إن كان بالكافر أم بالفعل نفسه بموجب مختار تولى إيجاده عليه، فإن كان بالكافر أوجب ذا جواز حصول جميع الأفعال الحكمية ممن لا يقصد تحيلها، وذا يؤدي إلى القول بالطبائع والنجوم وهو فاسد على أن فساد ذلك متقرر في البداهة وإن لم يؤد ذلك إلى مذهب باطل. وإن كان بالفعل نفسه أوجب ذلك جواز وجود جميع الأفعال الحكمية من السماوات والأرضين بلا موجد قصد إيجادها وتحصيلها، وذا محال. وإن كان بفاعل مختار تولى إيجاده على ما هو عليه من الوصف لصح ما ذهبنا إليه.
فإن قالوا هب أن صفة القبح للفعل وكون الفعل مؤذيًا متعبًا تولى غير الكافر والماشي إيجاده ولكن لم قلتم إن إيجاد نفس الكفر والمشي تولى إيجادهما غير الكافر والماشي؟ قيل : هذا السؤال إنما يتحقق فيما كانت الصفة وراء معنى الموصوف، كما فى الأجسام وصفاتها فيتخبط إنسان فيقول: تولى فاعل إيجاد الصفة وآخر إيجاد الموصوف فلا يكون محيلاً في نفس الدعوى، وإن كان الدليل يوجب لدى التحقيق فساد قول من يدعي تعدد الموجدين، فأما في الأعراض التي كانت صفاتها في الحقيقة راجعة إلى ذواتها فلا يستقيم مثل هذا الكلام.
فإن قالوا: لما كانت صفات الأفعال راجعة إلى أعيانها فالكفر إذا فعل وهو قبيح فيتولى العبد إيجاده من حيث الكفر، ويتولى الله تعالى إيجاده من حيث القبح، وكذا هذا في المشى والإيذاء والإيلام، وهذا معنى ما ذكر الكعبي أن العبد مختار من فعله مضطر في تألمه، والمختار عنده من تولى إيجاد الكفر، والمضطر عنده من تولى غيره إيجاد صفة فيه لا قدرة له عليها.
قيل : هذا السؤال على أصلكم فاسد من وجوه:
أحدها: أنه يوجب القول بجهات الفعل وهو عندكم باطل.
والآخر : اعتراف بما جعلتموه من أقوى الشبهات لكم في المسألة وهو دخول مقدور الفعل والقول بذلك باطل.
والثالث أن هذا عندكم إثبات الشرك بين الله تعالى وبين العبد في هذا الفعل، والقول بذلك باطل.
والرابع: أن موجد القبح الراجع إلى ذات القبيح كان موجدًا للقبيح، وعندكم موجد القبح سفيه والله تعالى يتعالى عن فعل السفه بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم وإن كان عندكم موصوفا بالقدرة عليه.
And he performs that with his limbs, intending that all of that occur in the quality of good (ḥasan) without evil (qabīḥ). And it is known that that occurs in what opposes good, which is evil, not in the good which he intended. As for what is known in that by sensation, then indeed a person is harmed by continuous walking and pained by prolonged necessary standing, and likewise every action which a person persists in acquiring and continues in committing. There is no doubt that an action is not intended by the agent so that he be pained and harmed by it, yet with this it occurs harmful and tiring. So what we claimed is correct: that the action emerges contrary to what the servant intended of the description, [emerging] as what he did not intend. So when it does not occur in that [intended] way, it indicates that it has an originator (mūjid) who brought the existing thing into existence according to what He willed. This is confirmed by the fact that the emergence of disbelief (kufr) and worship of an idol as what the disbeliever intends—namely good, correctness, and obedience to God Almighty by which he seeks nearness to Him—contradicts wisdom and opposes it; and its emergence in the way it emerges, namely evil (qubḥ), is what requires sound governance and perfect wisdom. Its existence in this description, even if the disbeliever did not intend it, is either [caused] by the disbeliever or by the action itself through a free agent (mukhtār) who undertakes its origination. If it is by the disbeliever, it necessitates the possibility of all actions of moral judgment (al-afʿāl al-ḥukmiyya) occurring from someone who does not intend their acquisition, and that leads to the doctrine of natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) and stars (nujūm), which is corrupt; indeed its corruption is established in innate intuition (badīha), even if it does not lead to a false doctrine. And if it is by the action itself, it necessitates the possibility of all actions of moral judgment from the heavens and the earths existing without an originator who intended their origination and acquisition, and that is impossible (muḥāl). And if it is by a free agent who undertakes its origination according to what it is of the description, then what we have gone to is correct.
If they say: Suppose that the quality of evil of the action and the fact that the action is harmful and tiring is undertaken by other than the disbeliever and the walker, but why do you say that the origination of the very disbelief and walking is undertaken by other than the disbeliever and the walker? It is said: This question is only valid when the quality (ṣifa) is beyond the meaning of the qualified (mawṣūf), as in bodies and their attributes, so a person might confuse and say: one agent undertakes the origination of the attribute and another the origination of the qualified, so it is not absurd in the claim itself, even if the proof, upon investigation, necessitates the corruption of the statement of whoever claims multiple originators. As for accidents (aʿrāḍ) whose attributes in reality refer back to their essences (dhāt), such speech is not sound.
If they say: Since the attributes of actions refer back to their entities (aʿyān), so disbelief when it is done and it is evil, the servant undertakes its origination from the aspect of disbelief, and God Almighty undertakes its origination from the aspect of evil; and likewise regarding walking, harming, and causing pain. This is the meaning of what al-Kaʿbī mentioned, that the servant is free (mukhtār) regarding his action but compelled (muḍṭarr) regarding his being pained; and according to him, the free agent is the one who undertakes the origination of disbelief, and the compelled is the one for whom another undertakes the origination of an attribute in him over which he has no power.
It is said: This question, according to your principle, is corrupt from several aspects:
One of them: It necessitates the statement of aspects (jihāt) of the action, which is, according to you, false.
And the second: An admission of what you have made among your strongest arguments in the issue, namely the entry of the capable act (maqdūr al-fiʿl), and the statement of that is false.
And the third: This, according to you, is an establishment of shirk (shirk) between God Almighty and the servant in this action, and the statement of that is false.
And the fourth: That the originator of evil which refers back to the essence of the evil thing is an originator of the evil thing; and according to you, the originator of evil is foolish (safīh), and God Almighty is exalted above doing foolishness (safah) without disagreement between us and you, even if according to you He is described with power (qudra) over it.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a complex theological argument concerning the nature of human actions and divine creation. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, is defending the Māturīdī position that God is the sole creator of all actions, including their qualities, while the human agent merely acquires (kasb) the action. The passage begins by noting that a person may intend a good action but it turns out to be evil or harmful, contrary to the agent's intention. For example, a person walks intending no harm but suffers pain from excessive walking. This shows that the actual quality of the action (good or evil) is not under the agent's control. The author argues that since the action does not occur as the agent intended, there must be another originator (mūjid) who brings it about according to His will. This is supported by the example of a disbeliever who intends his disbelief and idol worship as good and obedience to God, but in reality these are evil. The actual evil nature of these acts is consistent with divine wisdom. The author then considers three possibilities for the cause of the action's evil quality: the agent himself, the action itself, or a separate free agent. If the agent caused the evil quality, that would imply that anyone could produce all actions regardless of intention, leading to the false doctrines of natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) and stars (nujūm). If the action itself caused it, that would imply that actions could exist without an originator, which is impossible. Therefore, it must be a separate free agent (i.e., God) who creates the action with its quality. This is the Māturīdī conclusion. The passage then presents an objection: one might accept that the harmful quality is created by another, but still claim that the essence of the action (e.g., the walking or disbelief itself) is created by the human. The author responds that this distinction only works when the attribute is separate from the essence, as in bodies and their accidents. But in the case of actions, the attributes (like evil) are not separate; they are the very essence of the action itself. Therefore, one cannot split the creation between the agent and God. The objector then refines the objection using the Mu'tazilī view of al-Kaʿbī: the servant creates the action qua disbelief (the essence), and God creates the quality of evil (an attribute). The author refutes this with four arguments. First, it implies that the action has multiple 'aspects' (jihāt), which the Mu'tazila themselves reject. Second, it admits that the action falls under the agent's power (maqdūr), which the Mu'tazila deny for evil actions. Third, it posits a form of shirk (associating partners with God) in creation. Fourth, if the originator of the evil quality is the originator of the evil thing itself (since the quality is inseparable), then that originator is considered foolish (safīh) for creating evil, but God is beyond foolishness. Thus, the objection is invalid. This passage carefully defends the Māturīdī doctrine of kasb and refutes the Mu'tazilī view of human creation of actions.

Key Terms
قبح (qubḥ)
حسن (ḥasan)
كفر (kufr)
فاعل مختار (mukhtār)
موجد (mūjid)
طبايع (ṭabāʾiʿ)
نجوم (nujūm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a person intends good but gets evil, he is still fully responsible for the evil quality because he performed the act.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the evil quality is not intended, so it must come from another originator (God). The agent is responsible for the act via acquisition (kasb), but God creates the quality.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does the mismatch between intention and outcome prove?
A) Humans have free will over the quality of their actions.
B) The action must have an originator other than the human agent.
C) Actions are random and have no originator.
D) The human agent creates both the action and its quality.

Key Takeaway
The mismatch between intention and outcome proves that the quality of actions is determined by God, not humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you intended to do good but your action led to harm or evil. Did you attribute that outcome to God's decree or to your own failure? How does this lesson challenge you to rely on God's origin of all actions, including their moral qualities? What specific step will you take today to submit your intentions to God's judgment of good and evil?
?
The passage states that the disbeliever worships an idol intending it as obedience. In your own life, where might you be engaging in actions that you consider good but that are actually displeasing to God? Are you willing to re-evaluate your actions based on God's revelation rather than your own intention? What is one action you will repent from today because its true quality may be evil?
?
If the quality of every action—good or evil—is ultimately from God, how does that affect your sense of pride when you do good, or your despair when you fail? Can you truly thank God for the good actions you perform, and seek His mercy for the evil? Write down a personal prayer of gratitude for one action He made good, and a plea for forgiveness for one action He allowed to be evil.

Causality and Created Things
advanced
7 min
Refutation of the Objection: Splitting Essence and Quality
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilī objection by showing the inseparability of action and quality and listing four contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's four arguments against the Mu'tazilī claim that the servant creates the essence of the action while God creates its evil quality.

Core Idea
For actions, quality and essence are inseparable; dividing creation leads to absurdities and shirk.

After establishing that God creates the quality of actions, Nasafi anticipates a Mu'tazilī objection: perhaps the servant creates the action's essence (e.g., the walking or disbelief) while God creates its attribute (e.g., harm or evil). Nasafi first argues that for actions, attributes are not separate from essences, then presents a stronger objection from al-Kaʿbī, which he refutes with four powerful arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Understand the objection that God creates the evil quality while the servant creates the act itself. Note Nasafi's first response: attributes of actions are inseparable from their essences.
This is a key distinction from bodies and accidents.
2
Examine the refined objection (al-Kaʿbī)
The objector refines: the servant is free in the act, compelled in the attribute. Nasafi then gives four refutation points.
3
Learn the first refutation
First: it implies multiple 'aspects' (jihāt) of the action, which the Mu'tazila themselves reject.
4
Learn the second and third refutations
Second: it admits the action is under the servant's power (maqdūr), which Mu'tazila deny for evil acts. Third: it sets up shirk between God and servant in creation.
5
Learn the fourth refutation
Fourth: if the originator of evil is the originator of the evil thing, then that originator is foolish (safīh). But God is beyond foolishness.

Commentary
This passage presents a complex theological argument concerning the nature of human actions and divine creation. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, is defending the Māturīdī position that God is the sole creator of all actions, including their qualities, while the human agent merely acquires (kasb) the action. The passage begins by noting that a person may intend a good action but it turns out to be evil or harmful, contrary to the agent's intention. For example, a person walks intending no harm but suffers pain from excessive walking. This shows that the actual quality of the action (good or evil) is not under the agent's control. The author argues that since the action does not occur as the agent intended, there must be another originator (mūjid) who brings it about according to His will. This is supported by the example of a disbeliever who intends his disbelief and idol worship as good and obedience to God, but in reality these are evil. The actual evil nature of these acts is consistent with divine wisdom. The author then considers three possibilities for the cause of the action's evil quality: the agent himself, the action itself, or a separate free agent. If the agent caused the evil quality, that would imply that anyone could produce all actions regardless of intention, leading to the false doctrines of natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) and stars (nujūm). If the action itself caused it, that would imply that actions could exist without an originator, which is impossible. Therefore, it must be a separate free agent (i.e., God) who creates the action with its quality. This is the Māturīdī conclusion. The passage then presents an objection: one might accept that the harmful quality is created by another, but still claim that the essence of the action (e.g., the walking or disbelief itself) is created by the human. The author responds that this distinction only works when the attribute is separate from the essence, as in bodies and their accidents. But in the case of actions, the attributes (like evil) are not separate; they are the very essence of the action itself. Therefore, one cannot split the creation between the agent and God. The objector then refines the objection using the Mu'tazilī view of al-Kaʿbī: the servant creates the action qua disbelief (the essence), and God creates the quality of evil (an attribute). The author refutes this with four arguments. First, it implies that the action has multiple 'aspects' (jihāt), which the Mu'tazila themselves reject. Second, it admits that the action falls under the agent's power (maqdūr), which the Mu'tazila deny for evil actions. Third, it posits a form of shirk (associating partners with God) in creation. Fourth, if the originator of the evil quality is the originator of the evil thing itself (since the quality is inseparable), then that originator is considered foolish (safīh) for creating evil, but God is beyond foolishness. Thus, the objection is invalid. This passage carefully defends the Māturīdī doctrine of kasb and refutes the Mu'tazilī view of human creation of actions.

Key Terms
جهات الفعل (jihāt al-fiʿl)
مقدور الفعل (maqdūr al-fiʿl)
سفيه (safīh)
شرك (shirk)
قبح (qubḥ)
حسن (ḥasan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think it is reasonable to say the servant creates the act itself while God creates its moral quality.
Correction: Nasafi shows that for actions, the quality is inseparable from the act, so such a division is invalid. Furthermore, it leads to doctrinal errors like jihāt, maqdūr, shirk, and attributing foolishness to God.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Nasafi's four refutations of the refined objection?
A) It leads to the doctrine of aspects (jihāt), which the Mu'tazila reject.
B) It admits the act is under the servant's power (maqdūr), which the Mu'tazila deny for evil acts.
C) It implies that the action occurs without any originator.
D) It establishes shirk between God and the servant.

Key Takeaway
Splitting creation of action between servant and God leads to doctrinal errors, including shirk, and contradicts the nature of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have committed a sin. In your heart, do you sometimes feel that God created its evil quality, while you only created the action itself? Nasafi shows this reasoning leads to shirk. Will you now take full responsibility for your sin and repent wholeheartedly, knowing that both the act and its moral quality are yours alone?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you ever feel that the goodness of the deed is from you while its existence is from God? According to this lesson, such splitting is invalid. How will you now humble yourself, recognizing that every aspect of your deed is entirely from God's creation and grace, and none from your own origination?
?
The passage argues that splitting creation between God and the servant implies shirk. Search your soul: in what areas of your life do you act as if you are a co-creator with God, taking credit for some aspects and leaving others to Him? What concrete step will you take today to affirm that God alone is the creator of all things, including your actions?
