The Custom of Creation and Acquisition
He explains the divine custom of creating acts as acquired by the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari-Maturidi position resolves the apparent dilemma between God's absolute power and human free will through the concepts of divine custom (al-'ada) and acquisition (al-kasb).

Core Idea
God creates acts as the servant acquires them, preserving both divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

This passage addresses a central theological puzzle: if God creates all actions, how can humans be held accountable? The author presents the solution that God has established a custom of creating acts in accordance with the servant's intention to acquire them. By examining a hypothetical case, he shows that no contradiction arises between divine power and human choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the key terms: التخليق (creation), الانتساب (attribution), الكسب (acquisition), العادة (custom).
The passage is dense, but the structure is clear: a principle, then a hypothetical scenario.
2
Identify the core principle
The author states that every act is such that Allah has independent power over it, and the servant does not. But Allah's custom is to create the act when the servant intends to acquire it. This is the foundation of the solution.
This answers the objection that the Ash'ari view forces compulsion.
3
Analyze the hypothetical dilemma
The author presents a scenario: could Allah create an act as the servant's act even if the servant refrains? Both outcomes (act occurring or not) seem problematic. Follow the reasoning to see how the author escapes the dilemma.
Focus on the condition that God's will for the act as the servant's act depends on the servant's will for his power.
4
Understand the resolution
The author explains that it is inconceivable for Allah to will an act as the servant's act without the servant willing his power over it. Since power and act are inseparable, God's will is contingent on the servant's intention. Thus, no compulsion or incapacity follows.
This is the key insight: acquisition (kasb) bridges divine creation and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزمنا هذا في التخليق والانتساب على ما قررنا في شبهة الخصوم، لأن كل فعل في ذاته يوصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على الانفراد والاستبداد، والعبد لا يوصف بذلك، إلا أن الله تعالى أجرى العادة أنه يخلق كل فعل قصد العبد اكتسابه. ونفرض الكلام في حركة معينة فنقول : هي قبيل وجودها علمها الله تعالى قدرة إيجادها لو أراد إيجادها على سبيل الاستبداد، والعبد لا يقدر على اكتسابها ومباشرتها بدون إيجاد الله تعالى. ولا يقال لو أراد الله إيجادها فعلاً للعبد مع امتناع العبد عن ذلك هل يحصل ذلك؟ فإن حصل فقد لزم القول باضطرار العبد إذ الفعل الضروري هو الذي يوجد والذي قصد العبد الامتناع عنه، وإن لم يحصل فقد لزم القول بتعجيز الباري جل وعلا، لأنا نقول : لا يتصور عندنا أن يريدها فعلاً للعبد إلا وأن يريد العبد وجود قدرته عليها لاستحالة وجود الفعل الاختياري بلا قدرة ولا يتصور وجود القدرة بدون الفعل فيستحيل إرادة الله إياها أن تكون فعلاً للعبد مع إرادة العبد الامتناع
And this does not apply to us regarding creation and attribution, as we established in the opponents' doubt, because every act in itself is described that Allah has power over it individually and independently, and the servant is not described with that. However, Allah has established the custom that He creates every act the servant intends to acquire. We posit speech about a specific movement and say: Before its existence, Allah knew His power to create it if He wanted to create it independently, and the servant is not able to acquire it and perform it without Allah's creation. And it should not be said: If Allah wanted to create it as an act of the servant while the servant refrains from it, would that happen? If it happens, then it would necessitate the statement that the servant is compelled, since the compelled act is that which occurs and the servant intended to refrain from it. And if it does not happen, then it would necessitate the statement that the Exalted Creator is incapacitated. For we say: In our view, it is inconceivable that He intends it as an act of the servant unless the servant intends the existence of his power over it, because it is impossible for a voluntary act to exist without power, and it is inconceivable for power to exist without the act. So it is impossible for Allah to intend it as an act of the servant while the servant intends to refrain.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage explains the Sunni position on creation (takhliq) and acquisition (kasb). God has absolute and independent power over all acts. He creates the act when the servant intends to acquire it, by His custom. The dilemma about God creating an act against the servant's will is resolved: God's will for the act as the servant's act is contingent upon the servant's will for his power (qudra) over it. Since power and act are inseparable, God does not will an act without the servant's intention. Thus, no compulsion or incapacity follows.

Key Terms
التخليق
الانتساب
الاستبداد
العادة
الكسب
القدرة
الاضطرار
التعجيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view of acquisition makes God the sole agent and thus either compels humans or is limited by human choice.
Correction: The passage shows that God's custom is to create the act only when the servant intends to acquire it, and the hypothetical dilemma is inconceivable because God's will for the act as the servant's act is contingent upon the servant's will for his power. Thus, neither compulsion nor limitation of God follows.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for God's creation of the servant's act?
A) God's independent power alone
B) The servant's intention to acquire the act
C) The act's inherent nature
D) The opponent's claim

Key Takeaway
God creates every act according to His custom, but only when the servant intends to acquire it, resolving the tension between divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage states that it is impossible for a voluntary act to exist without your power, and your power cannot exist without the act. Will you begin each act with conscious intention and reliance on Him?
?
This lesson says God only creates acts when you intend to acquire them. What does that mean for your most trivial actions—like the words you speak or the food you eat? Are they truly 'yours'? How would your life look if you lived every moment aware that your intention invites divine creation? What is one action today you will perform with this awareness?

Human Kasb Does Not Limit Divine Power
Nasafi refutes that human kasb implies weakness in God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi doctrine of kasb (acquisition) avoids implying any weakness or limitation in God's omnipotence.

Core Idea
Human acquisition does not compete with God's power; God creates the act itself.

A key objection against the Maturidi view of human kasb is that if God's power to act depends on human abstention, then God appears weak. In this passage, Nasafi dismantles that objection by clarifying that the human act is God's creation. The human 'acquires' it, but the actual power belongs to God alone. Thus, kasb does not remove or limit God's power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi first describes the opponent's claim, then refutes it.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find where Nasafi states what the opponents claim: that if God's power is conditional on human non-interference, that would be a sign of weakness.
This is the first part of the passage.
3
Understand Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi argues that kasb does not entail incapacitation because the act is God's creation. Contrast this with the view that gives humans independent creative power (takhliq), which would indeed limit God.
The key phrase: 'because his acquisition is an object of power of God Almighty'.
4
Distinguish kasb and takhliq
Nasafi highlights that the Maturidi position (kasb) does not remove God's power, unlike the position that attributes independent creation to humans.
This distinction is crucial to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيما زعموا هو قادر على تحصيل مقدوره لو لم يمنعه غيره عن ذلك أو لم يعجزه، ويمتنع عن تحصيل مقدوره بمنع العبد إياه لا باختياره، وهذا هو أمارة العجز والقهر والمنع، ومثل هذا التعجيز أو المنع لا يلزمه بإثبات قدرة العبد على الكسب لأن كسبه مقدور الله تعالى، والله تعالى هو الموجد له فلا يكون في اشتغاله بالكسب إزالة قدرة الله تعالى عن مقدوره ليلزم التعجيز أو المنع بخلاف إثبات قدرة التخليق على ما قررنا
And in what they claim, He is powerful to bring about His object of power if another did not prevent Him from that or did not incapacitate Him, and He refrains from bringing about His object of power due to the servant's prevention of Him, not by His own choice. This is a sign of weakness, coercion, and prevention. Such incapacitation or prevention does not follow from affirming the servant's power over acquisition (kasb), because his acquisition is an object of power of God Almighty, and God Almighty is the one who brings it into existence. So in his engagement in acquisition, there is no removal of God's power over His object of power that would necessitate incapacitation or prevention, contrary to affirming a power of creation (takhliq) as we have established.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage refutes a potential objection: the opponents claim that if God's power were dependent on human abstention, then God would be weak or prevented. Nasafi responds by distinguishing between the Maturidi concept of acquisition (kasb) and a claimed power of creation (takhliq) in the human. In kasb, the act itself is created by God, and the human only acquires it. Therefore, when the human engages in an act, he is not removing God's power from that act, because God is the actual creator. The act is God's object of power first and foremost. Thus, no incapacitation (ta'jiz) or prevention (man') follows from affirming human kasb. This is unlike the view that gives humans an independent creative power, which would indeed imply limitations on God.

كسب
مقدور
تعجيز
تخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human free will (kasb) contradicts God's omnipotence by placing acts outside God's power.
Correction: Nasafi shows that in kasb, the human act is still God's creation and object of power, so human engagement does not remove God's power.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does human acquisition (kasb) not necessitate incapacitation or prevention of God?
A) Because God's power is limited anyway.
B) Because the acquired act is God's creation and object of power.
C) Because humans have no role at all.
D) Because kasb means humans create independently.

Key Takeaway
Human acquisition (kasb) does not limit God's power because the act itself is God's creation; only if humans had independent creative power would that be a problem.

Nasafi's Argument for Single Divine Agency Over Human Actions
Nasafi refutes the majority view on dual power impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of the view that a human action cannot be under both divine and human power, and his argument that God must have power over the actions He enables.

Core Idea
God gives power; therefore He has power over the act He enables.

In this passage, Nasafi presents a key argument for the Maturidi position that God is the ultimate creator of all actions, including those of humans. He examines two opposing views on whether an act can be subject to two powers — God's and the servant's — and uses a logical analogy to show the absurdity of denying divine power over human acts. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the technical terms like 'arad (accident) and qudra (power).
The passage is dense; don't worry if not everything is clear on first read.
2
Identify the two views on power
Nasafi presents two possibilities: (1) both God and servant have independent power over the act, or (2) God's power is not certain over the act because one act cannot have two agents. Which one does he reject?
Look for the phrase 'ولا وجه إلى هذا'.
3
Understand the refutation
Nasafi argues that since God grants the power of action to the servant, God must have power over that same action. Otherwise, it would be like a paralyzed person giving another the ability to walk — impossible.
This is the muq'ad (paralyzed) analogy.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Reflect on what this argument means for divine and human agency. Nasafi concludes that God's power is primary, and the servant's power is derived.
This supports the Maturidi view of acquisition (kasb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يؤيد ما ذكرنا أن كل فعل من أفعال العبد عرض وهو قبل حدوثه وخروجه من العدم إلى الوجود كان معلوم الباري جل وعلا بلا خوف، وهو عندهم جميعًا شيء، وعند البصريين منهم عوض، وعند الجبائي إن كان عند الوجد حركة فهو في حال العدم أيضا حركة، ثم لا شك أن في حال عدمه معلوم الله تعالى، والله تعالى يعلمه شيئا في حال عدمه، وعندهم وعند البصريين يعلمه عرضا وعند الجبائي منهم يعلمه حركة. ثم الحال لا يخلو إما إن كانت لله تعالى عليه قدرة كما للعبد عليه قدرة ويصح من كل واحد منهما الانفراد بإيجاده كما هو مذهب طائفة منهم، وإما إن كانت قدرة الله تعالى غير متيقنة لما كان مقدورًا للعبد لاستحالة كون مقدور لقادرين كما هو مذهب جمهورهم، ولا وجه إلى هذا؛ لأن الله تعالى هو الذي يتولى إعطاء العبد قدرة الفعل وهو المقدر له، ومن المحال أن يقدر ذات ما غيره على ما لا قدرة للمقدر عليه، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يقدر المقعد غيره على عين المشي الذي المقعد
And what supports what we have mentioned is that every action of the servant is an accident (arad), and before its occurrence and its emergence from non-existence into existence it was known to the Creator, Glorified and Exalted, without doubt. And it is, according to all of them, a thing (shay'). According to the Basrians among them, it is a 'substitute' ('iwad). According to al-Jubba'i, if it is a motion when found, then it is also a motion in the state of non-existence. Then there is no doubt that in the state of its non-existence it is known to God Almighty, and God Almighty knows it as a thing in the state of its non-existence. According to them and the Basrians, He knows it as an accident; according to al-Jubba'i among them, He knows it as a motion. Then the situation is not free from either: either that God Almighty has power over it just as the servant has power over it, and it is valid for each one to independently bring it into existence, as is the doctrine of a group among them; or that God's power is not certain over what is an object of power of the servant, due to the impossibility of an object of power belonging to two powerful agents, as is the doctrine of the majority of them. And there is no validity to this; because God Almighty is the One who undertakes giving the servant the power of action, and He is the one who determines it for him. And it is impossible for a being to empower another over something over which the empowerer has no power. And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible for one who cannot walk (muq'ad) to empower another to actual walking of the same walking that the one who cannot walk...
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage provides supporting arguments for the Maturidi position. It begins by emphasizing that every human action is an accident (arad) that was known to God before its existence. The author notes differing opinions on the nature of this accident: the Basrians call it a 'thing' or 'substitute', while al-Jubba'i considers it a motion even in non-existence. This sets up a key point: since the action is known to God as a specific entity, there is a question of who has power over it. Nasafi presents two opposing views: (1) that both God and the servant have independent power over the same act, held by a minority; (2) that God's power is not certain over what is under the servant's power, due to the impossibility of one act having two independent agents, held by the majority. Nasafi rejects the second view, arguing that since God gives the servant the power to act, He must have power over the act itself. If the servant's power were independent, this would imply that God could empower another over something He Himself has no power over, which is impossible. He gives an analogy: if this were allowed, then a paralyzed person (muq'ad) could empower another to walk the same walking that the paralyzed person cannot do—an absurdity. Thus, God must be the true creator of the act, and the servant's power is a part of that creation, not independent.

Key Terms
عرض
معلوم
قدرة
مذهب
بصريين
جبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a human action cannot be under both God's and the servant's power because one act cannot have two independent agents.
Correction: Nasafi argues that since God grants the power of action, He necessarily has power over that action. The act is under both powers but in a hierarchical manner, with God as the ultimate source.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject the majority view that God's power is not certain over what is under the servant's power?
A) Because the servant is the sole creator.
B) Because God gives the power to act and must have power over the act itself.
C) Because the act is not known to God.
D) Because the servant's power is illusory.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that if God gives the servant the ability to act, He must have power over that act; otherwise, an absurdity follows, affirming that all power ultimately belongs to God.

?

Are you living with a constant sense of dependence, or do you slip into self-congratulation? What would it look like today to consciously attribute your abilities to Him?
?
Nasafi argues that if God gave you power to do something, He must have that power Himself. Do you ever act as if God is powerless over your struggles or sins? How does this teaching challenge any hidden belief that your weaknesses are beyond His reach? What would change if you trusted that His power is always present even in your inability?
?
The analogy of the paralyzed person is meant to show absurdity in denying God's power over your acts. In what areas of your life do you functionally deny God's power, acting as if your choices are independent? What specific step can you take today to acknowledge His sovereignty over your next decision?

Refutation of Bishr al-Mutamir's Theory of Generated Acts
Bishr's view that colors and tastes can be created by others leads to shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Bishr al-Mu'tamir's view that humans can create attributes like color and taste leads to associating partners with Allah in creation.

Core Idea
Allowing creatures to create any attribute makes them partners with Allah.

This passage presents a direct refutation of a Mu'tazilite theory that attributes creative power to human actions. Bishr al-Mutamir held that certain qualities like colors and tastes are generated by human acts, not directly by Allah. The author argues that this logically makes such creatures partners in creation, which is a form of shirk.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that it begins by identifying Bishr as a leader among the Mu'tazilites and then states his view on creation of attributes.
The passage is short and direct.
2
Identify the attributes claimed to be creatable by others
List the six attributes Bishr believes can be created by other than Allah: color, taste, smell, sight, hearing, and perception.
These are sensory qualities and cognitive acts.
3
Understand the theological consequence
The author concludes that if creatures can create these attributes, they become partners with Allah in creating the world. Reflect on why this is a serious violation of tawhid.
This refutation relies on the principle that creation is exclusive to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبشر بن المعتمر أحد رؤسائهم يجوز خلق اللون والطعم والرائحة والبصر والسمع والإدراك من غير الله تعالى، فيصير خالق هؤلاء شركاء الله تعالى في تخليق العالم.
Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, one of their leaders, permits the creation of color, taste, smell, sight, hearing, and perception from other than Allah. Thus the creator of these becomes partners of Allah in the creation of the world.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage singles out Bishr al-Mutamir, a prominent Mu'tazilite, who held the view that generated acts (tawallud) allow humans to create attributes like color, taste, etc. The author argues that this makes such creatures partners with Allah in creating the world, which is a serious theological error.

Key Terms

خلق (creation)
التولد (generation)
شركاء (partners)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bishr's view does not deny God's power over all atoms but restricts creation of certain qualities to creatures.
Correction: The passage argues that attributing any act of creation to other than Allah, even limited qualities, amounts to shirk because it grants creatures a share in the creative act.

Checkpoint

B) Color, taste, smell, sight, hearing, and perception.
C) Only inanimate objects.

Key Takeaway
Bishr's theory of generated acts incorrectly grants creatures creative power over attributes, thereby compromising the principle of tawhid that creation belongs exclusively to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If someone argued that human effort creates success, how would you use the logic of this passage to affirm that Allah alone creates all results? Would you be bold enough to correct that subtle shirk in your own speech?

Al-Ka'bi's Objection and the Relational Names
Nasafi cites a Quranic verse and presents al-Ka'bi's objection, then refutes it by distinguishing essential from relational attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that obedience and disobedience are relational names, not essential to acts.

Core Idea
Obedience and disobedience are relational, not essential.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a key objection from al-Ka'bi, who argued that human and divine acts cannot be similar because human acts are characterized by abasement and corruption while divine acts are wisdom. Nasafi responds by citing a Quranic verse and then clarifying that the qualities of obedience and disobedience are not inherent to the act itself but are relational names (al-asma' al-idafiyya) that depend on the context of command and prohibition. This lesson presents the objection and the core refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse and its interpretation
The verse denies that anyone can create like Allah. Note that Nasafi interprets it as an interrogative of denial.
This sets the stage for the debate.
2
Understand al-Ka'bi's objection
Al-Ka'bi admits both human and divine acts are bringing into existence, but he claims they are dissimilar due to moral qualities (abasement vs wisdom).
3
Analyze Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi argues that motion, stillness, etc., are what they are in themselves. Obedience and disobedience are relational names (al-asma' al-idafiyya) arising from conformity or opposition to a command.
This is the key point: essential vs relational.
4
Reflect on the implication
If obedience is relational, then the same act could be obedience in one context and disobedience in another, without changing its essence.
This undercuts al-Ka'bi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قال الله تعالى: أَمْ جَعَلُوا لِلَّهِ شُرَكَاءَ خَلَقُوا كَخَلْقِهِ، فَتَشَبَه الخَلْقُ عَلَيْهِمْ وهذا، والله أعلم استفهام بمعنى الإنكار والنفي، فكان الله تعالى نافيًا أن يكون من أحد خلق كخلقه.
فإن قالوا: إن فعلنا وإن كان إخراجًا من العدم إلى الوجود، وفعله تعالى أيضا إخراج من العدم إلى الوجود إلا أنه لا تشابه بين فعلنا وفعله، لأن فعلنا خضوع وذلة أو عبث وفساد وفعله عز وجل حكمة وصواب وليس بخضوع ولا ذلة، والعبث لا يشبه الحكمة. هذا هو اعتراض الكعبي.
يقال له : إن الحركة حركة لنفسها، وكذا السكون والسواد والبياض، وكون الفعل طاعة أو معصية أو ذلة أو خضوعاً لمعاني وراء الذات، فإن الفعل يكون طاعة لخروجه على موافقة الأمر، والمعصية تكون معصية لخروج الفعل على مخالفة الأمر وارتكاب ما نهى عنه، فكانا من الأسماء الإضافية، وكذا الحركة لا تكون ذلة لنفسها ، وكذا لا تكون خضوعا لنفسها بل لقصد فاعلها، والقصد معنى في القلب والحركة تكون بالجوارح والمشتبهان لذاتيهما لا يختلفان باعتبار الأسامي الإضافية الثابتة لمعاني هي أغيار للذات.
ألا ترى أن سواد زيد وسواد عمرو لا يختلفان لاختلاف المحل، وسكون زيد لا يكون مشابها لحركته وإن كان الفاعل واحدًا لاختلافهما في ذاتيهما، فإذا لم يكن لاتحاد الفاعل أثر في تشابه ما اختلفا بذاتيهما لا يكون لاختلاف الفاعل أو معنى من معانيه أو اختلاف آلة الفاعلين أثر في اختلاف شيئين اشتبها لذاتيهما، وهذا لأن علة الاشتباه والاختلاف لما كان هو الذات، وباختلاف ما وراء الذاتين في المعاني لا تتغير الذاتان ولا باتحاد ما وراء الذاتين من المعنى يتحدان، ولا يتغير ما هو من مقتضيات الذاتين من التشابه والاختلاف.
وعرف بهذا حيد الكعبي في الاعتراض عن الحقائق إما جهلاً منه بذلك، وإما تمويها على الضعفة.
And Allah Most High said: 'Or have they assigned to Allah partners who created the like of His creation, so that the creation became confused to them.' This, and Allah knows best, is an interrogative in the sense of denial and negation, so Allah Most High was negating that anyone has a creation like His creation. If they say: 'Our act, although it is a bringing out from non-existence into existence, and His act Most High is also a bringing out from non-existence into existence, yet there is no similarity between our act and His act, because our act is abasement and degradation or frivolity and corruption, while His act, mighty and majestic, is wisdom and correctness, and is not abasement nor degradation, and frivolity does not resemble wisdom.' This is al-Ka'bi's objection. It is said to him: 'Motion is motion in itself, and likewise stillness, blackness, and whiteness. The act being obedience or disobedience or degradation or abasement is due to meanings beyond the essence (al-dhat). For the act becomes obedience by its proceeding in accordance with the command, and disobedience becomes disobedience by the act's proceeding in opposition to the command and committing what is forbidden. So these are relational names (al-asma' al-idafiyya). Likewise, motion is not degradation in itself, nor is it abasement in itself, but rather by the intention of its doer, and intention is a meaning in the heart, while motion is performed by the limbs. And the two things that resemble each other in their essences do not differ by consideration of the relational names that are established for meanings other than the essence. Do you not see that the blackness of Zayd and the blackness of 'Amr do not differ due to the difference of location, and Zayd's stillness is not similar to his motion, even if the doer is one, because they differ in their essences. So if the unity of the doer has no effect on the similarity of two things that differ in their essences, then the difference of the doer, or a meaning among his meanings, or the difference of the instrument of the doers, has no effect on the difference of two things that resemble each other in their essences. This is because the cause (illa) of similarity and difference is the essence itself. By the difference of what is beyond the two essences in meanings, the two essences do not change, nor by the unity of what is beyond the two essences of meaning do they become one, nor does what is required by the two essences in terms of similarity and difference change.' And by this is known the deviation (hayd) of al-Ka'bi in objecting against truths, either out of ignorance on his part, or out of deceit towards the weak.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's objection regarding the difference between human and divine acts. Nasafi begins by citing a Quranic verse (Surah Ra'd 13:16) that denies that anyone can create like Allah, using an interrogative form of denial. Then he presents al-Ka'bi's objection: both human and divine acts are bringing into existence, but human acts are characterized by abasement and corruption while divine acts are wisdom, so they cannot be similar. Nasafi responds by distinguishing between the essence (dhat) of an act and its accidental attributes. Motion is motion in itself; being obedience or disobedience is a relational attribute (ism idafi) arising from conformity or opposition to a command. He illustrates with the example of blackness: the blackness of Zayd and the blackness of 'Amr do not differ because they are in different subjects; they are identical in essence. Similarly, stillness and motion of the same person differ in essence, not because of the doer. Therefore, similarity or difference is based on essence, not on added attributes like obedience or place. He concludes that al-Ka'bi's objection stems either from ignorance or from deliberate deception of the weak.

طاعة
معصية
أسماء إضافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that obedience and disobedience are inherent properties of actions, such that an action is always good or bad regardless of context.
Correction: Nasafi shows they are relational: an act becomes obedience only when it follows a command, and disobedience when it violates a command. The act itself (e.g., motion) remains the same.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what makes an act an act of obedience?
A) The inherent nature of the act itself.
B) That it proceeds in accordance with a command.
C) That it is performed by a believer.
D) That it is a motion.

Key Takeaway
Obedience and disobedience are relational names, not essential properties of actions, so they cannot be used to argue for an essential difference between human and divine acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you often label as a 'sin' in your mind. Do you treat its sinfulness as if it were a fixed part of the action itself, or do you recognize it as a relational quality depending on Allah's command? How would your view of that action shift if you truly internalized Nasafi's point?
?
Al-Ka'bi argued that human acts are characterized by 'abasement and corruption.' Is there an action you perform that you feel inherently degrades you? How does knowing that such qualities are relational—not essential—free you from seeing yourself as essentially flawed in that action?
?
If obedience and disobedience are relational, then your relationship with Allah's command defines the moral value of your deeds. Are you more focused on the 'essence' of your actions (what they are in themselves) or on the 'relation' to what Allah has commanded? Which focus will guide your next decision?

Essence as the Basis of Similarity and Difference
Nasafi uses examples and a general principle to establish that essence determines similarity, not accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that similarity or difference between acts is determined by their essences, not by accidental attributes like the doer or intention.

Core Idea
Similarity is based on essence, not on relational attributes.

Having established that moral qualities are relational, Nasafi deepens his refutation by explaining why similarity and difference are determined by essence. He uses the example of blackness in two individuals and the distinction between stillness and motion in the same person to illustrate that accidental factors do not affect essential similarity. This lesson unpacks this logical argument and its conclusion that al-Ka'bi's objection is either ignorant or deceptive.

Guided Path
1
Read the additional point about intention
Nasafi clarifies that motion is not inherently degrading; degradation comes from the intention of the doer, which is a separate meaning in the heart.
This reinforces the relational nature of moral qualities.
2
Examine the blackness example
The blackness of Zayd and the blackness of 'Amr are identical in essence, despite being in different subjects. This shows that location does not affect essential identity.
3
Examine the stillness and motion example
Zayd's stillness and his own motion are different in essence, even though the doer is the same. So the doer's identity does not cause essential similarity.
4
Understand the general principle
Nasafi states that the cause (illa) of similarity and difference is essence itself. Accidental factors like differences in subjects or doers do not change essences.
This directly refutes al-Ka'bi's attempt to differentiate divine and human acts based on accidental qualities.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that al-Ka'bi's objection is either born of ignorance or a deliberate deception of the weak.
This shows the strength of Nasafi's argument.

Source Text

?

?
If essence determines similarity and difference, consider your relationships with others. Do you judge people based on their essential character (their hearts and intentions) or on accidental attributes like their actions, status, or mistakes? How can you train yourself to see the essence first?

Command and Place as Attributes of Motion
Nasafi presents a hypothetical scenario and the opponent's first response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why place is considered a necessary concomitant of motion while command is not, according to Nasafi's hypothetical argument.

Core Idea
Command is accidental to motion; place is essential.

In refuting al-Ka'bi, Nasafi presents a clever hypothetical: two motions commanded in different places. He forces the opponent to choose if they are similar or different, then exposes inconsistency in their reasoning. This passage establishes a crucial distinction between necessary and accidental attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the hypothetical scenario
Read the Arabic passage once, noticing how Nasafi sets up the scenario: two motions commanded in different places. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the choice given to the opponent.
2
Analyze the first response
The opponent says the motions are similar. Follow Nasafi's rebuttal: why do they differ by command but not by place? Identify the key distinction between command and place as attributes.
Notice the term 'al-qara'in al-lazima' (necessary concomitants).
3
Understand the reasoning about necessary concomitants
Nasafi explains that place is a necessary concomitant because motion cannot exist without it, while command is not. Reflect on how this refutes al-Ka'bi's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: ما قولك فيما إذا كانت حركة مأموراً بها في مكان ثم أمر بحركة أخرى في مكان آخر، أهاتان متجانستان أم مختلفان؟ فإن قال: هما متجانستان متشابهان. قيل: فما بالهما لم يختلفا باختلاف الأمكنة، واختلفا باختلاف الأمر والنهي؟ أن الأمر والنهي ليسا من القرائن اللازمة للحركة لتصور وجودها بدونهما وحصولهما غير مأمور بهما ولا منهي عنهما، والمكان من القرائن اللازمة لاستحالة وجود الحركة والسكون بدون المكان.
وإن قال: هما مختلفان لاختلاف الأمكنة قلنا فما بالهما اختلافتا أنهما طاعتان ولم يوجب اجتماعهما في الاتصاف بكونهما طاعتين تشابههما وإن اختلفت الأمكنة وأوجب افتراقهما في الاتصاف بكونهما طاعة أو معصية اختلافهما مع استوائهما في الذات الذي هو علة للاشتباه.
وكذا إذا أمر بسكون ثم أمر بحركة هل أوجب اجتماعهما في الاتصاف بكونهما طاعتين اشتباههما أم لا؟
Then it is said to him: What do you say about when there is a motion that is commanded in one place, then another motion is commanded in another place? Are these two similar (mutajanisatan) or different? If he says: They are similar, alike. It is said: Why do they not differ by the difference of places, yet they differ by the difference of command and prohibition? For command and prohibition are not necessary concomitants (al-qara'in al-lazima) of motion, because motion can be conceived without them, and they occur without being commanded or prohibited, whereas place is a necessary concomitant because motion and stillness cannot exist without place. And if he says: They are different due to the difference of places, we say: Why then do they not differ when they are both acts of obedience? And their being together in being described as both acts of obedience does not necessitate their similarity, even if places differ, yet their separation in being described as either obedience or disobedience necessitates their difference, while they are equal in essence, which is the cause of similarity. And likewise, if he commands stillness then commands motion, does their being together in being described as both acts of obedience necessitate their similarity or not?
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents a hypothetical argument to further refute al-Ka'bi. Nasafi poses a scenario: two motions are commanded in different places. If the opponent says they are similar, then why do they not differ by place but differ by command/prohibition? The answer is that command and prohibition are not essential to motion (motion can exist without them), whereas place is necessary (motion cannot exist without a place). If the opponent says they are different due to place, then why do two acts that are both obedient not become similar despite different places? This shows that similarity depends on essence, not on place or command. The argument continues with a case of stillness and motion: if both are commanded, do they become similar because both are obedient? The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: essence determines similarity, not accidental attributes like being commanded or place.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
مأمور
منهي
مكان
قرائن لازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Command is a necessary attribute of motion, like place.
Correction: Nasafi shows command is accidental; motion can exist without being commanded, but not without place.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's argument, why is place considered a necessary concomitant (qarina lazima) of motion?
A) Because motion cannot be conceived without place.
B) Because place is commanded.
C) Because place determines whether an act is obedience.
D) Because place is a relational attribute.

Key Takeaway
Place is a necessary concomitant of motion, but command is accidental and does not affect the essence of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you performed today that you considered 'good' mainly because you were told to do it. Did that command change the action's intrinsic nature? If not, what does that mean for your motivation? Will you now seek to understand the essence of your deeds beyond external rulings?
?
You often pray in different places—home, mosque, work. Do you unconsciously treat the location as part of the prayer's value? According to this lesson, place is necessary but does not alter the essence of the act. How might this realisation free you from attachment to specific places in worship?
?
If someone forbade you from giving charity, would the act of giving cease to be virtuous? This passage shows that command and prohibition do not touch the essence of acts. So why do you sometimes hesitate when human disapproval contradicts divine command? Are you ready to act on the essence of what is right, regardless of external rulings?

7 min
Obedience, Disobedience, and the Essence of Actions
Nasafi continues the hypothetical with the opponent's second response and a third case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues that the moral description (obedience/disobedience) does not affect the essence of an action, using the case of two motions in different places.

Core Idea
Essence determines similarity, not moral labels.

Nasafi continues his hypothetical argument: if the opponent claims motions differ by place, then why don't two acts of obedience become similar despite different places? He pushes the logic further to show that what makes an act similar is its essence (dhat), not accidental attributes like being commanded. This leads to an incomplete third case about stillness and motion, which you will analyze.

Guided Path
1
Read the second response and rebuttal
Read the Arabic passage for the second scenario: the opponent says motions are different due to place. Follow Nasafi's rebuttal about obedience and disobedience.
Note the word 'dhat' (essence) and 'illat al-ishtibah' (cause of similarity).
2
Analyze the logic of obedience and essence
Nasafi argues that two acts both described as obedience do not become similar in essence just because they share that label. The essence remains the cause of similarity, not accidental attributes.
Reflect on how this refutes al-Ka'bi's view that moral descriptions affect essence.
3
Consider the third case
The passage ends with a question about stillness and motion both being commanded. Although incomplete, try to infer what Nasafi's conclusion would be based on the established logic.
The answer is likely that they do not become similar in essence just because both are acts of obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: ما قولك فيما إذا كانت حركة مأموراً بها في مكان ثم أمر بحركة أخرى في مكان آخر، أهاتان متجانستان أم مختلفان؟ فإن قال: هما متجانستان متشابهان. قيل: فما بالهما لم يختلفا باختلاف الأمكنة، واختلفا باختلاف الأمر والنهي؟ أن الأمر والنهي ليسا من القرائن اللازمة للحركة لتصور وجودها بدونهما وحصولهما غير مأمور بهما ولا منهي عنهما، والمكان من القرائن اللازمة لاستحالة وجود الحركة والسكون بدون المكان.
وإن قال: هما مختلفان لاختلاف الأمكنة قلنا فما بالهما اختلافتا أنهما طاعتان ولم يوجب اجتماعهما في الاتصاف بكونهما طاعتين تشابههما وإن اختلفت الأمكنة وأوجب افتراقهما في الاتصاف بكونهما طاعة أو معصية اختلافهما مع استوائهما في الذات الذي هو علة للاشتباه.
وكذا إذا أمر بسكون ثم أمر بحركة هل أوجب اجتماعهما في الاتصاف بكونهما طاعتين اشتباههما أم لا؟
Then it is said to him: What do you say about when there is a motion that is commanded in one place, then another motion is commanded in another place? Are these two similar (mutajanisatan) or different? If he says: They are similar, alike. It is said: Why do they not differ by the difference of places, yet they differ by the difference of command and prohibition? For command and prohibition are not necessary concomitants (al-qara'in al-lazima) of motion, because motion can be conceived without them, and they occur without being commanded or prohibited, whereas place is a necessary concomitant because motion and stillness cannot exist without place. And if he says: They are different due to the difference of places, we say: Why then do they not differ when they are both acts of obedience? And their being together in being described as both acts of obedience does not necessitate their similarity, even if places differ, yet their separation in being described as either obedience or disobedience necessitates their difference, while they are equal in essence, which is the cause of similarity. And likewise, if he commands stillness then commands motion, does their being together in being described as both acts of obedience necessitate their similarity or not?
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents a hypothetical argument to further refute al-Ka'bi. Nasafi poses a scenario: two motions are commanded in different places. If the opponent says they are similar, then why do they not differ by place but differ by command/prohibition? The answer is that command and prohibition are not essential to motion (motion can exist without them), whereas place is necessary (motion cannot exist without a place). If the opponent says they are different due to place, then why do two acts that are both obedient not become similar despite different places? This shows that similarity depends on essence, not on place or command. The argument continues with a case of stillness and motion: if both are commanded, do they become similar because both are obedient? The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear: essence determines similarity, not accidental attributes like being commanded or place.

Misconception: Moral descriptions like 'obedience' or 'disobedience' affect the essence of an act, making similar acts different or different acts similar.
Correction: Nasafi argues that essence (dhat) is the cause of similarity; moral descriptions are accidental and do not change essence.

Essence (dhat) is the cause of similarity between actions, not accidental attributes like moral description or place.

The Two Reeds Argument: Why Cause Does Not Help Distinguish Actions
Imam Nasafi presents the two reeds argument as a decisive refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi's two reeds argument refutes the claim that human and divine actions can be distinguished by their external cause.

Core Idea
If the cause is invisible, the distinction collapses.

Imam Nasafi presents a powerful thought experiment to challenge those who think they can tell divine action from human action just by looking at the cause. The argument uses two identical reeds moving behind a wall—one moved by a human, the other by God—to show that without seeing the cause, no empirical distinction is possible. This passage pushes the opponent to consider cases where the cause is imperceptible, like an angel or jinn.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario of the two reeds
Read the opening passage carefully. Two identical reeds appear from behind a wall, one moved by a human, the other by God. Note that an observer sees only the motion, not the mover.
The motions are identical in essence from all aspects.
2
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent claims that by looking behind the wall to see the cause, one can distinguish the motions. If a human is seen moving one reed and no human for the other, then one knows which is divine.
This objection assumes the cause is always visible.
3
Grasp the reductio ad absurdum
Nasafi responds with a hypothetical: what if the mover is an invisible spirit (angel, jinn, devil)? Since such beings are imperceptible, the opponent's method fails. Therefore, distinguishing by cause is not reliable.
This exposes a flaw in the opponent's reasoning without denying causation itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يهدم جميع كلامه فصل القصبتين وهو أن قصبتين ظهرت أعاليهما من وراء جدار، يحرك أحديهما العبد والأخرى يحركها الله تعالى، فعاينهما إنسان لن يفصل البتة بين الحركة التي خلقها الله تعالى وبين الحركة التي خلقها العبد لاستوائهما في ذاتيهما من جميع الوجوه. فإن قالوا: يمكن الفصل بين الحركتين بالرجوع إلى السبب، فإنا ننظر وراء الجدار فإذا رأينا إنسانا يحرك أحديهما ولم نر محركًا من البشر للآخر علمنا أن الأولى فعل الخلق والثانية فعل الله تعالى. قلنا لهم أرأيتم لو أن المحرك لأحديهما كان هو الله تعالى والمحرك الآخر بعض الأرواح الناطقة من الملائكة أو الجن أو الشياطين الذين لم تجر المادة برؤيتنا إياهم وعندكم يستحيل رؤيته للطافته، فبم تفصلون بينهما؟
And what demolishes all his speech is the decisive argument of the two reeds: namely, that there are two reeds whose tops appear from behind a wall; one of them is moved by the servant and the other is moved by God. A human sees them. He will never be able to distinguish at all between the motion that God created and the motion that the servant created, because they are identical in their essences from all aspects. If they say: 'It is possible to distinguish between the two motions by reference to the cause; for we look behind the wall, and if we see a human moving one of them and we do not see a human mover for the other, we know that the first is the act of the creature and the second is the act of God.' We say to them: 'Do you not see? Suppose that the mover of one of them is God and the other mover is some rational spirit among the angels, jinn, or devils, which we cannot perceive because of their subtlety, and according to you it is impossible to see them due to their subtlety; how would you distinguish between them?'
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents a decisive refutation known as the 'argument of the two reeds' (فصل القصبتين). It is an objection against those who claim that human action can be empirically distinguished from divine action. The scenario is: two identical reeds are seen moving from behind a wall. One is moved by a human, the other by God. An observer cannot tell which motion is from God and which from the human because the motions are identical in essence. The opponent objects that one can distinguish by looking at the cause: see behind the wall, and if you see a human moving one reed and no human moving the other, you know the first is from the creature and the second from God. The response is a reductio: what if the mover of one reed is God and the mover of the other is an invisible rational spirit (angel, jinn, devil)? Since such beings are imperceptible due to their subtlety, and the opponent holds that seeing them is impossible, then the observer could not distinguish by cause. Therefore, the ability to distinguish by cause depends on visibility of the cause; if the cause is invisible, no distinction is possible. This undermines the opponent's claim that actions can be distinguished by their causes in all cases.

Key Terms

حركة
سبب
فصل
روح ناطقة
لطافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that actions have causes.
Correction: The argument does not deny causation; it denies that one can empirically distinguish divine from human action based solely on observing the cause, especially when the cause is imperceptible.

Checkpoint
In the two reeds argument, what is the purpose of the hypothetical scenario with the rational spirit?
A) To prove that angels exist
B) To show that distinguishing actions by cause fails when the cause is invisible
C) To argue that humans cannot move reeds
D) To claim that all actions are identical

Key Takeaway
The two reeds argument shows that distinguishing actions by cause is only possible if the cause is observable, which fails for divine action or invisible agents.

God Creates, Humans Acquire
Dialectical refutation of Mu'tazila on similarity of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi distinction between divine creation and human acquisition, and how it refutes the Mu'tazila claim of similarity between human and divine acts.

Core Idea
God creates; humans acquire. They are different in kind, not similar.

The Mu'tazila argued that human actions resemble God's creation, implying similarity. The Maturidi response shows that similarity requires two distinct things, but if human act is identical to God's act (as some hold), similarity is impossible. The Maturidi position avoids the problem by distinguishing creation from acquisition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the dialectical flow: the author refutes the Mu'tazila step by step.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila claim that human actions are similar to God's actions. Note the author's first point: similarity requires two distinct things.
3
Understand the identity view
The Najjariyya and Ahl al-Ḥadīth hold that human action is identical to God's action. Why does this make the Mu'tazila argument self-contradictory?
If the act is one, it cannot resemble itself.
4
See why similarity collapses
The author argues that if human act is identical to God's act, the concept of similarity cannot apply because a thing does not resemble itself. This invalidates the Mu'tazila's imposition.
5
Grasp the Maturidi distinction
Finally, the author presents the Maturidi position: God's act is creation and bringing into existence; human act is direct performance and acquisition. Since they are different in essence, similarity does not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يكشف عن عوار هذا الكلام أن الاشتباه يجري بين شيئين، وعندكم فعلكم غير فعل الله تعالى فكانت إحدى الحركتين عندكم فعل العبد والأخرى فعل الله تعالى وهما متشابهتان، فوجد منكم على زعمكم خلق كخلق الله تعالى، فأما عندنا من يسلم لكم من النجارية ومتكلمي أهل الحديث أن العبد له فعل ففعله عندهم عين فعل الله تعالى، فهو من حيث إنه خلق فعل الله تعالى ومن حيث إنه حركة أو سكون طاعة أو معصية فعل العبد، فعين الكسب هو عين الخلق، وعين الخلق هو عين الكسب، كما أن عين الحركة هو عين الشيء وعين الشيء هو عين الحركة وإثبات الاشتباه في شيء واحد محال إذ الشيء لا يشبه نفسه، فكان إلزامكم إياهم فاسد. فعل الله خلق وإيجاد وفعل العبد مباشرة واكتساب وعندنا فعل العبد وإن كان غير فعل الله إلا أن فعل الله تعالى خلق وإيجاد وفعلنا مباشرة واكتساب فلم يلزم التشابه، والله الموفق.
And among what uncovers the fault of this speech is that similarity occurs between two things, and according to you your action is other than the action of God, so one of the two motions according to you is the action of the servant and the other is the action of God, and they are similar. So there existed from you, on your claim, creation similar to the creation of God. However, according to us, those who concede to you from the Najjāriyya and the speculative theologians of Ahl al-Ḥadīth that the servant has an action, so his action according to them is identical to the action of God. So it is, insofar as it is creation, the action of God, and insofar as it is motion or stillness, obedience or disobedience, the action of the servant. So the identical of acquisition is identical to creation, and the identical of creation is identical to acquisition, just as the identical of motion is identical to the thing and the identical of the thing is identical to the motion. And establishing similarity in a single thing is impossible, since a thing does not resemble itself. So your imposition upon them is invalid. The action of God is creation and bringing into existence, and the action of the servant is direct performance and acquisition. And according to us, the action of the servant, even if it is other than the action of God, yet the action of God is creation and bringing into existence, and our action is direct performance and acquisition, so similarity does not follow. And God is the Successor.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that human actions are similar to God's actions. The author first points out that similarity (al-istibāh) requires two distinct things. The Mu'tazila hold that human acts are other than God's acts, so they have two actions that are similar. But they also claim that humans create like God creates—i.e., they have creation similar to God's creation. However, the author notes that some of their interlocutors (the Najjāriyya and the speculative theologians of Ahl al-Ḥadīth) hold that human action is identical to God's action. For them, the act is one: as creation it is God's act, as motion or obedience it is the servant's act. If that is the case, then there is no similarity between two things because it is one thing. The author then argues that if human act is identical to God's act, the concept of similarity cannot apply because a thing does not resemble itself. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's imposition upon those who hold identity is invalid. Finally, the author states the Maturidi position: God's act is creation (khalq) and bringing into existence (ījād), while human act is direct performance (mubāshara) and acquisition (iktisāb). Since they are different in essence, similarity does not arise. The passage ends with 'And God is the Successor.'

Key Terms

خلق
إيجاد
مباشرة
اكتساب
عين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidis deny human action entirely.
Correction: Maturidis affirm human action but as acquisition (iktisāb), not creation. Human action is real but is a direct performance and acquisition, not a creation ex nihilo.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the difference between God's action and human action?
A) They are identical
B) God's action is creation, human action is acquisition
C) Both are creation
D) Human action is creation

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position preserves divine uniqueness by distinguishing creation (God) from acquisition (human), avoiding any real similarity between divine and human acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you face a difficult choice, do you rely on yourself as the 'creator' of your decision, or do you see yourself as an acquirer who must seek God's help? Commit today to pause before each action and whisper, 'O Allah, create in me the will and ability, and grant me a good acquisition.'

The Two Sticks Analogy on Essence Distinction
The author uses an analogy of two sticks to refute distinction by essence and then counters a causal objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of the two sticks refutes the claim that identical motions can be distinguished by their essences, and how the author counters the objection based on cause.

Core Idea
Identical motions cannot be distinguished by essence, only by cause—and even cause can be hidden.

The author presents a powerful analogy: two sticks behind a wall, one moved by a human, the other by God. An observer sees identical motions and cannot tell them apart. This challenges the Mu'tazila view that human acts and divine acts are alike in essence. But what if we look behind the wall? The author anticipates this objection and responds with a twist: what if the mover is an invisible spirit? This lesson unpacks the logic of this refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Get a feel for the argument's flow before diving deeper.
Notice the author's concise style; the argument is condensed but powerful.
2
Understand the Analogy
Focus on the scenario: two sticks behind a wall, moved by different agents. The observer cannot distinguish the motions because they are identical in essence. Ask yourself: what is the author trying to prove with this analogy?
The key point is that essence alone cannot differentiate acts.
3
Examine the Objection
The opponent says: look behind the wall to see the cause. If you see a human mover, that act is human; if no human mover, it is divine. Identify this objection and ask: does this solve the problem?
The objection relies on the visibility of the cause.
4
Analyze the Counter-Objection
The author replies: what if the mover is an invisible spirit (angel, jinn, or devil)? According to Mu'tazila, such spirits are imperceptible. Then how would you distinguish? Reflect on why this undermines the objection.
The counter shows that relying on cause fails when the cause is beyond sensory perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يهدم جميع كلامه فصل القصبتين وهو أن قصبتين ظهرت أعاليهما من وراء جدار، يحرك أحديهما العبد والأخرى يحركها الله تعالى، فعاينهما إنسان لن يفصل البتة بين الحركة التي خلقها الله تعالى وبين الحركة التي خلقها العبد لاستوائهما في ذاتيهما من جميع الوجوه. فإن قالوا: يمكن الفصل بين الحركتين بالرجوع إلى السبب، فإنا ننظر وراء الجدار فإذا رأينا إنسانا يحرك أحديهما ولم نر محركًا من البشر للآخر علمنا أن الأولى فعل الخلق والثانية فعل الله تعالى. قلنا لهم أرأيتم لو أن المحرك لأحديهما كان هو الله تعالى والمحرك الآخر بعض الأرواح الناطقة من الملائكة أو الجن أو الشياطين الذين لم تجر المادة برؤيتنا إياهم وعندكم يستحيل رؤيته للطافته، فبم تفصلون بينهما؟
And what demolishes all his speech is the chapter of the two sticks. It is that two sticks, their upper parts appearing from behind a wall; the servant moves one of them and God moves the other. A man sees them, and he will never distinguish at all between the motion which God created and the motion which the servant created, due to their equality in their essences in all respects. If they say: It is possible to distinguish between the two motions by referring to the cause, for we look behind the wall, and if we see a human moving one of them, and we see no human mover for the other, we know that the first is the act of the created and the second is the act of God. We say to them: Have you considered if the mover of one of them is God and the mover of the other is some rational spirit among the angels or jinn or devils, whom matter does not permit us to see, and according to you their sight is impossible due to their subtlety, then how would you distinguish between them?
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents an analogy to refute the Mu'tazila claim that human acts and God's acts can be distinguished by their essences. The author describes a scenario: two sticks behind a wall, only their tips visible. One stick is moved by a human servant, the other by God. An observer sees the motions but cannot tell them apart because the motions are identical in their essences. This shows that if human and divine acts were alike in essence, they would be indistinguishable, undermining the Mu'tazila position that humans can create actions like God's. The opponent then objects that one can distinguish by referring to the cause (al-sabab): look behind the wall to see if there is a human mover. The author replies with a further challenge: what if the mover of one stick is God and the mover of the other is an invisible spirit (angel, jinn, or devil)? Since the Mu'tazila hold that such spirits are imperceptible due to their subtlety (al-latāfa), the observer cannot ascertain the cause. Thus the distinction by cause fails, and the analogy stands as a refutation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two identical motions can be distinguished by looking at their causes (the mover).
Correction: The passage shows that even cause may not be accessible if the mover is invisible, so distinction by essence or cause fails.

Checkpoint

A) To prove that human actions are identical to God's actions
B) To show that objectively identical motions cannot be attributed to different agents based on essence
C) To argue that humans can create like God
D) To demonstrate that God is the only mover

Key Takeaway
The analogy of the two sticks refutes the claim that motions can be distinguished by their essences, and the counter-objection about invisible spirits shows that distinguishing by cause is also not always possible.

Principle of Similarity and Refutation of Mu‘tazila
The author states a general principle about similarity and concludes that Mu'tazila position leads to shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi principle of similarity (al-istibah) is used to refute the Mu‘tazila claim that human acts are created by humans similar to God's creation, showing it leads to absurdity and shirk.

Core Idea
Two things similar in essence cannot be made different by their agents.

In this passage, the author establishes a key principle of rational theology: similarity (istibah) is determined by essence, not by external factors like the agent. He then applies this principle to show that the Mu‘tazila position on human creative power would force a similarity between divine and human creation, which the Quran denies, thereby refuting them and affirming that only God can create.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
Read the first part of the passage where the author states that two things similar in their essences do not differ due to difference of agents or other external factors. The only relevant factor is the essence itself or the meaning that necessitates similarity.
This is a fundamental rule in kalam for judging similarity and difference.
2
Examine the examples
The author lists examples: two motions from different directions, motion and stillness, blackness and whiteness, etc. He points out that these are not made similar by a single agent; likewise, different agents do not make actions different if they are similar in essence.
Notice how the examples illustrate the independence of essence from external factors.
3
Apply to the Mu‘tazila position
The author then applies the principle to the Mu‘tazila. According to Mu‘tazila, humans create their own actions, so human creation would be similar in essence to God's creation. But the Quran (13:16) denies that there are partners who create like God, implying that such similarity would make their worship of idols excusable – a clear shirk.
4
Draw the conclusion
The author concludes that affirming creative power (qudrat al-takhlīq) for anyone other than God leads to absurdity (al-muḥāl) and disbelief. This refutes the Mu‘tazila and reaffirms the Islamic doctrine that only God creates.
This is the key refutation that Maturidis use against Mu‘tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: قد سبق الكلام أن المشتبهين بذاتيهما لا يختلفان باختلاف الفاعلين ولا باختلاف معاني وراء ذاتيهما، ولا عبرة لاختلاف الفاعل واتحاده، إنما العبرة فلي معرفة حقيقة الاشتباه لذات المشتبهين أو المعنى الموجب للاشتباه لا لما وراء ذلك من الفاعل أو المكان أو الزمان أو الآلة أو السبب، لأن كل ذلك لا يوجب التغير في علة الاشتباه أو الاختلاف، ولهذا لم يكن بين الحركتين من جهتين مختلفتين وبين الحركة والسكون وبين السواد والبياض والاجتماع والافتراق والحلاوة والمرارة والنتن والرائحة الطيبة مشابهة عند اتحاد الفاعل، فكذا لا يوجب اختلاف الفاعلين اختلافا في الفعلين عند اشتباههما بذاتيهما. وتحقق ما رمنا تحقيقه من إثبات المشابهة بين خلق الله تعالى وخلق العباد على أصول المعتزلة، والله تعالى نفى ذلك فصاروا بذلك رادين على الله تعالى وجاعلين أنفسهم مستحقة للعبادة وأهلاً لها لأن الله تعالى بقوله: ﴿ أَمْ جَعَلُوا لِلَّهِ شُرَكَاءَ خَلَقُوا كَخَلْقِهِ فَتَشَبَهَ الْخَلَقُ عَلَيْهِمْ ﴾ أوجب لهم العذر في عبادتهم ما كانوا يعبدون وجعلهم إياهم شركاء الله تعالى في العبادة لو كان منهم خلق كخلقه، وكل ذلك كفر عصمنا الله تعالى عن مثل ذلك. وبالوقوف على ما بينا ظهرت صحة ما ادعينا أن إثبات قدرة التخليق لغير الله تعالى يؤدي إلى المحال، وبالله التوفيق.
Then we say: The discourse has already preceded that two things that are similar in their essences do not differ by difference of agents nor by difference of meanings behind their essences. And there is no regard to the difference or unity of the agent. The regard is only for knowing the reality of similarity for the essence of the two similar things or the meaning that necessitates similarity, not for what is beyond that of the agent, place, time, instrument, or cause, because all that does not necessitate change in the cause of similarity or difference. For this reason, there is not between two motions from two different directions, nor between motion and stillness, nor between blackness and whiteness, nor between conjunction and separation, nor between sweetness and bitterness, nor between stench and pleasant fragrance, similarity when the agent is one. Likewise, the difference of agents does not necessitate a difference in the two actions when they are similar in their essences. And it is realized what we intended to realize: establishing similarity between the creation of God and the creation of servants according to the principles of the Mu'tazila. But God denied that, so thereby they become rejecters of God and considering themselves deserving of worship and worthy of it, because God, by His saying: 'Or have they attributed to God partners who create as He creates, so the creation became similar to them?' He granted them an excuse in their worship of what they worshipped, and He considered them partners with God in worship if there were from them creation like His creation. And all of that is disbelief; God protect us from the like of that. And by pausing at what we have clarified, the correctness of what we claimed becomes apparent: that affirming creative power for other than God leads to absurdity. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a general principle of kalam regarding similarity (al-istibāh) and a concluding refutation of the Mu'tazila. The author first establishes that two things similar in their essences do not differ due to difference in agents, meanings, or other accidental factors. The only relevant factor is the essence itself or the meaning that necessitates similarity. He gives examples: two motions from different directions, motion and stillness, blackness and whiteness, etc., do not become similar just because the agent is one. Hence, differing agents cannot make two actions different if they are similar in essence. Then the author applies this: according to the Mu'tazila principles, human creation would be like God's creation in essence, so they must admit similarity. But God denies such similarity in the Quran (13:16), implying that if there were partners who create like God, their created things would be similar, and that would justify shirk. Since the Mu'tazila are effectively claiming the possibility of creation similar to God's, they are rejecting God's denial and thus falling into disbelief. The author concludes that affirming creative power (qudrat al-takhlīq) for anyone other than God leads to absurdity (al-muḥāl).

Key Terms
اشتباه (similarity)
ذات (essence)
فاعل (agent)
قدرة التخليق (creative power)
محال (absurdity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu‘tazila position on human creative power is a legitimate theological interpretation that does not affect divine uniqueness.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that it necessarily leads to shirk and absurdity, contradicting the Quran, because if humans truly create, their creation would be essentially similar to God's creation, which the Quran denies and would justify idol worship.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the consequence of affirming creative power for other than God?
A) It leads to anthropomorphism
B) It leads to absurdity and possible shirk
C) It is permissible
D) It is a matter of interpretation

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi principle of similarity shows that the Mu‘tazila claim of human creative power would necessarily lead to shirk and is therefore absurd, affirming that only God creates.

What one step will you take today to acknowledge that all creation is exclusively from Allah?
?
When you see someone else's good deeds, do you ever think 'they did it' as if they are the real creators of that act? How does the author's argument about the Mu'tazila expose the subtle shirk in over-crediting human agency? What will you change in how you speak about your own and others' actions?
?
If you truly believed that only God creates, how would it transform your feeling of anxiety about your own performance? What is your heart's response to being reminded that any claim of creative power for other than God is kufr?

The Servant's Act Without Creative Power
Proof that the servant has an act but not creative power, with objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi theologians prove that humans have genuine actions (af‘āl) without possessing the power of creation, thereby maintaining both divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

Core Idea
Humans truly act, but only Allah creates the act.

This passage presents a key Maturidi argument against the Jabriyya (extreme predestinarians) and also against those who might over-attribute creative power to humans. The author shows that human actions are real and distinguishable from compelled motions, yet their existence and nature ultimately depend on Allah’s creative power. The discussion centers on the distinction between having an act (fi‘l) and being the originator (mukhtari‘) of it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author's main claim is that humans have an act but not creative power.
The claim is stated at the beginning: 'الدليل على أن العبد له فعل وإن لم تكن له قدرة التخليق'.
2
Identify the types of evidence
The author cites three lines of evidence: the opponents' own admission, scriptural/rational proofs from earlier, and necessary knowledge. Find these in the text.
The phrase 'مساعدة الخصوم' refers to the opponents' admission.
3
Examine the first objection and answer
The first objection says: if the human has an act, he must be the originator. The author's answer: only Allah has the power of origination, as previously proven.
4
Examine the second objection and answer
The second objection says: without creative power, the human would have no act and be compelled. The answer: the human does have an act, and the distinction between voluntary and necessary acts is known by necessity.
Necessary acts are those we cannot avoid (e.g., blinking when something approaches), while voluntary acts are chosen.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the author balances human agency and divine omnipotence. The human act is real but not self-created; it is attributed to the human in terms of acquisition (kasb) but to Allah in terms of creation.
This is the Maturidi position on kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل على أن العبد له فعل وإن لم تكن له قدرة التخليق مساعدة الخصوم إيانا أن له فعلاً، وما مر من الدلائل الموجبة لذلك وهي الدلائل السمعية والعقلية والضرورة التي يصير دافعها مكابرًا على ما سبق ذكره في إبطال كلام الجبرية. فإن قالوا: إذا ثبت أن العبد له فعل ثبت أنه هو المخترع له. قلنا: قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن ليس لغير الله تعالى قدرة الاختراع على وجه لم يبق للشك فيه مجال. فإن قالوا: لو لم يكن للعبد قدرة الاختراع لم يكن له فعل، وكان مضطرًا فيما يحصل منه من الأفعال. قلنا قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن له فعلاً يعرف بطريق الضرورة الفرق بين الأفعال الضرورية وبين الأفعال الاختيارية.
Then the evidence that the servant has an act although he does not have the power of creation is the admission of the opponents themselves that he has an act, and the previous evidences that necessitate that, which are the scriptural, rational, and necessary evidences, such that the one who rejects them becomes obstinate, as previously mentioned in refuting the speech of the Jabriyya. If they say: If it is established that the servant has an act, it is established that he is the one who originates it. We say: We have established the evidence that there is no power of origination for anyone other than Allah Most High in a manner that leaves no room for doubt. If they say: If the servant does not have the power of origination, he would have no act, and he would be compelled in what occurs from him of actions. We say: We have established the evidence that he has an act, by which the distinction between necessary acts and voluntary acts is known by way of necessity.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the human servant has an act (فعل) but does not possess the power of creation (قدرة التخليق). The author begins by citing multiple lines of evidence: the opponents' own admission that humans have acts, as well as revealed (سمعية), rational (عقلية), and necessary (ضرورة) proofs that were previously mentioned in refuting the Jabriyya (those who deny human agency). Then two objections are presented and answered. The first objection: If the human has an act, then he must be the originator (مخترع) of it. The answer: We have already proven that no one other than God has the power of origination. The second objection: If the human has no power of origination, then he cannot have an act; all his actions would be compelled. The answer: We have already proven that the human has an act, and the difference between necessary and voluntary acts is known by necessity (بالضرورة). Thus, the human's act is established without requiring creative power.

Key Terms
فعل
قدرة التخليق
مخترع
ضرورة
اختياري
اضطراري
جبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an action is not created by the human, then it cannot be attributed to him.
Correction: The human can have an act (fi‘l) without being the originator (mukhtari‘). The act is attributed to the human in terms of acquisition (kasb), while creation is solely Allah's.

Checkpoint
How does the author prove that the human has an act without creative power?
A) By relying solely on the admission of opponents.
B) By using scriptural, rational, and necessary proofs, and by answering objections.
C) By denying the existence of voluntary acts.
D) By claiming that all acts are compelled.

Key Takeaway
The human has a genuine act, but only Allah is the creator (mukhtari‘); this is proven by necessity and reason.
