The First Objection: Action Does Not Equal Invention
First objection and response about invention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that having an action does not necessarily require the power of invention, because only Allah has creative power.

Core Idea
Action does not imply invention; creation is exclusive to Allah.

The opponent argues that if a human has an action, then he must be the inventor of it. This objection seems logical at first glance, but the author responds by citing an established proof that only Allah possesses the power of invention. This lesson unpacks that exchange and clarifies the distinction between acting and creating.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the structure: objection then response.
Notice that the author's response is definitive: 'We have established the proof...'
2
Understand the proof about invention
The author states that proof has already been given that no one other than Allah has the power of invention. Reflect on why this is foundational.
This proof is not repeated here; it is assumed from prior discussion.
3
Connect to the broader concept
Consider how this response defends divine transcendence while also preserving human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إذا ثبت أن العبد له فعل ثبت أنه هو المخترع له.
قلنا: قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن ليس لغير الله تعالى قدرة الاختراع على وجه لم يبق للشك فيه مجال.
فإن قالوا : لو لم يكن للعبد قدرة الاختراع لم يكن له فعل، وكان مضطرًا فيما يحصل منه من الأفعال.
قلنا قد أقمنا الدلالة على أن له فعلاً يعرف بطريق الضرورة الفرق بين الأفعال الضرورية وبين الأفعال الاختيارية.
For if they say: If it is established that the slave has an act, it is established that he is the inventor (mukhtari‘) of it. We say: We have established the proof (dalāla) that there is no power of invention (qudrat al-ikhtirā‘) for other than Allah the Exalted, in a manner that leaves no room for doubt. For if they say: If the slave did not have the power of invention, he would have no act, and would be compelled (muḍṭarr) in what occurs from him of actions. We say: We have established the proof that he has an act, and by way of necessity (ḍarūra) the difference is known between necessary actions (al-af‘āl al-ḍarūriyya) and voluntary actions (al-af‘āl al-ikhtiyāriyya).
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents two objections from an opponent and the author's responses. The first objection argues that if the slave has an act, then he must be the inventor of it. The author replies by stating that decisive proof has already been established that only Allah has the power of invention (ikhtirā‘). The second objection claims that without the power of invention, the slave would have no act and would be compelled. The author responds by asserting that proof has been established that the slave does have an act, and the difference between necessary actions (like involuntary movement) and voluntary actions is known through immediate necessity (ḍarūra). Thus, the slave is not compelled, but his action is not creative either.

Key Terms
فعل
قدرة الاختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a human performs an action, then he created it from nothing.
Correction: The action is not created by the human; only Allah has the power of invention. Human action is acquired, not invented.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who possesses the power of invention?
A) Humans
B) Angels
C) Only Allah
D) Both humans and Allah

Key Takeaway
Possessing an action does not make one an inventor; creation belongs to Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Second Objection: No Invention Means No Action?
Second objection and response about action and compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that humans have voluntary actions distinct from necessary actions, even without possessing the power of invention.

Core Idea
Voluntary actions are known by necessity, not by invention.

The opponent raises a second objection: if humans lack the power of invention, then they would have no real action and would be compelled. The author responds by affirming that humans do have actions, and we instinctively know the difference between involuntary and voluntary actions. This lesson examines that crucial distinction.

The term 'necessity' (ḍarūra) refers to immediate, self-evident knowledge.
3

Reflect on examples of involuntary actions (like blinking) and voluntary actions (like walking) to internalize the distinction.

Shifting the Burden in the Power Objection
Answer to an objection about power's relation to origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi theologian answers the objection that power (qudra) only relates to origination by redirecting the opponent to refute the proof that only Allah has creative power.

Core Idea
The objection is neutralized by forcing the opponent to refute the proof first.

The opponent claims that power can only relate to origination, and that its relation to the manner of origination is inconceivable. The reply does not directly defend the relation; instead, it reminds the opponent of the already-established proof that only Allah has the power of invention. The burden is shifted: the opponent must object to that proof rather than simply assert inconceivability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opponent's statement that power only relates to origination and that its relation to the manner of origination is inconceivable. Notice the logical move they are making.
The opponent is trying to limit power's scope to a specific act.
2
Examine the reply
The author responds by referencing the proof that only Allah has the power of invention and origination. He then demands that the opponent object to that proof instead of raising a new point.
This is a methodological argument about where the burden of proof lies.
3
Reflect on the methodological lesson
Consider why this shift is effective: it forces the opponent to engage with the core proof rather than create a side objection. This teaches a key principle of theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم : إن لا تعلق للقدرة إلا بالإحداث وتعلقها بوجه الإحداث غير معقول.
فإنا نقول لهم : قد أقمنا الدلالة على استحالة ثبوت قدرة الاختراع والإحداث لغير الله تعالى فعليكم أن تعترضوا على ذلك الدليل.
And with this they are answered regarding their saying: Indeed, power (al-qudra) has no relation (ta‘alluq) except to origination (al-iḥdāth), and its relation to the manner (wajh) of origination is inconceivable. For we say to them: We have established the proof for the impossibility of the power of invention (qudrat al-ikhtirā‘) and origination (al-iḥdāth) belonging to other than Allah the Exalted; so you should object (ta‘tariḍū) to that proof.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a final objection: that power (qudra) can only relate to origination (iḥdāth), and its relation to the 'manner' (wajh) of origination is inconceivable. The author's response is to redirect the argument: the opponent has already been presented with proof that only Allah has the power of invention and origination. Therefore, the opponent must first engage with that proof by objecting to it, rather than simply asserting inconceivability. This is a methodological point: the burden is on the opponent to refute the established proof.

Key Terms
قدرة
تعلق
إحداث
اعتراض
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the objection itself is a valid refutation because it seems to limit the scope of power. The passage corrects this by showing that the objection must be measured against the established proof.
Correction: The objection is not automatically valid; the opponent must first refute the proof that only Allah has creative power. Merely asserting the inconceivability of a relation is insufficient.

Checkpoint

A) Accept it without argument
B) Object to it (ta‘tariḍū)
C) Ignore it
D) Seek imagination to understand it

Key Takeaway
A valid objection must engage with the proof, not merely assert inconceivability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone presents you with a clear proof from revelation or reason, do you quickly shift the burden by raising doubts, or do you honestly engage with the proof? What is one area of your faith where you have been avoiding the foundational proof?
?
Are there beliefs you hold simply because you haven't been challenged, or have you truly examined the proofs? Commit to looking at one proof this week and writing down your honest objections.
?
The author tells the opponent to object to the proof. When you struggle with a teaching of Islam, do you first try to refute the proof, or do you accept it? What does your response reveal about your heart?

The Objection and Obligation to Follow Proof
Nasafi states the objection and gives the initial proof-based reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi responds to the objection that if the servant cannot originate acts, his power is nullified, and why following the proof is obligatory.

Core Idea
Proof obligates us to affirm the servant's act even when origination is impossible.

The opponents argue that denying the servant power of invention renders his power useless. Imam Nasafi replies by insisting that the rational proof already establishes the servant has an act within his power (maqdur), and we must adhere to the proof even if we cannot fully explain the mechanics. This lesson presents the initial response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the direct structure: 'if they say... we say'.
This is a classic objection-and-answer format in kalam.
2
Understand key terms
Identify the terms 'قدرة' (power), 'اختراع' (invention/origination), 'مقدور' (object of power). Understand how they relate to the objection.
The objection claims that without 'اختراع', power has no object.
3
See the role of proof
Notice that the author invokes an already established proof ('أقمنا الدليل') to counter the objection. The proof shows the servant has an act (فعل) that is his maqdur.
This highlights the priority of rational proof over speculative objections.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the two proofs together permit power to relate to an act without origination. Ask yourself why this is sufficient for now.
The detailed mechanism is explained later; here the focus is on accepting the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لو استحال ذلك لبطل تعلق قدرة العبد بشيء وتعطلت قدرته. قلنا : وقد أقمنا الدليل على أن العبد له فعل هو مقدوره فجيب الانقياد للدليل وإثبات الفعل للعبد وإن لم يخترعه، وثبت (۱) بمجموع الدليلين جواز تعلق القدرة لا بجهة الاختراع. ثم نقول : ما يخترعه الله تعالى فيما لا اختيار له لا يكون فعلاً له فبكون مخلوق الله تعالى ومفعوله لا فعله ولا فعل غيره عندنا، وعند الأشعرية والنجارية يكون فعل الله تعالى لأنه مفعوله والفعل والمعفول واحد، وما يخترعه فيمن له الاختيار، إن كان ذلك الشيء مما لا يتعلق به قدرة المحل فهو أيضا ليس بفعل للعبد بل العبد محله لا غير، وما يخترعه فيه باختيار العبد ذلك وله عليه قدرة فأثر تعلق قدرته (به (۲) كونه فعلاً، فيكون الله تعالى مخترعا فعل العبد باختياره لولا اختيار العبد وقصده اكتسابه لما خلقه الله تعالى فعلا له فأثر قدرته كون ذلك (۳) المخترع فعلاً له، هذا عن جهة التبرع مع أنا لا حاجة بنا إلى بيان ذلك على ما بينا من أن وجوب اتباع الدليلين يقتضي ما قلنا، والله الموفق.
For if they say: 'If that were impossible, then the relation of the servant's power to anything would be invalid, and his power would become inoperative.' We say: 'And we have established the proof that the servant has an act that is his power (maqdur), so it is obligatory to submit to the proof and affirm the act for the servant even if he does not invent it. And it is established (۱) by the combination of the two proofs the permissibility of the relation of power not by way of invention. Then we say: What God invents (yakhutari'uhu) in that which has no choice is not an act for it, so it is God's creation (makhluq) and effect (maf'ul), not His act, nor the act of another in our view. In the view of the Ash'aris and the Najjariyya, it is God's act because it is His effect, and the act and the effect are one. And what He invents in one who has choice, if that thing is not related to the power of the locus (mahal), then also it is not an act of the servant, rather the servant is merely its locus. And what He invents in it with the servant's choice of that, and he has power over it, then the effect of the relation of his power (به (۲) is its being an act. So God is the inventor of the servant's act by his choice; were it not for the servant's choice and intention to acquire it, God would not have created it as an act for him. So the effect of his power is that invented thing being an act for him. This is by way of gratuitousness, although we have no need to explain that, based on what we explained that the obligation to follow the two proofs necessitates what we said. And God is the one who grants success.'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a detailed objection-and-answer. The opponents argue that if the power of invention (ikhtira') is impossible for the servant, then the servant's power would have no object and be nullified. The author responds by reiterating that proof establishes the servant has an act that is within his power (maqdur), so one must accept that even if the servant does not invent it. The two proofs together show that power can relate to an act without originating it. Then the author clarifies the theology of divine creation and human action: what God creates in beings without choice is not an act for them but merely God's creation. For beings with choice, if the thing is not within the locus's power, it is not an act; but if the servant chooses it and has power over it, then the effect of that power makes it an act for the servant, even though God is the creator of that act. This is presented as a gratuitous explanation, but the essential point is that the obligation to follow the two proofs suffices.

الاختراع
الفعل
العبد
المقدور

D) To reject the proof

Key Takeaway
Proof takes precedence over speculative objections; the servant's act is affirmed even if origination is impossible.

Make a firm intention to act with sincerity, knowing your actions are attributed to you.

How does the response that power relates to an act without origination restore the meaning of your striving? After this lesson, will you change how you view your daily actions and rely on God while taking full responsibility?

When the Servant's Act Becomes His Act: A Theological Taxonomy
Nasafi classifies divine creation in beings without choice and with choice, explaining when the act is attributed to the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi distinction between what God creates in beings without choice and in beings with choice, and how the servant's choice and power make the created act his.

Core Idea
The servant's power and choice turn God's creation into the servant's act.

After establishing that the servant has an act, Imam Nasafi provides a detailed taxonomy of divine creation in relation to beings with and without choice. This clarifies why the servant is genuinely the agent of his actions, even though God creates them. This lesson explores that taxonomy and contrasts it with the Ash'ari and Najjariyya views.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed clarification
Read the second part from 'ثم نقول' to the end. Notice the classification based on whether the being has choice and whether the action is within the power of the locus.
This is a sophisticated theological distinction.
2
Analyze beings without choice
Understand what happens when God creates something in a being that has no choice (e.g., inanimate objects). According to Maturidis, it is not an act for that being, but for Ash'aris/Najjaris it is God's act.
Key point: 'لا يكون فعلاً له' – it is not its act.
3
Analyze beings with choice
For humans, distinguish two cases: (a) when the thing is not within the person's power – then it is not an act of the servant; (b) when the person chooses and has power – then the effect of power makes it an act.
The phrase 'فأثر تعلق قدرته به كونه فعلاً' is crucial.
4
Reflect on the role of choice
Note that God creates the act, but He does so only because the servant chooses and intends to acquire it (لولا اختيار العبد وقصده اكتسابه). The servant's choice is a necessary condition.
This preserves human responsibility while affirming divine creation.
5
Understand the gratuitous nature
The author says this explanation is by way of 'تبرع' (gratuitousness), emphasizing that the obligation to follow the two proofs suffices without such details.
This shows the author's confidence in the earlier proof-based response.

Divine Power Has Two Aspects: Invention and Acquisition
Author presents opponent's analogy and refutes it by distinguishing two modalities of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between divine knowledge and divine power fails, and how power relates to its object in two distinct respects.

Core Idea
Divine power relates to acts in two ways; knowledge has only one way.

The opponent claims that just as divine knowledge must match reality, divine power must give the same power to humans. The author rebuts by showing power has two aspects: invention (divine) and acquisition (human). This distinction is essential for the Maturidi theory of kasb.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's analogy
Start by reading the first part of the passage, where the opponent draws a parallel between knowledge and power. Notice how he argues from the necessity of truthful information.
The analogy hinges on the idea that if God informs us incorrectly, it becomes ignorance; similarly, if He empowers us differently, it would not be true empowerment.
2
Identify the author's refutation
After the phrase 'we answer this speech', the author directly rebuts the analogy. He asserts that empowerment can still occur even if the servant is not given the power of invention.
Look for the key phrase 'بل يكون ذلك إقدارًا وإن لم يقدرنا على الاختراع'.
3
Understand the two respects of power
The author explains that power can relate to its object in two ways: the aspect of invention (ikhtiraʿ) and the aspect of acquisition (iktisāb). One belongs to God, the other to the servant.
This is the central doctrinal point: maqdur is of two types, mukhtaraʿ and muktasab.
4
Contrast with knowledge
The author emphasizes that knowledge has only one relation: it must correspond to reality as it is. This contrast shows why the analogy fails.
If knowledge related differently, it would be ignorance, not knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلمنا أنه أسود أو أبيض وعلى ما كان كان ما ثبت لنا علمًا وإن علم هو شيئًا أسود وأعلمنا أبيض فما ثبت لنا لا يكون علمًا بل يكون جهلاً، فكذا هذا في قدرته وإقداره إيانا، فإذا قدر على شيء وأقدرنا عليه ينبغي أن يقدرنا على ذلك الوجه ولو أقدرنا لا على ذلك الوجه لا يكون ذلك إقدار ثم إنه تعالى يقدر اختراع الأشياء فإذا أقدرنا يقدرنا على جهة الاختراع، ولو لم يقدرنا على تلك الجهة لا يكون ذلك إقدارا. لما مر فإنا نجيب عن هذا الكلام فنقول : بل يكون ذلك إقدارًا وإن لم يقدرنا على الاختراع أن للعبد فعلاً يتعلق به قدرته، وأن ثبوت الاختراع من جهته مستحيل، وثبت بمجموع الدليلين أن المقدور نوعان: مخترع ومكتسب، والقدرة تتعلق بالمقدور بجهتين جهة اختراع وجهة اكتساب اختص الله تعالى بأحدهما واختص المحدث بالآخر، بخلاف العلم فإن هناك لم يثبت دليل يوجب أن يعلق العلم بالمعلوم بجهتين بل قام الدليل أن تعلقه بالمعلوم بجهة واحدة وهو أن يتعلق به على ما هو به، إذ لو تعلق به لا على هذه الجهة لكان جهلاً لا علمًا.
You informed us that it is black or white and according to what it was, what was established for us was knowledge; if He knows something as black but informs us as white, what is established for us is not knowledge but ignorance. So likewise this in His power and His empowering us: if He has power over something and empowers us over it, He should empower us in that respect; if He empowers us not in that respect, that is not empowering. Then He, exalted, has power to invent things; if He empowers us, He empowers us in the aspect of invention; if He does not empower us in that aspect, that is not empowering. As for the past [objection], we answer this speech and say: Rather, that is empowering even if He does not empower us over invention, because the servant has an act to which his power attaches, and establishing invention from his side is impossible. It is established by the combination of two proofs that the object of power (al-maqdur) is of two types: invented (mukhtaraʿ) and acquired (muktasab). Power relates to the object of power in two respects: the aspect of invention and the aspect of acquisition. Allah, exalted, is specialized in one of them, and the contingent (al-muḥdath) is specialized in the other. Unlike knowledge, for there no proof was established obligating that knowledge relates to the known in two respects; rather the proof established that its relation to the known is in one respect, that is, it relates to it as it is, for if it related to it not in this respect, it would be ignorance, not knowledge.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's analogy between divine knowledge and divine power regarding human acts. The opponent argues: if God knows something and informs us, it becomes knowledge for us only if He informs us as it really is; otherwise it is ignorance. Similarly, if God has power over something and empowers us, He must empower us in the same respect (i.e., invention), otherwise it is not true empowering. The author replies by distinguishing the modalities of power from knowledge. Power, he says, relates to its object in two distinct ways: the aspect of invention (ikhtiraʿ) and the aspect of acquisition (iktisāb). The first is exclusive to God, the second to the contingent creature (the servant). This is based on two proofs: the impossibility of invention by the servant, and the establishment of human act. In contrast, knowledge relates to the known in only one way: as it really is. If it related otherwise, it would be ignorance. Therefore, the analogy fails: divine power has two aspects, while divine knowledge has only one.

Key Terms
قدرة
علم
اختراع
اكتساب
مقدور
معلوم
جهل
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God has power over an act, human power must be of the same kind.
Correction: The author shows power has two distinct modalities: invention (God) and acquisition (human). The analogy with knowledge fails because knowledge has only one modality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, power relates to the object of power in how many respects?
A) One respect: invention only
B) Two respects: invention and acquisition
C) Three respects: invention, acquisition, and knowledge
D) Indeterminate number

Key Takeaway
Power has two modalities—invention (God) and acquisition (human)—while knowledge has only one: correspondence with reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that your power to act is not invention but acquisition. In what areas of your life do you still act as if you are the originator of your deeds, forgetting that your very ability to act is a loan from Allah? How will you remind yourself daily that you merely acquire what He grants?
?
The opponent's analogy failed because knowledge must match reality, but power has two modalities. Are you holding expectations from God that He should empower you in exactly the same way He acts? Where do you feel frustrated with His decrees because you assume your power must be like His?
?
If your every act is acquired, not invented, then even your obedience is a gift from Allah. Does this humble you or make you feel less responsible? How can you take full accountability for your actions while recognizing that you did not create them?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Refuting the Analogy of Knowledge and Power
Author refutes the inference from knowledge to power using the impossibility of shared invention and analogies of informing and empowering.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy between divine knowledge and divine power fails, and why human power cannot be in the aspect of invention.

Core Idea
Knowledge can be shared; power over invention cannot.

The opponent argues that just as human knowledge does not negate divine knowledge, human power does not negate divine power. In this passage, the author refutes this analogy by pointing out a critical difference: a thing being invented by two powers is impossible by consensus. He then strengthens his case with arguments from the nature of informing and empowering.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
The argument uses a 'if...then' structure — follow the logical flow.
2
Identify the initial analogy and its refutation
The author begins by restating the opponent's analogy: just as our knowledge relates to what Allah knows without removing His knowledge, so our power should relate to what He has power over. Then he refutes it by noting that invention by two powers is impossible by consensus (ijma'). Ask yourself: why does this difference matter?
The key term here is 'اختراع' (invention) — the direct creation of something from nothing.
3
Understand the supporting arguments
The author uses two additional arguments: (1) informing (i'lam) someone without prior knowledge is impossible — only one who knows can inform; (2) removing knowledge from what is known by another leads to ignorance, and similarly removing power leads to incapacity (ajz). The opponent's view would imply ta'jiz (incapacitating Allah), which is impossible.
These analogies reinforce that human power cannot be in the aspect of invention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نشتغل بإبطال ذلك فنقول : قياس العلم يقتضي ما قلنا لو عقلتم، فإن علمنا يتعلق بما هو معلوم الله تعالى وليس من ضرورة تعلق علمنا به زوال علم الباري جل وعلا بل بقي علمه متعلقا به، وبقي المعلوم معلومًا له وإن صار معلومًا لنا، فكذا قدرتنا ينبغي أن تتعلق بما هو مقدور الله تعالى وعند تعلق قدرتنا به يبقى مقدورًا له، وكانت قدرته للاختراع فيبقى كذلك وكون الشيء مخترعًا بقدرتين محال بالإجماع ، فتكون قدرة العبد متعلقة لا بجهة الاختراع ضرورة، فأما كون المعلوم معلومًا بعلمين فليس بمحال فيتعلق به علمه تعالى و علمنا، والذي يؤيد ما قلنا: أن اعتبار العلم يوجب ما ذهبنا إليه أن إعلام الغير ممن لا علم له به محال، وإنما يصح إعلام الغير ممن له العلم، فكذا إقدار الغير على شيء لا قدرة للمقدور عليه يكون محالاً، وكذا زوال العلم عما هو معلوم غيره لا يكون إلا عن جهل، فكذا زوال القدرة عما هو مقدور الغير لن يكون إلا عن عجز ، فكان فيما قلتم تعجيز الله تعالى وذلك محال.
Then we proceed to invalidate that and say: The analogy of knowledge necessitates what we said, if you understood. For our knowledge relates to what is known by Allah, and it is not necessarily entailed by the relation of our knowledge to it that the knowledge of the Blessed and Exalted should cease; rather His knowledge remains related to it, and the known remains known to Him even if it becomes known to us. So likewise our power should relate to what is an object of power (maqdur) of Allah, and upon the relation of our power to it, it remains an object of power to Him, and His power is for invention, so it remains so. That a thing should be invented by two powers is impossible by consensus. Therefore the servant's power relates not to the aspect of invention by necessity. As for a thing being known by two knowledges, that is not impossible, so His knowledge and our knowledge relate to it. What supports what we said is that considering knowledge obligates what we hold: that informing someone who has no knowledge of it is impossible; rather, informing someone is only valid from one who has knowledge. So likewise, empowering someone over something that the empowered has no power over is impossible. Similarly, the removal of knowledge from what is known by another only occurs through ignorance; so too the removal of power from what is an object of power of another would only occur through incapacity. Thus what you said entails incapacitating Allah, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's inference from knowledge to power. The author first restates the correct analogy: just as our knowledge of something does not remove Allah's knowledge of it, so our power over something should not remove Allah's power over it. However, there is a crucial difference: a thing being invented by two powers is impossible by consensus, so the servant's power cannot be in the aspect of invention. In contrast, a thing being known by two knowledges is possible. Then the author strengthens his case by considering the nature of informing (iʿlām) and empowering (iqdār). Informing someone who has no prior knowledge is impossible; informing is only valid from one who knows. Similarly, empowering someone over something that the person has no power over is impossible. Moreover, removal of knowledge from what is known by another would be ignorance, and removal of power from what is another's object of power would be incapacity. Therefore, the opponent's position would imply that Allah is incapacitated (taʿjīz), which is impossible.

Key Terms

إعلام
إقدار
زوال العلم
زوال القدرة
عجز
تعجيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that human power competes with divine power and must eliminate it.
Correction: The author shows that divine power remains, except that invention cannot be shared; human power does not operate in the aspect of invention.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that a thing cannot be invented by two powers?
A) Because it would imply divine incapacity
B) Because it is impossible by consensus (ijmāʿ)
C) Because the servant has no true power
D) Because knowledge can be shared but power cannot

Key Takeaway
The analogy between knowledge and power fails because a thing cannot be invented by two powers, whereas it can be known by two knowledges.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponent's view implicitly incapacitates Allah. Reflect: In what areas of your life do you act as if Allah's power is limited by your efforts or decisions? What would it mean to submit to His exclusive power over what is truly originated?
?
If informing someone without prior knowledge is impossible, how does that challenge your approach when you try to guide others? Do you rely on your own 'knowledge' or do you seek from Allah before you speak? In what ways might you be trying to 'empower' others in matters beyond your scope?

Refutation of Determinism and Divine Incapacity Accusations
Author refutes the objection that his position leads to determinism or incapacitating God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi position avoids both determinism and the charge of incapacitating God by distinguishing between absolute and added invention.

Core Idea
Human power is over added invention, not absolute invention; thus no determinism or divine incapacity.

This passage directly responds to two common accusations against the Maturidi view: that it leads to fatalism or that it implies God is unable to act. The author uses a parallel argument from God's knowledge to clarify the nature of human power and then refutes the specific objection about compulsion. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Maturidi theory of human acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the overall argument flow. Note that the text breaks off at the end.
The passage has two main sections: the direct argument and the rejoinder to the objection.
2
Analyze the argument from knowledge to power
Identify the parallel: just as God cannot inform us of what contradicts His knowledge, He cannot empower us to do what contradicts His power. Then note the distinction between 'absolute invented' and 'invented added to Him'.
This establishes that human power is over added invention, not absolute invention.
3
Understand the refutation of the objection
The author presents the objection about compulsion and divine incapacity, then responds by reiterating that the servant does not invent but acquires, and that God's power is over absolute invention.
The response hinges on the proof that there is no invention from the servant's side.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : لما كان يستحيل أن يعلمنا الله تعالى خلاف معلومه يستحيل أن يقدرنا الله تعالى على خلاف مقدوره، ومقدوره مخترع يضاف إليه لا مطلق المخترع، فبقدرنا أيضا على مخترع مضاف إليه لا على مطلق المخترع، ولو جاز أن يقدرنا على مخترع غير مضاف إليه لجاز أن يعلمنا معلومًا غير مضاف إليه وذا باطل، فكذا هذا. وعلى هذا الوجه يبطل أيضًا قولهم إن ما قلتم يؤدي إلى جعل العباد مضطرين أو إلى تعجيز الله تعالى فإن الله تعالى إذا أراد أن يخلق كسب العبد إن كان للعبد قوة الامتناع فقد عجز الله تعالى عن تخليقه وإن لم يكن له قدرة الامتناع فهو إذًا مضطر فإنا نقول: ثبتنا بالدليل أن لا اختراع من قبل العبد وأن له فعلاً كان
Then we say to them: Since it is impossible for Allah to inform us contrary to His knowledge, it is impossible for Allah to empower us contrary to His maqdur. And His maqdur is an invented thing added to Him, not the absolute invented. So our power is also over an invented thing added to Him, not over the absolute invented. And if it were permissible for Him to empower us over an invented thing not added to Him, it would be permissible for Him to inform us of a known thing not added to Him, and that is false. So likewise this. And in this way also is invalidated their statement that 'what you say leads to making servants compelled (muḍṭarrīn) or to incapacitating Allah'. For if Allah wills to create the servant's acquisition, then if the servant has the power of resistance, then Allah has been incapacitated from creating it; and if he does not have the power of resistance, then he is compelled. So we say: We have established by proof that there is no invention from the side of the servant, and that he has an act...
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two accusations: first, that the author's view leads to determinism (jabr) or incapacitating God (taʿjīz). The author first presents a direct argument: just as God cannot inform us contrary to His knowledge, He cannot empower us contrary to His power. God's maqdur (object of power) is an invented thing added to Him, not absolute invention; thus our power is also over an invented thing added to Him, not absolute. If He could empower us over an absolute invented thing, He could inform us of a known thing not added to Him, which is false. Second, the author addresses the objection that if God creates the servant's acquisition, either the servant has power of resistance (then God is incapacitated) or not (then the servant is compelled). The author responds by stating that they have established by proof that there is no invention from the servant, and that the servant has an act (the text breaks off, but the argument presumably continues). The key points are that the servant's act is not invention but acquisition, and God's power is not diminished.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi position reduces human acts to pure determinism, making God unable to act freely.
Correction: The author shows that human power is over 'added invention' (acquisition), not absolute invention. God's power is over absolute invention. Thus, human acts are neither compelled (since they are acquired) nor does God's power become limited (since His maqdur is absolute invention).

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would be the result if God empowered us over an invented thing not added to Him?
A) It would imply that God can inform us of a known thing not added to Him
B) It would imply that the servant has independent creative power
C) It would imply that God is incapacitated
D) It would imply that the servant is compelled

Key Takeaway
God's power and human power operate on different orders of invention, so human acts are neither entirely compelled nor do they limit God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What step will you take today to consciously act as an acquirer—responsible yet dependent?

The author argues that just as God cannot inform you contrary to His knowledge, He cannot empower you contrary to His power. Permissibility of One Object Under Two Powers
Nasafi concludes permissibility of one object under two powers from prior proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses prior proofs to conclude that a single act can be under both divine and human power without contradiction.

Core Idea
Two powers can coexist on one act without conflict.

Nasafi has already established two key points: the servant cannot have creative power, yet the servant does have real power over his actions. In this passage, he draws the conclusion from these two points. He shows that one object can be within the scope of both God's power and the servant's power, resolving a major theological puzzle.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two prior proofs
Read the passage and note the two references: the proof of impossibility of creative power for the servant, and the proof of establishment of act and power for him.
These two proofs are the foundation for the conclusion.
2
Understand the conclusion
The passage says that from these, one knows the permissibility of one object under two powers. This means both God and the servant can be the agents of the same act in different respects.
3
Consider the sufficiency claim
Nasafi says this proof is sufficient and dispenses with others. Why might he say that? Think about how these two proofs together cover the key aspects.
This highlights the elegance of the argument.
4
Examine the restatement
The last sentence reiterates that empowering another over what the empowerer cannot do is impossible, reinforcing that creative power belongs solely to God.
This clarifies that the two powers are not identical in nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالوقوف على ما بينا من دليل استحالة ثبوت قدرة التخليق للعبد، ودليل ثبوت الفعل والقدرة له يعرف جواز دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين بحمد الله تعالى دليل فيه كفاية وغنية عن الاشتغال بغيره من الدلائل.

وذلك

ثم نقول: قد سبق منا القول في الفصل الذي أثبتنا فيه استحالة دخول الاختراع تحت قدرة العبد، وأن إقدار الغير على ما لا يقدر عليه المقدر محال،
And by pausing at what we have clarified from the proof of the impossibility of establishing creative power for the servant, and the proof of the establishment of act and power for him, one knows the permissibility of one object of power falling under the power of two powers. Praise be to God, this is a sufficient proof, free from the need to engage with other proofs.

And that.

Then we say: We have already stated in the chapter in which we established the impossibility of creation falling under the power of the servant, and that empowering another over what the empowerer has no power over is impossible.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage draws a conclusion from the previous proofs. By considering the proof of the impossibility of creative power (qudrat al-takhlīq) for the servant and the proof of the establishment of act and power for him, one can know the permissibility (jawāz) of one object of power (maqdūr wāḥid) falling under the power of two powers (qudrat qādirīn) — i.e., God and the servant. The author says this is sufficient proof (dalīl fīhi kifāya) and dispenses with other proofs. He then reiterates the earlier argument that empowering another over what the empowerer has no power over is impossible (muḥāl). This reinforces that while creation is exclusively God's, the servant's power over his acquired acts is real, and both powers relate to the same act without contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two powers cannot coexist on one object because they would conflict or because God's power is exclusive.
Correction: Nasafi shows that it is permissible for one object to be under two powers when one power is creative (God) and the other is acquired (servant); they are not identical in nature and do not conflict.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi conclude from the two proofs mentioned in this passage?
A) One object cannot be under two powers
B) One object can be under the power of both God and the servant
C) Only God has power over actions
D) The servant has creative power

Key Takeaway
Nasafi establishes that a single act can be under both divine and human power because creative power is exclusive to God while the servant's power is acquired, and both can coexist without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Two Agents on One Act: Objection and Refutation
Nasafi presents an objection and refutes it with a logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against two agents acting on one act and Nasafi's refutation affirming divine power to create equal agents whose agreement is possible.

Core Idea
Allah can create equal agents; their agreement on an act is possible, not impossible.

The objection claims that two equal agents cannot act on one act because it leads to impossible outcomes. Nasafi refutes this by affirming Allah's power to create equal persons and showing that their agreement is logically possible. Let's examine the objection and its refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its example
Read the passage carefully, identifying the objection that an act from two agents is impossible, illustrated with the example of two men and a middle part.
Notice the structure: first the general claim, then the example.
2
Identify the two impossible outcomes
The objection claims that if two equal men try to move to the same spot, either neither moves or both arrive simultaneously. Both are considered impossible.
These outcomes are presented as reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand Nasafi's answer
Nasafi responds that Allah can create two equally powerful persons, and their agreement on an act is not impossible. He warns against rejecting a possible scenario out of fear of impossibility.
The key point: possibility remains even if equality is assumed.
4
Reflect on the logical principle
Nasafi emphasizes that repelling the possible to avoid the impossible is as erroneous as affirming the impossible to avoid the correct.
This underscores the importance of careful rational judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعل من فاعلين، وإما انقسام حركة جزء لا يتجزأ وكلاهما محال، وما أدى إلى المحال كان محالاً في نفسه، ثم مثل هذا بجزء متوسط بين رجلين أحدهما عن يمينه والآخر عن يساره لا يجوز أن يستوي الرجلان في القوة والإرادة للتحرك إلى ذلك الجزء، لأنهما لو استويا ولا يكون أحدهما أولى ببلوغ مراده أدى إلى أحد أمرين كل واحد منهما محال فإنهما إما أن لا يبرحا من مكانهما من غير منع، وإما أن يوافيا ذلك الجزء جميعًا، وكل واحد من الأمرين فاسد، فكذا هذا.
والجواب عن هذا أن يقال : لا وجه لإنكار قدرة الله تعالى أن يخلق شخصين مستويي القدرة والبنية، ومن ضرورة ذلك أن يكون في قدرة كل واحد منهما مثل ما في قدرة الآخر ليقع الفرق بين القوتين المستويتين والقوتين المختلفتين، ثم لا شك أن اتفاقهما على فعل ليس بممتنع، وليس هذا الممكن يجعله محالاً أو من جعل ذلك المحال ممكنا لا مكان هذا، وهكذا هذا على كل من دفع موهومًا خوفا من وجوب المحال عليه؛ لأن دفع الممكن هربًا من المحال كتصحيح المحال هربًا من إحالة الصحيح.
An act from two agents, or the division of a movement of an indivisible part, both are impossible, and that which leads to the impossible is itself impossible. Then he gave an example of this with a middle part between two men, one on his right and the other on his left. It is not permissible for the two men to be equal in strength and will to move towards that part, because if they were equal and neither is more deserving of reaching his desire, it would lead to one of two things, each of which is impossible: either they do not move from their place without prevention, or they both arrive at that part together. Each of these two cases is invalid, and likewise this.
The answer to this is to say: There is no ground for denying the power of Allah to create two persons equal in power and constitution. From that it necessarily follows that the power of each one of them is like the power of the other, so that the distinction between equal powers and different powers occurs. Then there is no doubt that their agreement upon an act is not impossible. This possible thing does not make it impossible, nor does making that impossible thing possible because of this possibility. And likewise this applies to anyone who repels a putative impossibility out of fear of the necessary occurrence of the impossible upon him; because repelling the possible to flee from the impossible is like affirming the impossible to flee from the impossibility of the correct.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the possibility of two equal agents acting on one act. The objection uses the example of a middle part between two men of equal strength and will: if they both try to move to that part, either neither moves or they both arrive together, both impossible. Therefore, the opponent argues, two agents cannot act on one act. Nasafi answers by affirming that Allah can create two equal persons with equal power. Their agreement on an act is not impossible; the opponent mistakenly treats a possible case as impossible. Nasafi further notes that repelling a possible scenario to avoid an imagined impossibility is as erroneous as accepting an impossibility to avoid a correct scenario.

Key Terms
فعل
فاعل
جزء لا يتجزأ
قوة
إرادة
محال
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two agents are equal in power and will, they cannot act on one act because it leads to logical impossibility.
Correction: Equality of agents does not preclude agreement; their acting together is possible and not contradictory.

Checkpoint
What are the two impossible outcomes that the objection claims would result from two equal men moving towards the same middle part?
A) They both move backwards or both move forwards.
B) They both stay in place without moving, or they both arrive at the part together.
C) One moves and the other stays still, or they collide.
D) They both move to the left or both to the right.

Key Takeaway
Agreement between equal agents is possible and does not violate rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where two seemingly equal possibilities seem to lead to a dead end, do you trust that Allah can still bring about a good outcome? How does Nasafi's argument against assuming impossibility challenge your own tendency to dismiss possibilities based on limited human reasoning?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth about Allah's power because it seemed to lead to a logical contradiction in your own mind? After reading this passage, are you willing to accept that what you thought was impossible may actually be possible for Allah? What would change in your life if you truly believed that?
?
Think of a conflict in your relationships where you assumed that two people with equal claims could not find a solution. How does Nasafi's point about agreement being possible reshape your approach to such situations? Will you now look for ways to make agreement happen rather than assuming it's impossible?

Two Equal Wills Are Possible
Nasafi asserts rational consensus and possibility of two equal wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational consensus supports the possibility of two equal wills and why the opponent's objection is invalid.

Core Idea
Separate loci make concurrent wills possible.

Nasafi continues his refutation by reminding the opponent that rational beings agree on the possibility of two equal wills. He argues that no evidence in the act shows impossibility; instead, the act itself indicates possibility. The opponent's mistake is treating a possible as impossible, which is heinous ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Nasafi begins with a strong assertion of rational consensus.
The passage is dense; focus on the key claim.
2
Identify the opponent's error
Nasafi says the opponent made a possible thing impossible. Identify what the opponent claims is impossible and why Nasafi says it's possible.
The opponent accepts Allah can create equal men but denies they can both will to move the same body.
3
Reflect on the accusation
Nasafi accuses the opponent of 'heinous ignorance'. Consider why this is a strong charge—because the opponent contradicts rational consensus and clear evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: لا نزاع لأحد من العقلاء في كون ثبوت الرجلين المستويين في القوة واتفاقهما على الإرادة ممكنًا، ولا دليل في الفعل يدل على إحالته بل فيه دليل إمكانه، وكون فعل واحد لفاعلين وتعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد مما للعقلاء فيه كلام، فهلا علمت بإفضاء ذلك الممكن إليه أنه ممكن حتى خالفت العقل والعقلاء، وجعلت الممكن ممتنعا، وهذا جهل فاحش.
ثم نقول له: إنك سلمت أن تخليق الله رجلين مستويين في القوة والبنية من جملة الممكنات، وإنما جعلت المستحيل وجود إرادتهما لتحريك ذلك الجسم ويجوز وجود إرادة كل واحد منهما لتحريك الجسم والاعتماد عليه للتحريك على الانفراد فبعد ذلك نقول: إذا وجدت من أحدهما إرادة تحريك الحجر لماذا امتنع وجود إرادة تحريك ذلك الآخر، ولا مضادة بين الإرادتين والفعلين لتفرق محليهما، ولا فساد لمحل قدرة أحدهما عند ورود إرادة الآخر، فإن تعلق بالمثل الذي ضربه قلنا هناك: إنما استحال وجود إرادة الاثنين لأن إرادتهما للتحرك إلى الجزء المتوسط لا تقع إلا مع حكمها وهو التحرك، ولما استحال شغلهما ذلك الجزء المتوسط استحال وجود ما لا يقع إلا مع حركتيهما وهو الإرادة ولا استحالة في وجود الاعتماد من الاثنين على ذلك الجسم وتحريكهما إياه، فإن نوزعنا في كون إرادة التحرك التحرك مع نثبت ذلك بالدليل على أنا بنينا ذلك على أصلنا لدفع الإلزام للإلزام على الخصم وهو مستقيم، ولأن العلم باستحالة شغل اثنين جزءًا واحدًا من الممكن ضروري والعلم باستحالة دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين ليس بضروري لو كان لكان استدلاليًا وبإفضائه إلى جعل ما هو ممكن ممتنعا علم بطلان الاستدلال، وفيما استشهد به الأمر بخلافه، لأن ما علم ضرورة لا يتصور خطأه وبطلانه، فلا يمكن جعل أحدهما نظير للآخر، والله الموفق.
Then we say to them: There is no dispute among rational beings regarding the possibility of the existence of two men equal in strength and their agreement upon will, and there is no evidence in the act indicating its impossibility, rather it contains evidence of its possibility. And the fact of one act from two agents and the relation of two powers to one object of power is something about which rational beings have discussion. So why did you not realize that this possible leads to that it is possible, so that you opposed reason and rational beings and made the possible impossible? This is a heinous ignorance.
Then we say to him: You have conceded that Allah's creation of two men equal in strength and constitution is among the possibilities, and you only considered impossible the existence of their will to move that body, while the existence of the will of each one of them to move the body and rely upon it for moving individually is permissible. Then after that we say: If from one of them the will to move the stone occurs, why does the existence of the will to move from that other become impossible? There is no opposition between the two wills and the two acts due to the separation of their loci, and there is no corruption of the locus of the power of one when the will of the other occurs. If he adheres to the example he gave, we say there: The existence of the will of both is impossible only because their will to move towards the middle part does not occur except with its effect, namely movement. And since their occupying that middle part is impossible, the existence of that which does not occur except with their two movements, namely will, becomes impossible. But there is no impossibility in the existence of reliance from both upon that body and their moving it. If we are disputed regarding the point that the will to move is [identical with] movement, we will prove that by evidence, since we built that upon our principle to repel the objection against the opponent, and it is correct. And because the knowledge of the impossibility of two [persons] occupying one part of the possible is necessary, and the knowledge of the impossibility of one object of power entering under the power of two agents is not necessary; if it were, it would be inferential, and by its leading to making what is possible impossible, the invalidity of the inference is known. And in what he cited as evidence, the matter is the opposite, because what is known necessarily cannot be conceived as erroneous and false, so one cannot be made analogous to the other. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the refutation. Nasafi first asserts that rational beings agree on the possibility of two equal men and their agreement on will. He then accuses the opponent of ignorance for denying this. He addresses an opponent who conceded that Allah can create equal men but considered it impossible for both to will moving the same body. Nasafi argues that if one wills to move a stone, why would the other's will be impossible? There is no opposition because their wills and acts are separate loci. He then distinguishes the earlier example of the middle part: in that case, impossibility arises because both cannot occupy the same point simultaneously, but in general, two agents can both rely on and move the same body. He also discusses necessary vs. inferential knowledge: the impossibility of two occupying one part is known necessarily, but the impossibility of one object under two powers is not necessarily known, and the opponent's attempt to analogize fails.

Key Terms
عقلاء
مقدر
تعلق قدرتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two equal wills cannot coexist because they would cause conflict.
Correction: Rational consensus and the lack of evidence for impossibility support their coexistence, especially when loci are separate.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi call the opponent's view 'heinous ignorance'?
A) Because the opponent denied a matter of rational consensus.
B) Because the opponent used weak evidence.
C) Because the opponent rejected Allah's power.
D) Because the opponent claimed two equal men cannot exist.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi grounds his refutation in rational consensus and the absence of evidence for impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you confidently declared something impossible in your life—whether in your desires, your faith, or your relationships—simply because you could not see how it could happen? How does Nasafi's charge of 'heinous ignorance' challenge you to reconsider that decision today?
?
Think of a time you opposed the consensus of rational people out of stubbornness or fear. What evidence were you ignoring that might have shown you the possibility? Will you seek that evidence now, even if it means admitting you were wrong?
?
Nasafi says the act itself contains evidence of its possibility. Look at a situation in your life where you have been waiting for God's help but doubting that He can. What evidence of His power is already present in your circumstances that you have been overlooking? Commit to one specific way you will act on that evidence this week.

The Middle Part Exception and Necessary Knowledge
Nasafi distinguishes the middle part case and invokes necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the middle part example is a special case and why necessary knowledge prevents analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Necessary knowledge blocks false analogy.

In this part, Nasafi addresses the opponent's concession and refines the argument. He distinguishes between the general case and the specific example of the middle part, where will is inseparably linked to movement. He then contrasts necessary knowledge (impossibility of two occupying a single point) with inferential knowledge (impossibility of one object under two powers), showing the opponent's analogy fails.

Guided Path
1
Follow the concession and distinction
The opponent concedes equal men are possible but denies their simultaneous will. Nasafi first argues based on separate loci, then addresses the middle part example.
Notice the shift to the middle part example: a specific case where both cannot occupy the same point.
2
Understand the necessary vs. inferential knowledge
Nasafi says the impossibility of two occupying one part is known necessarily (everyone knows it), but the impossibility of one object under two powers is not necessarily known—it is inferential and thus open to error.
This epistemological distinction is key to why the analogy fails.
3
See the conclusion
The opponent's attempt to analogize the two cases is invalid because what is known necessarily cannot be compared to what is inferential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponent made a false analogy—have you ever rejected a truth because it resembled a scenario you dismissed? How will you train yourself to compare only things that are truly alike in essence?
?
Your faith in Allah's oneness—is it a necessary truth for you, or have you only accepted it because it's familiar? If it were challenged, would your certainty crumble like inferential knowledge? What are you doing to make it as firm as the impossibility of two bodies in one space?

Objection: Two Men Moving an Indivisible Part
Nasafi presents an objection about two men moving a board with an indivisible part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of an objection from kalām against the principle that a single effect cannot be produced by two equally direct causes.

Core Idea
Two equal causes challenge the principle of one effect from one agent.

In this passage, Nasafi presents a clever objection from his opponents. They imagine a scenario where two identical men grasp a board carrying an indivisible particle, and together they move the board, displacing the particle. Since both men are equal in every respect, there is no reason to attribute the particle's motion to one rather than the other. This forces a deeper examination of causality and agency in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is constructed with a concrete scenario.
2
Identify the key premise
Identify the premise that the two men are equal in all characteristics and that the part moves with the board as a single unit.
These lead to the conclusion that the movement must be from both.
3
Consider the theological stakes
Why does this objection matter? It challenges the Maturidī view that a single act cannot have two equally direct agents. Think about how a Maturidī might respond.
The response would likely distinguish between direct and indirect causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزموه أيضا أن جزءا لا يتجزأ لو وضع على لوح وقبض على طرفي اللوح رجلان فحركاه فأزعج الجزء وكان الجزء مع أجزاء اللوح فردا لابد أن حركة الجزء كانت بينهما لانعدام دليل ترجيح أحد الرجلين لاستوائهما في المعاني كلها.
And they also required him [to answer] that if an indivisible part is placed on a board and two men grasp the ends of the board and move it, causing the part to be displaced, and the part together with the parts of the board is a single unit, necessarily the movement of the part would be between them due to the absence of evidence for preferring one of the two men because of their equality in all characteristics.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents an additional objection from the opponents. The scenario involves an indivisible atom placed on a board. Two men, equal in all respects, grasp the ends of the board and move it, thereby displacing the atom. Since the atom moves together with the board's parts as a single unit, its motion must be attributed to both men equally, because there is no reason to prefer one over the other. This challenges the Maturidi view that a single effect cannot have two equally direct causes. The objection aims to force the admission that one object of power (the atom's movement) can be produced by two agents. The passage only states the objection; the response would be given in subsequent text.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
لوح
ترجيح
استواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection proves that two agents can directly cause a single effect.
Correction: The Maturidī response would argue that the men directly cause the board's motion, and the atom's motion is a secondary effect caused by the board, not directly by the men. Thus, the effect (atom's motion) has only one direct agent (the board), preserving the principle.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the objection's claim that the movement of the indivisible part must be attributed to both men?
A) The men are both strong enough to move the board.
B) The part moves with the board as a unit, and the men are equal in all respects with no reason to prefer one.
C) The part is indivisible, so it cannot be moved by only one man.
D) The board has two ends, so both men must be involved.

Key Takeaway
This objection demonstrates how concrete thought experiments were used in kalām to test causal principles, particularly the uniqueness of agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe causes and effects in your daily life—like your own hands performing an action—do you instinctively stop at the immediate cause, or do you truly see God's agency behind every movement, including the movement of your own limbs?
?
This objection pushes the Maturidī to distinguish between direct and indirect causation. In your own faith, are there situations where you attribute a good outcome to your own effort and a difficult one to God's will, despite knowing that both come from Him alone?
?
If you were faced with a situation where two equally capable people (or factors) seem to produce a single result, do you feel a tension in acknowledging only one ultimate Cause (God) for all that occurs? How would you resolve that tension in your heart, not just in your intellect?

Objection and Reply on Two Equal Agents
Objection and refutation using indivisible atom analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dialectical objection about two equal agents is refuted by showing its implication would limit God's creative power.

Core Idea
If equality prevents joint action, then God cannot create equals – a self-defeating objection.

This passage presents an opponent's argument that two equal agents cannot jointly cause a single effect, using the analogy of an indivisible atom moved by two men. The author then turns the argument around: if that were impossible, then God's creating two identical persons would also be impossible – a conclusion the opponent would not accept.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection scenario
Read the description of the indivisible atom on a board moved by two equal men. Notice how the opponent claims the movement cannot be attributed to either man due to lack of preponderance.
2
Understand the opponent's reasoning
The opponent argues that because the two men are equal in all attributes, there is no basis to say one caused the movement, so joint causation is impossible. This leads to absurdity if both are considered agents.
The opponent uses this to challenge the coherence of multiple divine agents.
3
Analyze the author's counter-argument
The author responds by applying the same logic to God: if equal agents cannot produce an effect, then God cannot create two equal persons. Since the opponent accepts that God can create equals, their objection fails.
This is a classic dialectical move (reductio ad absurdum).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزموه أيضا أن جزءًا لا يتجزأ لو وضع على لوح وقبض على طرفي اللوح رجلان فحركاه فأزعج الجزء وكان الجزء مع أجزاء اللوح فردًا لابد أن حركة الجزء كانت بينهما لانعدام دليل ترجيح أحد الرجلين لاستوائهما في المعاني كلها. ثم يقال له: إذا قلت يستحيل أن يتفق الاثنان على التحريك عند استوائهما في المعاني لأنه يؤدي إلى محال فقل يستحيل أن يخلق الله شخصين مستويي القدرة والبنية لأنه يؤدي إلى محال إذ لا فرق بينهما.
And they also imposed upon him that an indivisible atom if placed on a board and two men grasped the ends of the board and moved it, dislodging the atom, and the atom together with the parts of the board is a single unit, then necessarily the movement of the atom was between them due to the absence of evidence for preferring either of the two men because of their equality in all attributes. Then it is said to him: When you say it is impossible for the two to agree on moving when they are equal in attributes because it leads to absurdity, then say it is impossible for God to create two persons equal in power and constitution because it leads to absurdity, since there is no difference between them.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer in a dialectical context. The opponent argues: If two men of equal strength grasp a board with an indivisible atom on it and both move it, the atom's movement cannot be attributed to one rather than the other due to lack of preponderance, so it must be from both. But if both are equal, they cannot both be the agent (implying impossibility). The author then turns the objection back: If the opponent claims that equal agents cannot produce a single act, then by the same reasoning God cannot create two equal persons (since that would lead to absurdity). Thus the objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
لوح
تحريك
استواء
ترجيح
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equality of agents necessarily makes joint action impossible, showing multiple agents can't coexist.
Correction: The author shows that this reasoning would also restrict God's power to create equal beings, which is accepted as possible, so the initial claim is not universally valid.

Checkpoint
What is the author's counter-argument to the opponent's objection about the atom?
A) The atom is divisible so the objection fails.
B) The opponent's reasoning would also imply God cannot create two equal persons, which they accept as possible.
C) The two men can move the atom by cooperating voluntarily.
D) The atom's movement is caused by God, not the men.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of joint causation from equal agents is not a universal principle, as it would also limit divine omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a logical argument that seemed to limit Allah's power, only to realize that you were accepting a similar argument in another area? Where in your own thinking might you be applying standards to Allah that you wouldn't apply to yourself?
?
This passage shows that human analogies about causation can inadvertently restrict our understanding of Allah's omnipotence. In what specific area of your life—health, provision, guidance—are you assuming that Allah is bound by the same limitations you see in created beings? Repent of that assumption now.
?
The opponent's error was starting with a physical scenario and then applying it to Allah without realizing the contradiction. Reflect: In what current struggle are you reasoning from your own limited experience rather than from what Allah has revealed about His perfect power? Commit to correcting that today.

Answer to the Objection on Creating Theft and Punishment
Nasafi answers the objection about God creating theft and then punishing for it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the objection that God cannot create an act and then punish the human for it.

Core Idea
The objector's own logic leads to absurdity—if humans do nothing, punishment is baseless.

The objection argues that if God creates all actions, including theft, then punishing the thief is unjust because the human did not create the act. Nasafi takes this premise and turns it back: if the human did not create the act, then what exactly did he do that warrants punishment? The objection collapses under its own weight.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where Nasafi presents the objector's question. Note the assumption that God creates the theft in the hand.
2
Identify the refutation
The second part of the passage contains Nasafi's response. He uses a conditional: if the human did nothing, then what did he do? Trace the logic.
3
Understand the key concepts
Notice the terms: 'thingness', 'accidentness', 'essenceness'. These refer to the ontological status of actions. The slave's power (qudra) does not affect these aspects.
4
See the final twist
The passage ends: 'Whatever they answer is an answer for them.' That means any defense the objector gives applies equally to their own position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الجواب عن قولهم: كيف يجوز أن يقال يخلق الله تعالى في يد إنسان سرقه ثم يأمر بقطعها؟ وكذا هذا في الزنا وكذا يخلق فعلاً ثم يعاقب عليه؟ قيل: وإذا لم يفعل السارق شيئاً ولا الزاني ولا الكافر، وكان الزنا والسرقة والكفر في العدم أشياء وأعراضاً وأعيانا ووجودها أعيانها أو ليس معنى وراءها ولم تتعلق قدرة العبد بالشيئية ولا العرضية ولا العينية، ولم تتعلق بمعنى وراءها فماذا فعله، وعلى أي فعل يقطع ويحد ويعاقب؟ فما أجابوا فهو جواب لهم.
And likewise the answer to their statement: 'How is it permissible to say that Allah Most High creates in the hand of a person a theft, then commands that it be cut off?' And likewise this regarding adultery, and likewise He creates an act then punishes for it? It is said: 'And if the thief did nothing, nor the adulterer nor the disbeliever, and adultery, theft, and disbelief in non-existence are things, accidents, and essences, and their existence is their essences or not a meaning behind them, and the slave's power did not pertain to 'thingness' nor 'accidentness' nor 'essenceness', nor to a meaning behind them, then what did he do, and for which act is he cut, punished, and penalized? Whatever they answer is an answer for them.'
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage addresses a classic theological objection: how can God create the act of theft in a person's hand and then punish that person by cutting off the hand? Nasafi turns the argument back. He says: if the thief did not create the act (since God creates all things), then what exactly did he do? The act as a thing, accident, and essence exists in non-existence before creation. The slave's power (قدرة العبد) does not affect the 'thingness' or 'essence' of the act. So if the human did not produce anything, on what basis is he punished? The opponent's own assumptions lead to an absurdity, and whatever they answer applies equally to their own position.

Key Terms
السرقة
الزنا
قدرة العبد
الشيئية
العرضية
العينية
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God creating an act means the human is not responsible, so punishment is unjust.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if no human role exists, punishment has no basis, but the objector's own position leads to the same problem.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the thief's power not pertain to?
A) The act's existence.
B) The act's thingness, accidentness, and essenceness.
C) The act's creation.
D) The act's moral value.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the objection by showing that if humans have no causal role, punishment is baseless, but the objector cannot explain human action either.

?

?
If you truly believed that your choices are real and that God holds you accountable for them, what would change about how you approach your daily habits? Will you commit to one specific change today?
?
The objector tried to escape accountability by blaming God. Where in your life have you subtly tried to shift blame away from yourself? What will you do this week to confront that excuse with the truth that you are responsible for your actions?

Answering the Objection on Punishment and Existence
Nasafi rebuts the objection about existence and punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi rebuts the objection that punishment is unjust if only God creates existence, and why his response exposes the weakness of the opponent's position.

Core Idea
The opponent's own logic can be turned against them.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a tricky objection from those who argue that if all existence comes from God, then punishment for human action is unfair. He responds with a clever counter-question and then explains that the real issue is the denial of human agency. He frames his answers as a 'favor' (تبرع) to reveal the opponent's inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent asks whether punishment is for the existence of the Creator or the existence of the creature. Understand the logic behind this question.
The opponent assumes that only God creates existence, so human action doesn't truly exist.
2
Examine Nasafi's counter-question
Nasafi responds with a parallel question: if nothing exists except a thing that no one did, is one punished for that thing? This mirrors the opponent's logic to show its absurdity.
The counter-question forces the opponent to apply their own reasoning consistently.
3
Understand the 'favor' (تبرع) and the proof
Nasafi steps back and calls these answers a 'favor'. He asserts that the real proof is that the slave has an act, even if not a creator, and is punished for that act. The answers are given to expose the weakness of the opponent's speech.
The 'favor' does not mean the arguments are optional; they are strategic.
4
Reflect on the final verdict
Nasafi concludes that the opponent's objections only apply to themselves—they are the ones truly condemned. This shows that the opponent's position is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الجواب عن قولهم: إذا لم يكن وجود إلا للخالق ولمخلوقه أفيعاقب على وجود الخالق أم على وجود المخلوق؟ قيل: وإذا لم يوجد إلا شيء لم يفعله أحد أفيعاقب على الشيء؟ فما أجابوا فهو جواب لهم، وهذا كله تبرع منا فإنا إذا أقمنا الدلالة على أن العبد وإن لم يكن مخترعا خالقا فله فعل وإن كان لا يقع كيفية ذلك في أوهامنا، فنقول: بأنه يعاقب على فعله، وكل هذه الأسئلة طلب لجعل فعلنا موهوماً وهو فاسد، فلا ينبغي أن نصغي إليها غير أنا بينا هذه الأجوبة على طريق التبرع ليتبين بذلك وهاء كلامهم وأنهم لا يلزمون إلا ما يلزمهم مثله ولا يعيبون خصومهم إلا بما هم معيبون به بل هم المعيبون دون خصومهم على ما قررنا والله الموفق.
And likewise the answer to their statement: 'If there is no existence except from the Creator and His creation, is he punished for the existence of the Creator or for the existence of the creature?' It is said: 'And if nothing exists except a thing that no one did, is he punished for the thing? Whatever they answer is an answer for them.' And all of this is a favor from us, for if we establish the proof that the slave—even if he is not an originator, a creator—has an act, even if the modality of that does not occur in our imaginations, we say: he is punished for his act. And all these questions are a demand to make our act imaginary, and that is invalid. So we should not listen to them, except that we have given these answers by way of favor, so that by that the weakness of their speech is made clear, and that they do not necessitate except that which equally necessitates them, and they do not condemn their opponents except by that with which they themselves are condemned; rather they are the condemned, not their opponents, according to what we have established. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and a lengthy methodological note. The Batiniyya ask: if existence only comes from God and His creation (meaning: from God alone in their view?), then is the punishment on God's existence or the creature's? Nasafi answers: if nothing exists except a thing that no one created, then punishment on that thing would be senseless. He then steps back and explains that these answers are given as a favor (تبرع) to show the weakness of their arguments. The key positive point is that the slave has an act, even though he is not an originator (مخترع) or creator (خالق). We may not understand the 'how' (كيفية) of that act, but it is real. The Batiniyya try to make human action imaginary (موهوم), but this is invalid. The author concludes that their objections only apply to themselves—they are the ones who are truly condemned.

Key Terms
الوجود
الخالق
المخلوق
الاختراع
التبرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nasafi's answers are just a favor, not real arguments, so they can be dismissed.
Correction: The 'favor' refers to the additional counter-question; the core proof of human action is real and sufficient. The favor is given to reveal the opponent's inconsistency.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi call these answers a 'favor' (تبرع)?
A) Because they are not needed since the proof of human action is sufficient.
B) Because they are given to show the weakness of the opponent's speech.
C) Because they are optional for the Muslims.
D) Because they are borrowed from other schools.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument about existence and punishment is self-defeating; the real basis for punishment is the human act, even if its modality is beyond our complete understanding.

?
