?
The opponent's question tries to make human action seem imaginary. In what areas of your life do you treat your own decisions as illusions—perhaps by saying 'I had no choice' or 'it was meant to be'? What would change if you fully embraced that your acts are truly yours, accountable before God, even when you don't understand how they coexist with His power? Are you willing to take that responsibility?
?
Nasafi shows that the opponent's arguments apply equally to themselves. In your own discussions about faith with others (or even with yourself), are you sometimes guilty of using arguments that could be turned against you? How can you ensure that your reasoning is consistent and sincere, not just a tool to win a debate? Would you be comfortable if God held you to the same standard you impose on others?

Refuting the Division of the Act into Parts
Nasafi refutes the partition of the act into parts from God and parts from the slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi argument refutes the objection that human acts must be divided into parts from God and parts from the slave.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be divided; the act is an accident, not a body.

The Mu'tazilite objection suggests that if a human act is not entirely from God nor entirely from the slave, it must be shared. Nasafi responds by pointing out that this division only applies to divisible bodies, not to accidents like acts. He then further argues that blame attaches to the act, not to God's origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Nasafi's answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the objection is stated first, then Nasafi's response begins with 'فقال لهم'.
2
Understand why the division fails
Nasafi says the division is only valid for bodies, which are divisible, not for accidents. Ask yourself: why is an act considered an accident in kalam?
Accidents are non-enduring attributes; they are simple and indivisible.
3
Follow the blame argument
Nasafi then asks about blame: if God's act (the created act) is blamed, how can God be blamed for His creation? He distinguishes between the slave's act and God's origination.
This is a further refutation shifting the ground.
4
Consider the reference to Abu al-Hudhayl
Nasafi brings up Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Murdar to show that their view leads to contradiction: if a stone's movement is the act of two movers, one commanded and one forbidden, the same movement would be both obedience and disobedience.
5
Summarize the refutation
In your own words, explain why Nasafi's argument is effective against the Mu'tazilite objection. Focus on the indivisibility of accidents and the distinction between act and origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن فعل العبد لا يخلو إما أن يكون كله من الله تعالى فيذم هو عليه، وإما أن يكون كله من العبد وهو ما قلنا، وإما أن يكون بعضه من الله تعالى وبعضه من العبد فيشتركان في الذم. فيقال لهم: لو عقلتم لما اشتغلتم بهذا التقسيم إذ هذا مما يصح في الأجسام لما تبعض وتتجزأ دون الأعراض التي يستحيل عليها التبعض والتجزؤ لانعدام تألفها وتركبها، وما لا بعض له لا كل له إذ هما من الأسماء الإضافية والتقسيم إلى الكل والبعض، فما يستحيل عليه البعض والكل جهل. ثم نقول لهم هل مفعول الله تعالى وهو فعله فيذم على فعله كما هو عندكم شيء وذات وعين وموجود ويذم على وجوده دون شيئيته وذاتيته وعينيته، على أنا إذا أثبتنا بالدليل أن له فعلاً ولا اختراع له فيذم هو على فعله ولا يذم الله تعالى على اختراعه لما نبين بعد هذا إن شاء الله تعالى كما يذم عندكم على وجود فعله دون شيئيته على أن هذا يلزم أبا الهذيل وأبا موسى المردار حيث جعلا حركة الحجر فعلاً للمحركين، ولو كان أحدهما مأموراً بالتحريك والآخر منهياً عنه فتكون الحركة في ذاتها شيئاً واحداً وهي طاعة ومعصية يحمد المطيع
And with this they are answered regarding their statement: 'The act of the slave is not free from either being entirely from Allah Most High, so He would be blamed for it, or entirely from the slave, which is what we say, or part from Allah and part from the slave, so they share in blame.' It is said to them: 'If you had understood, you would not have concerned yourselves with this division, for this is valid only for bodies which are divisible and partitionable, not for accidents, which are impossible to divide and partition due to the lack of their composition and combination. And what has no part has no whole, for they are relational terms, and the division into whole and part—so what is impossible to have part and whole is ignorance.' Then we say to them: 'Is the effect (maf'ul) of Allah Most High—which is His act—blamed for His act as it is according to you: a thing, essence, substance, and existent, and blamed for its existence without its thingness, essence-ness, and substance-ness? While we have established by proof that he (the slave) has an act and no origination, so he is blamed for his act and Allah Most High is not blamed for His origination, as we will explain after this, God willing. Just as you blame for the existence of His act without its thingness, and this applies to Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Musa al-Murdar, who made the movement of a stone an act of the movers, and if one of them was commanded to move and the other was forbidden, then the movement in itself is one thing, and it is obedience and disobedience—the obedient one is praised...'
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection based on a tripartite division of human acts: entirely from God, entirely from the slave, or shared. Nasafi argues that such division only applies to bodies (أجسام), which are divisible, not to accidents (أعراض), which are simple and indivisible. To speak of 'parts' of an accident is ignorance (جهل). He then turns to a further argument: if God's act (as an effect) is blamed, how can God be blamed for His creation? The slave has an act but not origination (اختراع), so he is blamed for his act, not God. He references Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Murdar who considered a stone's movement to be the act of the movers, leading to contradictions.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
التقسيم
الأجسام
الأعراض
الكل والبعض
أبو الهذيل
أبو موسى المردار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human acts are part from God and part from the slave, so both are blameworthy.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the act is an accident and cannot be divided; the division only applies to bodies. Moreover, blame attaches to the slave's acquisition of the act, not to God's creation of it.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject the division of the act into parts?
A) Because accidents are indivisible.
B) Because the act is too complex.
C) Because the division is not found in the Quran.
D) Because it implies dualism.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the division of the act by showing it applies only to divisible bodies, not to accidents, and by distinguishing the slave's act from God's origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever mentally apportion blame between yourself and God's creation of that moment? How does Nasafi's insistence that your act is a simple, indivisible accident force you to own it entirely? Will you, right now, repent without shifting even a sliver of responsibility?
?
Think of an area of your life where you feel stuck in a cycle of disobedience. Do you treat that sin as a complex composition of circumstances, making it easier to excuse? What would change if you saw it as an indivisible accident—a single, whole choice you make—and took full ownership of it today?
?
Have you ever used sophisticated theological reasoning to avoid confronting a personal failing? This passage exposes that as ignorance (جهل). Will you put aside intellectual defenses and simply face your Lord with the truth of your own act, asking for His forgiveness and help?

Dual Attribution and the Disbeliever's Act
Dual attribution principle and its application to the disbeliever's act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle that a single act can be simultaneously attributed to Allah as creator and to the human as acquirer, and how this resolves objections about the disbeliever's disbelief being his own act.

Core Idea
A single act can be both Allah's creation and the human's responsibility.

This passage presents a foundational Māturīdī principle: actions have two simultaneous attributions—divine creation and human acquisition—without contradiction. The author applies this to a classic objection: if disbelief is created by Allah, how can the disbeliever be blamed? By distinguishing between the act as created (مفعول) and as acquired (فعل), the Māturīdī position preserves both divine omnipotence and human moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dual attribution principle
The author uses the example of movement caused by wind to show that an action can be both an act of Allah (as creation) and an act of a creature (as acquisition). Notice that the act is not divided; it is one act with two attributions.
This principle is central to Māturīdī kalam.
2
Apply to the disbeliever's act
The author agrees that disbelief is the disbeliever's act, but clarifies it is not Allah's act—it is Allah's creation (مفعوله). This maintains that the disbeliever is responsible for his disbelief.
The distinction between 'act' (فعل) and 'created thing' (مفعول) is crucial.
3
Contrast with opposing views
The Najjārīyya and some Ahl al-Ḥadīth define the disbeliever as one in whom disbelief inheres, not one who performs it. They argue by analogy with inherent qualities like movement, death, colors.
This view removes moral responsibility from the human.
4
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum
The author turns the opponents' analogy against them: if inherent qualities are acts of Allah, then Allah would be described by them (e.g., black, hot, dead), which is blasphemous. Thus the interlocutor must either accept absurdity or admit contradiction.
This is a powerful refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهما عليها ويثاب ويذم العاصي منهما عليها ويعاقب، وكذا لو كان المحركين الريح كانت الحركة في نفسها فعلاً الله تعالى ويحمد عليه وفعلاً للمحرك المنهى عنه ومعصية منه يذم عليها ويعاقب، وإن لم تكن هي متجزئة في نفسها، والله الموفق. وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن الكافر من فعله الكفر. قلنا: نحن نسلم هذا، ولكن عندنا الكفر فعل الكافر لا فعل الله تعالى وإنما هو مفعوله، ومن سلم لكم أن الكفر فعل الله تعالى من النجارية ومتكلمي أهل الحديث فإنهم يمنعون أن يكون الكافر من كان الكفر فعله ويقولون : الكافر من كان الكفر فعله، ويقولون: من قام به الكفر لا من فعل الكفر كما أن المتحرك من قامت به الحركة لا من فعل الحركة والميت من قام به الموت لا من فعل الموت، وكذا هذا في السواد والبياض والحرارة والبرودة والحلاوة والحموضة والطول والقصر، فإما ما تزعمون أن الميت المريض الطويل القصير الأسود الأبيض الحار البارد الحلو الحامض هو الله تعالى لكون هذه المعاني فعلاً له فتنسلخون عن الدين، وإما أن تقروا بتناقض مذهبكم وبطلان شبهتكم.
...upon it and the obedient one is rewarded and the disobedient one is blamed upon it and punished, and likewise if the mover were the wind, the movement in itself would be an act of Allah the Exalted and He is praised upon it, and an act of the mover prohibited and a disobedience of his for which he is blamed and punished, even if it is not divisible in itself, and Allah grants success. And with this they answer their statement: 'Indeed the disbeliever is one whose disbelief is his act.' We say: We accept this, but according to us disbelief is the act of the disbeliever, not the act of Allah the Exalted, rather it is His created thing. And whoever concedes to you that disbelief is the act of Allah the Exalted—from the Najjārīyya and the theologians of Ahl al-Ḥadīth—they deny that the disbeliever is one whose disbelief is his act, and they say: 'The disbeliever is one in whom disbelief inheres,' and they say: '[He is] one in whom disbelief inheres, not one who does the act of disbelief, just as the moving one is one in whom movement inheres, not one who does the act of movement, and the dead one is one in whom death inheres, not one who does the act of death.' And likewise this applies to blackness, whiteness, heat, cold, sweetness, sourness, length, and shortness. Then either you claim that the dead, the sick, the long, the short, the black, the white, the hot, the cold, the sweet, the sour is Allah the Exalted because these meanings are His act—thus you strip yourselves of religion—or you admit the contradiction of your doctrine and the invalidity of your argument.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents a key Māturīdī principle: a single action can have two simultaneous attributions—as a divine creation (act of Allah) and as a human acquisition (act of the creature)—without implying any division in the act itself. The author uses the example of a movement caused by the wind: the movement as a created thing is an act of Allah and is praised, but as the movement of a creature commanded or forbidden, it is an act of that creature and may be blameworthy. This principle is then applied to answer the objection: 'The disbeliever is one whose disbelief is his act.' The author agrees that disbelief is the act of the disbeliever, but clarifies that it is not Allah's act; rather it is Allah's creation (مفعوله). The passage then addresses the view of the Najjārīyya and some Ahl al-Ḥadīth who define the disbeliever as one in whom disbelief inheres, not one who performs it. They argue by analogy with movement, death, colors, tastes, etc. The author turns this against them: if these inherent qualities (like blackness, heat) are acts of Allah, then the absurd conclusion follows that Allah would be described by them (e.g., black, hot, dead), which is blasphemy. Thus the interlocutor is forced either to accept that blasphemy or to admit the contradiction of their position.

Key Terms
فعل
مفعول
الكفر
الكافر
الحركة
النجارية
أهل الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah creates an action, the human cannot be responsible for it.
Correction: The Maturidi principle of dual attribution shows that an action can be both created by Allah and acquired by the human, who thus bears moral responsibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between disbelief and the disbeliever?
A) Disbelief is an act of Allah in the disbeliever.
B) Disbelief is an act of the disbeliever but created by Allah.
C) Disbelief inheres in the disbeliever and is not his act.
D) Disbelief is neither an act of Allah nor of the disbeliever.

Key Takeaway
A single action can be simultaneously attributed to Allah as creator and to the human as acquirer, preserving both divine omnipotence and moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly internalize that the act is both created by Allah and your own responsibility? Or do you secretly blame your circumstances or Allah's decree? What would it mean for your repentance if you fully owned your sins as your acts while trusting that Allah is the creator of all things?
?
The passage warns against treating negative qualities (like disbelief) as merely inherent rather than as actions you choose. Is there a character flaw or bad habit you have resigned yourself to as 'just the way I am'? How can you reclaim it as an action you can change through your own effort and Allah's help?
?
If you really believed that every good deed you do is created by Allah and yet is your action, would you feel more gratitude or more pride? Examine your heart: do you thank Allah for enabling your obedience while also taking personal responsibility for it? Which side do you neglect?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
The Servant as Acquiring, Not Creating
The author distinguishes between doer and creator to answer the objection that the servant would be a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Māturīdī distinction between a doer (fāʿil) and a creator (khāliq), and why the servant is an acquirer (muktasib) rather than a creator.

Core Idea
Creation requires independence; the servant's action is dependent, so it is acquisition.

The author addresses a common objection: if the servant performs a created act, doesn't that make him a creator? The response draws a careful distinction between the generic term 'doer' and the specific term 'creator'. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Māturīdī doctrine of kasb (acquisition).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the author’s concise style.
This passage contains the core answer to the objection.
2
Identify the definitions of doer and creator
The passage defines a doer as one who brings about the created thing. A creator is one who does so independently. Find these definitions in the text.
The contrast between independent and dependent action is crucial.
3
Understand the role of acquisition
The servant’s action is not independent, so he is an acquirer (muktasib). Reflect on why this preserves Allah’s exclusive creative power.
This distinction resolves the objection without making the servant a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم : إن العبد عندكم يفعل المخلوق ومن فعل المخلوق فهو خالق فيقال لهم من فعل المخلوق فهو فاعل، فبعد ذلك إن كان فعل على الانفراد فهو خالق، وإن كان فعل لا على الانفراد فهو مكتسب، وهذا على عبارة القائلين إن التكوين هو المكون، وعبارتنا أن من فعله المخلوق فهو فاعل ومن فعل المخلوق فهو خالق، وجميع ما يوردون من نحو هذه الأسئلة يجاب على هذا الطريق، ثم يقال لهم: على قياس قولكم ينبغي أن يقال: إن من فعل الشيء فهو مشيء فإن أقروا به أدخلوا الشيئية تحت الفعل ورجعوا عن قولهم: إن المعدوم شيء، وإن أنكروا بطل سؤالهم. ولهم مطالبات كثيرة لا وجه إلى استئصالها غير أن من وقف على حقيقة المذهب ووجوه الحجج في المسألة، وكان بصيرا بالمجادلة تيسر عليه الخروج عنها بمشيئة الله تعالى وعونه.
And with this they answer their statement: 'Indeed the servant, according to you, does the created thing, and whoever does the created thing is a creator.' So it is said to them: Whoever does the created thing is a doer (fāʿil). After that, if he does it independently, he is a creator; and if he does it not independently, he is an acquirer (muktasib). This is according to the expression of those who say that bringing-into-being (takwīn) is the same as the thing brought into being (mukawwan). But our expression is that the one from whom the created thing originates is a doer, and the one who does the created thing independently is a creator. And all the questions they raise like these are answered in this way. Then it is said to them: according to your analogy, it should be said: 'Whoever does a thing is a thing-ifier (mushayyiʾ).' If they affirm this, they bring thing-ness (shayʾiyya) under the action and retract their statement that 'the non-existent is a thing'; and if they deny it, their question becomes invalid. And they have many demands that have no way to be uprooted, except that whoever grasps the reality of the school and the aspects of proofs in the question, and is insightful in argumentation, it becomes easy for him to extricate himself from them by the will of Allah and His help.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection: 'The servant creates the created thing, so he would be a creator.' The author distinguishes two roles: a 'doer' (فاعل) and a 'creator' (خالق). Everyone who brings about a created thing is a doer, but a creator is one who does so independently. The servant's action is dependent on Allah's power, so he is an 'acquirer' (مكتسب), not a creator. The author notes that this distinction aligns with the view of those who identify the act of bringing into being (تكوين) with the thing brought into being (مكون), but the Māturīdī formulation is slightly different. Then a further analogy is presented: if the opponent's logic is followed, one could argue that whoever does a thing is a 'thing-ifier' (مشيء), which would lead to absurdities regarding the status of non-existent things (المعدوم). The author concludes that all such objections can be answered through this method, and that only those who deeply understand the school's true position and are skilled in debate can successfully navigate these challenges.

مكتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Doing a created act necessarily makes one a creator.
Correction: The author explains that doing a created act makes one a doer (fāʿil), but creation requires independent action, which only Allah possesses.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes a creator from a mere doer?
A) A creator brings about the created thing, a doer does not.
B) A creator acts independently, while a doer may be dependent.
C) A creator is always Allah, a doer is always the servant.
D) There is no distinction; the terms are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
The Māturīdī doctrine of kasb distinguishes dependent acquisition from independent creation, preserving divine exclusive creativity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Thingness Argument and Refutation
The author extends the argument to thingness to further refute the opponent’s objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Māturīdī scholar uses the concept of 'thingness' (shayʾiyya) to further refute the opponent’s objection and defend the status of non-existent things.

Core Idea
The opponent’s logic leads to absurdity: if doing makes a thing a thing-ifier, then the non-existent would have to be a thing.

After establishing the main distinction between doer and creator, the author now employs a secondary argument based on the opponent’s own reasoning. By extending the analogy to 'thing-ification', he shows that the opponent cannot consistently hold both that the non-existent is a thing and that doing a thing entails thing-ifier status. This argument reinforces the Māturīdī position against the Muʿtazilī view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and grasp the analogy
The passage presents a parallel argument: if doing a thing makes one a creator, then doing a thing should make one a 'thing-ifier'. Understand the logical extension.
The analogy is based on the same pattern as the main objection.
2
Consider the two possible responses
If the opponent affirms the analogy, they must accept that thingness is brought about by the doer, contradicting their claim that the non-existent is a thing. If they deny it, their original question becomes invalid.
This traps the opponent in a dilemma.
3
Reflect on the author's concluding remark
The author notes that many objections exist, but mastering the school’s principles and proof methods makes them easy to answer. Think about the importance of deep understanding.
This emphasizes the need for comprehensive knowledge.

The Ash'ari Definition of Evil Harm
The author explains the Ash'ari position that harm is only evil if it exceeds the command of the commander.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari criterion for evaluating the moral status of harm, specifically that harm is only evil when it violates a legitimate command.

Core Idea
Harm's evil depends on authority, not on harm itself.

The Ash'aris hold that harm is not inherently evil; its moral quality is determined by whether it exceeds the command of a rightful authority. This lesson presents their definition and illustrates it with the case of intentional killing versus lawful retaliation (qisas). You will see how the same harmful act can be considered evil in one context and not in another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two conditional statements and the example that follows.
The passage cuts off at the end; focus on the complete part.
2
Identify the two conditions
The passage presents two scenarios: harm without transgression (not evil) and harm with transgression (evil). Locate these in the text.
The key term 'مجاوز آمره' (transgressing the command) is central.
3
Analyze the example of killing
The example of intentional killing vs. qisas shows that the same physical harm (death of a parent) is judged differently based on authority. Reflect on why the killer is not considered evil in the qisas case.
This illustrates that harm is not intrinsically evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأشعرية يقولون : الإضرار متى لم تكن المضر به غیره مجاوزا آمره لا يكون شرا، ومتى كان مجاوزاً أمر آمره كان شراً، ولهذا يقال: من قتل غيره عمدًا إنه ألحق الشر بولده الصغير ولو قتله بقصاص ما كان شريراً ملحقا الشر بولده الصغير، وإن كان الضرر عليه في الحالين سواء، ثم الله تعالى بإيجاده الكفر ما
Then the Ash'aris say: Harm, when the one harmed by it is not transgressing the command of its commander, is not evil; and when it is transgressing the command of its commander, it is evil. For this reason it is said: Whoever kills another intentionally has brought harm upon his young child, but if he killed him by retaliation (qisas), he would not be evil bringing harm upon his young child, even if the harm is the same in both cases. Then Allah, exalted, by His bringing disbelief into existence...
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari doctrinal position on the nature of evil harm. According to the Ash'aris, harm is not inherently evil; rather, it becomes evil when it violates the command of the one who has authority over the matter. The author illustrates this with an example: a man who intentionally kills another causes harm to the victim's young child, and that act is considered evil. However, if the same man kills the same person as a legitimate retaliation (qisas), the harm to the child is identical, yet the man is not considered evil because the killing was commanded by law. Thus, the moral quality of the harm depends on whether it exceeds the command of the commander. The passage then begins to apply this to God's creation of disbelief, but the text cuts off, leaving the application incomplete.

Correction: According to the Ash'aris, harm is only evil if it exceeds the command of a commander; otherwise it may be neutral or even good.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari principle: harm is evil only when it violates authoritative command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Did you ever ask God, 'Why me?' or feel that the harm was unjust? How does the Ash'ari perspective challenge you to reconsider your reaction? Will you now view such trials as potentially non-evil because they come from God's command? ?
The example of retaliation shows that identical harm can be either evil or not based on authority. When you have caused harm to someone—through words or actions—did you ever excuse yourself by focusing on the harm itself? Or have you considered whether your action was within a legitimate authority? Examine your conscience: Are there harms you have inflicted that you justified because 'it wasn't that bad'? This lesson demands you ask: Did I violate a command of God? Repent for those that did.
?
The Ash'aris affirm that God's creation of disbelief is not evil because it does not transgress His command. This is a hard truth: if God creates unbelief in a person, that person is not wronged. How does this sit with your sense of fairness? Do you find yourself resisting this teaching because it clashes with your own notion of justice? Will you submit your intellect to this divine command, even if you cannot fully grasp it? Pray for the humility to accept that God's actions are not evil, even when they involve what seems to be harm.

The Partnership Argument Against Mu'tazila
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing their doctrine makes humans partners with God in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila view of human action leads to the unacceptable conclusion that humans are partners with God in creation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila theory of human action makes humans partners with Allah in creation.

Imam Nasafi uses a powerful analogy to expose a logical flaw in the Mu'tazila position. If, as they claim, both Allah and the human contribute distinct powers to every action, then each action results from the combination of two exclusive powers. But this is exactly the definition of partnership—and no one applies partnership to Allah. This lesson unpacks the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the structure of the analogy sink in before analyzing.
Notice the conditional 'if we see that Zayd and 'Amr...'
2
Understand the house-building analogy
Identify how the analogy works: two partners each have exclusive power over different parts; together they complete the building. This is partnership in common language.
3
Map the analogy to the Mu'tazila view
See how Nasafi then applies the same logic: the slave's action requires a power from Allah (to create) and a power from the slave (to invent), each exclusive. The act occurs by their combination.
This mapping leads to the conclusion that living beings are partners with Allah.
4
Grasp the unacceptable conclusion
Realize that for Nasafi, calling anyone a partner of Allah is blasphemy. So the Mu'tazila view must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider how this argument upholds tawhid and rejects any notion of shared creativity with Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا من كل فعل كأن العبد شريك الله، فإنا إذا رأينا أن زيدًا مع عمر إذا اشتركا في بناء الدار وما يقدر عليه هذا من أجزاء الدار لا يقدر عليه ذاك، وما يقدر عليه ذاك لا يقدر عليه هذا فجعل كل واحد منهما ما في قدرته حتى تم بناء الدار بهما لم يمتنع أحد من أرباب اللسان والعقلاء من القول زيدًا وعمر اشتركا في بناء الدار ثم إن الفعل من العبد لن يحصل إلا بوجود قدرة يخترعها الله تعالى لا قدرة للعبد عليها وفعل يخترعه العبد لا قدرة الله تعالى عليه وحصول الفعل بمجموع مقدوريهما، فكان الأحياء شركاء الله تعالى في تخليق العالم، وكل حي من كل فعل يفعله شريك الله تعالى.
And likewise, from every action, it is as if the slave is a partner of Allah. For if we see that Zayd and 'Amr, when they partner in building a house, and whatever parts of the house this one is able to do, that one is not able to do, and whatever that one is able to do, this one is not able to do, so that each one does whatever is in his power until the building of the house is completed by both, no one among the people of language and the rational people refrains from saying 'Zayd and 'Amr partnered in building the house.' Then, the action of the slave does not occur except with the existence of a power that Allah creates, over which the slave has no power, and an action that the slave creates, over which Allah has no power, and the occurrence of the action is by the combination of the two powers. Thus, living beings would be partners of Allah in the creation of the world, and every living being from every action it performs is a partner of Allah.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazila's theory of human action. It analogizes to two humans partnering in building a house, where each has exclusive power over some parts. Similarly, if, as the Mu'tazila claim, the slave's action requires a power from Allah (to create the act) and a power from the slave (to invent the act), and the act occurs by the combination, then each partner contributes exclusively, leading to the conclusion that humans are partners with Allah in creation. This is a criticism of the Mu'tazila view.

Key Terms
فعل
قدرة
شريك
تخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that this passage presents the Maturidi position on free will.
Correction: This is actually a critique of the Mu'tazila position. Nasafi is showing that their view leads to an absurd conclusion, not endorsing it.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi use the analogy of two people building a house?
A) To prove the Maturidi view
B) To show that shared power with exclusive parts constitutes partnership
C) To deny human free will
D) To praise cooperation

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their theory of action necessarily implies that humans are partners with God in creation, which is blasphemy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something difficult, do you feel a sense of pride that you alone did it? How does Nasafi's argument challenge that feeling, and what would it mean to truly attribute all success to Allah?
?
Consider a habit you struggle with. Do you rely on your own willpower, or do you recognize that even your ability to choose and act is given by Allah? How can this lesson change your approach to self-improvement?
?
If every good deed requires a power from Allah and an action from you, does that diminish your responsibility? Or does it make you more grateful? What is one concrete way you can express that gratitude today?

Maturidi View Does Not Entail Partnership
Nasafi uses the ownership analogy to refute the charge of partnership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi doctrine of acquired action (kasb) does not imply partnership with Allah, using the ownership analogy.

Core Idea
The same action empowered by both God and man is not partnership.

Nasafi addresses a common objection to the Maturidi theory of kasb: if both God and the human have power over the same action, does that mean they are partners? He argues no, using a clear analogy from ownership. By the end of this lesson, you will see how the Maturidi position preserves divine transcendence while affirming human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once aloud or silently. Notice the key terms: إقدار, مقدور, شركة. Then read the English translation.
The passage is dense but short. Focus on the structure: claim, analogy, conclusion.
2
Understand the two parts of the argument
First, Nasafi states that what is within Allah's power is also within the slave's power by Allah's enabling, and vice versa. Second, he says this is not partnership, using the ownership analogy.
The analogy: the same physical object is owned by both God (as Creator) and the slave (as grantee) without being partnership.
3
Reflect on why the analogy works
Ask yourself: Why does ownership not imply partnership? Because partnership requires two independent owners with equal claim, but God's ownership is absolute and creative, while the slave's ownership is derived and temporary. Similarly, God's power is creative, man's is acquired.
This preserves divine transcendence while affirming human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما قلنا فلا يوجب الشركة إذ عين ما هو مقدور الله تعالى مقدور العبد بإقداره، وما هو مقدور العبد مقدور الله تعالى، وهذا لا يعقل شركة كما في الملك إذ هو ما ملك العبد، عينه ملك الله وما هو ملك الله تعالى من الأعيان التي جعلها ملكًا لعباده عينه ملك العبد ولم يعد ذلك شركة في الملك بإجماع العقلاء.
As for what we have said, it does not necessitate partnership, because the very thing that is within the power of Allah is within the power of the slave by His enabling, and what is within the power of the slave is within the power of Allah. And this is not rationally conceived as partnership, just as in ownership: what the slave owns is exactly the property of Allah, and what is the property of Allah from the entities that He has made as property for His slaves is exactly the property of the slave, and this is not considered partnership in ownership by consensus of the rational people.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Maturidi position: There is no partnership because the same action is within the power of both God and man, but in different ways (God's power is creative, man's is acquired). This is analogous to ownership: the same object is owned by both God (as Creator) and man (as grantee), but that is not considered partnership. Therefore, the Maturidi view does not lead to shirk.

Key Terms
إقدار
مقدور
شركة
ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view that both God and man have power over the same action implies partnership (shirk).
Correction: Nasafi shows through the ownership analogy that having power over the same thing does not entail partnership, as ownership of the same entity by both God and man is not considered partnership by rational consensus.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi compare the Maturidi view of power to ownership?
A) Both involve partnership
B) Ownership of a property by both God and man is not considered partnership
C) Power is entirely God's
D) Humans create their own actions

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi doctrine of kasb does not require partnership because the same action is empowered by God creatively and by man by acquisition, analogous to ownership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a good deed, do you feel a subtle pride as if it were solely your achievement? How does this lesson's teaching that the same action is empowered by Allah and acquired by you challenge that feeling? What will you do the next time that pride arises?
?
The ownership analogy says that what you own is still Allah's property. If everything you have—your wealth, talents, even your faith—is actually Allah's, are you truly grateful, or do you act as if they are your own? Reflect on a specific possession or ability you consider 'yours' and imagine giving it back to Allah right now. What holds you back?
?
If you truly internalized that your actions are not independent but empowered by Allah, how would your daily choices change? Commit to one area of your life where you will start consciously acknowledging Allah's enablement, even in the smallest action.

Three Reasons for Denying Divine Creation of Acts
The author enumerates three possible reasons for Mu'tazila denial of divine creation of acts and refutes each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments the Mu'tazila used to deny that Allah creates human acts and how the Maturidi author refutes each one.

Core Idea
Divine creation of acts does not negate human free will.

The Mu'tazila denied that Allah creates human actions because they thought it would lead to compulsion (jabr) and remove human responsibility. The author summarizes their possible reasons and systematically refutes each. As you read, notice how he uses logical arguments and appeals to necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three reasons
Read the passage and list the three reasons the author proposes for the Mu'tazila denial. Note the Arabic terms.
The reasons are: lack of evidence, impossibility, and consequence of compulsion.
2
Examine refutation of lack of evidence
See how the author claims sufficient proofs have been given. Think about what those proofs might be (both transmitted and rational).
3
Examine refutation of impossibility
The author challenges them to provide a proof and refutes their specific arguments (two agents, partnership).
The author claims to have already fully addressed these.
4
Examine refutation of compulsion
The author argues that divine creation is compatible with free will because Allah creates voluntary acts, not compulsory ones. Understand the distinction.
He also refutes the idea that Allah's creation of one act forces the opposite.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Note how the author argues that making voluntary acts compulsory through creation would actually deny necessary knowledge and lead to absurdity.

Repetition that establishes the statement of the incapacity of the Mu'tazila. The summary of the issue: that the denial of the creation (of acts) by Allah Almighty is either because there is no indication of it, or because of impossibility, or because of what the assertion entails of necessitating compulsion and the impossibility of religious obligation and the removal of command and prohibition. If their denial is due to lack of evidence, then we have presented both transmitted and rational proofs sufficient for whoever advises himself and does not dispute his intellect. If their denial is due to impossibility, then they are required to prove it, and they have no proof except that it is not conceivable in their minds because a thing according to them is a deed of two agents and the attachment of two powers to it, or because asserting it necessitates partnership, and we have fully discharged the responsibility for all that. And if their denial is due to what it entails of necessitating compulsion, then we have answered that and repelled the doubts, praise be to Allah. Moreover, the knowledge that we are free agents is established by way of necessity, and the establishment of free will does not negate creation (by Allah) as has passed; indeed how does creation negate free will when He Almighty creates the voluntary act, not the compulsory act, and there is a distinction between them? And the statement that He Almighty intends to create one of them so the other occurs whether he wills or not is an affirmation of compulsion and incapacity for the Maker, Exalted and Glorified. So in making the voluntary act compulsory because it occurs through creation, their denial of necessary knowledge and making...
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the debate on the creation of human acts (takhliq). The author identifies three possible reasons for the Mu'tazila denial that Allah creates the acts: lack of evidence, impossibility, or the consequence of compulsion. He refutes each: if they deny due to lack of evidence, he asserts that sufficient proofs have been given. If due to impossibility, he challenges them to provide a proof, noting that their only arguments are that an act cannot have two agents or that it would imply partnership, both of which he claims to have already refuted. If due to compulsion, he argues that the knowledge of human free will is necessary, and divine creation does not negate that because Allah creates voluntary acts, not compulsory ones. He further argues that claiming Allah intends to create one of two alternatives and the other occurs regardless would imply compulsion to Allah, which is absurd. Thus, making the voluntary act compulsory through creation actually denies necessary knowledge.

Misconception: If Allah creates human acts, then humans have no free will.
Correction: Allah creates voluntary acts, not compulsory ones; human free will is established by necessary knowledge and is compatible with divine creation.

A) Lack of evidence.
B) Impossibility.
C) The consequence of compulsion.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila denial of divine creation of acts is based on three flawed reasons, and each is refuted by showing the compatibility of divine creation with human free will.

The Limit of Human Power: No Creation or Acquisition Beyond One's Own Locus
Establishing the Sunni principle of limited human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that, according to Ahl al-Sunna, human acts are limited to what is directly within their power, and effects like motion in wood or pain in animals are not human acts.

Core Idea
Only acts directly within your power are yours; all else is from Allah.

This lesson establishes a foundational principle of Sunni theology: human power (qudra) is limited to acts that directly occur within one's own locus. Any effect that follows from an action but occurs outside that locus—like a board moving when stepped on—cannot be attributed to the human. This principle is key to refuting the Qadari doctrine of generated acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: first a general principle, then its application to examples.
2
Understand the foundational principle
The passage states that the slave has no power of creation or acquisition except for what is in the locus of his power. This means only acts directly within one's ability are attributable to them.
This is a key Sunni theological stance.
3
See the consequence for external effects
The author applies the principle: motion in wood after stepping, pain in animal after striking—these are not acts of the servant, neither by creation nor acquisition. They occur outside the servant's locus of power.
The servant's act is the stepping or striking itself, not the resulting effects.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does the author begin the refutation with this principle? It sets the stage to deny that any further effects are attributable to humans.
This connects to the lesson's objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال القول بالتولد: فإذا ثبت أن ليس للعبد قدرة التخليق ولا اكتساب إلا لما يحل محل قدرته ثبت أن ما يوجد في الخشبة من الحركة عقيب اعتماد الرجل عليها، وما يوجد من الألم في الحيوان عقيب ضرب الرجل إياه وأشباه ذلك ليس بفعل للعباد لا بطريق التخليق ولا بطريق الاكتساب. وزعم جمهور القدرية أن ما يحدث من هذا الجنس في المحال عقيب أسباب يفعلها الإنسان في حيزه كلها أفعال الإنسان وهو خلقها لا صنع الله تعالى في شيء من ذلك، ويسمونها الأفعال المتولدة، فكان مرور السهم وحركاته وإصابته الهدف وإصابته الحيوان وما يحدث فيه من الانجراح والآلام والموت كل ذلك متولدًا من تحريك الرامي يده بالقوس ونزعه إياها، والعبد خالق كل ذلك ومكتسبه، وكذا الانكسار في الأواني والانخراق في الثياب. وأجاز بشر بن
The discussion on the refutation of the doctrine of tawallud (generated acts): When it is established that the slave has no power of creation (takhliq) nor acquisition (iktisāb) except for what is in the locus of his power, it is established that what is found in the wood of motion after a man's stepping on it, and what is found of pain in the animal after a man's striking it, and similar things, are not an act of the servants—neither by way of creation nor by way of acquisition. The majority of the Qadariyya claimed that what occurs of this kind in loci consequent to causes that a man does is all the acts of the man—he creates them, and Allah has no hand in any of that—and they call them the generated acts (al-afʿāl al-mutawallida). Thus the passage of the arrow, its movements, its hitting the target, its striking the animal, and what occurs in it of wounds, pains, and death—all that is generated from the archer's moving his hand with the bow and his drawing it—and the slave is the creator and acquirer of all that. Likewise the breaking in vessels and the tearing in clothes. And Bishr ibn [al-Muʿtamir] allowed...
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadari doctrine of generated acts (tawallud). The author begins by stating a foundational principle: once it is established that the slave has no power of creation (takhliq) or acquisition (iktisāb) except over what directly falls within his power (i.e., what is in the locus of his power), it follows that certain effects—such as motion in a wooden board after a person steps on it, or pain in an animal after a person strikes it—are not acts of the servant, neither by direct creation nor by acquisition. The author then presents the contrary view of the majority of the Qadariyya (Muʿtazila): they claim that these events, which occur in various loci as a result of human causes, are entirely the acts of the human. According to them, the human creates them, and Allah has no role in them. They call these 'generated acts' (al-afʿāl al-mutawallida). For example, the flight of an arrow, its movements, hitting the target, striking an animal, and the resulting wounds, pain, and death—all are 'generated' from the archer's action of moving his hand and drawing the bow. The human is the creator and acquirer of all of that. Similarly, the breaking of vessels and tearing of clothes are attributed to the human. The passage ends mid-sentence, mentioning that Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir allowed [something—likely further elaboration on tawallud, which continues in the next section].

اكتساب
محل القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Effects like motion in wood or pain in animals are acts of the human who initiated them.
Correction: The passage states these are not acts of the servant at all; they are outside the locus of human power.

Checkpoint

A) It is an act of the person by creation.
B) It is an act of the person by acquisition.
C) It is not an act of the servant at all.
D) It is an act of both the person and Allah.

Key Takeaway
Human power is limited to acts within one's own locus; effects outside that locus are not attributable to humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If you truly believed that no effect outside your direct locus is yours, would you worry less about things you cannot control? How will you remind yourself of this limit when anxiety strikes about consequences of your actions?

The Qadari Doctrine of Generated Acts (Tawallud)
Presenting the Qadari doctrine of generated acts with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadari doctrine of generated acts (tawallud), which attributes all causal effects to human creation, and the examples they used.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya claim that humans create all resulting effects, denying Allah's role.

This lesson presents the view of the majority of the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) regarding 'generated acts.' They argue that when a human performs an action, all subsequent effects—like the flight of an arrow, breaking of a vessel, or pain in an animal—are created by the human, not by Allah. The author cites examples to illustrate this doctrine before moving to refute it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that this is the claim of the Qadariyya, introduced as a false doctrine.
2
Identify the core claim
The Qadariyya assert that events occurring in loci (e.g., wood, animal, arrow) after human causes are all acts of the human, who creates them, and Allah has no part in them.
They call these 'generated acts' (afʿāl mutawallida).
3
List the examples provided
The author gives several examples: the arrow's flight and hitting, wounds and death, breaking of vessels, tearing of clothes. All are considered generated from the human's initial act.
Each example illustrates a chain of causation.
4
Understand the term 'tawallud'
Tawallud literally means 'generation'—the idea that one act necessarily generates another. The Qadariyya believed in necessary causal chains originating from human acts.
This is contrasted with Sunni occasionalism.
5
Note the incomplete ending
The passage mentions Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir, suggesting further cases or refinements, but the text is cut off.

Generated Acts: Not Attributable to the Servant
The author establishes that generated acts are not attributable to the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why generated acts (tawallud) such as wood moving from pressure or pain from a blow are not considered acts of the servant in Maturidi theology.

Core Idea
Generated acts are not the servant's doing, neither by creation nor acquisition.

This lesson tackles a key issue in Islamic theology: whether actions that appear to follow from human intervention are truly our acts. The author of our text argues that if we accept the servant's power is limited, then generated effects like wood moving or animals feeling pain cannot be ascribed to us. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If it is established that... then it is established that...'
The passage uses a logical 'if-then' to connect the limitation of human power to the conclusion about generated acts.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the words 'takhliq' (creation) and 'iktisab' (acquisition). These are the only two ways an act could be attributed to a person according to the author.
The author assumes human power only applies to what enters one's capacity—direct actions, not indirect effects.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that generated acts (like wood moving after leaning) are 'not an act of the servants' by either creation or acquisition. Reflect on why this matters for theological debates about causality.
This refutes the idea that humans cause effects in external objects—a key point against the doctrine of tawallud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣- الكلام في إبطال القول بالتولد : فإذا ثبت أن ليس للعبد قدرة التخليق ولا اكتساب إلا لما يحل محل قدرته ثبت أن ما يوجد في الخشبة من الحركة عقيب اعتماد الرجل عليها، وما يوجد من الألم في الحيوان عقيب ضرب الرجل إياه وأشباه ذلك ليس بفعل للعباد لا بطريق التخليق ولا بطريق الاكتساب.

Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of the doctrine of tawallud (generated acts). The author states a conditional argument: if it is established that the servant (the human) has no power of creation (takhliq) nor acquisition (iktisab) except for what occurs within his own capacity (i.e., what is directly in his control), then it follows that things like the movement of wood when a person leans on it, or the pain in an animal when struck, are not acts of the servant at all—neither by creation nor by acquisition. This sets the foundation for rejecting the idea that humans can bring about effects in external objects through their actions.

The Mu'tazilite Doctrine of Generated Acts
Mu'tazilite claim of human creation of generated acts with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that generated acts are fully created by humans, with no divine involvement.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila held that humans create the effects of their actions, not God.

The Mu'tazila, often called the Qadariyya, argued for human free will to the extreme. They claimed that when an act produces a result in an external object, that result is entirely the human's creation. In this passage, the author summarizes their view on 'generated acts' (al-af‘āl al-mutawallida) and provides examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms and the overall claim.
Pay special attention to the phrase 'no act of God in any of that'.
2
Identify the doctrine
What exactly do the Mu'tazila claim about generated acts? Underline the sentence where they say there is no act of God.
3
Examine the examples
List the examples provided: arrow flight, hitting target, wounding, death, breaking vessels, tearing clothes. How do these illustrate the doctrine of generation?
Think about the causal chain: human action → effect in external object.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the Mu'tazila would make this claim. How does it affect the understanding of divine causality? This will be contrasted with Sunni theology later.
This passage only presents the Mu'tazilite view; the author's critique comes later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم جمهور القدرية أن ما يحدث من هذا الجنس في المحال عقيب أسباب يفعلها الإنسان في حيزه كلها أفعال الإنسان وهو خلقها لا صنع الله تعالى في شيء من ذلك، ويسمونها الأفعال المتولدة، فكان مرور السهم وحركاته وإصابته الهدف وإصابته الحيوان وما يحدث فيه من الانجراح والآلام والموت كل ذلك متولدًا من تحريك الرامي يده بالقوس ونزعه إياها، والعبد خالق كل ذلك ومكتسبه، وكذا الانكسار في الأواني والانخراق في الثياب.
And the majority of the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) claimed that what occurs of this kind in the loci (i.e., external objects) after causes that the human performs within his domain are all acts of the human, and he creates them—there is no act of God, exalted, in any of that—and they call them the generated acts (al-af‘āl al-mutawallida). Thus the passage of the arrow, its movements, its hitting the target, its striking the animal, and what occurs in it of wounding, pains, and death—all that is generated (mutawallid) from the archer's moving his hand with the bow and drawing it, and the servant is the creator of all that and its acquirer; likewise the breaking of vessels and the tearing of clothes.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the majority of the Qadariyya (a term often used for the Mu'tazila) regarding generated acts. They claim that when a human performs an action that causes an effect in an external object (e.g., leaning on wood causes movement, shooting an arrow causes injury), that effect is entirely the human's act—he creates it, and God has no part in it. They call these effects 'generated acts' (al-af‘āl al-mutawallida). Examples include the arrow's flight and impact, the wounding and death it causes, and similar cases like breaking a vessel or tearing clothes. According to this view, the human is the creator and acquirer of all these effects.

Misconception: Generated acts are merely natural outcomes with no agent.
Correction: The Mu'tazila consider them fully created by the human, with no act of God.

A) They are acts of God by nature.
B) They are acts of the human who creates them, with no act of God.
C) They are acts with no agent.
D) They are necessary outcomes of natural laws.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila (Qadariyya) believed that the effects of human actions in external objects are entirely created by the human, with no divine involvement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you caused harm accidentally, like breaking something or hurting someone. Did you feel solely responsible, or did you see God's will in it? This passage forces a choice: either you are the sole creator of the harm, or God is involved. Examine your conscience: which belief gives you more humility?
?
If a child dies from a mistake a parent makes, who is responsible? The Mu'tazila say the parent created the death. Do you agree, or do you see God's wisdom in such tragedies? Let this question drive you to study the Ash'ari position and make a firm conviction about divine decree.

Bishr's view that sensory qualities are generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's radical extension of the doctrine of generated acts to include all sensory qualities and perceptions.

Core Idea
Bishr claimed humans create colors, tastes, smells, and even perceptions—not God.

This lesson examines an extreme Mu'tazilite position reported by Imam al-Nasafi. Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, a leading Mu'tazilite, expanded the concept of generated acts (mutawallidat) from physical effects to encompass all sensory qualities and perceptions. This view assigns to humans the power of creation in a way that directly challenges divine omnipotence.

Refuting Necessitarian Views on Generated Acts
The author presents and critiques the necessitarian views of an-Nazzām and al-Qalānisī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing generated acts to God by necessitation is theologically problematic and how the author refutes it.

Core Idea
Claiming God is necessitated by causes implies His compulsion, which is manifestly corrupt.

This passage critiques two theologians who argued that God necessarily creates effects whenever a cause occurs. While they intended to preserve divine agency, the author shows that their view actually implies God is compelled and has no power to abstain. The lesson unpacks their arguments and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand an-Nazzām's view
Read how an-Nazzām claims generated acts are God's acts by necessitation of creation (ījāb al-khilqa), with examples like pain upon striking.
He gives specific examples to illustrate his position.
2
Examine al-Qalānisī's parallel view
Notice that al-Qalānisī attributes acts to God by necessitation of nature (ījāb al-ṭab‘) and the author declares it essentially identical to an-Nazzām's view.
Both views are considered the same in outcome.
3
Identify the shared core
The gist of both schools is that when a cause exists, the effect must occur—impossible to be absent. This mirrors the naturalists (ahl al-ṭabā‘i‘) except they attribute the effect to nature, not God.
The key difference is the attribution to God vs. nature.
4
Grasp the refutation
The author argues that saying God is necessitated (ījāb) implies God is compelled (muḍṭarr), and that a human who performs a cause would force God to act, leaving God no power to refrain.
This is declared clearly corrupt and manifestly flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم النظام أن ما يسمى الأفعال المتولدة كل ذلك فعل الله تعالى بإيجاب الخلقة أي أن الله تعالى خلق الشخص الحيواني على وجه يوجب أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه الألم عند الضرب والسهم على وجه يوجب أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه المرور عند الرمي وكذا الزجاج مع الانكسار. وحكى عن أبي العباس القلانسي أن كل ذلك فعل الله تعالى بإيجاب الطبع وهو قريب من مذهب النظام بل لا فرق بينهما، وحاصل المذهبين استحالة انعدام ذلك عند وجود ما هو سببه كما هو مذهب أهل الطبائع إلا أن أولئك يضيفون ذلك إلى طبيعة المحل فحسب، وهما يضيفان إلى الله تعالى ولكن بإيجاب الخلقة والطبع، والقول بالإيجاب على الله قول باضطراره، ويؤدي إلى أن من فعل سببًا في محل يسير موجبًا على الله تعالى أن يفعل ذلك المتولد في المحل بحيث لا يكون له قدرة الامتناع، والقول به ظاهر الفساد بادي العوار.
And an-Nazzām claimed that what is called generated acts is all the act of God, exalted, by the necessitation of nature (ījāb al-khilqa), i.e., God created the animal person in such a way that it necessitates that God creates pain in it upon striking and the arrow in such a way that it necessitates that God creates passage in it upon shooting, and likewise glass with breaking. And it is reported from Abū al-‘Abbās al-Qalānisī that all that is the act of God by the necessitation of nature (ījāb al-ṭab‘), and it is close to the school of an-Nazzām—indeed no difference between them. And the gist of the two schools is the impossibility of the absence of that when its cause exists, just as is the school of the people of natures (ahl al-ṭabā‘i‘), except that those (people of natures) attribute that solely to the nature of the locus, whereas these two attribute it to God but by necessitation of nature and habit. And the saying that necessitates upon God is a saying of His compulsion, and it leads to the result that whoever performs a cause in a locus makes it necessary upon God to perform that generated act in the locus such that He has no power of abstention. And this saying is clearly corrupt, manifestly flawed.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the views of an-Nazzām and Abū al-‘Abbās al-Qalānisī. An-Nazzām claimed that all generated acts are actually acts of God, but by necessitation (ījāb) of the nature He created. For example, God created the animal in such a way that when struck, pain necessarily occurs; He created the arrow so that when shot, flight necessarily happens. Similarly, al-Qalānisī attributed generated acts to God by necessitation of nature (ījāb al-ṭab‘). The author notes that both views are essentially the same, and their core is that when a cause exists, the effect is impossible to not occur—similar to the view of the 'people of natures' (naturalists), except that naturalists attribute the effect to the nature of the object, while these two attribute it to God but with necessitation. The author then refutes this by saying that to say God is necessitated is to say God is compelled (muḍṭarr), and it implies that a human who performs a cause forces God to produce the effect, with God having no power to refrain. This is declared clearly corrupt and manifestly flawed.

Key Terms
النظام
أبو العباس القلانسي
إيجاب الخلقة
إيجاب الطبع
أهل الطبائع
الاضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God necessarily creates effects whenever a cause occurs.
Correction: The author shows this implies God is compelled and has no power to abstain, which is theologically corrupt.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main objection to the views of an-Nazzām and al-Qalānisī?
A) They deny any role for human action.
B) They make God necessitated and compelled, which is corrupt.
C) They attribute generated acts to nature rather than God.
D) They claim generated acts have no agent.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes necessitarian views by showing they imply divine compulsion, which is clearly flawed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a cause-and-effect event in your life, do you unconsciously assume that Allah had no choice but to produce that outcome? How does that belief secretly erode your sense of His absolute sovereignty and freedom? Will you train yourself to say 'Allah willed it' without any sense of necessity?
?
If you truly believed that every cause in your hands does not compel Allah but He creates the effect by His own free choice, how would that change the way you pray and rely on Him after you have done your part? Are you willing to release the false security of 'natural laws' and instead entrust your affairs to the One who is not bound by them?
?
The author says that claiming God is necessitated leads to man forcing God. Have you ever thought that your good deeds or your du'ā' somehow put God in your debt, compelling Him to respond? What would it mean to repent from that assumption and relate to Him purely as the independent, unconstrained Lord?

The Maturidi Doctrine of Habit and Divine Freedom
The author states the Maturidi view on divine freedom and habit in generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi view that God is not obligated to create generated effects, but He habitually does so by His free choice.

Core Idea
God is free to create effects; their regularity is His habit, not His necessity.

In this passage, the author presents the distinctive Maturidi position on generated acts—actions or effects that appear to arise from natural causes. He affirms that God is under no obligation to produce these effects, even when their usual causes are present. However, God has freely chosen to make it His habit to create them consistently, explaining the regularity we observe in the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the key phrases: 'جائز' (possible), 'لا يجب عليه' (not obligated), and 'أجرى العادة' (made it a habit).
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that it is possible for a locus (e.g., a body) to remain free of an expected effect (e.g., pain) even when the usual cause exists. This is the starting point for understanding divine freedom.
This claim directly challenges necessitarian views.
3
Understand the rationale: divine freedom
The reason given is that God is not obligated (lā yajibu) to produce any effect. Whatever He does, He does by His own choice (bi-ikhtiyārihi). No external constraint forces Him.
4
Grasp the role of 'adah (habit)
Although God could refrain, He has made it His habitual practice (ajrā al-ʿāda) to create these effects consistently. This explains the reliable order of nature without binding God.
Habit is not a law of nature; it is a consistent divine choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا أن خلو المحل عن هذه المعاني عند وجود ما يعد من العرف سببًا لها جائز، والله تعالى لا يجب عليه أن يفعل شيئًا منها في المحل وما يفعل يفعل باختياره، غير أنه أجرى العادة بأن يفعل ذلك كله.
And according to us (the Maturidis), the locus being free of these meanings when what is considered by custom a cause for them exists is possible. And God, exalted, is not obligated to do anything of them in the locus, and what He does, He does by His choice, except that He has made it a habit (ʿāda) to do all that.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents the positive Maturidi position on generated acts. The author states that it is possible for the locus (the object in which the effect is expected) to remain free of the effect (e.g., no pain when struck) even when the usual cause exists. This is because God is not obligated (lā yajibu) to create the effect; He does whatever He wills by His free choice (bi-ikhtiyārihi). However, God has made it His habitual practice (ʿāda) to create these effects in conjunction with their causes. This doctrine of 'ādah (habit) affirms divine freedom while explaining the regularity of natural phenomena.

Key Terms
العادة
الاختيار
الوجوب
الجواز
الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that because effects regularly follow their causes, God is bound by necessity to produce them.
Correction: The Maturidi position denies this obligation. God is free to not create the effect, but He consistently chooses to do so out of His habit, not necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view presented, why do generated effects usually occur after their causes?
A) Because God is obligated to create them.
B) Because God made it a habit to create them.
C) Because the nature of the object necessitates them.
D) Because the human creates them by his power.

Key Takeaway
God is not obligated to create effects, but He habitually does so by His free choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God's actions are predictable by cause and effect, forgetting that He could change His habit at any moment? How would your life change if you truly internalized that every effect is a direct act of God's free choice?
?
If God is not obligated to create effects, then every blessing you experience is pure favor. Does your gratitude reflect that, or do you take things for granted as 'natural' outcomes?
?
How does the doctrine of habit (ʿāda) challenge your sense of control over your own life? Are you trusting in causes and means rather than in God's free will?

Thumāma ibn al-Ashras: Generated Acts Have No Agent
Reports Thumāma's claim that generated acts are agentless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Thumāma ibn al-Ashras's radical claim that generated acts occur without any agent.

Core Idea
Some acts have no doer at all.

This lesson examines an extreme Mu'tazilite view on generated acts (mutawallid). Thumāma ibn al-Ashras argued that these events are not caused by any agent—neither human nor divine. This position challenges the very notion of causality in theology.

Guided Path

Generated acts are effects that follow from an action, like pain from a slap.
3

Reflect on why this view is problematic for Islamic theology and natural causality. Compare with other views on the same topic.
This view denies any causal connection, which undermines both divine and human agency.

Source Text

Three Mutazilite Views on Generated Acts and Their Refutation
Presents three Mu'tazilite views on generated acts and their refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of Bishr al-Mu'tamir, al-Nazzam, and al-Qalanisi on generated acts, and why the author refutes them by arguing that obligation upon God implies compulsion.

Core Idea
Obligation upon God negates His free will and leads to absurdity.

The issue of generated acts (al-mutawallidat) was a key point of debate between the Mu'tazila and the Ahl al-Sunna. This passage presents three distinct Mu'tazilite theories on how these acts are caused, then refutes the latter two by exposing their logical consequence: that God would be compelled to act. As you read, notice how each view tries to explain causality without attributing true creation to humans, yet falls into a deeper problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Notice the sequence: Bishr's view, then Nazzam's, then Qalanisi's, then the refutation.
These are direct quotes from the text.
2
Identify each of the three views
Find where each view begins. Bishr says humans create the acts entirely. Nazzam says God creates them by necessity of nature. Qalanisi says God creates them by necessity of disposition.
The author notes that Nazzam and Qalanisi are essentially the same.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation is in the last few lines. The author argues that saying God is obligated (ijab) implies He is compelled (idtirar), and that leads to the absurdity that humans can impose obligations on God.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this refutation is important for Islamic theology: it preserves God's absolute freedom and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتمر أحد رؤسائهم أن يكون السمع والبصر وما وراء ذلك من أنواع الإدراك أنواع الألوان والطعوم والروائح متولدة عن فعل الإنسان وكل ذلك يكون مخلوقا له مخترعًا من جهته لا صنع الله تعالى فيه، وزعم النظام أن ما يسمى الأفعال المتولدة كل ذلك فعل الله تعالى بإيجاب الخلقة أي أن الله تعالى خلق الشخص الحيواني على وجه يوجب أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه الألم عند الضرب والسهم على وجه يوجب أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه المرور عند الرمي وكذا الزجاج مع الانكسار. وحكى عن أبي العباس القلانسي أن كل ذلك فعل الله تعالى بإيجاب الطبع وهو قريب من مذهب النظام بل لا فرق بينهما، وحاصل المذهبين استحالة انعدام ذلك عند وجود ما هو سببه كما هو مذهب أهل الطبائع إلا أن أولئك يضيفون ذلك إلى طبيعة المحل فحسب، وهما يضيفان إلى الله تعالى ولكن بإيجاب الخلقة والطبع، والقول بالإيجاب على الله قول باضطراره، ويؤدي إلى أن من فعل سببًا في محل يسير موجبًا على الله تعالى أن يفعل ذلك المتولد في المحل بحيث لا يكون له قدرة الامتناع، والقول به ظاهر الفساد بادي العوار.
Al-Mu'tamir, one of their leaders, holds that hearing, sight, and beyond that the types of perception, types of colors, tastes, and smells are generated (mutawallida) from human action, and all of that is created for him, invented from his side, with no act of God the Exalted in it. Al-Nazzam claimed that what are called generated acts (al-af'al al-mutawallida), all of that is an act of God the Exalted by necessity of nature (bi-ijab al-khilqa), meaning that God the Exalted created the animal person in a way that necessitates that God the Exalted creates pain in it upon striking, and the arrow in a way that necessitates that God the Exalted creates passage in it upon shooting, and likewise glass with breaking. And it is related from Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi that all of that is an act of God the Exalted by necessity of disposition (bi-ijab al-tab'), and it is close to the school of al-Nazzam, rather there is no difference between them. The summary of the two schools is the impossibility of non-existence of that when its cause exists, as is the school of the naturalists (ahl al-taba'i'), except that those [naturalists] attribute that to the nature of the locus only, while these two attribute it to God the Exalted but by necessity of nature and disposition. The statement of obligation (ijab) upon God is a statement of compulsion (idtirar) upon Him, and it leads to the point that whoever causes a cause in a slight locus imposes an obligation upon God the Exalted to create that generated thing in the locus such that He has no power to refrain. And holding this is clearly corrupt, manifestly defective.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents the views of three Mu'tazilite figures on the issue of generated acts (al-mutawallidat) and then refutes them. First, al-Mu'tamir (Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir) holds that sensory perceptions (like hearing and sight) and qualities (colors, tastes, smells) are generated from human action, meaning humans create them. Second, al-Nazzam claims that these are acts of God by necessity of nature (bi-ijab al-khilqa): God created the human body in such a way that pain necessarily follows striking, etc. Third, al-Qalanisi similarly says it is by necessity of disposition (bi-ijab al-tab'). The author notes that both Nazzam and Qalanisi are close to the naturalists (ahl al-taba'i'), except that naturalists attribute effects to the nature of the locus, while these two attribute them to God but under obligation. The author then refutes: saying God is obligated (ijab) implies He is compelled (idtirar), and it leads to the consequence that anyone who causes a cause can impose an obligation on God to create the effect, such that God cannot refrain. This is clearly false.

Key Terms
mutawallidat
ijab al-khilqa
ijab al-tab'
idtirar
ahl al-taba'i'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributing generated acts to God under obligation preserves His power, but it actually limits it.
Correction: The author argues that 'obligation' (ijab) implies compulsion (idtirar), which contradicts God's absolute freedom and power.

Checkpoint
What is the main criticism the author levels against the views of al-Nazzam and al-Qalanisi regarding generated acts?
A) They deny God's power
B) They imply that God is compelled and obligated to act
C) They attribute creation directly to humans
D) They deny the existence of causes

Key Takeaway
Generated acts cannot be attributed to God under compulsion, nor to humans as independent creators; rather, they follow from God's custom (sunnah) without obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something through your own effort, do you genuinely believe God is the sole creator of that outcome, or does your heart secretly credit your own skill? How does this lesson challenge that tendency?
?
Have you ever felt that God 'owes' you something because of an action you took—like prayers or good deeds? Reflect on the concept of obligation upon God: does your heart ever lean toward expecting God to act because of your deed? What does this passage teach you about that assumption?
?
If God were compelled by His own creation to produce effects from causes, would you still worship Him as truly free and sovereign? How does your trust in God change when you realize He is never obligated by any cause?
