The Maturidi View on Causality and Divine Choice
States the Maturidi doctrine that effects are not necessary and God acts by choice and custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi doctrine that effects are not necessary consequences of causes but follow by divine custom and free choice.

Core Idea
God acts by choice, not necessity; effects follow by custom.

The Maturidi school navigates between necessitarian causality and pure occasionalism. This passage clarifies that while causes are customary signs, God remains free and unconstrained. Let's examine the precise statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the compact style and key terms.
The phrase 'jara al-ada' (established custom) is central.
2
Identify the three components
The passage makes three points: (1) the effect may be absent even when the cause is present, (2) God is not obligated to create the effect, (3) what He does is by choice, but He has established a custom of creating effects after causes.
These three points together define the Maturidi position.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: How does this view differ from saying 'fire burns by nature'? How does it preserve divine sovereignty?
This connects to the broader Islamic theology of tawheed and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندنا أن خلو المحل عن هذه المعاني عند وجود ما يعد من العرف سببًا لها جائز، والله تعالى لا يجب عليه أن يفعل شيئًا منها في المحل وما يفعل يفعل باختياره، غير أنه أجرى العادة بأن يفعل ذلك كله.
And with us: that the locus being empty of these meanings when that which is considered customarily a cause for them exists is permissible. God the Exalted is not obligated to do any of them in the locus. What He does, He does by His choice. However, He has established the custom to do all of that.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi position (Ahl al-Sunna) on generated acts. The key point is that even when what is usually considered a cause (sabab) is present, the effect (the 'meaning' like pain) may not occur; it is merely permissible for it to occur. God is under no obligation to create the effect. If He does create it, it is by His free choice (ikhtiyar). However, God has established a regular custom (jara al-ada) such that effects normally follow their causes. This view avoids the Mu'tazilite positions that either make humans creators or that impose obligation on God.

Key Terms
jara al-ada
ikhtiyar
sabab

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi denial of necessary causality means events happen randomly with no predictability.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that God has established a custom (jara al-ada) to create effects after causes, so regularity is affirmed but not as logical necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position, what happens when a cause (e.g., striking) exists in a locus?
A) The effect must occur by natural necessity
B) The effect may not occur, and God acts by choice
C) The locus necessarily produces the effect
D) The effect occurs only if humans create it

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position affirms God's free choice and customary causality without necessitating effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not obligated to create effects from causes, then every breath you take is a direct gift from Him, not a natural guarantee. When did you last thank Him for something you assumed would happen 'by nature'? Today, before you eat, pause and acknowledge that this food reached you only by His choice.
?
The regularity of life can lull you into forgetfulness that God could suspend His custom at any moment. Is there an area of your life where you are relying on 'how things usually work' instead of on Allah? What would change if you truly believed that tomorrow's sunrise depends entirely on His will?
?
You believe that God is not obligated to you—yet He has chosen to sustain you every moment. How does this truth reshape your gratitude and your fear? Will you repent of taking His custom for granted and begin to live with conscious dependence on His choice?

Thumama's Denial of an Agent for Generated Acts and Its Refutation
Presents Thumama's view that generated acts have no agent and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying an agent for generated acts leads to the negation of the proof for the Creator, and how the Maturidi response defends divine agency.

Core Idea
Denying agency for generated acts destroys the very proof of God's existence.

Thumama ibn al-Ashras held the view that generated acts (mutawalladāt) have no agent. This may seem like a technical point about causality, but as the author shows, it has devastating consequences. If we admit that precise acts can come into existence without an agent, then the argument from design and causation collapses into pure negation (ta‘ṭīl). In this lesson, we examine this claim and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand Thumama's claim
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp what Thumama said about generated acts having no agent.
2
Identify the consequence
Notice how the author shows that this claim invalidates the proof for the Maker, because it allows ordered acts to occur without a Creator.
3
Reflect on ta‘ṭīl
Understand why this is called pure negation (ta‘ṭīl) and why the author seeks refuge from such a saying.
Ta‘ṭīl means stripping God of His attributes or denying His existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم ثمامة بن الأشرس أن ما سمى عندهم متولدًا كل ذلك أفعال لا فاعل لها، وأبطل بهذه البدعة على نفسه طريق إثبات الصانع حيث جوز أن كثيرًا من الأفعال المحكمة المتقنة تخرج من العدم إلى الوجود وتختص بالوجود بعد العدم من غير تخصيص مخصص وإيجاد موجد، وهو التعطيل المحض – نسأل الله العصمة عن القول بمثله.
And Thumama ibn al-Ashras claimed that what they call generated (mutawallad) is all acts without an agent. And by this innovation he invalidated against himself the way of proving the Maker, since he allowed that many precise and well-ordered acts come into existence from non-existence and are particularized with existence after non-existence without the particularization of a particularizer and the bringing into existence of an existentiator—and this is pure negation (ta'til). We ask God for protection from saying something like this.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Thumama ibn al-Ashras, who claimed that generated acts have no agent (fā‘il). The author immediately refutes this by saying that Thumama thereby destroys the proof for the existence of the Creator (al-Ṣāni‘). The reasoning: if precise and well-ordered actions can come into existence without a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ) or existentiator (mūjid), then one cannot argue from the order of the universe to a Creator. This is pure ta‘ṭīl (negation of God's attributes or acts). The author expresses seeking refuge from such a saying. This shows that denying agency for effects leads to atheism.

Key Terms
fā‘il
ta‘ṭīl
mukhaṣṣiṣ
mūjid

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Thumama's view is only about secondary causes and does not affect the existence of God.
Correction: The author shows that if generated acts have no agent, then the meticulous order in the world could exist without a Creator, thereby undermining the primary proof for God's existence.

Checkpoint
What consequence does the author draw from Thumama's denial of an agent for generated acts?
A) It affirms God's direct creation
B) It invalidates the proof of the Maker
C) It supports the Maturidi position
D) It shows humans have creative power

Key Takeaway
Denying an agent for generated acts is not a harmless opinion but amounts to pure negation (ta‘ṭīl) and undermines the proof for God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see well-ordered events in your life—like the sunrise, your health, or even your own actions—do you truly recognize them as directly brought into existence by Allah? Or do you sometimes let your mind slip into treating them as mere coincidences or automatic processes? This lesson warns that denying an Agent for such acts is pure negation. How can you train your heart to see Allah's hand in every detail, and what practical step will you take today to do so?
?
Think of a situation where you felt something happened 'by chance' or 'just happened' without deeper meaning. Did your thought at that moment subtly deny Allah's creative involvement? How does this lesson challenge you to repent from such thoughts and replace them with a firm belief that every single event is particularized and brought into existence by Him alone?
?
If you hold a view that includes human free will, are you certain that your understanding does not lead to a form of ta'til? This lesson shows that Thumama's error was denying an agent for some acts, which then invalidated the proof for God. Examine your own heart: do you ever attribute your successes or failures to your own power without simultaneously affirming that Allah is the true Agent? What change in your daily gratitude and reliance does this require?

The Majority Mu'tazilite View on Generated Acts: Will and Blame
Explains the majority Mu'tazilite view on generated acts and moral blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the majority Mu'tazila argue that generated acts are attributable to the human agent in both reality and moral judgment, based on the agent's will and intention.

Core Idea
A generated act is yours because your will shapes it, and blame confirms it.

This passage presents the core Mu'tazilite position on generated acts—actions that are caused indirectly. The majority of Mu'tazila assert that these acts are truly the agent's, both in reality and in terms of moral accountability. The author explains their reasoning using analogies from physical causation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the passage and note the claim: generated acts exist in accordance with the agent's will and intention, just like direct acts.
The agent is the one who performs the cause, not the direct effect.
2
Examine the analogy with direct acts
The author gives examples: if you want a light movement, you give a light push; if a strong movement, a strong push. This shows the role of intention.
3
Distinguish between reality and judgment
Identify the two aspects: in reality (haqiqa), the effect is the agent's act; in judgment (hukm), the agent is blamed and punished for it.
The distinction is crucial for understanding Mu'tazilite causation.
4
Reflect on the moral argument
The author argues that blame proves agency: if wounding were not your act, you would not deserve punishment.
This connects theology to moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما جمهور المعتزلة فإنهم يزعمون أن تلك المعاني في محالها توجد على وفق إرادة فاعل السبب وقصده كما توجد أفعاله القائمة به على وفق إرادته وقصده فإنه إذا أراد أن يتحرك الجسم حركة يسيرة دفعه دفعة خفيفة، ومتى أراد أن يتحرك حركة قوية دفعه دفعة قوية، وكذلك الحال في الألم وذهاب السهم وأشباه ذلك، هذا من حيث الحقيقة، ومن حيث الحكم أن فاعل أسبابها يلام عليها ويعاقب ويعذب عليه ويعاقب فإن الإنسان يعاتب ويعاقب على جرح الغير وإيلامه، ولو لم يكن ذلك فعله لما توجهت عليه اللائمة ولا استحق عليه العقوبة المتلفة أو الناهكة إذ استيجاب ذلك واستحقاقه على فعل الغير محال.
As for the majority of the Mu'tazila, they claim that those meanings in their loci come to exist in accordance with the will and intention of the agent of the cause, just as his acts subsisting in him come to exist in accordance with his will and intention. For if he wants the body to move a slight movement, he pushes it with a slight push; and when he wants it to move a strong movement, he pushes it with a strong push. The same is the case with pain, the flight of the arrow, and similar things. This, in terms of reality (min hayth al-haqiqa). And in terms of judgment (min hayth al-hukm): the agent of their causes is blamed for them, punished, and tortured. For a person is rebuked and punished for wounding another and causing him pain. If that were not his act, blame would not be directed at him, nor would he deserve destructive or severe punishment, since deserving that for the act of another is impossible.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the majority of Mu'tazila (Jumhūr al-Mu‘tazila) on generated acts. They hold that the effects (meanings) in their loci occur according to the will (irāda) and intention (qaṣd) of the agent who causes the cause. The agent's causing is analogous to his direct acts: if he intends a light movement, he gives a light push; if a strong movement, a strong push. Similarly for pain and arrow flight. Then they distinguish between reality (ḥaqīqa) and judgment (ḥukm). In reality, the effect is the act of the agent because it occurs by his will. In terms of judgment, the agent is blameworthy and deserves punishment for the effect (e.g., wounding). If it were not his act, blame would be unjust. This argument is based on moral accountability: people are punished for causing harm, so they must be the agents of that harm.

Key Terms
irāda
qaṣd
ḥaqīqa
ḥukm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila deny that God creates effects, but they affirm God's creation through human will.
Correction: The Mu'tazila hold that generated acts are created by God but are attributed to the human agent because they occur by his will and intention, making him morally responsible.

Checkpoint
According to the majority Mu'tazila, why is a person blameworthy for a generated act like wounding?
A) Because God creates the act directly
B) Because the act occurs according to his will and intention
C) Because the act has no agent
D) Because the act is outside his control

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila ground moral responsibility for generated acts in the agent's will, making the effect his act both in reality and in judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you cause harm indirectly—through a careless word, a reckless decision, or even a push—do you truly accept that it is your act? According to this lesson, your will makes it yours. Are you willing to stand before Allah and own every consequence you set in motion, even unintentional ones?
?
You've blamed someone for a mistake they didn't directly commit, like a teacher blamed for a student's failure. How does this Mu'tazilite argument—that blame is only deserved for one's own act—challenge your habit of shifting responsibility onto others?
?
If you lived today fully convinced that every effect you cause by your will is your deed—whether a kind gesture or a harmful one—how would your actions change before nightfall? What is one concrete step you can take now to align your will with what is good, knowing it will be held against you?

Maturidi Proof Against Generation (Tawallud)
Maturidi proof against tawallud using tamānu‘ argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi proof against generation (tawallud) relies on the impossibility of creative power for anyone other than Allah and the argument of mutual prevention (tamānu‘).

Core Idea
Human acquisition is not creation; calling it so would contradict tawheed.

This lesson examines a key Maturidi argument against the theory of generation (tawallud), which claims that an act performed by a human can generate an effect in another substance. The proof builds on earlier established principles and invokes the powerful tamānu‘ argument to defend divine unity. As you read, notice how the argument connects causality to tawheed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Note that the text cuts off mid-sentence—this is due to a page break.
The argument is incomplete, but the key structure is clear.
2
Identify the two parts of the proof
The passage has two sections: first, a reference to previous proofs that creative power cannot belong to other than Allah; second, a conditional statement beginning with 'Then we say' that begins a second argument.
The second argument is cut off, but its premise is clear.
3
Reflect on the tamānu‘ argument
Notice how the passage uses the proof of mutual prevention (tamānu‘) to argue that if human power could relate to something outside its locus, that would invalidate this proof for tawheed. Why would that be a problem?
The tamānu‘ argument shows that two gods would conflict—so attributing creation to humans is analogous to positing another creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل أهل الحق على نفي التولد: ودليلنا في المسألة ما سبق من الدلائل التي تدل على أن ثبوت قدرة الاختراع لغير الله تعالى محال فإن الاكتساب لذاتها لما يقوم بمحل قدرته لما يوجب إثبات قدرته على اختراع شيء وتعلقها بما ليس في محل قدرته إبطال دلالة التمانع التي هي دلالة توحيد الصانع جل وعلا، وذلك دليل كاف. ثم نقول: إن الحادث في المحل عقيب السبب لو كان فعلاً لفاعل السبب، لكان
The proof of the People of Truth on negating generation (tawallud): And our proof in this issue is what has preceded of proofs that indicate that the establishment of the power of invention (qudrat al-ikhtira') for other than God the Exalted is impossible. For acquisition (iktisab) for its essence, since it subsists in the locus of his power, does not necessitate establishing his power over inventing something, and its relation to that which is not in the locus of his power invalidates the proof of mutual prevention (dalalat al-tamana'), which is the proof of the oneness of the Maker, the Mighty and Majestic. And that proof is sufficient. Then we say: The originated thing in the locus subsequent to the cause, if it were an act of the agent of the cause, it would
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth, i.e., the Maturidis) against the theory of generation (tawallud). They refer to previously established proofs that creative power (qudrat al-ikhtirā') cannot belong to any but God. Human acquisition (iktisāb) of acts is not creative; it does not establish that humans have power to invent something ex nihilo. Moreover, if human power were to relate to something outside its locus (i.e., the effect in another substance), it would invalidate the proof of mutual prevention (dalālat al-tamānu‘), which is the proof for the oneness of the Creator (tawḥīd al-Ṣāni‘). The tamānu‘ argument shows that if there were two gods, they would conflict. Extending human power to external effects would be analogous to positing another creator. Then a second argument begins: if the effect were the act of the agent of the cause, then... (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
qudrat al-ikhtira'
iktisab
dalalat al-tamana'
tawheed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions can generate effects in external objects, and this does not conflict with tawheed.
Correction: The passage shows that attributing creative power (qudrat al-ikhtira‘) to humans would invalidate the tamānu‘ proof of divine unity, so generation (tawallud) is impossible. Human acquisition (iktisāb) is not creative and does not establish such power.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of invoking the proof of mutual prevention (tamana') in the Maturidi argument against generation?
A) To prove the existence of God
B) To show that attributing creative power to humans contradicts tawheed
C) To prove that generated acts have no agent
D) To prove that God creates all acts directly

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi proof against generation argues that accepting human creative power would contradict the tamānu‘ argument for the oneness of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The tamānu‘ argument shows that if two creators existed, the universe would be impossible. How does this affect your understanding of dependence on God? What would it mean for your daily reliance on God if you truly believed that no one else can create anything?

The Disjunction of Power in Generated Acts
Initial disjunction and two reasons against same-power generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial logical disjunction used by Ahl al-Haqq to argue that a generated effect cannot be the act of the agent of the cause, and the two reasons why the same power cannot govern both cause and effect.

Core Idea
A single originated power cannot relate to two distinct objects.

Imam al-Nasafi begins the core proof against tawallud by setting up a logical dilemma. He considers the possibility that the agent of the cause also has power over the effect. If so, either it is the same power or a different power. He then shows why the same power is impossible, using two decisive reasons. Pay close attention to the structure of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the disjunction carefully
Notice that the author presents a stark either-or: the agent either has power over the effect or not. If not, it's not his act. If yes, then either the same power as the cause or a new power.
This is a standard kalam technique—exhausting possibilities.
2
Identify the two reasons against the same power
The author gives two explicit reasons why the same power cannot relate to both the cause and the effect. Read each reason and understand its basis.
Reason 1: one power, one object. Reason 2: power cannot precede its object.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: if the generated effect required a new power, that would sever its dependence on the cause. The author will address this in the next lesson.

ودليلنا في المسألة ما سبق من الدلائل التي تدل على أن ثبوت قدرة الاختراع لغير الله تعالى محال فإن الاكتساب لذاتها ( ٦) لما يقوم بمحل قدرته لما يوجب إثبات قدرته على اختراع شيء وتعلقها بما ليس في محل قدرته إبطال دلالة التمانع التي هي دلالة توحيد الصانع جل وعلا، وذلك دليل كاف. ثم نقول : إن الحادث في المحل عقيب السبب لو كان فعلاً لفاعل السبب، لكان لا يخلو ؛ إما أن كانت له عليه قدرة، وإما أن لم تكن عليه قدرة، فإن لم تكن تعلقت قدرته به فلا يكون فعلاً له، وإن تعلقت قدرته به لكان لا يخلو إما أن تعلقت به القدرة التي تعلقت بالسبب، وإما أن تعلقت به قدرة أخرى، ولا وجه إلى أن يقال تعلقت به عين هذه القدرة لوجهين: (۱) أحدهما: أن تعلق قدرة واحدة محدثة بمقدورين متجانسين أو متضادين أو مختلفين محال إذ كل قدره محدثه لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور واحد ، وكذا كل علم محدث أكثر أصحابنا لا يتعلق إلا بمعلوم واحد. والوجه (۲) الثاني: أن القدرة على الضرب سابقة على الألم وقوة الرمي سابقة على مرور السهم والإصابة والجرح والآلام والموت، وقد مر أن سبق القدرة المحدثة على المقدور محال (۳) ولا (٤) وجه أن يقال بأن ذلك المعنى يوجد بقدرة أخرى سوى قدرة سببه هي قدرة عليها، لأنه لو كان كذلك لما كان ذلك المعنى في المحل حاصلاً عن السبب المتقدم، ولكان القادر ممكنا من تحصيله بلا تحصيل سبب (0) كما يقدر على تحصيل كل كسب له في حيز قدرته من غير تقدم سبب، وحيث استحال ذلك دل أنه لم يقدر عليه بقدرة هي قدرة عليه على الخصوص، ولأنه لو حصل بقدرة أخرى لصلح أن يقدر على ضده بدلاً عنه وقت وجوده بعين تلك القدرة على قول من يقول : إن الاستطاعة تصلح للضدين وبقدرة سوى هذه بدلاً عنها صالحة لضده، وحيث استحال أن يوصف بالقدرة على تسكين السهم أو الحجر بعد الرمي أو الإرسال وتخليق اللذة في بدن المضروب والمجروح بعد الضرب.
Our proof in this issue is what preceded of proofs that indicate that the establishment of a power of invention for other than God—exalted is He—is impossible. For acquisition (al-iktisāb) of its essence, since it subsists in the locus of his power, necessitates proving his power over inventing something, and its relation to what is not in the locus of his power invalidates the proof of mutual exclusion (al-tamānuʿ), which is the proof of the oneness of the Maker, may He be glorified and exalted. And that is a sufficient proof. Then we say: If the event that occurs in the locus after the cause were an act of the agent of the cause, it would not be free: either he has power over it, or he does not have power over it. If his power is not related to it, then it is not his act. But if his power is related to it, then it is not free: either the power that was related to the cause is related to it, or another power is related to it. And there is no way to say that the very same power is related to it, for two reasons: (1) One of them: that the relation of one originated power to two objects—whether similar, opposite, or different—is impossible, for every originated power is related only to one power. Likewise, every originated knowledge, according to most of our scholars, is related only to one known thing. (2) The second reason: that the power over the striking precedes the pain, and the force of the throwing precedes the flight of the arrow, the hitting, the wounding, the pains, and the death. It has already passed that the precedence of an originated power over its object is impossible. And there is no way to say that that meaning exists by another power besides the power of its cause, which is a power over it, because if it were so, that meaning would not be obtained in the locus from the preceding cause; and the powerful agent would be able to obtain it without obtaining the cause, just as he is able to obtain every acquisition of his within the scope of his power without the precedence of a cause. And since that is impossible, it indicates that he has not been empowered over it by a power that is specifically a power over it. And because if it were obtained by another power, it would be valid for him to have power over its opposite instead of it at the time of its existence by that same power, according to the one who says that capability (al-istiṭāʿa) is valid for opposites, and by a power other than this instead of it, valid for its opposite. And since it is impossible for him to be described as having power over stopping the arrow or the stone after the throwing or releasing, and over creating pleasure in the body of the beaten and wounded after the beating.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) against the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud (generation of acts). The proof begins by referencing earlier arguments that non-God cannot have the power of invention (ikhtira'). It then presents a detailed logical argument: If the effect occurring after the cause (e.g., pain after a blow) were the act of the agent of the cause, then the agent must have power over it. If he has no power, it is not his act. If he has power, then either it is the same power that related to the cause, or a different power. The same power cannot relate to two different objects (the cause and the effect), because a single power relates to only one object. Moreover, the power over the cause (e.g., the push) precedes the effect (e.g., pain), but a power cannot precede its object (the act must be simultaneous). The effect cannot be by a separate new power either, because then it would not be dependent on the cause, and the agent could produce it without the cause. Also, if it were by a separate power, the agent could produce the opposite of the effect at the same time, which is impossible. Therefore, the effect cannot be the act of the agent of the cause. This refutes tawallud.

مقدور
سبق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for a single originated power to produce two different events, one after the other (e.g., a single power causing both a throw and the resulting pain).
Correction: An originated power is tied to a single object and cannot precede its object; thus the same power cannot govern events occurring at different times.

Checkpoint
According to the first reason, why can't the same power relate to both the cause and the generated effect?
A) Because the power would need to be infinite
B) Because an originated power can only relate to one object
C) Because the effect is not within the agent's capacity
D) Because the cause and effect are opposites

Key Takeaway
A single originated power cannot relate to two distinct objects, and it cannot precede its object, so the same power cannot produce both a cause and its generated effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your actions, do you assume that you have full power over their outcomes? In what area of your life today have you arrogated to yourself a power that belongs only to Allah?
?
If your created power is limited to one object at a time, how should that change the way you approach your daily tasks? Will you commit to acknowledging Allah's sovereignty over every result that follows your efforts?
?
The passage teaches that a single power cannot produce two distinct events. Think of a time you tried to control both the cause and its effect. How did that reveal a hidden belief that you are the ultimate agent? What step can you take tonight to repent of that assumption?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Refuting Generation by a Separate Power
Refutation of the alternative that the generated effect is by a new power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the generated effect cannot be produced by a new power distinct from the cause's power, and how this completes the refutation of tawallud.

Core Idea
If the effect came from a separate power, it would not depend on the cause.

Having ruled out the same power, Imam al-Nasafi now examines the alternative: that the generated effect arises from a different power, one specifically delegated for that effect. He argues that this would sever the necessary connection between cause and effect, making the effect independent of the cause—a position he shows to be absurd. The lesson also includes a final reductio about simultaneous opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the dismissal of the separate-power hypothesis
The author first denies that the effect can come from a power other than the cause's power, then provides two arguments: loss of causal dependence and the possibility of producing the opposite at the same time.
2
Understand the first argument: loss of dependence
If the effect were by a separate power, the agent could produce it without the cause. But that is impossible because the effect is observed to depend on the cause.
This highlights the causal link that tawallud attempts to explain.
3
Understand the second argument: simultaneous opposites
If a separate power were responsible for the effect, the agent could, in principle, produce its opposite at the same time using that same power (according to the view that capability is for opposites). But we never see that, proving the power is not over the effect.
4
Connect to the overall proof
Realize that both possibilities (same power and separate power) have been refuted, so the generated effect cannot be an act of the agent. This systematically refutes tawallud.
The conclusion is that only Allah creates all originated things.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
Why a Single Power Cannot Produce Both Cause and Effect
Nasafi presents a dichotomy and two reasons why the same power cannot produce both cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single created power cannot be connected to both a cause and its delayed effect, and why this refutes the attribution of posthumous effects to an agent.

Core Idea
A created power cannot span two objects or precede its own effect.

Nasafi begins by laying out a logical dichotomy: either the agent has power over the effect or not. If not, the effect is not his act. If yes, then the power in question must be either the same as the power connected to the cause or a different one. In this passage, he explains why it cannot be the same power, giving two fundamental reasons rooted in the nature of created power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Nasafi's argument unfold.
Notice the logical structure: dichotomy first, then two reasons.
2
Identify the dichotomy
Find the two cases: either the agent has power over the effect or not. Understand the consequence of each.
If no power, it's not his act. If power, then we examine the type of power.
3
Understand the first reason (dual object)
Nasafi argues that a single created power cannot be connected to two objects simultaneously. Why? Because every created power is limited to one object. Created knowledge also knows only one known at a time.
This applies whether the objects are similar, opposite, or different.
4
Understand the second reason (precedence)
The power to strike or throw precedes the effect (pain, wound, death). But it has been established that a created power cannot precede its object—the effect must be simultaneous with the power. Thus, the same power cannot produce both the cause and the later effect.
This refutes the idea that the same power can span a time gap.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخلو ؛ إما أن كانت له عليه قدرة، وإما أن لم تكن عليه قدرة، فإن لم تكن تعلقت قدرته به فلا يكون فعلاً له، وإن تعلقت قدرته به لكان لا يخلو إما أن تعلقت به القدرة التي تعلقت بالسبب، وإما أن تعلقت به قدرة أخرى، ولا وجه إلى أن يقال تعلقت به عين هذه القدرة لوجهين: أحدهما: أن تعلق قدرة واحدة محدثة بمقدورين متجانسين أو متضادين أو مختلفين محال إذ كل قدرة محدثه لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور واحد ، وكذا كل علم محدث أكثر أصحابنا لا يتعلق إلا بمعلوم واحد. والوجه الثاني: أن القدرة على الضرب سابقة على الألم وقوة الرمي سابقة على مرور السهم والإصابة والجرح والآلام والموت، وقد مر أن سبق القدرة المحدثة على المقدور محال ولا وجه أن يقال بأن ذلك المعنى يوجد بقدرة أخرى سوى قدرة سببه هي قدرة عليها، لأنه لو كان كذلك لما كان ذلك المعنى في المحل حاصلاً عن السبب المتقدم، ولكان القادر ممكنا من تحصيله بلا تحصيل السبب كما يقدر على تحصيل كل كسب له في حيز قدرته من غير تقدم سبب، وحيث استحال ذلك دل أنه لم يقدر عليه بقدرة هي قدرة عليه على الخصوص، ولأنه لو حصل بقدرة أخرى لصلح أن يقدر على ضده بدلاً عنه وقت وجوده بعين تلك القدرة على قول من يقول : إن الاستطاعة تصلح للضدين وبقدرة سوى هذه بدلاً عنها صالحة لضده، وحيث استحال أن يوصف بالقدرة على تسكين السهم أو الحجر بعد الرمي أو الإرسال وتخليق اللذة في بدن المضروب والمجروح بعد الضرب والجرح وتحصيل الحياة بعد الجرح الفاحش بدلاً عن الموت؛ دل أنه لا يجوز أن يقدر عليه بقدرة خاصة له ولأنه لو قدر على إيجاد حركة أو سكون في جسم يقرب منه من غير اعتماد عليه واتصال به لقدر أن يفعل ذلك في جسم ببغداد وهو ببخارى، أو جسم بأقصى المغرب، ولصحت قدرته على إيجاد ذلك في أي شاء وذلك ظاهر الفساد، وإذا بطل أن يكون قادرًا على ذلك المعنى بقدرة سببه وبقدرة أخرى سواها بطل أن يكون قادرًا عليه أصلاً، ولأنا نعلم جواز موت الرامي بعد رميه قبل الإصابة وبعدما مات لا يتصور منه قدرة ولا علم، فلو كان ما يوجد من الأفعال بعد ذلك أفعالاً له وهي محكمة متقنه لجاز خلو كل فعل محكم عن قدرة فاعله وعلمه وحياته ولجاز حصوله بموات عاجز جاهل، وقد مر هذا، والله الموفق. شبه الخصوم واهية]
It cannot be free from: either he had power over it, or he did not have power over it. If his power was not connected to it, then it is not his act. If his power was connected to it, then it cannot be free from either that the power connected to the cause is connected to it, or another power is connected to it. There is no way to say that this very power is connected to it for two reasons: First, that the connection of one created power to two objects, whether similar, opposite, or different, is impossible, since every created power is connected only to one object; similarly, every created knowledge according to most of our scholars is connected only to one known. Second, that the power to strike precedes the pain, and the force of throwing precedes the passage of the arrow, the hitting, the wound, the pains, and death. It has already been mentioned that the precedence of created power over its object is impossible. There is no way to say that that occurrence exists by another power besides the power of its cause, which is a power over it, because if that were the case, that occurrence would not arise in the subject from the preceding cause, and the powerful agent would be able to produce it without bringing about the cause, just as he is able to produce every acquisition of his within the sphere of his power without a preceding cause. Since that is impossible, it indicates that he did not have power over it by a power that is specifically a power over it. And because if it occurred by another power, he would be able to will its opposite instead of it at the time of its existence by that same power, according to the one who says that capability suits opposites, and by another power instead of this one that suits its opposite. Since it is impossible that he can be described as having power to stop the arrow or stone after throwing or releasing, and to create pleasure in the body of the struck or wounded after the blow and wound, and to produce life after a severe wound instead of death, it indicates that it is not permissible that he has power over it by a specific power. And because if he had power to produce motion or rest in a body close to him without leaning on it and without contact with it, he would be able to do that to a body in Baghdad while he is in Bukhara, or to a body in the far West, and his power would be valid to produce that in anything he wishes, and that is clearly corrupt. And if it is invalid that he has power over that occurrence by the power of its cause and by another power besides it, then it is invalid that he has power over it at all. And because we know the permissibility of the archer's death after his shot before the hitting, and after he dies, no power or knowledge can be conceived from him. So if the acts that occur after that were his acts, and they are precise and well-ordered, it would be permissible that every precise act be free of the power, knowledge, and life of its doer, and it would be permissible that it occurs from an inanimate, incapable, ignorant thing. This has already been mentioned, and God grants success. The sophistries of the opponents are invalid.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the view that effects occurring after an agent's death can be attributed to that agent, or that power can operate without direct connection. Nasafi begins by laying out a dichotomy: either the agent has power over the effect or not. If not, it is not his act. If yes, then the power connected to the cause must either be the same power connected to the effect, or a different power. He rejects the possibility that it is the same power, giving two reasons. First, a single created power cannot be connected to two objects simultaneously, whether those objects are similar, opposite, or different; just as created knowledge (according to most Maturidi scholars) knows only one object at a time. Second, the power to strike precedes the pain, and the force of throwing precedes the arrow's flight, hitting, wounding, and death. But it is already established that a created power cannot precede its object (the effect must be simultaneous with the power). Therefore, the effect (e.g., pain, wound, death) cannot be produced by the same power that preceded it. Nasafi then considers the possibility that a different power, besides the power of the cause, produces the effect. He refutes this by noting that if such a separate power existed, the effect would not follow directly from the preceding cause, and the agent could produce the effect without the cause—which is absurd. Moreover, if a different power produced the effect, the agent could (according to some) produce its opposite at the same time with the same power, which is impossible. He gives concrete examples: no one can stop an arrow or stone after release, nor create pleasure after a blow, nor restore life after a fatal wound—indicating that no such separate power exists. He then extends the argument to distance: if power could act without direct contact, one could move objects in distant cities like Baghdad while in Bukhara, which is clearly false. Having disproven both alternatives—that the cause's power produces the effect and that another power does—Nasafi concludes that the agent simply has no power over such posthumous or distant effects. He supports this with the example of an archer who dies after shooting but before hitting the target. If the arrow's hitting were still his act, then it would be possible for a precise act to occur without the agent's power, knowledge, and life, which is absurd. Thus the opponents' objections are invalid.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
مقدور (object of power)
سبب (cause)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a single power can produce multiple effects spread over time, as long as they are causally linked.
Correction: Nasafi shows this is impossible because a created power cannot be connected to two objects simultaneously, nor can it precede its object. The power and its effect must be simultaneous and one-to-one.

Checkpoint
What are the two reasons Nasafi gives against the same power being connected to both the cause and the later effect?
A) Power cannot be connected to two objects, and power cannot precede its object.
B) Power is eternal, and effect is temporal.
C) The cause and effect are different in kind, so different powers are needed.
D) The agent loses power after the cause.

Key Takeaway
A single created power cannot be connected to two objects nor precede its object; therefore, the same power cannot produce both a cause and a delayed effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your single intention to do something good (like giving charity) will automatically produce multiple blessings or effects in the future? How does this lesson’s reminder that your created power can only be connected to one action at a time challenge you to focus on the present act rather than its imagined outcomes?
?
Think of a time you blamed yourself for a delayed consequence of your action—perhaps a harsh word that caused long-term hurt. How might this lesson free you from the burden of thinking your power caused that consequence, and instead lead you to rely on Allah as the sole Creator of all effects?
?
When you sin, do you sometimes feel that your power alone produced the sin and its entire chain of harm? How does recognizing the limit of your power—that it cannot precede its object—humble you and drive you to seek Allah’s forgiveness for the sin itself, without exaggerating your own causal role?

Refuting the Existence of a Separate Power for the Effect
Nasafi refutes the possibility of a separate power for the effect and concludes that no power exists over posthumous effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a separate power for the effect is impossible, and how concrete examples demonstrate that posthumous effects cannot be attributed to the agent.

Core Idea
If a separate power could produce the effect, causation and agency would become absurd.

Having shown that the same power cannot produce both cause and effect, Nasafi now considers the possibility that a different, separate power produces the effect. He refutes this with a series of arguments and concrete examples, culminating in the famous archer example that illustrates the absurdity of attributing effects after the agent's death.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. This is the second part of the argument, starting from 'There is no way to say...'.
Notice how Nasafi systematically eliminates each possibility.
2
Understand the refutation of a separate power
Nasafi argues that if a separate power produced the effect, the effect would not follow from the cause, and the agent could produce the effect without the cause. This is impossible.
This establishes that the effect cannot be due to a power other than the cause's power.
3
Examine the examples
Nasafi gives concrete cases: no one can stop an arrow after release, create pleasure after a blow, or restore life after a fatal wound. These show that no such separate power exists.
These examples illustrate the impossibility of controlling effects after the initial action.
4
Understand the distance argument
If power could act without direct contact, one could affect objects far away (e.g., in Baghdad from Bukhara). This is clearly false.
This shows that power requires immediate connection or proximity.
5
Understand the archer example and conclusion
An archer may die after shooting but before hitting. If the hit were still his act, it would be an act without power, knowledge, or life—absurd. Therefore, posthumous effects cannot be attributed to the agent.
This is the decisive refutation of the opponents' view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخلو ؛ إما أن كانت له عليه قدرة، وإما أن لم تكن عليه قدرة، فإن لم تكن تعلقت قدرته به فلا يكون فعلاً له، وإن تعلقت قدرته به لكان لا يخلو إما أن تعلقت به القدرة التي تعلقت بالسبب، وإما أن تعلقت به قدرة أخرى، ولا وجه إلى أن يقال تعلقت به عين هذه القدرة لوجهين: أحدهما: أن تعلق قدرة واحدة محدثة بمقدورين متجانسين أو متضادين أو مختلفين محال إذ كل قدرة محدثه لا تتعلق إلا بمقدور واحد ، وكذا كل علم محدث أكثر أصحابنا لا يتعلق إلا بمعلوم واحد. والوجه الثاني: أن القدرة على الضرب سابقة على الألم وقوة الرمي سابقة على مرور السهم والإصابة والجرح والآلام والموت، وقد مر أن سبق القدرة المحدثة على المقدور محال ولا وجه أن يقال بأن ذلك المعنى يوجد بقدرة أخرى سوى قدرة سببه هي قدرة عليها، لأنه لو كان كذلك لما كان ذلك المعنى في المحل حاصلاً عن السبب المتقدم، ولكان القادر ممكنا من تحصيله بلا تحصيل السبب كما يقدر على تحصيل كل كسب له في حيز قدرته من غير تقدم سبب، وحيث استحال ذلك دل أنه لم يقدر عليه بقدرة هي قدرة عليه على الخصوص، ولأنه لو حصل بقدرة أخرى لصلح أن يقدر على ضده بدلاً عنه وقت وجوده بعين تلك القدرة على قول من يقول : إن الاستطاعة تصلح للضدين وبقدرة سوى هذه بدلاً عنها صالحة لضده، وحيث استحال أن يوصف بالقدرة على تسكين السهم أو الحجر بعد الرمي أو الإرسال وتخليق اللذة في بدن المضروب والمجروح بعد الضرب والجرح وتحصيل الحياة بعد الجرح الفاحش بدلاً عن الموت؛ دل أنه لا يجوز أن يقدر عليه بقدرة خاصة له ولأنه لو قدر على إيجاد حركة أو سكون في جسم يقرب منه من غير اعتماد عليه واتصال به لقدر أن يفعل ذلك في جسم ببغداد وهو ببخارى، أو جسم بأقصى المغرب، ولصحت قدرته على إيجاد ذلك في أي شاء وذلك ظاهر الفساد، وإذا بطل أن يكون قادرًا على ذلك المعنى بقدرة سببه وبقدرة أخرى سواها بطل أن يكون قادرًا عليه أصلاً، ولأنا نعلم جواز موت الرامي بعد رميه قبل الإصابة وبعدما مات لا يتصور منه قدرة ولا علم، فلو كان ما يوجد من الأفعال بعد ذلك أفعالاً له وهي محكمة متقنه لجاز خلو كل فعل محكم عن قدرة فاعله وعلمه وحياته ولجاز حصوله بموات عاجز جاهل، وقد مر هذا، والله الموفق. شبه الخصوم واهية]
It cannot be free from: either he had power over it, or he did not have power over it. If his power was not connected to it, then it is not his act. If his power was connected to it, then it cannot be free from either that the power connected to the cause is connected to it, or another power is connected to it. There is no way to say that this very power is connected to it for two reasons: First, that the connection of one created power to two objects, whether similar, opposite, or different, is impossible, since every created power is connected only to one object; similarly, every created knowledge according to most of our scholars is connected only to one known. Second, that the power to strike precedes the pain, and the force of throwing precedes the passage of the arrow, the hitting, the wound, the pains, and death. It has already been mentioned that the precedence of created power over its object is impossible. There is no way to say that that occurrence exists by another power besides the power of its cause, which is a power over it, because if that were the case, that occurrence would not arise in the subject from the preceding cause, and the powerful agent would be able to produce it without bringing about the cause, just as he is able to produce every acquisition of his within the sphere of his power without a preceding cause. Since that is impossible, it indicates that he did not have power over it by a power that is specifically a power over it. And because if it occurred by another power, he would be able to will its opposite instead of it at the time of its existence by that same power, according to the one who says that capability suits opposites, and by another power instead of this one that suits its opposite. Since it is impossible that he can be described as having power to stop the arrow or stone after throwing or releasing, and to create pleasure in the body of the struck or wounded after the blow and wound, and to produce life after a severe wound instead of death, it indicates that it is not permissible that he has power over it by a specific power. And because if he had power to produce motion or rest in a body close to him without leaning on it and without contact with it, he would be able to do that to a body in Baghdad while he is in Bukhara, or to a body in the far West, and his power would be valid to produce that in anything he wishes, and that is clearly corrupt. And if it is invalid that he has power over that occurrence by the power of its cause and by another power besides it, then it is invalid that he has power over it at all. And because we know the permissibility of the archer's death after his shot before the hitting, and after he dies, no power or knowledge can be conceived from him. So if the acts that occur after that were his acts, and they are precise and well-ordered, it would be permissible that every precise act be free of the power, knowledge, and life of its doer, and it would be permissible that it occurs from an inanimate, incapable, ignorant thing. This has already been mentioned, and God grants success. The sophistries of the opponents are invalid.

Commentary
This passage is a proof refuting the view that effects occurring after an agent's death can be attributed to that agent, or that power can operate without direct connection. Misconception: One might think that a separate power in the subject (e.g., the target) produces the effect independently of the agent.
Correction: Nasafi shows this is impossible because it would break causal continuity and lead to absurd consequences, such as the agent being able to produce the effect without the cause.

Actions After Death Cannot Be Attributed to the Deceased
Al-Nasafi argues that post-death actions cannot be attributed to a deceased agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why actions occurring after an agent's death cannot be attributed to that agent, based on rational proof.

Core Idea
A dead agent cannot be the true agent of any action.

This passage presents a powerful rational argument against attributing posthumous effects to a deceased agent. Using the example of an archer who dies before the arrow hits, Al-Nasafi demonstrates the logical contradiction in claiming that actions after death belong to the deceased. Understanding this argument helps clarify the nature of agency and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its flow, then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage begins with a reference to a previous discussion about wounds and life.
The opening 'والجرح وتحصيل الحياة' connects to an earlier argument.
2
Identify the archer example
Find the part about the archer (الرامي) who dies after shooting but before the arrow hits. Note that after death, no power or knowledge can be imagined from him. Then ask: can the arrow's motion be his action?
3
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
Follow Al-Nasafi's logic: if the post-death motion were the archer's action, then precise actions could occur without power, knowledge, or life in the agent, which is absurd. Understand how this refutes the opponents' claim.
This is the core proof.
4
Understand the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that the opponents' specious arguments (شبه الخصوم) are invalid. Reflect on how this argument guards against attributing causal efficacy to the dead.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجرح وتحصيل الحياة بعد الجراح الفاحش بدلاً عن الموت؛ دل أنه لا يجوز أن يقدر عليه بقدرة خاصة له ولأنه لو قدر على إيجاد حركة أو سكون في جسم يقرب منه من غير اعتماد عليه واتصال به لقدر أن يفعل ذلك في جسم ببغداد وهو ببخارى، أو جسم بأقصى المغرب، ولصحت قدرته على إيجاد ذلك في أي شاء وذلك ظاهر الفساد، وإذا بطل أن يكون قادرًا على ذلك المعنى بقدرة سببه وبقدرة أخرى سواها بطل أن يكون قادرًا عليه أصلاً، ولأنا نعلم جواز موت الرامي بعد رميه قبل الإصابة وبعدما مات لا يتصور منه قدرة ولا علم، فلو كان ما يوجد من الأفعال بعد ذلك أفعالاً له وهي محكمة متقنه لجاز خلو كل فعل محكم عن قدرة فاعله وعلمه وحياته ولجاز حصوله بموات عاجز جاهل، وقد مر هذا، والله الموفق. شبه الخصوم واهية
And the wound and the acquisition of life after the severe wound instead of death; it indicates that it is not permissible that a body be empowered over it by a power specific to it, and because if he were able to produce motion or stillness in a body close to him without relying on it and without connection to it, he would be able to do that in a body in Baghdad while he is in Bukhara, or in a body in the far west, and his power would be valid to produce that in whatever he wills, and that is obviously corrupt. And if it is invalidated that he be capable of that meaning by a power of its cause and by another power besides it, it is invalidated that he be capable of it at all. And because we know the possibility of the archer's death after his shot before the target is hit, and after he dies, no power or knowledge can be imagined from him. So if what exists of actions after that were his actions, and they are precise and well-ordered, it would be permissible for every precise action to be devoid of its agent's power, knowledge, and life, and it would be permissible for it to occur from a lifeless, powerless, ignorant thing. And this has been mentioned, and Allah is the Guide. The specious arguments of the opponents are invalid.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof that actions occurring after an agent's death cannot be attributed to that agent. The author begins by referencing a prior example about wounds and life, then argues that if a person could produce motion in a nearby body without direct connection, he could do so at any distance, which is absurd. Then he refutes the possibility of a power that is not based on direct connection. The core proof uses the example of an archer who dies after shooting but before the arrow hits; since the dead archer has no power or knowledge, the arrow's motion cannot be his action. If such actions were his, they would be effects without a capable agent, leading to absurdity. The author concludes that the opponents' specious arguments (shubah) are invalid.

Key Terms

محكمة متقنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an agent set something in motion before death, the motion after death is still attributable to that agent as its 'effect'.
Correction: The archer example shows that after death, the agent lacks power and knowledge, so any subsequent motion cannot be his action. Attributing it to him would imply that precise effects can arise without an intelligent cause.

Refuting Rational Analogies on Divine Custom
Al-Nasafi refutes rational analogies by showing absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi uses reductio ad absurdum to refute the opponents' claim that divine custom follows human causes.

Core Idea
If divine custom were tied to human actions, mountains would fly upon a gesture.

Al-Nasafi now tackles the rational arguments of his opponents. He shows that if divine custom (ʿāda) were to operate in accordance with human intentions, then absurdities would follow, such as attributing the creation of motion in mountains to a person's standing. This exposes the weakness of the opponents' analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice how Al-Nasafi begins by dismissing the rational specious arguments as weak.
2
Identify the opponents' premise
The opponents assume that divine custom (ʿāda) creates effects subsequent to human actions, implying a causal link.
3
List the absurd examples
Al-Nasafi gives several examples (stone, mountain, pain, flying mountains, collapsing heavens) to show the logical consequences of the opponents' premise.
Each example leads to an attribution of divine acts to humans, which is absurd.
4
Understand the logical conclusion
The opponents' premise leads to patent ignorance; if they reject the examples, they invalidate their own proof.
This establishes the weakness of the opponents' rational analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما تعلقوا به من الشبه المعقولة فواهية لما أن خصومهم لا يسلمون حصول ذلك على موافقة قصده لقصده بإرادة الصانع جل وعلا وإجرائه العادة أنه يفعل ذلك عقيب صنع العبد في حيز قدرته لما مر من دليل استحالة ذلك أن يوجد بقدرة العبد، ثم يجب على هذا الاعتلال أن الله تعالى لو أجرى العادة عند مباينة العبد الحجر أن يخلق في الحجر صعودًا أو أجرى العادة أن يخلق عند قيامه حركة في الجبل أن يكون ذلك فعل العبد، فلو أجرى العادة أن يخلق الألم الشديد عند الضرب الخفيف، والألم الخفيف عند الضرب الشديد أن يكون كل واحد منهما مسببًا لما تقدم من السبب فإن مروا على هذا تجاهلوا ويلزموا أشياء يزداد ظهور تجاهلهم من نحو أن يقال: لو أجرى الله العادة بطيران الجبال في السماء ومرها في الهواء مر السحاب عقب إشارة الناس إليها وبتهافت السماوات السبع عند أكل الناس ورجوعها إلى مكانها عند امتناعهم عن ذلك أن يكون كل ذلك صنع فاعلي هذه الأسباب وهذا تجاهل، وإن أبوا هذا نقضوا دليلهم، ويجب بهذا أن يكون حصول السمن في الطفل عند حسن قيام الحاضنة بتربيته وحصول السمن في الدابة عند قيام السائس بمصالحها ومراعاة وقت الغذاء والعلف والسقي لها من فعل الحاضنة والسائس وكذا حصول الزروع والبقول والرياحين وأغصان الأشجار والزراجين عند قيام الزراع بسقيها وتسميدها وتشذيبها يوجب أن يكون ذلك فعلاً لها، ويكون للعباد قدرة تخليق الأجسام وهذا الحاد ظاهر. وما ألزم من فصل الأحكام فكل ذلك متعلق باكتسابه ما حل بمحل قدرته من الفعل الذي جعله الله
As for what they cling to of rational specious arguments, they are weak because their opponents do not concede that it occurs in accordance with his intention by the will of the Maker, Exalted and High, and His carrying out the custom that He does that subsequent to the servant's act within his sphere of power, due to the previous proof of the impossibility of that existing by the servant's power. Then, according to this reasoning, if Allah, Exalted, were to carry out the custom that upon the servant's separating from the stone He creates ascending in the stone, or carry out the custom that upon his standing He creates motion in the mountain, that would be the servant's act. So if He were to carry out the custom that He creates intense pain upon a light hit, and light pain upon a severe hit, that each of them would be caused by the preceding cause. If they proceed on this, they become ignorant, and they commit to things that increase the manifestation of their ignorance, such as if it is said: If Allah were to carry out the custom of mountains flying in the sky and their passing in the air like clouds following people's pointing to them, and the collapsing of the seven heavens upon people's eating and their return to their place upon their refraining from that, that all of that would be the making of the doers of these causes—and this is ignorance. And if they reject this, they invalidate their own proof. And according to this, it would be necessary that the occurrence of fatness in a child upon the good care of the nurse in raising him, and the occurrence of fatness in the animal upon the caretaker's management of its interests and attention to the time of food, fodder, and watering for it, be the act of the nurse and the caretaker. Likewise, the occurrence of crops, greens, fragrant plants, branches of trees, and vines upon the farmer's watering, fertilizing, and pruning them would necessitate that that be their act, and that servants have the power of creating bodies—and this innovation is manifest. And whatever is imposed concerning the separation of judgments, all of that is related to his acquisition (kasb) of what occurs in the locus of his power from the act which Allah has made...
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage refutes the opponents' rational analogies (shubah ma'qula) that attempt to attribute effects to human agents based on divine custom (ʿāda). The author argues that the opponents' own premises would lead to absurd consequences. For example, if God's custom were to create pain based on the intensity of a hit, then a light hit with intense pain would be attributed to the striker. Similarly, if flying mountains or collapsing heavens followed human gestures or eating, those would be attributed to humans, which is patent ignorance. The argument extends to everyday examples like a nurse causing a child's fatness or a farmer causing crops to grow, which would imply that humans have the power of creation (takhliq al-ajsām), a view the author deems manifestly false. The conclusion ties back to the doctrine of kasb (acquisition), where human acts are acquired from Allah's creation.

Key Terms
شبه معقولة
إجراء العادة
سبب
مسبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine custom (ʿāda) implies a necessary causal link between human actions and subsequent effects.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that such a link would lead to absurd consequences like attributing the creation of motion in mountains to a person's standing. The true doctrine is that Allah creates directly and humans only acquire (kasb).

Checkpoint
Which absurd consequence does Al-Nasafi mention to refute the opponents' view of divine custom?
A) That plants could grow without water.
B) That mountains could fly upon human gesture.
C) That angels create human actions.
D) That Allah creates all actions directly.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the opponents by demonstrating that attributing effects to humans based on divine custom leads to absurdities, thereby invalidating their premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a good outcome after your effort, do you truly believe it is from Allah alone, or do you subconsciously attribute it to your own action? How would this passage correct your inclination to claim ownership of outcomes?
?
This passage shows that if divine custom were tied to human acts, absurdities would result. How often do you assume that your actions have necessary effects, forgetting that Allah is the real creator? What is one habit you can change today to align with this tawhidic understanding?
?
Al-Nasafi calls attributing such events to humans 'ignorance.' In what areas of your life are you still attributing results to your own power rather than Allah's will? What would it mean to live with the constant awareness that no cause is independent of Allah?

Everyday Examples and the Doctrine of Kasb
Al-Nasafi uses everyday examples to argue against attributing creative power to humans and affirms kasb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how everyday examples of causes (nurse, farmer) are used to argue that only Allah creates bodies, and human action is a form of kasb (acquisition).

Core Idea
Attributing a child's fatness to the nurse falsely implies humans create bodies.

Al-Nasafi now extends his argument to everyday situations. If a nurse's good care causes fatness in a child, or a farmer's watering causes crops to grow, then by the opponents' logic, these humans would be creators of bodies. This is manifestly false, affirming that all creation is from Allah and humans only acquire (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to follow the argument.
Note that the passage ends mid-sentence; the conclusion about kasb is implied.
2
Analyze the everyday examples
Identify the examples: a nurse causing a child's fatness, a caretaker causing an animal's fatness, and a farmer causing crops to grow.
3
Trace the logical implication: takhliq al-ajsam
If these human actions are considered causes, then humans would have the power of creating bodies (takhliq al-ajsam), which Al-Nasafi calls manifest innovation.
4
Conclude with the doctrine of kasb
The passage ends by relating all such actions to the servant's acquisition (kasb) from Allah's creation.
This affirms that Allah is the sole creator and human actions are acquired.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Refutation of Mu'tazilite Generated Acts: Reductio ad Absurdum
Al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite claim and refutes it via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the Mu'tazilite theory that human acts generate effects by God's custom.

Core Idea
If a human act could generate an effect by God's custom, then absurdities would follow, proving the theory false.

The Mu'tazilites argued that human acts can generate effects through God's established custom. Imam al-Nasafi begins his refutation by showing that this claim leads to logical absurdities. By examining what would follow if God's custom were to create effects after human acts, he demonstrates that the theory is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Nasafi starts by stating the Mu'tazilite claim and then immediately attacks it.
2
Identify the reductio
Al-Nasafi uses a conditional argument: if God's custom were to create an effect after a human act, then absurd consequences would follow. Find the specific examples he gives.
The examples include stone ascent, mountain movement, and pain mismatches.
3
Trace the absurdities
List the absurd consequences al-Nasafi derives: severe pain from light striking, mountains flying, heavens falling, etc. Understand why these are absurd from a theological perspective.
These absurdities show that the Mu'tazilite view cannot be consistently maintained.
4
Understand the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that if the Mu'tazilites accept these absurdities, they are ignorant; if they reject them, they refute their own proof. This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
The conclusion is that the Mu'tazilite theory is internally incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما تعلقوا به من الشبه المعقولة فواهية لما أن خصومهم لا يسلمون حصول ذلك على موافقة قصده لقصده بإرادة الصانع جل وعلا وإجرائه العادة أنه يفعل ذلك عقيب صنع العبد في حيز قدرته لما مر من دليل استحالة ذلك أن يوجد بقدرة العبد، ثم يجب على هذا الاعتلال أن الله تعالى لو أجرى العادة عند مباينة العبد الحجر أن يخلق في الحجر صعودًا أو أجرى العادة أن يخلق عند قيامه حركة في الجبل أن يكون ذلك فعل العبد، فلو أجرى العادة أن يخلق الألم الشديد عند الضرب الخفيف، والألم الخفيف عند الضرب الشديد أن يكون كل واحد منهما مسببًا لما تقدم من السبب فإن مروا على هذا تجاهلوا ويلزموا أشياء يزداد ظهور تجاهلهم من نحو أن يقال: لو أجرى الله العادة بطيران الجبال في السماء ومرها في الهواء مر السحاب عقب إشارة الناس إليها: وبتهافت السماوات السبع عند أكل الناس ورجوعها إلى مكانها عند امتناعهم عن ذلك أن يكون كل ذلك صنع فاعلي هذه الأسباب وهذا تجاهل، وإن أبوا هذا نقضوا دليلهم، ويجب بهذا أن يكون حصول السمن في الطفل عند حسن قيام الحاضنة بتربيته وحصول السمن في الدابة عند قيام السائس بمصالحها ومراعاة وقت الغذاء والعلف والسقي لها من فعل الحاضنة والسائس وكذا حصول الزروع والبقول والرياحين وأغصان الأشجار والزراجين عند قيام الزراع بسقيها وتسميدها وتشذيبها يوجب أن يكون ذلك فعلاً لها، ويكون للعباد قدرة تخليق الأجسام وهذا الحاد ظاهر. وما ألزم من فصل الأحكام فكل ذلك متعلق باكتسابه ما حل بمحل قدرته من الفعل الذي جعله الله في العادة الجارية كالسبب لما يوجد في المحل، وإن كان لا صنع له فيه ثم الأمر في الشاهد بالتسديد والتعليم جائز، واللوم على تركه سائغ، وحمد الطبيب على الصحة الحاصلة في بدن من عالجه وملامته على ازدياد في المرض عقيب معالجته وكذا في حصول زيادة في ضوء البصر والظلمة فيه والقول بكون هذه الأشياء من فعل العباد خارج عن الإجماع، ولو ارتكبت المعتزلة هذه الفصول فقد قصدوا إثبات شركاء الله تعالى في جميع أجناس المخلوقات، وظني أنهم لا يبالون من هذا وأمثاله عند رجائهم ترويج باطلهم وتصحيح فاسدهم، وبالله العصمة والمعونة.
As for what they cling to of rational doubts, they are weak because their opponents do not accept the occurrence of that (effect) in accord with the intention of the intention of the Creator, may He be exalted, and His running of the custom that He does that after the act of the servant within the sphere of his power, due to the proof already given of the impossibility of that coming into existence by the power of the servant. Then, according to this reasoning, it would be necessary that if God, exalted, were to run the custom, when the servant separates the stone, that He create ascent in the stone, or run the custom that He create movement in the mountain when he stands, that that would be the act of the servant. And if He were to run the custom that He create severe pain upon light striking, and light pain upon severe striking, that each of them would be caused by the preceding cause. If they persist in this, they are ignorant and would be bound to things that increase the appearance of their ignorance, such as if it were said: 'If God were to run the custom of mountains flying in the sky and moving in the air like clouds following people's pointing to them, and of the falling of the seven heavens when people eat and their returning to their place when they abstain, that all that would be the act of the doers of these causes' — and this is ignorance. And if they reject this, they refute their own proof. And by this it would be necessary that the occurrence of fatness in the child upon the good care of the nurse in raising him, and the occurrence of fatness in the animal upon the care of the groom in its interests and observing the time of feeding and fodder and watering for it, be from the act of the nurse and the groom; likewise the occurrence of crops, vegetables, basil, tree branches, and grapevines upon the care of the farmer in watering, fertilizing, and pruning them would necessitate that that be an act of theirs, and that servants would have the power to create bodies — and this innovation is apparent. And whatever is required of the separation of judgments, all that is related to his acquisition of what occurs in the locus of his power of the act which God has made, in the ongoing custom, like the cause for what exists in the locus, even if he has no act in it. Then the matter in the witnessed world of guidance and instruction is permissible, and blame for leaving it is valid, and praising the physician for the health resulting in the body of the one he treated, and blaming him for the increase of illness after his treatment; likewise in the occurrence of increase in the light of sight and darkness in it. And the claim that these things are from the acts of servants is outside consensus. And if the Mu'tazila commit these chapters, they intend to establish partners with God in all kinds of created things, and I think they do not care about this and its likes when they hope to promote their falsehood and validate their corruption. And with God is protection and assistance.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts (tawallud). The author, Imam Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, begins by stating that the rational doubts they cling to are weak because their opponents do not accept that the effect occurs according to the custom of God after the servant's act. He then uses a reductio ad absurdum: if one accepts that God's custom is to create an effect after a human act, then absurd consequences follow, such as creating ascent in a stone when the servant separates it, or creating movement in a mountain when he stands. Further absurdities include severe pain from light striking and light pain from severe striking. If they persist, they become embroiled in even greater absurdities, like mountains flying or heavens falling upon eating. If they reject these, their own proof is undermined. The argument then extends to everyday examples: if a nurse's care causes fatness in a child, that would attribute creation to the nurse; similarly for a groom with animals and a farmer with plants. This would imply that servants have the power to create bodies, which is an innovation (hadath). The author then clarifies that all such effects are related to the servant's acquisition (kasb) of the act that God has made a cause in the ongoing custom, even though the servant has no creative act in the effect. In the witnessed world, guidance, blame, and praise are permissible, but claiming these are from the servant's act alone violates consensus. The passage concludes by accusing the Mu'tazila of intending to establish partners with God in creation, and that they persist despite obvious absurdity.

Key Terms
شبه معقولة
إجراء العادة
توليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts is a rational explanation of causality.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the theory leads to logical absurdities and self-contradiction, thus it is not rational.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what would follow if God's custom were to create movement in a mountain when a servant stands?
A) The mountain moves by itself.
B) The servant would be the doer of the movement.
C) The movement is impossible.
D) God creates the movement directly without any human role.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts leads to logical absurdities and self-refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a clear cause and effect in your life (e.g., working yields money), do you ever feel that your action alone produced the result, forgetting that God could have made it otherwise? How does this passage challenge that feeling?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted on a rational system where human acts generate effects. Are there areas in your life where you cling to your own 'rational' explanations of how things work, rather than submitting to God's absolute power and wisdom? What would it take to let go of that control?
?
If God could make mountains fly upon a person's pointing, then any outcome in your life is directly from Him, not from your effort. Does this truth frighten you or liberate you? How will you live differently today because of it?
