Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Maturidi Doctrine of Acquisition (Kasb) and Refutation of Human Creation
Al-Nasafi uses everyday examples to refute Mu'tazilite theory and affirms acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts by showing it implies human creation and then affirming the Maturidi doctrine of acquisition (kasb) as the correct understanding of causality.

Core Idea
If human acts could generate effects, then humans would be creators of bodies, but in reality, effects are acquired by humans and created by God.

The Mu'tazilite view, if consistently applied, would lead to the absurd conclusion that humans create bodies, such as a nurse causing fatness in a child or a farmer growing crops. Al-Nasafi uses these everyday examples to demonstrate the theological impossibility of human creation, and then clarifies the Maturidi position: the servant acquires the act, but God creates the effect through His custom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on everyday examples
Read the Arabic passage that extends the absurdity to examples from daily life: nurse, groom, farmer. Note how al-Nasafi argues that accepting the Mu'tazilite view would imply these humans create bodies.
These examples are chosen to show the practical absurdity of the theory.
2
Understand the absurd implication of human creation
Al-Nasafi argues that if effects follow human acts by God's custom, then nurses, grooms, and farmers would be considered creators of bodies, which is an innovation (hadath) and violates tawhid.
This highlights the theological danger of the Mu'tazilite theory.
3
Learn the concept of acquisition (kasb)
Al-Nasafi presents the positive Maturidi doctrine: the servant acquires (yaktasibu) the act that God has made a cause in His custom. The effect is related to the servant's acquisition, but God alone creates it.
Acquisition reconciles human responsibility with divine omnipotence.
4
See the final accusation of shirk
Al-Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazilite view, pushed to its logical conclusion, amounts to establishing partners with God in creation. This is a strong condemnation.
The lesson ends with a clear warning against the implications of the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما تعلقوا به من الشبه المعقولة فواهية لما أن خصومهم لا يسلمون حصول ذلك على موافقة قصده لقصده بإرادة الصانع جل وعلا وإجرائه العادة أنه يفعل ذلك عقيب صنع العبد في حيز قدرته لما مر من دليل استحالة ذلك أن يوجد بقدرة العبد، ثم يجب على هذا الاعتلال أن الله تعالى لو أجرى العادة عند مباينة العبد الحجر أن يخلق في الحجر صعودًا أو أجرى العادة أن يخلق عند قيامه حركة في الجبل أن يكون ذلك فعل العبد، فلو أجرى العادة أن يخلق الألم الشديد عند الضرب الخفيف، والألم الخفيف عند الضرب الشديد أن يكون كل واحد منهما مسببًا لما تقدم من السبب فإن مروا على هذا تجاهلوا ويلزموا أشياء يزداد ظهور تجاهلهم من نحو أن يقال: لو أجرى الله العادة بطيران الجبال في السماء ومرها في الهواء مر السحاب عقب إشارة الناس إليها: وبتهافت السماوات السبع عند أكل الناس ورجوعها إلى مكانها عند امتناعهم عن ذلك أن يكون كل ذلك صنع فاعلي هذه الأسباب وهذا تجاهل، وإن أبوا هذا نقضوا دليلهم، ويجب بهذا أن يكون حصول السمن في الطفل عند حسن قيام الحاضنة بتربيته وحصول السمن في الدابة عند قيام السائس بمصالحها ومراعاة وقت الغذاء والعلف والسقي لها من فعل الحاضنة والسائس وكذا حصول الزروع والبقول والرياحين وأغصان الأشجار والزراجين عند قيام الزراع بسقيها وتسميدها وتشذيبها يوجب أن يكون ذلك فعلاً لها، ويكون للعباد قدرة تخليق الأجسام وهذا الحاد ظاهر. وما ألزم من فصل الأحكام فكل ذلك متعلق باكتسابه ما حل بمحل قدرته من الفعل الذي جعله الله في العادة الجارية كالسبب لما يوجد في المحل، وإن كان لا صنع له فيه ثم الأمر في الشاهد بالتسديد والتعليم جائز، واللوم على تركه سائغ، وحمد الطبيب على الصحة الحاصلة في بدن من عالجه وملامته على ازدياد في المرض عقيب معالجته وكذا في حصول زيادة في ضوء البصر والظلمة فيه والقول بكون هذه الأشياء من فعل العباد خارج عن الإجماع، ولو ارتكبت المعتزلة هذه الفصول فقد قصدوا إثبات شركاء الله تعالى في جميع أجناس المخلوقات، وظني أنهم لا يبالون من هذا وأمثاله عند رجائهم ترويج باطلهم وتصحيح فاسدهم، وبالله العصمة والمعونة.
As for what they cling to of rational doubts, they are weak because their opponents do not accept the occurrence of that (effect) in accord with the intention of the intention of the Creator, may He be exalted, and His running of the custom that He does that after the act of the servant within the sphere of his power, due to the proof already given of the impossibility of that coming into existence by the power of the servant. Then, according to this reasoning, it would be necessary that if God, exalted, were to run the custom, when the servant separates the stone, that He create ascent in the stone, or run the custom that He create movement in the mountain when he stands, that that would be the act of the servant. And if He were to run the custom that He create severe pain upon light striking, and light pain upon severe striking, that each of them would be caused by the preceding cause. If they persist in this, they are ignorant and would be bound to things that increase the appearance of their ignorance, such as if it were said: 'If God were to run the custom of mountains flying in the sky and moving in the air like clouds following people's pointing to them, and of the falling of the seven heavens when people eat and their returning to their place when they abstain, that all that would be the act of the doers of these causes' — and this is ignorance. And if they reject this, they refute their own proof. And by this it would be necessary that the occurrence of fatness in the child upon the good care of the nurse in raising him, and the occurrence of fatness in the animal upon the care of the groom in its interests and observing the time of feeding and fodder and watering for it, be from the act of the nurse and the groom; likewise the occurrence of crops, vegetables, basil, tree branches, and grapevines upon the care of the farmer in watering, fertilizing, and pruning them would necessitate that that be an act of theirs, and that servants would have the power to create bodies — and this innovation is apparent. And whatever is required of the separation of judgments, all that is related to his acquisition of what occurs in the locus of his power of the act which God has made, in the ongoing custom, like the cause for what exists in the locus, even if he has no act in it. Then the matter in the witnessed world of guidance and instruction is permissible, and blame for leaving it is valid, and praising the physician for the health resulting in the body of the one he treated, and blaming him for the increase of illness after his treatment; likewise in the occurrence of increase in the light of sight and darkness in it. And the claim that these things are from the acts of servants is outside consensus. And if the Mu'tazila commit these chapters, they intend to establish partners with God in all kinds of created things, and I think they do not care about this and its likes when they hope to promote their falsehood and validate their corruption. And with God is protection and assistance.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite theory of generated acts (tawallud). The author, Imam Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, begins by stating that the rational doubts they cling to are weak because their opponents do not accept that the effect occurs according to the custom of God after the servant's act. He then uses a reductio ad absurdum: if one accepts that God's custom is to create an effect after a human act, then absurd consequences follow, such as creating ascent in a stone when the servant separates it, or creating movement in a mountain when he stands. Further absurdities include severe pain from light striking and light pain from severe striking. If they persist, they become embroiled in even greater absurdities, like mountains flying or heavens falling upon eating. If they reject these, their own proof is undermined. The argument then extends to everyday examples: if a nurse's care causes fatness in a child, that would attribute creation to the nurse; similarly for a groom with animals and a farmer with plants. This would imply that servants have the power to create bodies, which is an innovation (hadath). The author then clarifies that all such effects are related to the servant's acquisition (kasb) of the act that God has made a cause in the ongoing custom, even though the servant has no creative act in the effect. In the witnessed world, guidance, blame, and praise are permissible, but claiming these are from the servant's act alone violates consensus. The passage concludes by accusing the Mu'tazila of intending to establish partners with God in creation, and that they persist despite obvious absurdity.

Key Terms
فعل العبد
قدرة العبد
اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human acts can create effects (e.g., a farmer causes crops to grow).
Correction: Al-Nasafi argues that effects are acquired by humans but created by God alone. Humans do not have creative power.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the correct relationship between a human act and its effect?
A) The human creates the effect through his own power.
B) The effect is an acquisition by the human, created by God.
C) The effect is entirely from God with no human role.
D) The human and God cooperate in creating the effect.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi doctrine of acquisition affirms that humans are responsible for their acts, but God alone creates the effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something you worked hard for, do you truly believe that God alone created that success, or does your heart secretly attribute the outcome to your own effort as its ultimate cause? How does this lesson expose that subtle idolatry?
?
Think of a failure or a mistake you have made. Do you tend to blame yourself as if you had full creative power over the outcome, or do you acknowledge that God alone controls the results? What would it mean for you to accept your responsibility for the act while trusting God's sole control over its effect?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed to defend God's justice but ended up establishing partners in creation. Are there areas in your life where your desire to feel in control or to be just leads you to attribute creative power to yourself or others? Will you repent of that and affirm that only God creates?

Refuting Tawallud: Human Acts Do Not Create Effects
Refutation of Mu'tazila theory of tawallud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi refutation of the Mu'tazila theory of tawallud defends divine oneness by denying that human actions create their apparent effects.

Core Idea
Human actions are causes in custom, not creators of effects.

The Mu'tazila claimed that human actions generate their effects in external objects, like a physician causing health. This passage from Imam al-Nasafi refutes that view, showing it leads to shirk. We'll explore his argument that while actions are customarily linked to effects, only God creates them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the author begins by defining the customary course and then gives examples to refute tawallud.
Focus on the logical flow from description to accusation.
2
Understand the concept of al-’ādah al-jāriyah
Identify that the author accepts a customary connection but denies creative agency. This is key to the Maturidi position on causality.
3
Analyze the examples
The author gives examples of the physician, guidance/teaching, and light/darkness. For each, note that human action is followed by an effect, but the human does not create it.
These examples illustrate the difference between customary cause and actual creation.
4
Grasp the theological charge
The author concludes that the Mu'tazila position implies partners for God. Reflect on why attributing creation to humans is considered shirk.
This shows the high stakes of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في العادة الجارية كالسبب لما يوجد في المحل، وإن كان لا صنع له فيه ثم الأمر في الشاهد بالتسديد والتعليم جائز، واللوم على تركه سائغ، وحمد الطبيب على الصحة الحاصلة في بدن من عالجه وملامته على ازدياد في المرض عقيب معالجته وكذا في حصول زيادة في ضوء البصر والظلمة فيه والقول بكون هذه الأشياء من فعل العباد خارج عن الإجماع، ولو ارتكبت المعتزلة هذه الفصول فقد قصدوا إثبات شركاء الله تعالى في جميع أجناس المخلوقات، وظني أنهم لا يبالون من هذا وأمثاله عند رجائهم ترويج باطلهم وتصحيح فاسدهم، وبالله العصمة والمعونة.
In the customary course (al-’ādah al-jāriyah) it is like a cause (sabab) for what occurs in the subject, even though it has no agency in it. Then the command in the witnessed realm regarding guidance and teaching is permissible, and blame for abandoning it is valid. Praise of the physician for the health occurring in the body of the one he treated, and blame of him for the increase in illness following his treatment, and likewise regarding the occurrence of increase in the light of sight and darkness in it. The statement that these things are from the actions of servants goes against consensus. If the Mu‘tazila have committed these chapters, they intended to establish partners for God Almighty in all kinds of created things, and I think they do not care about this and the like when they hope to promote their falsehood and correct their corrupt belief. And with God is protection and assistance.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu‘tazila theory of tawallud (generation of effects). The author argues against the idea that human actions generate effects in external objects, such as the physician's act causing health or the break causing the breaking of a solid body. He first describes the customary course (al-’ādah al-jāriyah) where the act appears causally linked to the effect, but emphasizes that the act has no actual agency (lā ṣun‘a lahu) in the effect; rather, God creates the effect following the act. He notes that in the observable world (al-shāhid), commanding guidance and teaching (al-tasdīd wa al-ta‘līm) is permissible, and blame for abandoning it is valid, indicating that humans are accountable for their actions without being the creators of their outcomes. He gives examples: the physician is praised for the health that occurs in the patient's body, and blamed for the worsening of illness after treatment—yet the health or illness is not his creation; it is from God. Similarly, the increase or decrease of light in the eye is not caused by the person. The author states that the claim that these things are the actions of servants (af‘āl al-‘ibād) violates consensus (ijmā‘). He then accuses the Mu‘tazila of intending, through such chapters, to establish partners for God in all kinds of created things—since they attribute effects to creatures as true creators (khāliqūn). He concludes that he believes they do not care about this serious implication as long as they can promote their falsehood and preserve their corrupt school. The passage ends with a plea for divine protection and assistance.

Key Terms
التولد
العادة الجارية
الإجماع
السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that human actions actually create their effects in external objects, e.g., the physician creates health.
Correction: The author asserts that while actions are customarily followed by effects, the effect is created by God alone; the human act has no creative agency.

Checkpoint
What is the main charge the author levels against the Mu‘tazila in this passage?
A) They deny the existence of God.
B) They intend to establish partners for God in all kinds of created things.
C) They claim that the physician creates health.
D) They reject the idea of customary causality.

Key Takeaway
Human actions are customary causes, not creators; claiming otherwise implies partners for God and violates consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a time when you blamed yourself harshly for a mistake. In this lesson, the physician is blamed but not the creator of illness. Who truly has power over outcomes? How should that affect your self-condemnation?
?
The Mu‘tazila thought they were defending human accountability, but the author says their theory leads to shirk. Is there any belief you hold about your own power that might, if examined, attribute to yourself something that belongs only to Allah? What would it take to uproot that?

How Actions Are Attributed to God Despite Human Agency
Explains how actions are attributed to God despite human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why actions are attributed to God in Islamic theology, even though He does not directly perform them, and how this relates to human free will and divine decree.

Core Idea
Actions are attributed to God through the conditions He creates, not through direct involvement.

This lesson explores a subtle point in Islamic theology: how can actions be attributed to God when humans perform them? The author uses Quranic examples to show that attribution occurs because God provides the circumstances—like respite and blessings—that people misinterpret as approval. This clarifies the Maturidi position on divine decree and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument and the Quranic examples.
The passage uses a chain of reasoning: actions exist at the time of obligation, so they are attributed to God, similar to how increase in faith is attributed to the Qur'an.
2
Identify the Quranic examples
List the examples given: Qur'an increases faith or abomination, supplication increases turbulence, idols lead astray. Understand how each illustrates attribution without direct action.
The author shows that attribution can be metaphorical or causal, not literal.
3
Understand the theological reasoning
Focus on the argument: God creates conditions (blessings, respite) that people misinterpret as approval, leading them to persist in their actions. Hence, actions are attributed to God by way of causality, not direct creation.
This safeguards God's transcendence while explaining the source of human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما وجدت عند التكليف فتضاف إليه، وإن لم يكن منه فيها فعل كما يضاف إلى القرآن: فَزَادَهُمْ إِيمَانًا فَزَادَتْهُمْ رِجْسًا وإلى الدعاء أنه: وَزَادَهُمْ تَفُورًا وإلى الأصنام: إِنَّهُنَّ أَضْلَلْنَ كَثِيرًا مِّنَ النَّاسِ وقد يقولون: إنها تضاف إليه لأنها حملت عقيب أحوال أوجدها الله تعالى تصير تلك الأحوال حاملة لهم عليها من نحو الإنعام والإمهال مع علمهم بقدرته، والتقرر عندهم أن من عصى من له القدرة عليه لا يمهله بل يعالجه بالتعذيب والتنكيل، ثم الله تعالى لم يعاقبهم بل أمهلهم، ولم يكتف بالإمهال حتى أدر عليهم سوابغ نعمه وتركهم يتقلبون في آثار أفضاله ويترددون في أثناء منته وإنعامه، فظنوا أن ذلك لرضا الله تعالى بصنيعهم واستحسانه ما هم عليه من النحل والمذاهب حتى ادعوا الأمر فقالوا والله أمرنا بها، فصار ذلك مبعثةً لهم على التمسك بما هم عليه فأضيف إليه كما يضاف إلى الدنيا الغرور لاغترار الناس بها لما هي عليه من حالة الزهرة والبهجة
And they only exist at the time of obligation (taklīf) so they are attributed to Him, even though there is no act from Him in them, just as increase in faith or abomination is attributed to the Qur'an, and to supplication that it increased them in turbulence, and to idols: 'They have led many people astray.' And they may say: They are attributed to Him because they occur subsequent to states that God Almighty created, making those states a cause for them, such as bestowal of favors and respite while they know His power, and it is established with them that whoever disobeys one who has power over him will not give him respite but will punish and torment him. Then God Almighty did not punish them but gave them respite, and was not content with respite until He poured upon them abundant blessings and left them turning about in the traces of His favors and moving about in the midst of His bounty and favor. So they thought that this was due to God's pleasure with their deed and His approval of their religions and doctrines, until they claimed the command and said: 'By God, He commanded us to do it.' So that became a motivation for them to cling to what they were upon, so it was attributed to Him just as worldly life is attributed to delusion because people are deluded by it due to its state of splendor and adornment.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage explains a key theological principle: actions are attributed to God even though He does not directly perform them, because they occur within a context of conditions He creates. The author gives several Quranic examples: increase in faith is attributed to the Qur'an, increase in abomination to the same revelation, turbulence to supplication, and misguidance to idols. The reasoning is that these actions arise after God provides favorable circumstances—such as blessings and respite—which people misinterpret as divine approval. Because God does not immediately punish them, they assume He is pleased with their deeds, leading them to persist and even claim divine command. Thus, the actions are attributed to God by way of causality, not direct agency, similar to how the world is called 'deceptive' because it deludes people through its attractive appearance.

تخليق

هداية
إضلال

A) Because God creates all human actions directly.
B) Because they occur after conditions God creates, which people misinterpret.
C) Because the actions are good in themselves.
D) Because God commands every action.

Key Takeaway
Actions are attributed to God because He creates the circumstances that lead to them, not because He performs them directly.

Refuting the Medicine Analogy: Divine Cause vs. Natural Custom
Refutation of the medicine analogy used by the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy from medicine to divine acts fails due to its reliance on naturalistic causality, and how the occasionalist view attributes all benefit and harm to God alone.

Core Idea
God alone causes benefit and harm; nature is just His custom.

The opponent tries to defend the possibility of God creating evil by drawing an analogy from medicine: just as combining beneficial medicines in excess causes harm, so too God's combining beneficial things can cause corruption. But this analogy rests on the assumption that medicines have inherent causal powers. In this passage, the author refutes that assumption, redirecting causality entirely to God and explaining that what we call 'natural' is merely His established custom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the two-part structure: an objection and then the author's response.
2
Understand the opponent's objection
Identify the analogy: just as a certain amount of medicine is beneficial, but too much becomes harmful, so God's acts can combine benefits to produce harm.
The opponent uses this to justify the possibility of evil from God.
3
Identify the author's counter-argument
The author points out that this analogy presupposes that medicines have inherent natures (ṭabā'iʿ) that cause benefit or harm. In the Islamic view, only God causes benefit and harm.
The author introduces the concept of 'custom' (ʿāda) — God consistently creates benefit in certain contexts, but He is not bound by natures.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this refutation upholds divine omnipotence and sovereignty. If things had inherent causal powers, God's power would be limited. The occasionalist framework affirms that God is the sole agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : وكيف يوجب انضمام ما يوجب الصلاح إلى ما يوجب مفسدة، ولو جاز أن يحصل الفساد بالصلاح لجاز أن يحصل الصلاح بالفساد، وكل شيء يسبب ضده، وهذا خروج عن المعقول.
فإن قالوا مثل هذا جائز فإن قدرًا من الدواء نافع ومثل قدره من عين ذلك الدواء نافع، وكذا الثالث والرابع إلى ما لا نهاية له لو جمع بين ما هو نافع وبين ما هو نافع حتى زاد على القدرة لأوجب المضرة دون المنفعة فكذا هذا.
قلنا هذا الكلام بني على قواعد أهل الطبائع حيث يجعلون الأدوية نافعة والسموم ضارة، وعندنا الضار النافع هو الله تعالى غير أنه أجرى العادة أنه ينفع
Then we say: How is it that joining what causes benefit to what causes corruption necessitates corruption? And if it were permissible that corruption arises from benefit, then it would be permissible that benefit arises from corruption. And everything causes its opposite. This is outside the realm of reason. If they say: Such a thing is permissible, for a certain amount of medicine is beneficial, and the same amount of the same medicine is beneficial, and likewise the third and fourth up to infinity; if one were to combine what is beneficial with what is beneficial until it exceeds the capacity, it would necessitate harm without benefit. So likewise this. We say: This speech is built on the principles of the naturalists, who consider medicines beneficial and poisons harmful. According to us, the one who harms and benefits is God, exalted, except that He has established the custom that it benefits.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage refutes the opponent's objection using an analogy from medicine. First, the author argues that combining beneficial and corruptive things to produce corruption is irrational; it would imply a reversal of causality. The opponent then gives an example: a certain dose of medicine is beneficial, but if too much is taken, it becomes harmful. The author responds that this analogy is based on the presuppositions of the naturalists (ahl al-ṭabā'iʿ), who believe that medicines have inherent beneficial properties and poisons have inherent harmful properties. In contrast, the Islamic position is that benefit and harm come solely from God, not from the nature of things. God has merely established a custom (ʿāda) that certain things are beneficial, but He is the true agent. Thus, the analogy fails because it assumes a causal efficacy in created things that belongs only to God.

Key Terms
الطبائع
العادة
ضار
نافع
انضمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the occasionalist denial of natural causality means there is no regularity or predictability in the world.
Correction: The passage affirms that God has established a custom (ʿāda) so that certain things are consistently beneficial or harmful; this provides regularity without attributing independent causal power to objects.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the fundamental flaw in the opponent's medicine analogy?
A) It assumes medicines have inherent natures that cause benefit or harm
B) It assumes that God's power is limited
C) It assumes that benefit and harm can come from God
D) It assumes that everything causes its opposite

Key Takeaway
The medicine analogy fails because it attributes inherent causal powers to created things, whereas in truth only God causes benefit and harm, acting through His custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you take medicine and recover, do you truly believe that God alone caused the healing, or does a part of your heart still attribute power to the medicine itself? How can you train your heart to see God's custom behind every apparent cause?
?
If all benefit and harm come only from God, why do you still fear harmful things (like accidents or illness) as if they have independent power? What would it take for you to trust God's custom completely and fear only Him?
?
This lesson shows that attributing inherent power to created things is a subtle form of shirk. Are there any areas in your life—your job, your health, your relationships—where you unconsciously rely on 'natural causes' rather than on God alone? Write down one such area and commit to changing your perspective this week.

Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
Physical Benefit Has Measured Limits
Al-Maturidi explains the mechanism of physical benefit and its limit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why adding a measured beneficial quantity to itself causes harm, and how this refutes the analogy to divine acts.

Core Idea
Physical benefit is removal of a measured deficiency; excess beyond the measure causes harm.

Al-Maturidi addresses an objection that combining two benefits causes harm, as seen with medicine. He explains that benefit is defined as removing a specific deficiency, and each deficiency has a precisely measured remedy. Adding more than that remedy does not add benefit but creates a new imbalance, leading to harm. This lesson unpacks that physical principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on physical benefit
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting how Al-Maturidi defines benefit as the removal of a need (deficiency).
The example of fever and cooling medicine is central.
2
Identify the concept of measured quantity (قدر مقدر)
Notice that every deficiency has a specific measured quantity of remedy. This quantity is known and precise.
3
Understand why excess is harmful
Reflect on why adding more than the measured quantity is not beneficial. It introduces a new imbalance (e.g., excessive cooling), causing harm.
Harm arises from adding what is not beneficial (the excess), not from adding benefit to benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عند شرب قدر من الدواء ويضر عند شرب أكثر منه، ولو كان أجرى العادة على غير هذا الوجه لكان جائزاً، ولو قلب الآن العادة كان أيضا في حد الممكنات فلا نسلم إذا إن انضمام ما هو نافع إلى ما هو نافع يوجب المضرة، وهذا أيضا قلب المعقول كالأول. بهذا الجواب دفع أبو الحسن الأشعري كلامهم وأجاب الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي عن هذا الكلام فقال : إن انضم النفع إلى النفع لا يضر إنما يضر ضم ما ليس ينفع إلى النفع في التقدير وبه تقدير النفع للعليل، وها هنا القول إنه لا غاية لما به الصلاح وثم لما به النفع غاية؛ لذلك اختلف الأمران، وشرح هذا الكلام والكشف عنه: أن النفع في الحقيقة دفع الحاجة، والحاجة نقص يتمكن في الذات، وذلك كنقص الجوع وألم البرد والعلة الحادثة، ثم كل نقص في ذاته مقدر فإن الحمى مثلاً تحدث من غلبة الحرارة على البدن ولتلك الغلبة وزوال مزاج البدن عن الاعتدال، الذي أوجب هذا المرض قدر مقدر قد يكون قويًا وقد يكون ضعيفا، فقدر ما يغلب تلك الحرارة من المبردات ويعيد المزاج إلى الاعتدال معلوم، فإذا وجد تناول ذلك القدر في نفسه نفع على معنى أنه دفع الحرارة الغالبة التي أوجبت في البدن بعض المرض وزوال الاعتدال فإذا ضم إلى هذا القدر ما يزيد عليه فتلك الزيادة ليس عملها في رفع الحرارة التي حصل بها المرض؛ لأن ذلك حصل بالقدر المقدر بل عمل الزيادة في إثبات زيادة تبريد يزيل الاعتدال إذ فعله التبريد لا النفع وإنما يحصل النفع والضرر لاختلاف حال المحل فيحدث به مرض آخر ويحتاج إلى دفعه فكانت الزيادة ضارة لا نافعة، فأما إلى القدر الذي يقاوم الحرارة فكل جزء منه يدفع شيئًا من أجزاء الحرارة فيكون دافعا للبعض فيكون نافعًا فهذا معنى قوله - رحمه الله -: أن ضم النفع إلى النفع لا يضر إنما يضر ضم ما ليس ينفع إلى النفع فأما ما هو صلاح في الدين فليس له حد ولا نهاية إذ ما من صلاح إلا ويتوهم ما هو أصلح منه ، فإذا لم يكن للصلاح في نفسه نهاية لا يتصور حصول ضد الصلاح بالمجاوزة عن القدر المقدر له بل كلما ضم صلاح إلى صلاح ازداد الصلاح، وإذا لم يكن للصلاح نهاية لم يكن لأسباب الصلاح نهاية فمن أثبت لأسباب الصلاح الداخلة تحت قدرة الباري جل وعلا نهاية فقد قال بنهاية مقدوره وهو إثبات العجز وهو باطل، وليس هذا من الشيخ أبي منصور بتسليم أن الدواء نافع بل النافع هو الله تعالى إلا أنه جعل سببًا للنفع غير أنه - من دأبه أنه كثيرًا ما يسلم تسليم جدل ما لا يقول به ثم يبين مع فساد استدلال الخصوم ليكون ذلك أبين الضلالتهم وجهالتهم وأغيظ لهم، وكيف نسلم ذلك وقد ثبت أن الضار والنافع في الحقيقة هو الله تعالى، وقد ذكر ذلك – رحمه الله - في مواضع من كتبه وقد روى عن أبي بكر الصديق عنه أنه قال : الطبيب أمرضني، وروى عن عمر الله أنه نهى عن أكل الجبن وقيل له : إنه يضر؟ فقال: لو كنت أعلم أنه يضرني لعبدته وهذا إخبار منه الله أن الضار في الحقيقة هو الله ، تعالى إلا أنه جرى معهم في الظاهر وأراهم بطلان ما يتعلقون به لو كان الأمر على ما يزعمون وهذا طريقه لأهل النظر مسلوكة، والله الموفق. وبما مر ذلك أن الصلاح في نفسه يتزايد يظهر بطلان تشبيههم تعجيز الله تعالى
When drinking a quantity of medicine it benefits and when drinking more than it harms, and if the habit were run on a different way it would be permissible, and if the habit were now reversed it would also be in the realm of possibilities. So we do not concede that joining what is beneficial to what is beneficial necessitates harm, and this is also a reversal of the rational like the first. With this answer Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari repelled their speech, and the Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi answered this speech saying: if benefit is joined to benefit it does not harm, rather harm is caused by joining what is not beneficial to the beneficial in the estimation, and by it is estimation of benefit for the sick. And here the statement is that there is no end for that by which is righteousness, and then there is an end for that by which is benefit; therefore the two matters differ. And explanation of this speech and its disclosure: that benefit in reality is the removal of need, and need is a deficiency that takes hold in the essence, such as the deficiency of hunger, the pain of cold, and an accidental illness. Then every deficiency in its essence is measured: for fever, for example, arises from the dominance of heat over the body, and for that dominance and removal of the body's temperament from balance, which necessitated this illness, there is a measured quantity that may be strong or weak. So the quantity that overcomes that heat from coolants and restores the temperament to balance is known. So if that quantity is taken, it is beneficial in itself in the sense that it repels the dominant heat that caused some illness and loss of balance in the body. Then if what exceeds it is joined to this quantity, that increase has no effect in removing the heat that caused the illness, because that was achieved by the measured quantity, rather the effect of the increase is in establishing additional cooling that removes balance, since its action is cooling not benefit, and benefit and harm only occur due to the differing condition of the subject, so it causes another illness and needs its removal, so the increase is harmful not beneficial. As for the quantity that counteracts the heat, every part of it repels some of the parts of the heat, so it is a repeller of some, so it is beneficial. So this is the meaning of his saying, may God have mercy on him: that joining benefit to benefit does not harm, rather harm is caused by joining what is not beneficial to the beneficial. As for what is righteousness in religion, it has no limit or end, since there is no righteousness except that one can conceive of what is more righteous than it. So when righteousness in itself has no end, it is not conceivable that the opposite of righteousness occurs by exceeding the measured quantity for it. Rather, whenever righteousness is joined to righteousness, righteousness increases. And if righteousness has no end, then the causes of righteousness have no end. So whoever posits an end for the causes of righteousness that fall under the power of God, Exalted and Majestic, has spoken of an end to His power, and that is the affirmation of incapacity, which is false. And this from Shaykh Abu Mansur is not a concession that medicine is beneficial, rather the Benefactor is God, but He made it a cause for benefit, except that – it is his habit that he often concedes in dialectical concession what he does not affirm, then he clarifies along with the corruption of the opponents' inference to make their misguidance and ignorance clearer and more vexing to them. And how could we concede that when it is established that the Harmer and Benefactor in reality is God? And he, may God have mercy on him, has mentioned that in places in his books, and it is narrated from Abu Bakr al-Siddiq that he said: 'The physician made me ill,' and it is narrated from Umar, may God be pleased with him, that he forbade eating cheese, and it was said to him: 'It is harmful?' He said: 'If I knew that it harms me, I would worship it.' And this is information from him that the Harmer in reality is God. However, he went along with them in appearance and showed them the falsehood of what they cling to, if the matter were as they claim. And this method is followed by the people of reflection. And God is the Grantor of success. And by what passed, that righteousness in itself increases shows the falsehood of their comparison of the incapacity of God.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The opponents argue that if two beneficial things are combined, they cause harm, as with medicine where a moderate dose is beneficial but a larger dose is harmful. They use this analogy to argue against the possibility of infinite goodness from God. Al-Maturidi first shows that the physical realm provides a clear distinction: benefit is the removal of a measured deficiency. Each deficiency (hunger, illness) has a specific quantity of remedy. Adding more beyond that quantity does not add benefit but introduces a new imbalance, causing harm. Thus, harm arises not from adding benefit to benefit, but from adding what is not beneficial (the excess) to the beneficial. He then contrasts this with religious goodness (al-salāh fī al-dīn): it has no limit or end, because for any good one can conceive of a greater good. Therefore, adding goodness to goodness always increases goodness, never causing harm. Consequently, positing a limit to the causes of goodness under God's power would imply a limit to God's power, which is false (bāṭil). The passage concludes by clarifying that Al-Maturidi does not actually concede that medicine is inherently beneficial—the true Benefactor and Harmer is God alone. He uses dialectical concession to expose the fallacy of the opponents' reasoning, citing sayings of the early caliphs to reinforce that God alone is the real agent of benefit and harm.

Key Terms
نفع
ضر
قدر مقدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that combining two beneficial things always remains beneficial without limit.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that physical benefit is tied to a measured quantity; exceeding that measure turns the addition into harm.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Maturidi, why does taking more than the measured quantity of medicine cause harm?
A) Because benefit is inherently harmful when combined.
B) Because the excess does not remove the original deficiency but creates a new imbalance.
C) Because God prevents two benefits from being combined.
D) Because the opponents are always correct.

Key Takeaway
In physical matters, benefit is the removal of a measured deficiency; exceeding the measure turns the addition into harm, not benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you constantly seek 'more'—whether in health, wealth, or worship. Are you sure you are aiming for the measured benefit that removes a real deficiency, or are you adding excess that may be causing harm? How will you recalibrate?
?
The passage teaches that adding beneficial things beyond their limit creates a new deficiency. When have you experienced this in your spiritual life—such as over-praying or over-fasting leading to burnout or neglect of other duties? What would the correct measure look like for you?
?
Al-Maturidi says that benefit is the removal of a specific deficiency. What is the deepest deficiency in your heart (e.g., lack of sincerity, reliance on self), and what is the precise measure of spiritual practice that would address it without leading to excess?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Infinity of Religious Goodness and God as the True Agent
Al-Maturidi contrasts infinite religious goodness with limited physical benefit, and defends divine transcendence through dialectical method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why religious goodness has no limit, and why God alone is the true Benefactor and Harmer, and how Al-Maturidi uses dialectical method to refute opponents.

Core Idea
Religious goodness has no end; setting a limit to God's power is false.

Having refuted the physical analogy, Al-Maturidi now contrasts it with religious goodness (al-ṣalāḥ fī al-dīn). He argues that unlike physical benefit, religious goodness has no upper limit because one can always conceive of a greater good. Therefore, adding goodness to goodness always increases it, never causes harm. This leads to the conclusion that there is no limit to God's power to create goodness. Finally, he clarifies that his concession of medicine being beneficial was dialectical, not doctrinal; God alone is the true cause of benefit and harm, as proven by reports from the early caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on religious goodness
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the contrast between physical benefit and religious goodness.
Notice the phrase 'لا غاية لما به الصلاح' (no end for righteousness).
2
Understand the argument against limiting God's power
Reflect on why positing a limit to righteousness would imply a limit to God's power, which is false.
Al-Maturidi considers this an affirmation of incapacity (ta‘jīz).
3
Appreciate the dialectical method and true doctrine
Al-Maturidi clarifies that his earlier concession was for argument's sake. The true belief is that God alone is the Benefactor and Harmer.
The citations from Abu Bakr and Umar reinforce this point.

Why does Al-Maturidi argue that religious goodness has no limit?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where medical treatment seems ineffective, do you truly believe that God alone is the Harmer and Benefactor, or do you find yourself feeling desperate about the apparent failure of causes? How can you internalize Al-Maturidi’s dialectical method to guard your faith from implicit attribution of power to other than God?
?
The passage says religious goodness has no limit. If you sincerely believed that, how would it change your effort in seeking closeness to God? Are you settling for a limited amount of worship, fearing that more would somehow be too much?
?
Al-Maturidi conceded to opponents only in appearance to expose their falsehood. In your discussions with people who deny God's sole agency, do you sometimes compromise your belief just to avoid conflict? What would it look like to follow his method of outwardly accommodating while inwardly holding the truth, and then gently revealing the fallacy?
